Introduction*
Bob Becking and Hans M. Barstad
The fifth meeting of the Edinburgh Prophecy Network took place in the city of Utrecht, the Netherlands. The meeting was made possible by a generous gift from the Onderzoeksinstituut voor Filosofie en Religiestudies1 of the Faculty of Humanities of Utrecht University. We would like to thank Dr Christoph Baumgartner, associate professor for Ethics and co-chair of this institute for his willingness to support our meeting. Thanks to this financial support the expenses of two key-note speakers could be taken care of. The topic of this meeting was formulated as: Prophets and Prophecy in Stories. This topic was chosen out of two observations:
– In the Hebrew Bible an interesting part of our knowledge of prophecy is based on the parts prophets play in various stories. – The Hebrew Bible does not stand in isolation, but is part of a cultural continuum in which – with regional and temporal variations – stories on prophets were narrated and written down.
The aim of our meeting was to scrutinize the image of prophecy through the analysis of the narrative processes. Our expectation was that despite all sorts of variations, some features would become clear of the anthropological phenomenon of prophecy and its function in narratives. We are glad that a great majority of colleagues who presented a paper were willing to contribute to this volume.2 The volume opens with an outlook at the Context of Scripture. Meindert Dijkstra, “Prophets, Men of God, Wise Women: Dreams and Prophecies in Hittite Stories”, analyses a statement by Billie Jeans Collins. She argued that although prophets were active in Anatolia, we hardly know who they were and how they worked.3 She obviously thinks of prophets in the ‘Biblical’ sense as divine messengers different from male and female diviners and their methods
* We would like to thank the anonymous peer-reviewer for the many valuable remarks made on the contents of the various contributions. 1 Research Institute for Philosophy and Religious Studies. 2 The interesting papers by Reinhard Kratz, Christian Lange, and Mineke Schipper will be published elsewhere. 3 B.J. Collins, The Hittites and Their World (Archaeology & Biblical Studies 7), Atlanta 2007, 169.



2
Becking and Barstad
of divination. However, as soon as we define prophecy as an occasional form of divination, it appears that Hittite tradition knew to tell many a story about prophets, men of God and wise women. Some of them rather comical, as for instance the man of God who is seen by hunter Kessi in a dream being killed by a diorite stone falling from heaven. Also oracular dreams play an important role in royal stories, as for instance in the Hittite version of the “King of Battle” (Sar Tamhari) about Sargon of Akkad.
The majority of the essays are connected to stories in the Hebrew Bible. This section is opened by Anne-Mareike Wetter, “The Prophet and the King: Is There Such a Thing as Free Prophetic Speech?” She notes that in most of the Western world, the ‘freedom of speech’ is regarded as an inalienable human right, valued higher than the sensibilities of individuals or groups, and certainly higher than the dignity of political, religious, or economic leaders. On the other hand, scholars like Pierre Bourdieu and Stanley Fish argued that ‘freedom of speech’ is and always has been an illusion – anyone may be free to ‘speak’, but only those with “access to the legitimate instruments of expression, and therefore the participation in the authority of the institution” are actually heard.4 Her contribution investigates the dynamics of free speech in biblical prophecy, and the tension, inherent in the concept, between candour (in terms of content) and conformity (in terms of ‘instruments of expression’). She suggests that the biblical prototype of the prophet as one “calling in the wilderness” – marginalized, persecuted, and occasionally martyred – is in fact part of the ‘institutionalized’ ideal of prophecy (understood as critical speech) as construed by the theologians and scribes to whom we owe much of the biblical canon. The first part of her enquiry explores the social, discursive, and ritual(ized) background against which the performance of ‘critical prophetic speech’ could legitimately take place according to a selection of relevant texts. Secondly, she enquires whether the concept now referred to as the human right of ‘freedom of speech’ can be retraced, at least in part, to biblical narratives of prophets confronting the powers that be. Graeme Auld, “Isaiah and the Oldest ‘Biblical’ Prophetic Narrative”, pays attention to the role prophets played in the ‘historical’ narratives of Ancient Israel. He notes that only a few narratives involving prophets and kings are shared by Samuel-Kings and Chronicles in the Hebrew Bible: Gad and David, Micaiah and Jehoshaphat, Isaiah and Hezekiah, and Huldah and Josiah. Renewed attention to ideologically neutral linguistic features of such texts (infinitives absolute, markers of time, use/non-use of
tends to )הנה and יש
4 P. Bourdieu, Language and Symbolic Power, Cambridge 1991, 109.



Introduction 3
corroborate their priority over the much larger number of non-synoptic prophetic narratives. William L. Kelly, “Prophets, Kings, and Honour in the Narrative of 1 Kgs 22”, pays attention to the story of Micaiah ben Imla. Prophets often disagreed in the ancient Near East, and many scholarly attempts have been made to find ‘discernment criteria’ for delineating the true prophet from the false one. However, the paradigm of ‘true’ and ‘false’ prophets/prophecy belies a more complex social reality. Kelly engages these issues through a reading of the Micaiah ben Imlah narrative of 1 Kgs 22, where the conflict over ‘truth’ is better understood in light of the social dynamics of honour and shame. The claims to the ‘truth’ made in situations of prophetic conflict in the ancient Near East cannot be disassociated from the social realities of power, status, experience and authority. Close attention to narrative shape with an eye toward sociocritical issues in 1 Kgs 22 seems to demonstrate that the legitimisation of royal war aspirations vis-à-vis prophetic oracles – a well-known motif throughout the ancient Near East – signals a context where honour is key. Pairing narratological analysis (Sternberg, Bal) with socio-critical research (Long, Overholt), it is shown that Micaiah ben Imlah’s assertion that a ‘lying spirit’ was sent by Yahweh from the divine council to deceive the prophets can be understood as a discrediting attempt at undermining the status of his ‘opponents’. Analysis of the honour and shame dimensions of the conflict, whether construed as interprophetic or between prophet and king, will contribute to a more nuanced understanding of prophetic conflict. Reinoud Oosting, “Text and History of Isaiah 20”, observes that the short narrative about the prophet Isaiah in chapter 20 contains clear historical references to the revolt of Ashdod against King Sargon II of Assyria in 711 BCE. At the same time, the references to Egypt at the end of the narrative raise questions about the origin of this passage. Some exegetes assume that Isaiah 20 originally addressed the Philistines and later was transformed into an anti-Egyptian text.5 Others maintain that ‘this narrative has only ever existed in the final form that it now has. The pursuit for an original, logically more consistent ‘Isaianic core’ behind these verses is therefore off track’.6 Finally, Blenkinsopp claims that the narrative in Isaiah 20 has a Deuteronomistic origin.7 Starting from the examination of the narrative in Isaiah 20 the syntactic patterns employed in this
5 Cf. R.E. Clements, Isaiah 1–39 (NBC), Grand Rapids 1980, 173–74.
6 C. Balogh, The Stele of Yhwh in Egypt: The Prophecies of Isaiah 18–20 concerning Egypt and Kush (OTS 60), Leiden 2011, 319. 7 See J. Blenkinsopp, Isaiah 1–39 (AB 19), New Haven 2000, 321; J. Blenkinsopp, Opening the Sealed Book: Interpretations of the Book of Isaiah in Late Antiquity, Grand Rapids 2006, 34.



4 Becking and Barstad
passage and the linguistic and literary parallels between Isaiah 20 and other passages in the Book of Isaiah a different position is defended. A systematic overview of the linguistic and literary features of this passage helps in tracing the development and the origin of the narrative. On the basis of the textual features of Isaiah 20, there is good reason to assume that this chapter is not the work of one author. It seems that the original narrative about the prophet Isaiah has been reworked by a Deuteronomistic redactor. Thomas Wagner, “From Salvation to Doom: Isaiah’s Message in the Hezekiah Story”, argues that the Hezekiah Story contains a unique image of Isaiah’s prophetic ministry. Isaiah turns from a prophet of salvation to a prophet of doom within the story in a three-step process. His final message of doom is given to the king but it will affect to a later generation. Focusing on the Book of Isaiah, he lines out the change of interpreting the former prophetic message by inserting the Hezekiah Story in the book. For later redactors of the book two aspects became substantial: the influence of the individual on divine announcements, and the postponement of judgment. Blaženka Scheuer, “Huldah: a Cunning Career Woman?”, discusses the stories on the prophetess Huldah who appears in one of the more crucial times in the history of Judah. The peculiar fact that it is a minor, female prophet that is chosen to deliver the message of future destruction to King Josiah has been extensively discussed in biblical scholarship. Against the background of the various understandings of this fact in scholarly discussion, she seeks to explore the meaning and symbolism of the name of Huldah: Could the understanding of her name in later traditions influence the reading of the story in 2 Kings? This question is explored from two perspectives. First, although Huldah, meaning “weasel,” is viewed favourably in tradition as well as in scholarship, some Rabbinic sources describe her negatively as an arrogant person, as a rat. Is this, rather strong, attack on Huldah’s person and deed unique for Rabbinic sources, or could it be traced in Christian tradition of the time as well? Second, the link between women and weasels is an ancient one, based in a Greek myth of Hercules’ birth. According to the myth, a midwife is punished for her actions by being turned into a weasel. In ancient times, weasels were therefore often kept as pets. Finally possible influences of this fact on the Christian understanding of Huldah are explored. Matthijs J. de Jong, “Rewriting the Past in Light of the Present: The Stories of the Prophet Jeremiah”, analyses some of the stories on the prophet Jeremiah against some tendencies to be observed in Ancient Near Eastern and Greek texts on divination. In those cultures texts also were the end result of a process of writing and rewriting. In one important respect the rewriting of Jeremiah’s oracles differed from the rewriting of the Assyrian and Greek oracles. In contrast



Introduction 5
with the latter two, the divine guidance provided by Jeremiah was not heeded by Judah’s main political decision-makers. The message was proved right in the end, but what his message was designed to prevent did happen: Jerusalem was destroyed and the state of Judah abolished. This required a twist in the literary version of Jeremiah’s message. The divine foreknowledge allegedly implied in Jeremiah’s oracles was the insight that the divine guidance would not be heeded, and that, for this reason, Jerusalem would not survive, and the state of Judah would come to an end. The literary version thus included the notion of rejection of the message, and its fatal consequences. But to call this a twist is, of course, our modern, critical perspective. For those who kept alive the Jeremiah tradition it was not a twist at all. For them it was crystal clear, what from the outset had been the true meaning of Jeremiah’s oracles, and they diligently spelled out this true meaning in the expanding Jeremiah-corpus. They shaped the image of Jeremiah as a figure prophesying utter doom for a disobedient and evil society, driven by the sincere belief that this was the true meaning and the enduring relevance of the words of God that Jeremiah had spoken. Pancratius C. Beentjes, “King Asa and Hanani the Seer: 2 Chronicles 16 as an Example of the Chronicler’s View of Prophets and Prophecy”, continues his research on prophecy in the Book of Chronicles. He observes that to the Chronicler, the alliance between King Asa of Judah and King Benhaddad of Aram gives rise to introduce the seer Hanani (2 Chron. 16:7–10), who is unique to the Hebrew Bible. Hanani’s speech appears to be a fine example of the Chronicler’s method of working and his theology. Attention will be paid to the structure of his address, its context, and some connection threads of it. Bob Becking, “Haggai and Zechariah in the Stories of Ezra and 1 Esdras”, analyses the role that Haggai and Zechariah play in the Biblical Book of Ezra as well as in the prseudepigraphic rewriting 1 Esdras. He arrives at the conclusion that in both narratives, these prophets are of great importance in the movement that will lead to the ending of the crisis of the rebuilding of the temple. In 1 Esdras, however, the way-out of the stalemate position is already paved by the story of the three guardsmen. When looking at the final composition of EzraNehemiah, it becomes clear that the ‘good prophets’ Haggai and Zechariah are mirrored by ‘bad prophets’ who try to hinder the divine programme for restauration.
In the final section of this volume, attention is paid to the post-biblical period. Hannes Bezzel, “Habakkuk in the Lion’s Den: Dan 14:33–39 (Bel 33–39)”, analyses the encounter between Daniel and the prophet Habakkuk in the apocryphic collection of Daniel legends known as “Bel et Draco”. Habakkuk is, in this story, snatched away by an angel to deliver some food to the former who is



6 Becking and Barstad
sitting in the famous den of lions in Babylon. According to Kottsieper, this story represents an independent tradition which deals with the problem of getting kosher food in the diaspora.8 Bezzel argues that the short legend should rather be regarded as written for its present context. Its intention is to bring together two men which obviously were understood to be kindred spirits: Daniel and Habakkuk. The “food” which Habakkuk has to offer to Daniel is his prophetic book with its centre seen in Hab 2:4b. Thus, the short legend serves the purpose of self-encouraging for some (proto)apocalyptic circles. The next three essays deal with the function of prophets in Jewish and Islamic stories. The essay by Elizabeth Hayes, “More than just a Pretty Coat: The Story of Joseph the Dreamer from Jewish, Christian and Islamic Perspectives”, is about a dreamer. The story of the dreamy dreamer Joseph (Genesis 37–47) has a rich history of interpretation, not only in Judaism, but in Christianity and Islam as well. The extent to which Joseph is viewed as a prophet varies considerably between these three interpretive traditions. Additionally, the story has been told and re-told in mediums as varied as poetry, painting, music and drama. Joseph’s marvellous garment is celebrated in Andrew Lloyd Webber’s musical ‘Joseph and the Amazing Technicolor Dreamcoat’ and his embalmed remains make an appearance alongside Charlton Heston ‘The Ten Commandments’. Hayes supplies a compare and contrast synopsis of the history of interpretation of the Joseph story by emphasizing the prophetic actions of this dreamer of dreams and the way these actions are presented in story, song, poetry and painting. Eric Ottenheijm, “Elijah and the Messiah (B.Sanh. 98a)”, is looking for an answer to the following question: How and why did the Rabbis construe their own Elijah? Like the synoptic Gospels, Rabbinic traditions deploy appearances of Elijah to construe allegiance to and comment Biblical Prophecy. The towering figure of Elijah informs narrative strategies of healing by pious teachers called the ‘early Chassidim’. Other traditions depict Elijah as aiding teachers. Moreover he features as a prophetic teacher, addressing vexing questions on eschatological beliefs or legal issues. Finally, Elijah is alluded to by implication in a Rabbinic technical term, ‘tequ’ (‘let it stand unresolved’), as referring to his role in the eschatological decisions on halakhic or theological debates. However, his role in narratives is ambiguous where his appearance seems to question the validity of Rabbinic culture of study of the Torah (exegesis and dialectical study) from the point of prophecy and radical piety (mystical knowledge). These functions find their intertextual authority in the ascent to
8 I. Kottsieper, ‘Zusätze zu Daniel’, in: O.H. Steck, R.G. Kratz, I. Kottsieper, Das Buch Baruch. Der Brief des Jeremia. Zusätze zu Ester und Daniel (ATD.A 5), Göttingen 1998, 211–85.



Introduction 7
Heaven (2 Kings 2:11) and the mentioning of Elijah as the herald of eschatology (Mal. 3:23; Eccl. 48:10) but also seem to represent, albeit in a disguised form, older forms of religious authority. Rabbinic sources thus present a particular rereading of Elijah in their context of Rabbinic study culture. In our presentation we will discuss texts featuring the appearance of Elijah to R. Joshua ben Levi, a famous third century teacher. Ottenheijm assesses Elijah’s role as both commentating and authorizing the Rabbinic elite from the point of view of Biblical prophecy and zealous piety. Marcel Poorthuis, “From Noah to Nuḥ: the Making of a Prophet”, highlights the variety of images of the prophet Noah/Nuḥ in Jewish, Christian and Islamic traditions. Noah is generally considered one of the forefathers of humanity. His qualifications for prophethood are far less universally recognized. Being aware of impending disaster he builds the arch for his family. The New Testament even emphasizes the unexpected and sudden character of the catastrophe at hand, without intimating in any way that Noah might have warned the people. In marked contrast, Syriac Christian and Arabic Islamic sources strongly emphasize Noah as a prophet constantly warning the people despite their mockery. The final paper of this volume relates on an early modern story on prophetic activity. Willem Frijhoff, “An Early Modern Young Prophet: The Heavenly Messages of Evert Willemsz Bogaert and their Recognition, 1622–23”, tells the story of a boy, Evert Willemsz Boogert who while staying at the orphanage in the city of Woerden received visions during a period of numbness. The reports on his spiritual experiences were brought to the local ecclesiastical and political powers. They recognized the veracity of Evert’s ‘prophecies’. Eventually they made it possible to have his dream come true: becoming a minister in the Reformed Church. In this capacity Evert Boogert, then named Bogaardus, served as first clergyman the community in New Amsterdam, later to become New York. Although the essays in this volume differ in scope and topic, time and context they nevertheless make one thing clear. It turns out that prophets in stories are part of a claimed – and sometimes invented – tradition. This social context of the narrators of the stories turned out to be formative for the shaping or reshaping of their prophetic characters. Transposed to the narrative context, prophets started a new life that on occasion was in continuation with their earlier deeds and doings, but more than once was the result of the malleability of the tradition.






Part 1
Ancient Near East
∵

Prophets, Men of God, Wise Women: Dreams and Prophecies in Hittite Stories
Meindert Dijkstra
1 Introduction
At the end of her paragraph about Hittite divination, Billie Jean Collins says that prophets were active in Anatolia, but that we hardly know who they were and how they worked.1 She obviously then thinks of prophets in the ‘Biblical’ sense as divine messengers different from male and female diviners and their methods of divination. However as soon as we define inspired prophecy and either spoken or dreamed divine messages as occasional forms of divination, Hittite tradition too knew many a story about oracles and prophecies. And also about seers, men of God and wise women. Sometimes they do so in a rather humoristic manner, as for instance in the tale of Kešši, the hunter and his beautiful wife. Kešši sees in one of his seven dreams a man of god being killed together with his household by a diorite stone (Ullikummi?) falling from heaven. Understandably, Kešši is very scared by this dream in which even a man of god cannot predict his own and his household’s disaster and Kešši’s mother needs all her wits to change his mind. All kinds of oracles and oracular dreams play an important role in royal stories too. Many of the texts I discuss here were found in one of the major archives of the Great Temple and Palace (Buyukale) in Boghazköy, the site of the capital of the Hittite Empire, Hattuša. The majority of the Hittite texts (including the Hurrian texts) stem from the Late Bronze Period (ending ca 1180 BCE). The texts are mostly written on clay tablets in cuneiform script and their Hittite language preserves one of the most ancient Indo-European languages we know about. First, I would like to make some general remarks on divination, prophecy and ritual practitioners in Hittite tradition. Secondly, I will say a few words on the use of religious texts ascribed to famous seers of the past in Hittite tradition. Finally, I hope to give a few examples of the use of dreams, divine messages and the use of other means of divination in Hittite stories and rituals.
1 B.J. Collins, The Hittites and Their World (Archaeology & Biblical Studies 7), Atlanta 2007, 169.



12 Dijkstra
2 Seers, Wise Women and Men of God
Indeed as far as I know, the many Hittite (and Hurrian) religious texts from Anatolia do not mention a kind of prophetic office comparable with that of the West Semitic nābîʾ, let alone remind us of the religious and political activity in the stories of the Biblical prophets and their books. Of course, we find letters and other documents, in which oracular reports and other kinds of divine messages such as dreams are forwarded to the Hittite king as in Mari and elsewhere.2 However we look in vain for collections of divine oracles, such as were copied and edited for the benefit of Neo-Assyrian Kings Esarhaddon and Assurbanipal, and their royal propaganda. But there are still some religious officials to be found in historiographic documents, narratives, rituals and prayers who proclaimed divine messages to the king, the royal house, royal officials and the Hittite people at large. The different procedures for divine consultation are spelled out several times in the famous prayers of Muršili II. For instance, he addresses the Stormgod of Hatti and the other gods about a plague, saying in his ‘Second’ Plague prayer: “[Or] if people have been dying for some reason, then let me either see it in a dream, or let it be established through an oracle, or let a man of god (lúDINGIRLIM-ni-an-za) declare it, or, according to what I instructed all the priests, they shall regularly sleep holy.”3 It reveals par example the broad spectrum of divine consultation, solicited and unsolicited, which was apparently dependent on a broad range of religious practitioners such as seers, augurs, extatics, men of god, wise women, godmothers and so on. In short, we meet men and women of god, associated with a particular god or goddess in his or her temple, who provide a warning but also a comforting role in the shady world between ‘the thousand gods of Hatti’ and people, in particular the king, the queen and the royal family and household. As elsewhere,
2 H.A. Hoffner Jr, Letters from the Hittite Kingdom (Writings from the Ancient World 15), Atlanta 2009, 24, especially Nos 3, 50 & 92; A. Mouton, ‘Au sujet du compte rendu oraculaire Hittite KBo 18.142,’ in: D. Groddek, M. Zorman (eds), Tabularia Hethaeorum – Hethitologische Beiträge Silvin Košak zu 65. Geburtstag (DBH 25) Wiesbaden 2007, 553; further the letter fragment KUB 57, 8 (CTH 187), A. Mouton, Rêves hittites: contributions à une histoire et une anthropologie du rêve en Anatolie ancienne (CHANE 28), Leiden, Boston 2007, 95–96; KBo 16, 52 (CTH 210); Mouton, Rêves hittites, 96–98. This is still a small harvest compared to the collection of letters from Mari and the Neo-Assyrian letters. An interesting feature is that in two of these letters a royal dream leads to an oracular investigation, a phenomenon also known from Mari. 3 I. Singer, Hittite Prayers (Writings of the Ancient World 11), Atlanta 2002, 60; for a similar passage Singer, Hittite Prayers, 58 (§ 2); 66 etc.



Prophets, Men of God, Wise Women 13
we find many documents with questions from an oracular expert with a simple answer “Yes” or “No”, intended to discover why something bad had happened and which of ‘the thousand gods’ was angry with the ritual patron.4 Methods to obtain such oracles were extispicy, in particular of the sheep’s innards TE.(MEŠ) = TÊRĒTE.(MEŠ) – as in Mesopotamia,5 observations of animal behaviour, bird oracles and casting of lots. The practitioners were indicated by the Sumerogram lúHAL or lúAZU, the Hurritic lúPurapši, all equivalent of the Babylonian lúbarû ‘seer’. It is obvious that Hittites borrowed this method of observing and interpretation of omina from Mesopotamia. However, in these mantic and ritual texts, experts and traditions from Hurritic provenance, in particular from Kizzuwatna (present day Cilicia) and Mitanni, and even northern Syria Mukish (Alalakh) and Halab (Aleppo) often seem to bridge the gap between the Anatolian, West Semitic and Mesopotamian world and cultures.6 Not surprisingly, the Hittite archives also contained collections of omina in the Hurritic language (basically CTH 774).7 Recently I established that the Hurritic word for bird eradi, has also the connotation of (bird)-omen,8 perhaps as a result of MUŠEN.HURRI, the Hurritic bird-extispicy. It was a special type of extispicy performed by the lúAZU/HAL and apparently an indigenous HurriticHittite method of divination. It seems that ‘the man of God’ and his female counterpart comes closest to what we traditionally would call a prophet. Sometimes the term lúDINGIRLIMni-an-za (šiuniyant-), which is used in Mursili’s Plaque prayer, is even translated by ‘prophet’.9 The Hittite rendering lúšiuniyant- seems to suggest a person

Kammenhuber, Orakelpraxis, Traüme und Vorzeichenschau bei den Hethitern (THet, 7), Heidelberg 1976.

Dijkstra, ‘The First Tablet of the Hurritic Bilingual Song of Release in the light of Hurritic Mythological Tradition,’ UF 44 (2013), 133. 9 Compare, for instance, the translation of CTH 378.II § 11 in Collins, The Hittites and their World, 166; G. 14 Dijkstra
possessed by the deity, a divine spirit or a demon.10 In the tale of Kešši, a different term DINGIR-aš-ša LÚ-ya ‘(and) the man of god’ is apparently found. It is not certain whether the same practitioner is here meant. The last term reminds us also of the LÚ dU/IM ‘man of the Storm-god/Teššup’, who is able to stop the fury of the Weather-god or Storm-god in the so-called mugawar-myths.11 The idea that someone possessed by a divine spirit, in other words as ‘a divine’, might declare a solicited or unsolicited message from the gods, certainly takes us into the sphere of Biblical prophecy or, for that matter, into the West Semitic divinatory roots of the extatic nabiʾ. The problem is that we have as yet found no examples of his prophetical performance for comparison, as Billie Collins rightly observed. But there exists at least one example of a lúpurapši ‘seer’ delivering an ‘oracle of salvation’. A divine message of the kind as we find them in Neo-Assyrian royal inscriptions, collections of oracles, the inscription of Zakir of Hamath and also the Old Testament. It is quoted in the tenth Tablet of the Hišuwaš-festival (CTH 628), again a ritual of Kizzuwatnian origin. This festival to honour the Storm-god of Manuzziya included a ceremony for the military success of the king:12
One of the musicians who stands in the gate of the god will blow the horn and one of the purapši-priests who stands on the roof, will speak in front of the king to encourage him the following words: “O king, be not afraid! The Storm-god will put for you, o king the enemies and the lands of the enemy under your feet and you will smash them like empty jars. To you, o king life, health, future heroism and prosperity of the gods will constantly be given. Do not be afraid of anybody for you will have them defeated”.

Kloekhorst, Etymological Dictionary of the Hittite Inherited Lexicon, Leiden, Boston 2008, 764. DINGIR.MEŠ DINGIR.MEŠ-ni-ya-an-du is found in a Tasmišarri and Ašmunikkal text CTH 778.I KBo 9, 137+KBo 23, 22: III 24, which, notwithstanding the broken context, may suggest an act of deification: ‘Let the gods deify [them / the kings?]’; KBo 8, 77: 7; KUB 14, 10+26, 86 IV 11 = CTH 378 IIC.
11 dIM-aš li-la-ni-ya-an-za ù-ez-zi na-an LÚ dIM a-ra-ez-zi ‘ ‘If the Weather-God become furious, the man of the Weather-god may stop him’ and similar formulas in CTH 324, 325, 334 etc.

Prophets, Men of God, Wise Women 15
It is however also the study of prophecy and prophetical practices and genres in the Bible that warns us not to connect specific prophetic practices exclusively to particular practitioners. Nebiʾîm work miracles and miracle working men of God and seers speak a ‘Botenwort’, a message like a nabiʾ. A nabiʾ is occasionally a diviner and a seer might act like a nabiʾ without making a seer or man of God synonymous with a nabiʾ. An etymological approach in our search for prophets might be useful sometimes, but it can also be a pitfall. In other words, it might be useful when researching extra-Biblical texts for prophets and prophetical phenomena first to search for comparable activities and religious practices and only to ask afterwards what kind of practitioner is said to perform them, if he or she is mentioned by his or her vocation at all.
3 Seers, Wise Women and Religious Tradition
A more interesting example for comparison with Biblical prophetical tradition is the phenomenon of authoritative religious tradition itself, certainly if it concerns the religious tradition of what famous seers and wise women have said and prescribed in the past. From Ugarit we already knew that the myths of Baal were not only written down by the famous royal scribe Ilumilk, but also were passed on orally by atn prln, ‘the seer Atanu’, the chief priest according to the colophon of CTU2. 1.6. Less known is, I think, that we find similar remarks of traditional provenance in Hittite texts, such as the Illuyanka Tales, written down by Pihazitti, the scribe under the supervision of Walwazitti, the chief scribe, but according to the word of Kella, the GUDU-priest. In the Hittite archives from Kizzuwatna (Cilicia), we find a number of rituals that were passed on as the oral tradition of three wise men, Ammihatna, Tulbi and Mati, the purapši-priests from the land of Kummani. In a ritual for the purification and restoration of the temple of the goddess Hebat, we meet another trio of lúAZU-priests (CTH 702):13 Dakuya, Asnunikkal and Mati. lúAZU is like lúHAL, the usual Sumerogram for the seer-priest or haruspex, for which some texts also have the Hurritic-Kizzuwatnian vocational term lúpurapši. Comparison shows that these Sumerograms and the Hurritic vocational term are synonymous and so interchangeable. It can hardly be doubted that this term is a derivative of Hurritic w/fur- ‘to see’ and a variant of prln / w/purulinni ‘the seer’ (Ugarit,
13 Recently I joined the pieces 2608/c and 1259/c to 5/q+280a (my join, see Konkordanz S. Košak, hethiter.net/:hetkonk (v.1.9)), which confirms this assemblage to be the greater part of CTH 702 Column IV = B // KUB 9, 2 = A and KBo 19, 148 (Bo 68/99) = C. 1259/c+5/q preserve also a small part of the obverse // KUB 9, 2 I 8–21.



16 Dijkstra
Emar, Boghazköy) derived from the same verb.14 The same Ammihatna was also SANGA-priest (a kind of sacrificial priest) of the oracle-goddess Išhara. Because in rituals the lúÁZU/HAL/purapši appears alongside the SANGA/Sakkuni-priest, their function was not identical. Ammihatna was both a SANGA-priest and a purapši-priest, in short a cultic official who was both a sacrificial priest and a seer. I know that this observation is still far away from what we understand to be Biblical prophetic tradition, but it bring us fairly close to the idea of the cultic prophet, which also once existed in ancient Israel.15 I am not here going to repeat or discuss the question whether or not cultic prophecy existed in the religions and cultures of ancient Israel. As we all know now at last, the Old Testament does not reflect one culture or religious tradition, let alone one form of prophetic practice. Where in the past theology often claimed a uniform prophetical tradition, a unifying monotheistic movement and a unique message in the Old Testament, comparative religion of Israel has shown a rather great diversity of prophetical personalities, practitioners, genres and practices. I agree with Lester Grabbe and others that the existence of cultic prophets is now accepted by a majority of scholars and that the question remains not so much whether there was cult-related prophecy but where we meet such kind of practice among the Biblical prophets and where we find cultic prophecy reflected in prophetical tradition. It is in cultic prophecy that Israel and the ancient Near East meet and share a common religious tradition in the Late Bronze and Early Iron periods and this applies also to Hittite culture and religion and its sources in the Hurritic and Mesopotamian cultures. God moves in a mysterious way, also in the traditions of the ancient near East. We have reason to believe that the Hittites were deeply influenced by Hurrian and North Syrian religious tradition. We cannot only point to the famous purapši-priests and traditions from Kizzuwatna mentioned above. In the 14th century when Kizzuwatna was incorporated in the Hittite empire, and when the wife of Hattušili III, the famous queen Puduhepa, ordered rituals from her homeland to be copied in the libraries of the capital of Hattuša16 in the 13th century, this land was already a cultural crossroad where also North Syrian traditions from Mukish (Alalakh) and Halab (Aleppo) were fostered. So we meet there too the similarly important traditions of the wise woman (SALŠU.GI = hašāwa-) Allaiturahi from Mukish (Alalakh) (CTH 780–781) and the
14 Th. Richter, Bibliographisches Glossar des Hurritischen, Wiesbaden 2012, 326–327; different, however, purapši Richter, Bibliographisches Glossar, 328. 15 A.R. Johnson, The Cult Prophet in Israel, Oxford 1962 is the classic study and L.L. Grabbe, Priests, Prophets, Diviners, Sages. A Socio-Historical Study of Religious Specialist in Ancient Israel, Valley Forge 1995, 113 is a nice summary.
16 Strauss, Reinigungsrituale aus Kizzuwatna, 11; Wilhelm, Hurriter, 99–102.



Prophets, Men of God, Wise Women 17
Hurrian woman, Ašdu (CTH 490),17 as well as compendia of the words and prescriptions of Ehel-Teššup, the seer (lúHAL) of Aleppo.18 In other words, the Hittites and Hurrians were obviously familiar with the idea of an authoritative, if not canonical, tradition of religious literature. In the light of such evidence, it is hardly surprising from a comparative point of view that in the much younger Israelite tradition Moses, the prophet and man of God,19 became the spokesman of a great number of canonical ritual prescriptions and that we find in the Book of the Twelve Prophets a certain prophet Joel, who was apparently working as a cultic prophet in the temple of Jerusalem, and from whom a ‘day of the Lord’ ritual to be used in days of drought, famine and a locust plague, was passed down to us.
4 Dreams and Other Divine Messages in Hittite Stories, Prayer and Ritual
The Apology of Hattušili III (CTH 81) tells us that dISHTAR – beyond this Sumerogram most probably the Hurrian goddess Shawushga, later known as the Hurrian Astarte is hidden – revealed in a dream by his father Muršili II and his brother Muwatalli that he should be appointed to be her priest because of his weak constitution.20 However, later in his career he became commander of the army of his brother’s campaign against Ramesses II (battle of Qadesh 1275 BCE) and on the way back married Puduhepa, the daughter of Pentipšarri, priest of Kizzuwatna at the behest of the goddess. Later the goddess revealed in a dream to Puduhepa that Hattušili would become king instead of his older brother Urhi-Teššup.21 The Hittite kings and queens hardly took a decision without the support of a dream. As such the dream is the most common form of an unsolicited omen in the period of the Hittite Empire. But dreams could
17 S. Görke, Das Ritual der Astu (CTH 490): Rekonstruktion und Tradition eines hurritischhethitischen Rituals aus Bogazköy/Hattusa, (CHANE 40), Leiden, Boston 2010. 18 For example, Mouton, Rêves Hittites, 102–105, P. Dardano, Die hethitischen Tontafelkataloge aus Hattuša (CTH 276–282) (StBoT, 47), Wiesbaden 2006, 130–131; 152–153; 212–213, also, for instance, the ritual against bad dreams! 19 M. Dijkstra, ‘Moses – the man of God,’ in: R. Roukema et al. (eds), The Interpretation of Exodus. Studies in Honour of Cornelis Houtman (CBETh 44), Leuven 2006, 17–36.
20 Th.P.J. van den Hout, ‘Apology of Hattušili III (1.77)’, in: COS 1, 199–203; A. Mouton, Rêves Hittites, 90–91; A. Mouton, ‘L’importance des rêves dans l’existence de Hattušili III,’ in: Th.P.J. van den Hout (ed.), The Life and Times of Hattušili III and Tuthaliya IV – Proceedings of a Symposium Held in Honour of J. de Roos, 12–13 December 2003, Leiden 2006, 9–16.
21 A reference to this course of events is also found in another text, CTH 85 I.15–21; see Mouton. Rêves Hittites, 92.



18 Dijkstra
also be oracles, if they were solicited by dream incubation by special priests, apparently regularly so on the king’s instruction.22 Visiting the temple in order to receive a dreamed message was not a Hittite invention. The royalty were inspired by the stories of myth and legend to trust dreams as a mean of divine communication, even by such legendary predecessors as the Hurritic hero Kešši, the Hunter, the Sumerian king Gilgameš or the Babylonian kings Sargon and Naram-Sin of Akkad, all famous men of the past. And even the gods sometimes dream like men in Hittite myths. They receive divine messages and occasionally also prophesy. There is a mythological tale called ‘Ea and the Beast’ (CTH 351.1)23 in which we find a kind of dialogue between the wise Babylonian god Ea and an unknown creature. In this myth, Ea listens to a long prophetic speech of someone called a šuppalanza- apparently a domestic animal, though we do not know what kind. More interesting is that in a broken context this text twice mentions a special category of gods alongside the birth-goddesses and fairy godmothers, the DINGIR.MEŠ-aš DINGIR.MEŠ-ni-an-da-aš (šuiniaš šuiniyantaš). The last word is the same word that is sometimes translated as ‘prophet’ or ‘man of god’ and therefore in his edition Archi rendered the expression as ‘the fortune telling gods’:24 gods that are possessed so to speak with a divine spirit, which make them able to prophesy. It seems that ‘the Beast’ is one of them. Unfortunately, the text is still difficult to understand. Three fragments are known at present but a coherent translation is still not possible. So much can be said: the fortune-telling god in this myth announces to god Ea the advent of a new ruler of the gods, who will drive the old gods into the Netherworld. The text seems to be part of the Kumarbi cycle dealing with the contest for the kingship in Heaven. It is also familiar with the concept of Hurritic-Hittite theogonic tradition, in which generations of gods fight and succeed one another in the kingship of Heaven.25

Prophets, Men of God, Wise Women 19
The best known and the most famous example is the Hittite Song of Going Forth (CTH 344), which also influenced Hesiod’s Theogony in early Greek tradition, but there are Hurritic and Hittite fragments of similar texts.26 An obvious difference between this and the other texts is its long prophetic speech announcing a new divine ruler of the world. It is somewhat reminiscent of the so-called prognostic or Accadian Prophecies announcing a new set of regimes by someone speaking in the past. Notable is the idea of wise Ea asking this prophesying Beast whether this coming ruler is already grown up or still ripening. In his answer the Beast refers to the wisdom of the ancient gods, but why and how remains unclear yet. We can only hope for more fragments joining or duplicating this intriguing text that, as far as I know, has no parallel in ancient Near Eastern texts. The Hurrian tale of Kešši and the Babylonian legend Šar Tamhari (King of Battle) were rendered and recited in Hittite language and fashion in Anatolia. However these stories found their way all over the ancient Near East in the Late Bronze period as far as Egypt. To begin with the tale of Kešši and the legend Šar Tamhari were discovered in the library of Echnaton (Amenophis IV) in Achet-aton (Tell el-Amarna), only a small piece of Kešši and half of a onecolumn tablet of Šar Tamhari. However, from the many archives of Hattuša, many more fragments of the same literary works have been discovered. They are now conveniently known as Hittite Šar Tamhari (CTH 310)27 and Hittite Kešši (CTH 361.I).28 After the Amarna discovery of Šar Tamhari, fragments from Neo-Assyrian libraries in Assur (VAT 10290) and Nineveh (K 13228) have been found,29 but they are only small fragments compared to the texts known from Amarna and Hattuša. However the flake from the Nineveh version contained scraps of an episode narrating how Sargon went into the Temple of

Dreams play an important role in the Sargon tradition, for instance in the Sumerian story of Sargon and King Ur-Zababa.30 Recently, I found a new fragment of the Hittite version joining to the main manuscript, which splendidly confirms the consultation of Ishtar and her dream-revelation to Sargon in this Hittite version of Šar Tamhari.31 Unfortunately the passage is still fragmentary, but enough has been preserved to get an idea of the procedure and message:

Column I
[4] times [he bowed] 5 times [.Then Sargon] [started] to ]speak [a word] to ISHTAR: “Oh ISHTAR, my Lady, is there [ a road to go?”]
[There]upon ISHTAR [quickly hastened to him] in a hurry [her] fe[et . . . from . . . ] she came up to . . . [ [ ] It opened up(?) 9 times[ [ for the] fury. And in no way [from . . . ] my ar[my will suffer(?)], their feet by cold wind(?) will not be fro[zen?]
Sargon awoke again from his sleep And he started to say his word to his warriors: “Let them call my warriors, who have declared the road too dangerous for me!”
Most probably in this story Sargon entered the temple of ISHTAR, bowing 4 and 5 times addressing the goddess with an enquiry. Then the goddess hastens herself and approaches the supplicant. After that a dream or vision is mentioned, which reveals or testifies its message nine times. Unfortunately, it remains obscure what exactly was revealed to King Sargon, but at the end of the section the goddess appears to be speaking about the army of Sargon as ‘my army’. Sargon awakes again from sleep, calls his warriors and communicates the message of the goddess. It is a kind of standard revelation in royal literature. In perusing the studies of Alice Mouton for the role of dreams in Hittite divination, it is amazing to discover how often dreams gave guidance to Hittite life and history. Dreams play an important role in letters, prayers, rituals and indeed in the writing of tales and history. Hittites were extremely afraid of bad dreams, which they considered to be life-shortening. They sought also divine assistance against such life-threatening dreams.



22 Dijkstra
Recently, I have reconstructed a prayer addressed to the god El-Kunirsha (CTH 342.2), the Lord of Dreams/Sleep.36 I will end this contribution with some remarks about this prayer. Of course, there is much more to say, but hopefully these few examples tell enough. This prayer is performed on behalf of the ritual patron by a salŠU.GI, ‘old woman’, a female divinatory practitioner and magician. The epithet is also often given to the famous wise woman and magician Allaiturahi from Mukish in northern Syria.37 The prayer is part of a ritual similar to other Allaiturahi texts. Also the fact that the prayer is addressed to an original West Semitic deity El-Kunirsha, the Hittite adaptation of El qone ʾarṣ, ‘El, the Creator or Owner of the Earth’ seem to plead for a prayer from the Allaiturahi tradition.38 Unfortunately no colophon saying has been preserved as yet in so many words. This El-Kunirsha is also one of the major characters in a Hittite myth: El-Kunirsha, Ashertu and the WeatherGod (CTH 342.1), of which several manuscripts were discovered in the libraries of Hattuša. Former research often identified it as a myth of Canaanite, if not Ugaritic origin. There is nothing to prove such an assumption, except the possibility of a general North Syrian provenance of this god and this myth. El-Kunirsha does not appear in Ugarit, but he was known in the first millennium BCE in the sphere of the god Baal Shamem and it is possible that his cult followed the wide dispersion of the cult of Baal Shamem in the ancient Near East.39

Usually, para epp- (without locative particle) means ‘to hold forth, show, proffer (a gift)’ (CHD (P) 118 2b 1’). It is once found with a dream as an object (KUB 52, 72 Obv.; CHD (P) 118b). How this accords with the expected role of the deity El-Kunirsha, addressed as a deliverer in this prayer, remains obscure because the closing lines of the prayer are still broken or missing. 44 Presumably El-Kunirsha or the SALŠU.GI on behalf of the god. 45 CHD (P), 49–50 SISKUR(mukeššar) pai-: probably, this ‘me’ is the ritual patron speaking.



24 Dijkstra
In Ugarit, the old and wise god El, head of the pantheon, was known to have ominous dreams (CTU2 1.6 III,1ff),46 but the idea that he as god El-Kunirsha had power of the sleep and dreams of people, is new. He is depicted as a deity that can send bad dreams (literally hold forth to people) like other disasters and take them away. As a ‘Deliverer’, he can be asked to deliver the ritual patron from shortened years, bad dreams, wild animals and perhaps also diseases and demons. Even more interesting from a comparative perspective is, however, his chthonic character in Hittite tradition. He became here one of the older gods of the Netherworld, even the husband of the brother of the Sun-goddess of the Netherworld.47
5 Conclusions
This survey was not exhaustive. I hope I have shown that modes of revelation and divination in the Hittite and Hurrian world did not differ profoundly from those in the West Semitic or Mesopotamian world. In particular, dreams and interpretation of dreams by means of all kinds of solicited oracles and through handbooks played a major role. Even more interesting for our research was the knowledge and application of a kind of canonical tradition, impersonated by famous seers and wise women of the past. It is once more a reminder that if we study Old Testament prophecy we deal not so much with ipsissima verba of the prophets but with the tradition of prophetical personages adorned with religious authority, that is: a canon of prophets. And finally, even the belief in YHWH, as the El who neither slumbers nor sleeps, may have deep roots in ancient near Eastern tradition. Even if we admit that there is a significant difference between these Yahwistic beliefs, which developed and were adopted in the second half of the 1st millennium BCE and such views as expressed in these Late Bronze Hittite texts, we should also honestly take into account the historical and cultural distance when comparing these religious beliefs. And we should be impressed that, even in an early religious, polytheistic context

Prophets, Men of God, Wise Women 25
beyond dreams, oracles and other religious practices, not only a deus revelatus but also a deus absconditus could be hidden, who remained silent at times but who could be asked to speak and to awake, to appear to the worshipper, to send a message in a dream and even to deliver his suffering servant from divine anger and all the perils of the night.

The Prophet and the King: Is there Such a Thing as Free Prophetic Speech?*
Anne-Mareike Wetter
1 Introduction
‘Everyone loves a rebel’, and yet ‘a prophet is not accepted in his own homeland’ (Luke 4:24). Both sayings are equally applicable to prophets figuring in the narrative literature of the Hebrew Bible. To be more precise: especially prophets who, according to these stories, are not accepted by their contemporaries seem to be identified by the authors or redactors as rebels who deserve, perhaps not the love, but certainly the attention of the reader. Although examples to the contrary are present as well (e.g., Nathan or Huldah), the stereotypical prophet seems to be one ‘crying in the wilderness’ – at least figuratively, if not literally. The inverse relation between ‘acceptance within the literary context’ on the one hand and ‘appropriateness of acceptance by audience and/or readers’ on the other is not at all unique to prophets in the Hebrew Bible. As Irene van Renswoude points out, ‘dissident speakers of late Antiquity and the early Middle Ages often styled themselves as outcasts and as marginal speakers’1 in order to create or obtain a ‘license to speak’2 from the institution within – and against – which they moved. The image of one ‘crying in the wilderness’, so familiar for the reader of the biblical texts, is applicable to late Antique Christian critical speakers and philosophers of Ancient Greece as much as it is to Elijah, Jeremiah, or Micaiah. I. van Renswoude even discovers similar tendencies in the case of contemporary ‘prophets’3 such as Edward Snowden or, closer to home, Geert Wilders and other proponents of what Baukje Prins has termed ‘New Realism’.4 All of them seem to be instances or even prototypes of
* I wish to thank Irene van Renswoude and my colleagues at Utrecht University for critical and constructive feedback on earlier versions of this article.
1 I. van Renswoude, License to Speak: The Rhetoric of Free Speech in Late Antiquity and the Early Middle Ages (2011). Doctoral Dissertation, Utrecht University, Utrecht. 2 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 5. 3 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 355. 4 ‘New Realism’, according to Baukje Prins’ analysis, is a relatively new discourse that has made its appearance on the Dutch political stage in the late 1990’s, spurred on by the apparent failure of the multi-cultural society; see, e.g., B. Prins, ‘The Nerve to Break Taboos. New Realism in the Dutch Discourse on Multiculturalism’, Journal of International Migration and



30 Wetter
the same schema – a schema that serves to legitimize not only individual critical speakers but also the right to freely voice one’s opinions per se. Against the background of new theorization concerning ‘free’ and ‘critical speech’, biblical notions of prophecy deserve reconsideration. This task is especially urgent in view of contemporary political discourses about free speech as both an inalienable right and a brainchild of Western culture, which in turn is construed as built on ‘our’ Judeo-Christian heritage.5 The purpose of this paper, then, is to investigate the dynamics involved in creating what Van Renswoude has termed a ‘license to speak’ in a selection of narratives ascribed to the Deuteronomistic school. The theoretical notion of a ‘license to speak’ is operationalized by means of an approach rooted in insights from cognitive linguistics.6 Concretely, I investigate whether the texts offer a more or less consistent, ‘prototypical’ image of a true prophet – i.e., one who ought to be granted a license to speak – and whether this image can be fruitfully understood in terms of ‘frank/critical speech’ on the one hand and (the right to) ‘free speech’ on the other. In the absence of direct access to the individuals on whose actions and words the biblical image of the ‘true prophet’
Integration 3 (2002), 363–379; B. Prins, ‘Beyond Innocence. The Genre of New Realism and its Contenders’, R. Braidotti, C. Esche, M. Hlavajova (eds), Citizens and Subjects: The Netherlands, For Example: A Critical Reader, Utrecht 2007, 253–264). (In-)famous proponents of this discourse are, in order of provocativeness, Frits Bolkestein, Pim Fortuyn, and Geert Wilders. Prins focuses predominantly on the content of this discourse and the (often inflammatory) language used to express it, although she makes some remarks on Fortuyn’s more general appearance as well. 5 As this paper is being written, a new discourse is gaining momentum in political debates in the Netherlands. Neo-conservative politicians (often those whom Baukje Prins summarizes under the heading of ‘New Realism’) point to ‘our’ Judeo-Christian heritage as the foundation of values such as democracy, gender equality, and, significantly, the right to freely voice dissident opinions; cf. e.g., E. van den Hemel, ‘(Pro)claiming Tradition: The “Judeo-Christian” Roots of Dutch Society and the Rise of Conservative Nationalism’, in: R. Braidotti, B. Blaagaard & E. Midden (eds), The Postsecular Condition, 2014, 53–76 for an analysis of this phenomenon. The present paper is emphatically not intended as grist to the mill of these politicians. I am not trying to prove that the origins of free speech as a political right can be traced to the Hebrew Bible, and certainly not that this right is tied exclusively to ‘our Judeo-Christian heritage’. Nevertheless, the similarities in the way in which critical speakers are framed or framed themselves in biblical, antique, early medieval, and modern contexts invite closer investigation. 6 For an introduction to this method and its application to biblical studies, see E.J. van Wolde, Reframing Biblical Studies. When Language and Text Meet Culture, Cognition, and Context, Winona Lake 2009.



The Prophet and the King 31
may (or may not) be based,7 my focus is on the texts alone, and on the way in which a ‘license to speak’ is created and granted within the texts, i.e., by the authors and editors of these texts. Against the background of current debates on free speech, the analysis can be construed as a reverse genealogy. With Van Renswoude, I travel from Antiquity and the Early Middle Ages to the present, and back to the prophets of the biblical narratives, investigating not only in how far the image created there is indeed similar to that of more recent free speakers, but also whether the biblical texts offer something comparable to our political notion of the freedom of expression.
2 Free Speech/Frank Speech/License to Speak
According to Van Renswoude, the image of the critical speaker as a lone and often persecuted warden of truth in opposition to a blind and unyielding authority is no coincidence, nor does it necessarily reflect actual historical circumstances.8 She analyzes this phenomenon within a theoretical framework of ‘free’ and ‘frank’ or ‘critical’ speech, and argues:
Now as much as then, critical speech is the result of careful construction, negotiation and ritual setting. Speaking ‘freely’ is dependent on the willingness of others to listen. [. . .] The type of discourse nowadays refer[ed] to as ‘free speech’, which the Greeks called parrhesía and the Romans libertas or licentia, required as much rhetorical skill and careful strategy as any other form of rhetoric.9
‘Free speech’ in the sense of a political concept or right is generally connected to the rise of democracy in Ancient Greek about the 5th century BCE.10 However, as scholars like Stanley Fish and Pierre Bourdieu have pointed out, ‘there is no such thing as free speech’ – although the right to express dissident opinions may be granted by law, the form of this expression is never ‘free’.11 Apart from
7 Cf. e.g., L.-S. Tiemeyer, ‘Ezekiel: A Compromised Prophet in Reduced Circumstances’, L.L. Grabbe & M. Nissinen (eds), Constructs of Prophecy in the Former and Latter Prophets and Other Texts (Ancient Near East Monographs), Atlanta 2011, 175–195. 8 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, e.g., 172. 9 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 5–6. 10 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 17.
11 S. Fish, There Is No Such Thing As Free Speech. And it’s a Good Thing Too, Oxford, New York 1994; P. Bourdieu, Language and Symbolic Power, Cambridge 1991.



32 Wetter
the political concept of the right to ‘free speech’, then, there is a discursive and rhetorical concept, or perhaps rather an ideal of ‘critical’ or ‘frank’ speech, which must abide by certain rules in order to be heard.12 Van Renswoude has coined the term ‘license to speak’ to describe the intriguing dynamics at work between speaker and audience by which the former legitimates his or her utterings, and by which the latter in turn grants this legitimacy. This dynamic involves (much) more than ‘linguistic logic’. In Bourdieu’s words:
It is clear that all efforts to find, in the specifically linguistic logic of different forms of argumentation, rhetoric and style, the source of their symbolic efficacy are destined to fail as long as they do not establish the relationship between the properties of discourse, the properties of the person who pronounces them and the properties of the institution which authorizes him to pronounce them.13
Put more simply: words alone are not just a poor area of investigation, but actually ineffectual if spoken by the wrong person, or within the wrong institutional context. It is the interplay between person, message, style, and context that determines whether a ‘license to speak’ will be granted. The legal right to speak freely is no sufficient, nor even a necessary condition for voicing criticism – and certainly not for being heard.14 Van Renswoude points to the Greek custom of the ‘passing of the skeptron’15 as eloquent (pardon the pun) symbol for the rules involved in any instance of ‘free speech’.
12 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 5. 13 Bourdieu, Language, 111. It seems reasonable to expand the triad of discourse, person and institution to include habitus – those standardized, often unconscious, culturally determined body techniques that betray belonging to a group or a profession. Just picture the three men in Monty Python’s Life of Brian, two of whom are immediately recognized as prophets by their intonation and grand gestures. The third, however, fails to impress the audience – not because the content of his speech makes any less sense than that of the others (neither of them make much sense at all), but because he delivers it without the habitus expected in a true prophet. Unfortunately, the biblical texts offer much less information about the habitus characterizing a prophet’s appearance than does The Life of Brian. Consequently, habitus as a separate focus of analysis will not receive much attention here. 14 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 4–5. 15 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 5.



The Prophet and the King 33
3 Mug Shot of a Critical Speaker
What, then, are the properties legitimizing a ‘critical speaker’? And how far are these properties dependent on or independent of literary and social, cultural, and historical context? Is it possible to construct a genealogy of free speech whether as a political right or a rhetorical ideal, or both? Attempting to sketch at least part of such a genealogy, Van Renswoude discovers one recurring motif in antique and early medieval literature in the image of ‘one crying in the wilderness’. Indeed, critical speakers such as Ambrose of Milan and Agobard of Lyon seem to have taken on the role of an outcast deliberately.16 On the one hand, the effectiveness of this strategy can be explained pragmatically. The reasoning ‘if someone was killed or exiled for his words, he had probably spoken the truth’17 is apparently independent of time and place. Yet historical and cultural context may be more important than appears at first sight. Do we accept the link between legitimacy on the one hand and persecution on the other as indisputable because it is, or because we are conditioned to do so by the socio-historical discourse (in the broadest sense of the term) within which we move? Is, in other words, the notion of the exiled and prosecuted truth-speaker a sort of Jungian universal image, or can the propensity to put one’s trust in an apparent outcast be historically situated? In Van Renswoude’s account, both approaches – the ‘inconvertible logic’18 of the persecuted criticaster and the historical conditionality of the notion are allowed to exist side by side. Her primary focus lies on the influence of Greek, Roman, and early Christian models of free speech (parrhesia, oratio libera) on the (self-)stylization of late antique and early medieval Christian critical speakers. However, she also suggests a possible link between this (self-)stylization and the image of a true prophet that arises from biblical narratives:
The biblical image of the ‘watchman of the house of Israel’ was key to late antique and early medieval understanding of the relevance of frank criticism for society. By drawing on the charisma and authority of the Old Testament prophets, critics legitimised the use of frank speech and enhanced its persuasive force. Perhaps under the influence of the ‘prophetic trend’, the semantic field of free speech changed. In the sixth and seventh centuries, words and expressions that used to be part of the
16 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 82. 17 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 82. 18 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 82.



34 Wetter
classical repertoire of free speech (libertas, libera vox veritatis, libere loqui) as well as the New Testament of vocabulary of free speech ( fiducia, constantia) were replaced with an Old Testament vocabulary associated with the prophets (correctio, increpatio, admonitio).19
According to Van Renswoude, the biblical image, inspired mostly but not exclusively by the books of Ezekiel, Isaiah, and Jeremiah, adds several distinct ingredients to the schema of a legitimate critical speaker. One is a lack of elocutionary prowess, which underlines the necessity of divine inspiration.20 Gradually, she asserts, the rhetorical ideal of pagan philosophers was replaced by the ideal of ‘pure speech’ and ‘inspired language that was in essence antirhetorical’.21 Another new ingredient is the pastoral aspect of free speech, which presented the critical speaker as a ‘watchman of the house of Israel’.22 While the former two aspects – marginality or persecution and ‘slowness of tongue’ (Ex. 4:10) – move on the level of rhetoric and the legitimation of individual critical speakers (‘frank speech’), the latter – the conception of the speaker/prophet as a ‘watchman’ – has the potential to function as legitimation of free speech as a political concept or ‘right’. Van Renswoude’s findings suggest that the late antique and early medieval ideal of free speech arises mostly from a selection of books ranked with the ‘Latter Prophets’, and less from narratives about prophets in the ‘Former Prophets’. My focus is precisely on the narrative image of prophecy in the Hebrew Bible, and on the question whether the same prototypes populate these narratives, shaping and legitimizing the concept of ‘free speech’.
4 Prophet/Prophecy23
The appropriation of the biblical prophets in late antique and early medieval contexts thus paints a very particular image of these figures: as ‘slow of tongue’, hesitant to take up their task, and watchers over the well-being of Israel. More
19 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 359. 20 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 171, 315. 21 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 359. 22 Van Renswoude, License to Speak, 359. 23 Concerning the definition of prophecy, I make grateful use of Nissinen’s reflections on ‘Prophecy as a Construct’; see M. Nissinen, ‘Prophecy as Construct, Ancient and Modern’, R.P. Gordon, H.M. Barstad (eds), “Thus Speaks Ishtar of Arbela”: Prophecy in Israel, Assyria, and Egypt in the Neo-Assyrian Period, Winona Lake 2013, 11–35.



The Prophet and the King 35
importantly, within these later contexts, the biblical figures serve to legitimize both specific characteristics of individual critical speakers and free speech per se. Does this appropriation concur with the prophets we encounter in the biblical narratives? Is the schema of ‘critical speaker’ indeed compatible with that of ‘prophet’, as far as these narratives are concerned? In the terms of anthropology and religious studies, prophecy is not principally concerned with ‘free speech’. Rather, it is a ‘form of religious mediation, or divination, the purpose of which is to transmit allegedly divine knowledge to human society’.24 Consequently, a ‘prophet’ can be defined as the mouthpiece of a deity, who, whether upon request or unsolicited, transmits this knowledge to his or her contemporaries. As Nissinen also points out, however, prophecy as ‘a socially and historically contingent phenomenon’25 resists overgeneralizing definitions:
Every time someone is referred to as a prophet, the reference is made to a representative of a type of person, presupposing a classification that determines the characteristics of that particular type, as well as a conceptual framework within which the classification is valid. Prophecy, therefore, is an idea needed, defined, and maintained by human communities that provide the matrix without which the idea of prophecy would not make sense.26
Following Wellhausen et alia,27 biblical prophecy has long been defined more in terms of ‘critical speech’ than in terms of ‘divination’.28 Of course, the Wellhausian notion of the prophet as the free and frank speaker who proclaims divine truths in opposition to worldly power structures has been severely criticized as based on romantic and/or Hegelian ideas about the
24 Nissinen, ‘Construct’, 11. Cf., e.g., Wilson’s famous application of this definition to biblical prophecy; R.R. Wilson, Prophecy and Society in Ancient Israel, Philadelphia 1980. 25 Nissinen, ‘Construct’, 11. 26 Nissinen, ‘Construct’, 34. 27 Weber’s notion of the prophet as a charismatic individual moving outside established power structures can be read as an expression of this line of thought as well (M. Weber, Ancient Judaism, New York 1952). As Nissinen observes, this notion is persistent enough to appear in current introductions to the Old Testament, despite radical deconstruction of its origins as rooted in a particular strand of protestant exegesis (Nissinen, ‘Construct’, 27). 28 The latter term in fact carries connotations of ‘false prophecy’ for many readers of the biblical texts, rooted as it appears to be in the ‘abominable’ practices of surrounding peoples (cf. e.g., Num. 22:7; Dtr. 18:10; 1 Sam. 15:23; 2 Kgs 17:17; Jer. 14:14).



36 Wetter
development of Ancient Israelite religion.29 Consequently, the tendency to treat the prophets of the Hebrew Bible as a unique phenomenon whose role was very different from the prophets and diviners of surrounding peoples has made room for a comparative approach in which Israelite prophecy is analyzed within a more general framework of ancient Near Eastern Prophecy.30 As far as prophecy as a historical phenomenon is concerned, this paradigm shift is a welcome reminder that the religion of Israel did not develop in a cultural vacuum, nor always in opposition against its neighbors. And yet the literary, biblical image of the prophet, especially in the so-called Deuteronomistic history, does seem oddly out of place in the line-up of oracles, diviners, intercessors, and royal PR managers emerging from extra-biblical ancient Near Eastern sources.31 Conversely, the comparison with antique critical speakers hints that the schema of ‘free speaker’, which seems to lie at the basis of the Wellhausian construal, may be a fruitful framework of investigation after all.
5 Discourse/Person/Institution
Nissinen differentiates between ‘prophet’ and ‘prophecy’ as two distinct objects of investigation.32 However, within a theoretical framework of ‘free speech’, the two are inseparable: message (in the sense of both form and content) and messenger (in the sense of both individual characteristics and social
