The Prophet and the King 37
position) are mutually constitutive, legitimizing each other in a subtle game of raised and fulfilled (or frustrated) expectations.33 This is where Bourdieu’s triad of discourse, person, and institution enters the game, broadening our perspective on prophetic speech to include, besides person and (content of) message, the rootedness of both in conventions of speech and performance34 as well as the necessity to negotiate the approval of those in authority. In the case of biblical prophetic speech, at least three different ‘institutions’ can be distinguished: (1) a prophet’s audience within the narrative, which may itself consist of individuals with more or less political and religious authority, (2) the authors or editors of the text, and (3) the reader, whether implied or real. Each of these groups may or may not recognize the speaker as an instance of the schema ‘true prophet’ and consequently grant or deny him (rarely her) a ‘license to speak’.
6 Schema/Prototype
The human communities within which authors and editors of the Hebrew Bible worked are largely beyond our reach, and this applies even more forcefully to the communities within which the Israelite prophets functioned. However, cognitive linguistics promises tools to at least approximate the realm of thought in which they functioned, by abstracting from the biblical texts the categories, schemata, and prototypes that populated this ideological realm and structured Israelite (thinking about) being in the world. Basic to this approach is the insight that human cognition functions via categories, or schemata.35 Such schemata are neither binary nor universal; rather, they are polythetic and contingent upon socio-cultural, historical, and even geographic background.36 There is a continuous mutual exchange between a schema and
33 Needless to say, Nissinen’s claim remains valid in the sense that we have access only to the prophecies (and these, too, in edited form), not to the historical figures of any individual prophets. Nissinen himself makes this distinction very clear in M. Nissinen, ‘How Prophecy Became Literature’, SJOT 19 (2005), 153–172. 34 ‘Rootedness in conventions of speech and performance’ lies at the basis of form-critical analyses of biblical prophecy as well. However, while form-criticism assumes the possibility and desirability of deducting the original historical context of prophetic speech, my focus lies predominantly on the literary image of the prophet. 35 Van Wolde, Reframing. 36 An example of the way in which geography determines our schemata is the use of ‘upper’ and ‘lower’ to describe the layout of Egypt. Within a worldview in which ‘up’ equals ‘north’, and ‘low’ equals south, this use is counter-intuitive. Within the specifically Egyptian logic,



38
Wetter
concrete instances (objects, persons, events etc.) that are categorized according to this schema and simultaneously serve to further flesh it out. A prototype, as defined within cognitive linguistics, is an ideal instance of a particular schema or category within a specific socio-cultural background.37
According to the Wellhausian construal, which is to some extent mirrored in Van Renswoude’s analysis of late antique and early medieval critical speakers, a ‘true’ prophet is recognized on the basis of a schema that is at least partly defined by strained relations with established worldly or even religious authorities. Whether the reader should grant a prophet a ‘license to speak’ then depends on the question in how far the same prophet is denied a ‘license to speak’ within the dynamics of the narrative. Needless to say, the present paper can only scratch on the surface of this issue. Nevertheless, the few examples that will be explored here can at least point out the direction which further research on the correlations between prophecy in the Hebrew Bible and antique, medieval and modern concepts of free and critical speech may take. Since dealing with ‘institutions’ is one of the foci of my analysis, I have selected individuals marked as prophet (
נביא ) who find themselves in direct contact with the powers that be (usually the king): Samuel, Nathan, Elijah, and Micaiah.
7 ‘True Prophets’ – A Series of Mug Shots
What makes a prophet ‘true’, in biblical terms? Dtr 15:22 is very clear on the issue: if the words of a prophet do not come to pass, he does not speak in the name of YHWH. However, as, e.g., Serge Frolov points out, the issue is more complex: a prophet’s message may be ambiguous, to say the least, and in some cases, the prophet may even be the communicator of divine deception rather than truth.38 Besides, as Freedman and Frey argue convincingly, especially prophecies that have ‘come true’ according to the text should raise the suspicion of the reader, since here the likelihood is the greatest that a prophet’s words have been tampered with in order to make them fit later developments.39
however, in which the course if the Nile determines the definition of these spatial categories, ‘up’ equals ‘south’ makes perfect sense. 37 Van Wolde, Reframing, 35. 38 S. Frolov, ‘1 Samuel 1–8. The Prophet as Agent Provocateur’, in: Grabbe, Nissinen, Constructs of Prophecy, 77–85.
39 Freedman, Frey, ‘False Prophecy’.



The Prophet and the King 39
The reader therefore needs other criteria in order to separate the prophetic wheat from the chaff, criteria that concern the person of the prophet as much as they do his (or her) message.
7.1 Samuel
As Frolov puts it, Samuel is ‘the first biblical character . . . who not only walks and talks like a prophet but also is consistently characterized as such’.40 Samuel indeed functions as an extremely conscientious mouthpiece of YHWH, and is generally recognized as such by his audience. He seems an ideal candidate for the role of ‘prototypical true prophet’ of the Hebrew Bible. At the same time, however, Samuel is an extremely atypical instance of the schema ‘prophet’, in the sense that he embodies the link between two very different forms of political organization: tribal alliance and monarchy. The role of the prophet within each differs considerably. Within the tribal alliance, the prophet (i.e., Samuel) holds political as well as religious power (e.g., 1 Sam 7:15). His audience – the group at which he aims his ‘critical speech’ – consists of the people at large. Within the monarchy, there is a precarious balance between the religious authority of the prophet and the political authority of the king. Samuel continues to address his admonitions to the people as a whole (e.g., 1 Sam 12), but the king is always included in his warnings: ‘If you persist in doing evil, both you and your king will be swept away’. Samuel retains the right to openly criticize king Saul, and even announces his replacement by ‘one better than him’ (1 Sam 15:28). Saul is stunned, acknowledges his sins, and begs Samuel to reconsider (1 Sam 15:24–25). When Samuel persists in his judgment that ‘YHWH has rejected you from being king over Israel’ (1 Sam 15:26), Saul does not argue – the word of the prophet overrules that of the king. Samuel, then, this apparent first prototype of the prophet functioning within a monarchical setting, is not at all one ‘crying in the wilderness’. Both the people and the king accept his words as valid, although this does not necessarily entail that they always heed his admonitions (e.g., 1 Sam 8:19). Although never referred to as such, Samuel does fulfill the function of a watchman of Israel, who warns the people against the consequences of their actions. Other mediums and diviners are banished from the land (1 Sam 28:3), but Samuel enjoys an authority that surpasses the king’s. Neither does he lack elocutionary force – addressing large crowds or confronting kings appear to be part of his daily routine. Last but not least, although he shows some reluctance before communicating YHWH’s first commission (the judgment concerning
40 Frolov, ‘Agent Provocateur’, 78.



40 Wetter
Eli’s sons, 1 Sam 3:15), it does not take much to persuade him to take up his office. His license to speak does not depend on conforming to a schema similar to that of antique and medieval critical speakers – the outcast who is willing to suffer for the truth. As acknowledged representative of YHWH, he is respected both by his audience within the story and by the potential reader.
7.2 Nathan
The next in line, Nathan, functions within a very different setting. He acts as a full-fledged royal advisor at the court of king David. He is not linked to a sanctuary, nor does he fulfill any of the leadership roles (military commander, judge) that Samuel had taken upon himself. His only task is to inform the king about YHWH’s plans (2 Sam 7), and confront him with his sins (2 Sam 12:1–14). According to Wilson, Nathan is an ‘establishment prophet’41 who, together with Gad, forms a couple of ‘central intermediaries [who] had the responsibility of maintaining the stability of the social structure, and they therefore promoted orderly change and opposed anything that threatened the social equilibrium’.42 Indeed, Nathan’s messages can best be understood within a framework of what Beate Pongratz-Leisten has termed Herrschaftswissen43 – divine knowledge, communicated to a ruler in order to enable him to reign effectively. As Nissinen has shown, such knowledge has a distinctly critical potential; the ultimate aim, however, is to sustain a ruler’s power.44 Unsurprisingly, Nathan does not encounter any of the hardships belonging to the schema of a prophet as ‘one crying in the wilderness’. Even his confrontation with David about Bathsheba (2 Sam 12) does not incite the king’s anger – rather, it underlines David’s suitability as leader of a people whose ultimate king is YHWH.
7.3 Elijah
Although an occasional prophet populates the next chapters of the Deuteronomists’ account (1 Kgs 11:29; 13:11–29; 14:2; 16:17), Elijah is the first after Samuel and Nathan to leave a lasting impression on the reader – and the first indeed to fit the schema of the persecuted warden of truth. His strained relations with Ahab, apostatizing king of Israel, and his foreign wife Jezebel, are
41 Wilson, Prophecy, 265. 42 Wilson, Prophecy, 302.
43 B. Pongratz-Leisten, Herrschaftswissen in Mesopotamien: Formen der Kommunikation zwischen Gott und König im 2. und 1. Jahrtausend v. Chr. (State Archives of Assyria Studies 10), Helsinki 1999. 44 Nissinen, ‘Potential’, 29–31.



The Prophet and the King 41
proverbial. Ahab calls him ‘troubler of Israel’ (1 Kgs 18:17) – not quite undeservedly, since Elijah had started his career by announcing a lengthy drought that would be lifted only at his – Elijah’s – command (1 Kgs 17:1). ‘Crying in the wilderness’ is certainly applicable to this figure, much of whose life is spent in isolation, hiding from Ahab’s and especially Jezebel’s thirst for revenge (1 Kgs 17:2; 19:3). His messages have little to do with Herrschaftswissen in the strict sense – they are certainly not intended to keep Ahab on the throne. It is not until the last encounter between Elijah and the king that the latter repents of some of his sins (1 Kgs 21:27). Yet the reader is made to understand that Elijah is a prototype of a true prophet. His license to speak, however, does not depend on the stylization as an outcast per se. It is the introduction of Ahab as ‘more evil in the eyes of YHWH than anyone before him’ (1 Kgs 16:30) that legitimizes anyone who speaks out against him as a true messenger of YHWH. The persecution Elijah has to endure serves at least as much to underline Ahab’s and Jezebel’s wickedness as it does to substantiate his claim to the skeptron. Put differently: Elijah’s perseverance, even under persecution, validates his character as one worthy to communicate the words of YHWH, but it does not validate these words themselves – at least not as much as the miracles associated with Elijah and, perhaps even more importantly, the contrast between Elijah’s words and Ahab’s deeds do.
7.4 Micaiah
Next in line, still during the reign of Ahab, is Micaiah.45 He makes his appearance as the last prophet of YHWH in Israel, summoned after all other prophets have given their support to the plans of Ahab and Jehoshaphat, king of Israel, to wage war against Aram. Ahab is hesitant to call him: ‘I hate him because he never prophesies anything good about me, but evil’ (1 Kgs 22:8). Nevertheless, at the urging of Jehoshaphat, Micaiah is summoned, and declares the encouraging prophecies of all his colleagues void. He does not, however, simply expose them as ‘false prophets’, who willingly go against the word of YHWH. Instead, he declares that YHWH himself has put a ‘deceiving spirit’ (1 Kgs 22:21–23) into their mouths in order to entice Ahab to go to battle – and be killed. As was the case with Elijah, Micaiah suffers the wrath of the king, who has him imprisoned ‘until I return safely’ from the battle (1 Kgs 22:27). Alas for the king – and,
45 On Micaiah and the dynamics of social expectations and honor, see the contribution by William L. Kelly in this volume: ‘Prophets, Kings, and Honour in the Narrative of 1 Kgs 22 (pp. 64–75).



42 Wetter
who knows, for Micaiah as well – only the king’s dead body returns to Samaria (verse 37). As Barbara Schmitz points out, the issue of ‘true’ versus ‘false’ prophecy is less than straightforward in this passage.46 Although most scholars tend to draw the line between the four hundred court prophets (including one Zedekiah, who performs a prophetic sign) on the one hand and Micaiah on the other, labeling the former as ‘false’ and the latter as ‘true’,47 Schmitz suggests that the narrative itself does not necessitate this evaluation. Rather, the text hints ‘dass die klassischen Bewertungen für Prophetie nicht (mehr) aufgehen, wie es z.B. das Prophetengesetz in Dtn 18,9–22 anvisiert hat’.48 According to Schmitz, the task of judging the validity of each prophetic uttering in the narrative falls to the reader. However, this task is less complicated than may appear from the mixed assortment of prophetic figures and voices in this chapter. Indeed, I would claim that Micaiah’s license to speak, as far as the reader is concerned, is established before he enters the scene, by Ahab’s derogatory remarks about him (1 Kgs 22:8). Ahab’s reputation as a king who ‘does evil in the eyes of YHWH’ is sufficiently substantiated to guess that any prophet who criticizes him must be genuine. As was the case with Elijah, the chapter thus presents a variation on the ‘crying-in-the-wilderness’ schema, whereby the legitimacy of the prophet and his message does not depend on marginalization per se, but on marginalization by a very specific individual.
8 Conclusions: Between Candor and Conformity
Almost 30 years ago, in his anthropological approach to prophecy in Ancient Israel, Wilson already insisted that prophets used ‘stereotypical speech patterns’ and ‘certain standard behavioral patterns’ that marked them as prophets.49 He claims, in short, that ‘[i]n order to be taken seriously by their society, the prophets acted and spoke in the way that the society expected prophets to speak and act’. In a sense, Wilson anticipates Fish and Bourdieu’s conclusions about the impossibility of free speech by linking a prophet’s authority to his positive reception by a ‘support group’ – or community, or institution, if you will. There are, however, two difficulties with Wilson’s approach. The first is
46 B. Schmitz, Prophetie und Königtum (FAT 60), Tübingen 2008, 317ff. 47 See Schmitz, Prophetie, 319–320 for an enumeration of scholarly positions. 48 Schmitz, Prophetie, 326. 49 Wilson, Prophecy, 251–252.



The Prophet and the King 43
perhaps most fundamental: the biblical sources tell us much less about the phenomenon of prophecy in Ancient Israel than they do about the ideas and ideologies of authors or editors of the post-exilic period.50 The second, however, is the most interesting for the approach taken in this paper. Paradoxically, more often than not conformity to the pattern of behavior associated with a prophet does not ensure that the latter is heard – in the sense of ‘taken seriously’ – within the dynamics of the narrative. Although a reader might instantly recognize a speaker’s use of the ‘legitimate instruments of expression’ marking him as a legitimate prophet, the persons he confronts within the story do not. In the terms of cognitive linguistics, the audience within the story regularly recognizes an individual as instance of a schema ‘prophet’, but refuses to grant him a license to speak nonetheless. Of course, as Nissinen puts it, it is ultimately ‘up to the community to decide whether or not a prophecy is worth communicating, which conclusions should be drawn from it, and what are the criteria of true and false prophecy’.51 It is the community (or the editors) who posthumously passes the skeptron to the prophets. To put it bluntly: in order to gain a place in the ranks of prophets considered as ‘central’ by the editors of the biblical canon, a certain measure of ‘peripherality’ vis-à-vis the religious, political, and economic establishment seems to have been an asset. This statement must be nuanced, however: only marginalization that results from opposition against an establishment that had already been characterized as acting in opposition against the commandments of YHWH by the authors/ editors functions as legitimation of a prophet and his or her message. It is clear from the biblical texts that the authors/editors support critical speech directed against institutions or individuals who fail to live up to divine precepts. But do they also hold up ‘free speech’ as an inalienable right? Do biblical critical speakers deserve protection, independent of the message they proclaim? To pose the question in this way is to answer it: although throughout the Hebrew Bible, a ‘good king’ is marked by the willingness to let himself be corrected by the words of a ‘true prophet’, freedom of speech is granted only to individuals worthy of the skeptron in the eyes of the authors and editors. Prophets who fail to live up to the author’s ideals are silenced radically: anyone exposed as a ‘false prophet’ deserves death (Dtr 13:5). Discourses claiming the ‘Judeo-Christian heritage’ as foundation of the political right of free speech are destabilized by the realization that many contemporary
50 Cf. Nissinen, ‘Literature’. 51 Nissinen, ‘Literature’, 155.



44 Wetter
champions of free speech52 would have been brought to death under Deuteronomic law. The charge, then as well as now, would have been neither lèsemajesté nor agitation, but blasphemy.
52 Dutch examples are Pim Fortuijn and Theo van Gogh, both of whom were assassinated, one by a representative of the radical left, the other by a radical Muslim.

Isaiah and the Oldest ‘Biblical’ Prophetic Narrative
Graeme Auld
1 Introduction
Many interesting features relate to the so-called ‘synoptic’ material within Samuel-Kings: the portions of these books which are shared with Chronicles. It has been argued that they are a major source of the familiar books, and even the root-system out of which these books have developed.1 However that may be, this paper starts from a plain fact. In a number of apparently significant respects, these shared portions of text are remarkably untypical of SamuelKings as a whole. Detaching these texts from their familiar contexts offers a fresh perspective for examining their contents, including their narrative portrayal of divine/human intermediation. R.F. Person rightly insists that what is distinctive in the Chronicler’s attitude to prophecy and intermediation should be plotted over against the synoptic material rather than the developed books of Samuel and Kings.2 A recent paper3 identifies four ideologically neutral indicators of the distinctiveness of the synoptic texts as a whole. (1)
ישׁ , ‘there is/are’, though it is found in Chronicles (4×) and is used much more frequently in Samuel-Kings (24×), is wholly absent from the texts they share. (2) הנה is not common in synoptic texts (only some 10×); and again it is used more densely in the rest of Samuel-Kings than in non-synoptic Chronicles. (3) The chronological framework of the shared monarchic narrative exhibits only quite minimal variation in terminology between Samuel-Kings and Chronicles. And here too the range of temporal markers used is less in the synoptic texts than in Chronicles, and less in Chronicles than in Samuel-Kings. Most notably, quite the commonest temporal expressions in Samuel-Kings as a whole ( היום and היום הזה )
1 A.G. Auld, Kings without Privilege: David and Moses in the Story of the Bible’s Kings, Edinburgh 1994; A.G. Auld, Samuel at the Threshold: Selected Works of Graeme Auld (SOTSS), Aldershot 2004; A.G. Auld, I & II Samuel: A Commentary (OTL), Louisville KY 2011; R.F. Person, The Deuteronomic History and the Book of Chronicles: Scribal Works in an Oral World (AIL 6), Atlanta GA 2010. 2 R.F. Person, ‘Prophets in the Deuteronomic History and the Book of Chronicles: A Reassessment’, M.R. Jacobs and R.F. Person (eds), Israelite Prophecy and the Deuteronomistic History: Portrait, Reality, and the Formation of a History (AIL 14), Atlanta GA 2013, 187–88.
3 A.G. Auld, ‘The Shaping of Israelite History in Samuel and Kings’, RB 121 (2014), 195–216, including full references.



46 Auld
are wholly absent from the shared texts. (4) The prevalence of the infinitive absolute (in its various uses) is similar in both synoptic and non-synoptic portions of Chronicles. But this grammatical feature is used very much more in non-synoptic Samuel-Kings. It is also used rather more in synoptic portions of Samuel-Kings than in the parallel portions of Chronicles (probably under influence from the density of usage in non-synoptic portions). To underscore this four-fold distinctiveness of the shared texts, it should be added that a temporal marker (3) is never found near an infinitive absolute (4), while such linkage is a common feature of the book of Samuel (some 10×). Further examples of such neutral indicators will be reported later in this discussion. This paper is in two main parts: the first reviews all but one of the synoptic passages relating to prophecy and intermediation; the second, the wholly exceptional situation concerning Hezekiah and Isaiah.
2 Synoptic Overview
The following shared texts report divine/human communication, with or without a mediator:

2.1 Ahijah is Original
1 Kgs 12:15||2 Chron. 10:15 does not inform us about the content of the word spoken by Ahijah. It has been urged that this reference to a message by agency of Ahijah from Shiloh would have made no sense to a reader unfamiliar with the (non-synoptic) narrative featuring Ahijah in 1 Kgs 11: when we read the synoptic verse in the context of 2 Chron. 10, we can understand it (only) because we are already familiar with the parent text in 1 Kgs 11:29–39.5 However sensible this objection may seem at first sight, two strong arguments can be advanced against it. Firstly, there are two analogous situations in the familiar book of Kings. In the cases of both Jehu ben Hanani (1 Kgs 16:7, 12) and Jonah ben Amittai (2 Kgs 14:25), we find an oracle mentioned but no content reported. Secondly, in the synoptic verse, Ahijah is named differently from the other instances in Kings. Only in 1 Kgs 12:15||2 Chron. 10:15 is he called simply ‘Ahijah the Shilonite’.6 However, at the beginning of each other (non-synoptic) narrative in which he features, he is (re-)introduced with a title, whether ‘the prophet’ or ‘his [Yahweh’s] servant’ or both together:
in אחיה ]השילני[ הנביא
in 14:18. We עבדו אחיהו הנביא in 15:29; and עבדו אחיה הנביא ; 1 Kgs 11:29; 14:2 should deduce that synoptic 1 Kgs 12:15||2 Chron. 10:15 has influenced the drafting of Kings in divergent directions. Firstly, the model of this (puzzlingly brief?) synoptic note has been followed in drafting the (non-synoptic) notes about Jehu and Jonah. Next, the narrative in 1 Kgs 11:29–39 may well have been written precisely to supply the content of the word spoken by agency of Ahijah, and so satisfy the curiosity of readers of 1 Kgs 12:15||2 Chron. 10:15.7 We shall discuss below how the distinctive wording of this brief but apparently influential sentence relates to two further synoptic contexts.

Against such a background, the absence of any such role in the restoration of Joash after his grandmother Athaliah’s usurpation of power constitutes a remarkable break in pattern. It is mostly the king that takes the initiative in communication between king and deity (2 Sam. 5; 7; 1 Kgs 3; 8; 9; 22; 2 Kgs 22). Only once (in 2 Sam. 24) is it certainly the deity, while the two reports in 1 Kgs 12 are so brief as to be ambiguous on the issue. Most of the intermediaries (from Nathan to Huldah) are termed [ נביא[ה . Of the three exceptions, Gad is a ‘seer’ ( חזה ); Ahijah is simply ‘someone from Shiloh’; and Shemaiah is ‘the man of God’ ( איש האלהים ). While only Gad is termed חזה (2 Sam. 24:11), the related abstract noun חז]י[ון concludes the summary of the whole communication mediated to David through Nathan: 2 Sam. 7:17). These may have influenced the wording in 2 Sam. 7:2 (MT) of David’s request to Nathan ( ראה נא ); however, the parallel in 1 Chron. 17:1 reads simply הנה , and that is also attested by LXX in both verses.9 In synoptic texts, only David and Solomon are called Yahweh’s ‘servant’, whether by themselves in their prayers ( 10)עבדך or in divine speech to them through a prophet ( 11.)עבדי The sole exception – better, extension – to this is found in 2 Kgs 8:19, where the narrator writes ‘for the sake of David his servant’ ( למען דוד עבדו ). Correspondingly, only David (2 Sam. 7:18) and Solomon (1 Kgs 8:22) out of all the kings and intermediaries reported on are explicitly described as speaking directly to Yahweh. direct approach of David and Solomon to Yahweh, and דרש of the enquiries made about the divine will by Jehoshaphat and the king of Israel (1 Kgs 22) and by Josiah (2 Kgs 22), and in each case through prophets. We may add that it is only in these two ‘prophetic’ narratives that הנה is used, twice each by Micaiah and Huldah, and once by a royal messenger.13 If we can assume that this distinction in the root-text between שאל and דרש was noticed by those who extended the text as they wrote the book of Samuel, then it gives greater poignancy to the word-play in the earlier chapters about first Hannah ‘asking’ Yahweh for a son, and then the people ‘asking’ Yahweh for a king who turned out to be named ‘Asked-for’ ( שאול ). Non-synoptic Samuel uses דרש only of Saul consulting Samuel (1 Sam. 9:9; 28:7). And we find it in several non-synoptic contexts in Kings: 1 Kgs 14:5; 2 Kgs 1[5×]; 3:11; 8:8. In Chronicles, דרש is very much more common, some 35× from Saul (1 Chron. 10:13–14) onwards.14
2.3 Shifts in Perspective
The issue of context and appropriate perspective is particularly important for correct understanding of the brief reports in 2 Sam. 5:19, 23. Read in traditional canonical order, these ‘askings’ by David come at the end of a long series: Judg. 1:1; 18:5; 20:18, 23, 27; 1 Sam. 10:22; 14:37; 22:10, 13, 15; 23:2, 4; 28:6; 30:8; 2 Sam. 2:1. In each of these fifteen previous askings, as in 2 Sam. 5||1 Chron. 14, the Hebrew idiom is שאל ב־ . In Judg. 18:5; 20:18; 1 Sam. 14:37; 22:13, 15, as in synoptic 1 Chron. 14:10, 14, it is ‘the deity’ that is consulted ( שאל באלהים ); in the other passages, it is Yahweh ( שאל ביהוה ), as in the synoptic parallel 2 Sam. 5:19, 23. The attendant circumstances of half of these briefly described consultations are as opaque as the synoptic pair. But six of them (Judg. 18:5; 1 Sam. 14:37; 22:10, 13, 15; 30:8) mention the involvement of a priest. Then the still more detailed Judg. 20:27–28 talks of the ark and of Phinehas ministering before it, but does not explicitly state that this priest helped to secure the divine response. Then 1 Sam. 28:6 reports that Yahweh made no response by any one of three methods to Saul’s enquiry: not by dreams, not by Urim, and not by prophets. The relevance of context is this. When read as the final members of a long series, the circumstances of David’s enquiries in 2 Sam. 5:19, 23||1 Chron. 14:10, 14,
13 As already noted, הנה introduces David’s words to Nathan in 1 Chron. 17:1 and in synoptic 2 Sam. 7:2 (LXX), while only 2 Sam. 7:2 (MT) uses ראה . 14 R. Thelle, in her careful overview of such issues (‘Reflections of Ancient Israelite Divination in the Former Prophets’, in: M.R. Jacobs and R.F. Person [eds], Israelite Prophecy and the Deuteronomistic History: Portrait, Reality, and the Formation of a History [AIL 14], Atlanta GA 2013, 7–33), notes that שאל is used mainly in Judges and Samuel and דרש in Kings. The present paper explores the roots of these preferences.



50
Auld
just like the other less detailed and hence more opaque reports within the series, may be clarified by the other more detailed members of the set: even where it is not stated, the mediating role of a priest may be fairly intuited. Read on their own, however, or rather as part of a different series – in the broader context of the synoptic texts under review in this paper, where several features invite us to pair David with Solomon over against the following kings – David’s ‘askings’ may instead imply unmediated access to the deity. As reported in 1 Chron. 14, David’s ‘asking’ of the deity follows the first stage in his collection of the ark (1 Chron. 13). It is neither stated nor denied that there is a link between his recovery of this potent symbol of divine presence and his asking for guidance. However, divine irruption ( פרץ ) is a key element in both narratives (2 Sam. 5:20||1 Chron. 14:11 and 6:8||13:11). And the author of Judg. 20:27–28 may have intuited such a link between ark and divination. On the other hand, there is no explicit priestly role in the account of the ark’s return.
2.4 Exceptions
Within these synoptic contexts, two areas of divergence must be noted. The smaller one relates to Gad and Manasseh, and the much larger to Hezekiah. The verb ‘speak’ ( דבר ) commonly has ‘Yahweh’ as subject in synoptic portions, but almost always within reported speech.15 However, the actual narrator does claim Yahweh as speaker at three points. One of these is straightforward, and was discussed above: ‘. . . in order to establish his word which he/Yahweh had spoken by hand of Ahijah the Shilonite . . .’ (1 Kgs 12:15||2 Chron. 10:15). Within the narrative of David’s census, however, we find textual variety: Yahweh in Chronicles simply speaks to Gad, David’s seer; but in Samuel, it is Yahweh’s word that comes to Gad – and he is no longer simply termed David’s ‘seer’ but is first called ‘the prophet’ as well.
2 Sam. 24:11 1 Chron. 21:9
וידבר יהוה אל־גד חזה דויד ודבר יהוה היה אל־גד הנביא חזה דוד
Thirdly, and similarly, Chronicles suggests unmediated address by Yahweh to Manasseh and his people, while Kings reports the role of ‘his servants the prophets’ in Yahweh’s declaration of judgment.

Assessment of this third divergence is complex: neither ולא שמעו nor ולא הקשיבו is attested anywhere synoptically;16 and ‘his servants the prophets’ is a theme of Kings never found in Chronicles. 2 Kgs 17:13–14 anticipate both expressions from 21:9–10. Discussion of the Hezekiah narratives will follow some further observations about word-usage. But, before passing on, we should note that Yahweh’s ‘word’ is attested in synoptic narratorial contexts only in 2 Sam. 24:11 (but not 1 Chron. 21:9); 1 Kgs 12:15||2 Chron. 10:15; and 1 Kgs 22:5, 19||2 Chron. 18:4, 18.
2.5 Additional Significant Word-Usages
Scrutiny of the language used in the portions of the synoptic texts under review in this paper had the unintended benefit of adding further non-ideological indicators of the distinctiveness of these texts within the wider context of Samuel-Kings as a whole, as well as of Chronicles, to the four noted at the beginning. (5) Huldah (2 Kgs 22:19) is the only synoptic prophet who reinforces her report of divine speech with
נאם יהוה (‘utterance of Yahweh’); and this phrase is never repeated in Chronicles.17 (6) In the same verse, she notes that King Josiah has ‘wept’ in response to the divine word; and no other character in Chronicles weeps.18 (7) Micaiah, protesting his integrity to the kings’ messenger (1 Kgs 22:14), is the only synoptic character to reinforce his words with ‘as Yahweh lives’ ( חי יהוה ). This oath never reappears in Chronicles, but is common in Samuel (17×)19 and Kings (13×).20 (8) The synoptic context 1 Kgs 22:32 (but not the actual parallel in 2 Chron. 18:31) uses אך .אך itself appears synoptically only once, and nearby. Indeed it may be from the neighbouring

Non-synoptic Chronicles uses אך only twice more (1 Chron. 22:12; 2 Chron. 30:11), but Samuel 14×21 and Kings 14×.22 Items (7) and (8) deserve fuller discussion. While 5(נאם יהוה ) is one of the most frequently used formulae throughout the books of the Latter Prophets (it is absent only from Habakkuk and Jonah), 7( חי יהוה ) is much more prominent in Samuel and Kings; and its use in these books is part of a wider phenomenon. The whole cluster ‘life/live/living’ ( חי\חיה\חיים ) is very rare in synoptic contexts, and is found there only in 1 Kgs 8:40; 12:6; 22:14; 2 Kgs 11:12; 14:9, 17. Perhaps even more remarkably, there are only two further instances of this word-cluster in all of Chronicles; and each of these seems instructive in a different way. The positive statement about Joab at the end of 1 Chron. 11:8 that he ‘let live’ or ‘restored to life’ the remnant of Jerusalem ( ויחיה את־שאר העיר ) may have been an earlier component of the report of Jerusalem’s capture, but suppressed from 2 Sam. 5:9 as later drafts of Samuel became more hostile to David’s lieutenant.23 Then the use of חיים within non-synoptic 2 Chron. 25:12 could have been influenced by the nearby synoptic pair 25:18, 25 (= 2 Kgs 14:9, 17). If our proposal about 1 Chron. 11:8 is acceptable, then 2 Chron. 25:12 will supply the only instance of the Chronicler actually adding to his source-text an element from this cluster. By contrast, ‘life/live/living’ is a very frequent theme in Samuel-Kings,24 and also both Deuteronomy and Ezekiel, as well as in sections of Jeremiah.25 Independent testimony to later-biblical interest in this ‘life’ cluster comes from a different synoptic exercise: the comparative reading of 2 Kgs 18–20 and Isa. 36–39. One pair of identical instances in 2 Kgs 19:4, 16||Isa. 37:4, 17 concerns

Presumably, just as in the case of the infinitive absolute, heavy usage of אך in non-synoptic Samuel and Kings will have influenced the redrafting of some synoptic passages.
3 Isaiah and Hezekiah
We have just noted some examples of divergence within the tradition. As we pass to the second main section of our discussion, it is important to stress how exceptional such divergence is. Whether we are reviewing the presence or absence of the eight ‘indicators’ in synoptic texts, or whether we are reviewing their usage of stock ‘prophetic’ terminology, what compels attention is the stability of the textual evidence. After comparing the relevant portions of Samuel-Kings with Chronicles and comparing the several ancient witnesses to each, we may be confident that this older ‘prophetic’ narrative has been preserved almost completely unchanged in the available witnesses to the familiar biblical books.31 However, as we turn to the more extended narratives about Hezekiah and Isaiah, we enter a world of very different textual relationships.
3.1 Overview
The Hezekiah narratives in 2 Kgs 18–20 (together with the very similar alternative version in Isa. 36–39) and 2 Chron. 29–32 differ more from each other than those about any other king in Jerusalem since David and Solomon; and Isaiah ben Amoz does figure in both. Apart from the almost standard features of the outer framework,32 the two narratives are most readily compared and contrasted in 2 Kgs 18:17–20:19 and 2 Chron. 32:9–31. Our eight ‘non-partisan’ verbal indicators, taken together, offer a fresh perspective for such a comparison. Neither account uses
1(יש ) at all; they share the same temporal transition (3):
30 They are also linked in Deut. 17:19; Josh. 4:14; Eccl. 5:17, 19; 8:15 (cf. כל־ימי חייך in Gen. 3:14, 17; Deut. 4:9; 6:2; 16:3; Josh. 1:5; Ps. 128:5; כל־ימי חייה in Prov. 31:12; and מספר ימי חייהם in Eccl. 2:3). Other close links in Deuteronomy include 4:4, 10: 5:3; 30:20; 31:13, 27. 31 This remarkable degree of textual stability appears to tell against Person’s insistence (‘Prophets in the Deuteronomic History’) that the alternative instantiations of a shared tradition manifest in Samuel-Kings and Chronicles are typical of an oral world. 32 2 Kgs 18:1–3||2 Chron. 29:1–2 and 2 Kgs 20:20–21||2 Chron. 32:32–33.

2 Kgs 20:1a||2 Chron. 32:24a); and both use an ( בימים ההם חלה חזקיהו]עד[למות infinitive absolute (4) at the same stage in the argument (2 Kgs 18:30||2 Chron. 32:13). The Kings (and Isaiah) version uses all of the other five indicators (some repeatedly), but the Chronicles version none of them at all: no 2( הנה ), no נאם 5(יהוה ), no ‘weeping’ (6), no ‘life’ (7), and no 8( אך ). In short, and from this (limited?) perspective, the Chronicler’s account of Hezekiah, the Assyrians, and Isaiah, is entirely typical of the range of synoptic reports of kings and prophets reviewed earlier in this paper. By contrast, the much longer account in Kings (and Isaiah) includes several features of non-synoptic Samuel and Kings. As for other recurring members of the ‘prophetic’ family vocabulary already reviewed, neither שאל (‘ask’) nor דרש (‘enquire’) is used within either report.33 ‘Yahweh’s word’ ( דבר יהוה ) has been used synoptically in 2 Chron. 11:2||2 Kgs 12:22 and 18:4, 18||1 Kgs 22:5, 19 and appears often elsewhere in Chronicles. However, this common expression is absent from 2 Chron. 32, although it is used in 2 Kgs 20:4, 16, 19 (||Isa 38:4; 39:5, 8). And similarly ‘speak’ ( דבר ) has Yahweh as its subject in 2 Kgs 19:21; 20:9, but never in 2 Chron. 32. It is hard to see why no mention of Yahweh speaking or of Yahweh’s word – not to speak of the other features of synoptic prophetic stories faithfully preserved by the Chronicler – survived in 2 Chron. 32 if they were part of the Chronicler’s source. In terms of our neutral indicators, the crucial difference between these Hezekiah narratives is that ‘life’ and ‘living’ (7), while totally absent from 2 Chron. 32, are not just present in the Kings (and Isaiah) version but are actually a prominent element of the longer narrative they offer. As already noted (in 2.5 above), it is ‘living God’ that Sennacherib mocks (2 Kgs 19:4, 16), and it is the continuing life of Jerusalem’s king that is brought into question (20:1, 7). Then Chronicles uses ‘days’ (3) just once (32:26) in addition to the shared clause just noted (32:24); but it is used repeatedly in 2 Kgs 19:3, 25; 20:1, 5, 6, 8, 17, 19 (as also in 18:4 and 20:20 which lie outside the portion being compared). And
הנה
(2), used in only two of the other synoptic prophetic stories (1 Kgs 22:13, 23, 25; 2 Kgs 22:16, 20), is found in 2 Kgs 18:21; 19:7, 11, 35; 20:5, 17. However, the other very striking difference is that Isaiah is named only once within the shorter narrative of 2 Chron. 32, while he is named 10× in text shared by Kings and Isaiah34 and 3× more in Kings pluses.35

3.2 Assyrian Embassy
Every example of these several features that are distinctive of the version in Kings (and Isaiah) of the Hezekiah and Isaiah story is concentrated in the second part (2 Kgs 19–20) of that longer story, with the sole exception of one instance of הנה in 2 Kgs 18:21. Therefore, it is appropriate to start our more detailed (synoptic) comparison within 2 Kgs 18, where such features are almost completely absent. 2 Kgs 18:17–37 is twice as long as 2 Chron. 32:9–19; but both discuss the same key question: is anyone able to ‘deliver’ ( הציל ) Jerusalem from the king of Assyria? In almost every verse, the shorter 2 Chron. 32:9–19 includes significant links with its parallel in Kings. No technical prophetic language is used.36 On the other hand, what the Assyrian embassy has to say functions as a sort of false prophecy. One function of the following tables is to illustrate the complexity of the apparently innocent phrase ‘its parallel’: expressions which are clearly related to each other often do not appear in these ‘parallel’ texts in the same relative order. Wido van Peursen and Eep Talstra nicely illustrate37 how much more difficult it is to present diagrammatically comparisons between the Hezekiah stories in Kings and Chronicles than those in Kings and Isaiah. The attention they draw to same words uttered by different persons in each version and shifts between narrated text and direct speech is also helpful; however, the alleged direction of these shifts is unrelated to the ‘computer-assisted analysis’.

Auld
The recent paper noted above39 documents the very high level of stability in the chronological markers attested in synoptic portions of Kings and Chronicles. The variation exhibited at the start of this report is all the more remarkable, given that the two versions will share בימים ההם at 2 Kgs 20:1||2 Chron. 32:24. ‘After this’ ( אחר זה ) in 2 Chron. 32:9, though apparently unremarkable, is never used elsewhere in HB.40 On the other side, 2 Kgs 18:13 opens with a precise dating formula: ‘In 14 year of king Hezekiah . . .’. ‘In x year of . . .’ (ל־ X בשנה ) is used in Jeremiah at beginning (1:2) and end (52:31), and in 39:2 (to report the beginning of the end of Jerusalem). It is also used in two significant introductions in the book of Ezekiel (1:1; 40:1), but is most prominent in Ezek. 26–33 (7×).41 This form of words is used only once synoptically (2 Kgs 23:23||2 Chron. 35:19): ‘In 18 year of king Josiah [. . . this Passover was achieved in Jerusalem]’; but is never found elsewhere in Chronicles. However, it is employed to mark certain key stages in the narrative told by Kings: 1 Kgs 6:1 (‘in 480 year of the exodus from Egypt . . . Solomon built the temple’); 2 Kgs 22:3 (‘in 18 year of king Josiah . . . the king sent to the temple’); and 25:27||Jer. 52:31 (‘in 37 year of the exile of Jehoiachin king of Judah . . . Evil-Merodach . . . raised the head of Jehoiachin’). The first and last of these examples introduce substantial Kings pluses;42 and the second underlines that the renewal of the temple by Josiah belonged to the same year as a celebration of Passover without parallel since Israel’s early days in her land. Yet we should note that, when either Kings or Chronicles offers greater chronological precision than the other in a synoptic context, it is unwise to suppose that it was better informed historically.43 Greater detail is a feature of the longer Kings version. 2 Chron. 32:9, 16 talk simply of Sennacherib’s ‘servants’; but the longer Kings version details three officials on either side (18:17–18, 26, 37) – and two of Hezekiah’s three reappear in 19:2. Similarly, Kings details five cities taken previously by Assyria (18:34) – and adds three more in 19:12–13 when the five are mentioned again – while 2 Chron. 32 does not cite specific examples. Then, only the longer Kings-Isaiah version makes reference to Pharaoh and Egypt. From the perspective of the shorter synoptic narrative, the expansive author of Kings was neatly achieving (at least) two exegetical goals by creating this date. At a more local level,
39 Auld, ‘Shaping’. 40 While unique, it most closely resembles Gen. 15:1; 22:1; 39:7; 40:1; 1 Kgs 13:33; 17:17; 21:1. 41 Ezek. 26:1; 29:17; 30:20; 31:1; 32:1, 17; 33:21. 42 Furthermore, it was noted above that 2 Kgs 25:29–30 pair significant markers (3 & 7) in .כל־ימי חייו
43 Only Kings specifies that, out of Solomon’s 20 years of construction, 7 were spent on temple and 13 on palace (1 Kgs 7:1); and only 2 Chron. 16 specifies the years of the fighting between Asa and Baasha.

he was aligning Hezekiah still more closely with synoptic Amaziah (also king for 29 years), who lived for 15 years after the campaign against his capital by the king of Israel (2 Kgs 14:17||2 Chron. 25:25). More grandly and by deliberate use of the unusual dating formula, he was setting the deliverance of both city and king within a historical arc from the completion of Solomon’s temple in Jerusalem through Josiah’s concern with that temple and his passover reform to a fresh status for exiled Jehoiachin. While sympathetic to Bob Becking’s ‘basic assumption . . . that the numbers for the reigns of the kings of Israel and Judah in the Book of Kings are not to be assessed as a deliberate and meaningful “invention” by the redactors of the Book’,44 I would limit the scope of such a claim to the narrative as shared by Kings and Chronicles.
3.3 Hezekiah’s Prayers
The second and third sections of the two narratives are even less commensurate than 2 Kgs 18:17–37 and 2 Chron. 32:9–19. The few elements they do share are readily listed.
2 Chron. 32:20–23,24–26 2 Kgs 19; 20:1–11 Elsewhere in 2 Kgs 18–20

44 ‘Chronology: A Skeleton without Flesh? Sennacherib’s Campaign as a Case-Study’, L.L. Grabbe (ed.), ‘Like A Bird in a Cage’. The Invasion of Sennacherib in 701 BCE (JSOTS 363), London: Sheffield Academic Press, 2003, 53).



60
Auld
There is neither ‘asking’ ( שאל ) nor ‘enquiry’ ( דרש ) in either version of the Hezekiah story; but the king communicates with the deity by ‘praying’ ( התפלל ) – a term which has not been met in synoptic texts since David and Solomon.45 He prays first about the situation of his city and people (2 Kgs 19||2 Chron. 32:20–23), and then about his own mortal sickness (2 Kgs 20:1–11||2 Chron. 32:24–26). A divine emissary ( מלאך ), not encountered synoptically since the story of David’s census (2 Sam. 24:16–17), causes the departure of the Assyrian army (2 Kgs 19:35||2 Chron. 32:21). Jerusalem had been saved on that earlier occasion too, though the messenger’s role was destructive of many of David’s people. However, perhaps the most striking result of this synoptic comparison is a negative one: that Isaiah, though named once in 2 Chron. 32 alongside Hezekiah, is not reported as interacting with the king. 2 Chron. 32:20 states simply ‘And Hezekiah the king prayed – and Isaiah ben Amoz the prophet – about this.’ In addressing Yahweh directly in prayer, the Chronicler’s King Hezekiah stands in royal continuity with synoptic David and Solomon. And Isaiah, far from being the instigator of his king’s approach to the deity or even an intermediary between the two, appears here to be an afterthought. The overview early in this paper of what the title called ‘the oldest “biblical” prophetic narrative’ occasioned surprise that there is no prophetic role in the momentous story of Athaliah and Joash. If Isaiah ben Amoz was not after all an original element of the synoptic Hezekiah story, that earlier ‘lack’ may be less surprising than first appeared. The situation is very different in the fuller Kings/Isaiah version of Hezekiah. There the king does finally (2 Kgs 19:15) pray to Yahweh in response to advice offered by Isaiah many verses earlier (2 Kgs 19:4). It was noted above that the most obvious links in 2 Kgs 18:17–37||Isa. 36 to the rest of the book of Isaiah are not shared with 2 Chron. 32:9–19. It is arguable that an earlier draft of the story of Hezekiah and Sennacherib within the synoptic royal narrative made no mention of Isaiah, and that an older shorter draft without Isaiah was expanded in one direction in Chronicles and much more fully in another direction in Kings/Isaiah.46 In his discussion of Isaiah and the Assyrian Crisis, B.S. Childs was of the view that the Chronicler had produced a ‘midrash’ of the Kings/Isaiah narrative: ‘product of an exegetical activity by a circle of scholars in interpreting
45 2 Sam. 7:27; 1 Kgs 8 (9×). 46 The minimal presence of the prophet casts grave doubt on Haag’s view (see n. 36 above) that the Chronicler had adhered rather faithfully to a story that had originated among the disciples of Isaiah – a story which was then elaborated more substantially in Kings.



Isaiah and the Oldest 61
‘
Biblical
’
Prophetic Narrative
an ancient text’.47 However, in light of the analysis above, it seems even more likely that the longer version of the story in Kings, with its several additions drawing on other portions of Samuel-Kings, should be described as ‘midrashic’ development of the shorter base text that had been available to the Chronicler.
4 Outlook
One element of the Chronicler’s version of the Hezekiah story has an interesting parallel in the peroration in Kings on the fall of northern Israel. As noted above, the key verb ‘deliver’ ( הציל ), used many times in both versions of the Hezekiah story, is found just once more in Kings: immediately beforehand (2 Kgs 17:39), close to the end of the long peroration. Similarly ‘trust in’ ( האמין ) of 2 Chron. 32:15 is used in Kings only in 2 Kgs 17:14; and each of הציל and האמין is closely associated in 2 Kgs 17 with ‘but they did not listen’ ( 48.)ולא שמעו These indicators invite the suggestion that האמין , a significant element of Isa. 7:9 and 28:16 now attested only in the Chronicler’s version (2 Chron. 32:15), had been original to the Hezekiah story but was overwritten by the synonymous בטח , when that verb became much more prominently used in the Kings/Isaiah version.49 There is little space here to develop the implications this paper may have for other issues. But one can at least be mentioned. The underlying Hezekiah narrative had been integral to the Jerusalem royal story. But were the origins of the shared longer narrative more at home in Kings or Isaiah? The question of the relative priority of our inherited texts of 2 Kgs 18–20 and Isa. 36–39 is a different one, although there are overlaps. For example, the fact that the Kings version is generally more expansive may properly influence the literaryhistorical decision on whether 2 Kgs 18:14–16 was a later addition to Kings or was deleted when the Hezekiah narrative was incorporated within Isaiah. R.F. Person, Jr has proposed that these verses were added for the purpose of ‘downplaying Hezekiah as a model king’50 – I think sensibly, despite the

Less persuasive is retaining the model of an earlier and a later ‘source’ at all, instead of using the same evidence towards alternative models such as expansive rewriting (‘rolling corpus’ or Fortschreibung). In these more organic models, the newer material has to be understood within the context of the older rather than as distinct from it and over against it. An important exegetical consequence of such a choice is the assessment of the relation of king and prophet in the later material. Prophets, Kings and Honour in the Narrative of 1 Kgs 22*
William L. Kelly
Cultures that depend on prophets and diviners to access the divine will are very familiar with the threat of charlatanism and the social conflicts between rivals. Ancient Israel was no exception, and these problems reared their heads at times in the texts of the Hebrew Bible. The famous narrative of 1 Kgs 22:1–38 is a key text for this theme, with its differing messages given by prophets to the kings of Israel and Judah, and this paper explores the text in a socio-historical perspective.1 The dynamics of prophetic conflict and the trustworthiness of prophetic speech, as it concerns this particular narrative, were conditioned by the values, status, and power of the characters involved. In this study, after briefly summarising the narrative’s setting and plot, three particular points of the story will attract particular focus: the kings’ consultation of prophets prior to battle (1 Kgs 22:2–9), the public delivery of prophetic oracles (22:10–12, 15–18), and the prophets’ attempt at discrediting their rivals (22:19–23, 24–25). We will examine issues related to the cultural value of honour, the social mechanisms which distribute it and the status it confers.
1 Overview of 1 Kgs 22
The story in 1 Kgs 22:1–38 opens with the royal preliminaries to battle and the consultation of prophets before attempting to regain control of Ramoth-gilead
* I wish to thank Prof. Becking and Prof. Barstad for accepting an earlier version of this paper for the Prophecy Network meeting in Utrecht, and for including the present version in this volume. The following abbreviations are used in this paper: AEM = Archives épistolaires de Mari, 1–2. J.-M. Durand, Archives épistolaires de Mari I/1 (ARM, 26/1), Paris 1988; D. See Long in this regard: ‘The theological approach to prophetic conflict is neither incorrect nor inappropriate. But like any method, it limits while illuminating.’ B.O. Long, ‘Social Dimensions of Prophetic Conflict’, Semeia 21 (1981), 33.



Prophets, Kings and Honour in the Narrative of 1 Kgs 22 65
in the Transjordan. The activities of the prophets tend to dominate interest in the passage, but the central figure nevertheless remains Ahab.2 He is named only in 1 Kgs 22:20 and 22:39–55, while the rest of the narrative simply refers to him as the מלך ישראל .3 However, the text is situated in the context of his reign, which is introduced in 1 Kgs 16:21–33 and concluded in 22:29–40. This unit comprises nearly a quarter of the books of Kings and demonstrates Ahab’s particular significance to the story. The story begins with a visit from the southern monarch Jehoshaphat of Judah. We are not informed about Jehoshaphat’s reasons for visiting the northern kingdom but his presence would presumably be at Ahab’s behest, his kingdom being the stronger of the two at this particular time. Ahab believes he has rightful ownership of a contested border city (cf. Deut 4:43; Josh 20:8; 21:38; 1 Kgs 4:13) and begins a process of gathering strength in advance of a military campaign. After Jehoshaphat pledges his allegiance to Ahab and commits troops to his cause (1 Kgs 22:4), the narrative then takes a long detour in detailing the process by which they seek to divine the will of Yahweh regarding this affair. Prophetic activity then unfolds in four episodic parts. First, a cohort of prophets are asked a yes-or-no question as to whether the battle should proceed (1 Kgs 22:2–9). Their answer is unanimous in the affirmative:

Kelly
22:6 ( אדני ביד המלך ). For reasons unsaid the kings seek out a second opinion and summon Micaiah ben Imlah, the prophet whose reputation suggests he will dissent from the chorus of other prophetic voices. This leads to a second scene rich with detail about the royal court and the positive words given by the prophets (22:10–12). In the third part Micaiah takes centre stage, initially agreeing with the other prophets (22:15) before delivering an anticipated word of doom (22:16–18) along with a vision report of the divine council (22:19–23). Finally, one of the prophets from the group of yea-sayers confronts Micaiah and strikes him, sparking a terse exchange between the two (22:24–25). The narrative continues with Ahab jailing Micaiah, and the two kings proceeding unabated with Ahab’s plan. The negative oracle apparently discouraged Ahab enough to make him disguise himself before the battle, allowing Jehoshaphat to receive the attention of the Aramaeans during the fighting (1 Kgs 22:30–33). The denouement arrives with a stray arrow striking the disguised Ahab, subsequently causing him to bleed to death (22:34–38). Social factors inherent in the process of prophetic consultation are clearly guiding a good deal of this narrative and how its characters are evaluated. When the two kings express a desire to receive supportive oracles before war, or when prophetic messages become public performances, we see such evaluation at work. Anthropologists working with cultures of the Mediterranean4 have discussed this valuation in terms of honour, which is ‘a sign of the recognition of the excellence or worth of a person’ or, essentially, a right to respect.5
4 The notion of a cultural ‘Mediterranean’ unified by generalised social values of honour and shame is a point of great debate; Herzfeld named this tendency the ‘sin of “Mediterraneanism”.’ M. Herzfeld, ‘Honour and Shame: Problems in the Comparative Analysis of Moral Systems’, Man 15 (1980), 348. See also M. Herzfeld, ‘The Horns of the Mediterraneanist Dilemma’, American Ethnologist 11 (1984), 439–54; Idem, Anthropology through the Looking-Glass: Critical Ethnography in the Margins of Europe, Cambridge 1987; J. Pina-Cabral, ‘The Mediterranean as a Category of Regional Comparison: A Critical View’, CA 30 (1989), 399–406; U. Wikan, ‘Shame and Honour: A Contestable Pair’, Man 19 (1984), 635–52; A. Albera, ‘Anthropology of the Mediterranean: Between Crisis and Renewal’, History and Anthropology 17 (2006), 109–33; P. Horden, N. Purcell, The Corrupting Sea: A Study of Mediterranean History, Oxford 2000, 486–523. 5 J.K. Campbell, Honour, Family and Patronage: A Study of Institutions and Moral Values in a Greek Mountain Community, Oxford 1964, 268; F.H. Stewart, Honor, Chicago 1994, 29–47. Precisely defining the term is difficult, and a great deal of effort in anthropological literature ‘has been expended, not entirely fruitfully, on the questions of what honour is’, see P. Horden, N. Purcell, The Corrupting Sea, 502. Although a bit dated, classic works in the field are still considered valuable sources of information since they document people groups



Prophets, Kings and Honour in the Narrative of 1 Kgs 22 67
By such a definition, honour is a public value gained by the successful performance of one’s social role. When individuals have a public role to play, and their society relies on them to perform their role well, the society has to know that such individuals will perform well when called upon. It is incredibly valuable for a society to know who is dependable and who is not. This knowledge translates into a kind of trust which is given to those dependable individuals, and it elevates their social role and voice. This is essentially how honour works in the public sphere. In settings of social conflict honour functions as ‘a mechanism which distributes power’.6 When these social conflicts involve individuals in the public sphere, honour is exchanged like a kind of social currency. Bourdieu’s dialectic of ‘challenge and riposte’ suggests that the stakes of social conflicts are paid in honour, which reinforces or advances a person’s status.7 As it will be shown, these social mechanisms are at play in 1 Kgs 22:1–38 as the social status of characters function as hinges upon which the story turns.

his wish to go to battle, the argument goes, while Jehoshaphat is humbly deferential to the will of Yahweh. It seems this view follows too closely with the bias of the larger narrative frame which is baldly critical of Ahab, and glosses over aspects of Jehoshaphat’s behaviour which invite criticism. Jehoshaphat, ‘regardless of motivation’, proclaims his loyalty to Ahab prior to his suggestion to consult Yahweh; this fact is part of the Chronicler’s later criticism of Jehoshaphat.9 Furthermore, even if one wants to highlight his piety in 1 Kgs 22:5, Jehoshaphat ultimately disregards Micaiah’s negative oracle and follows Ahab to battle.10 In any case, consulting the divine will in times of crisis is ‘a routine event in the political life of an ancient people’,11 something which is also well attested in the biblical record.12 Ahab is certainly engaged in a series of actions which validate and justify his royal power and rule, and there are many examples of kings following the same pattern of behaviour. Mesha of Moab claims a divine imperative for his military victories which provide a positive valuation of his rule.15 Neo-Assyrian texts record encouraging oracles delivered to Esarhaddon prior to and during the civil war of 681 BCE,16 and other kings, including Tiglath-pileser I and Ashurnasirpal II, cite divine commands for their military campaigns.17 In these and similar instances the respective king gains a positive evaluation through these successes aided by the gods.18 Is Ahab asking a question to the prophets on an already settled matter?19 Given how standard this practice was in the cultures of the ancient Near East, it seems not. positive response of the prophets in 1 Kgs 22:6 illustrates their role in legitimising the rule of the king and in demonstrating continuity between heavenly and earthly rule. Jehoshaphat, however, asks if another prophet is available for consultation. Is it suspicion, caution or piety that prompts him? The narrative is silent on the matter, but perhaps the unanimously supportive message of the prophets prompts a desire for another perspective. By way of comparison, AEM 1/1 199 (A.925 + A.2050) records an instance where Zimri-Lim asks for a second prophetic opinion, apparently out of pragmatic caution against overconfidence. Thus piety might not be the impetus for Jehoshaphat’s actions here either.
3 Public Delivery of Prophetic Oracles (1 Kgs 22:10–12, 15–18)
After Jehoshaphat’s request for a second opinion, the narrative provides a more detailed look at the scene where the supportive prophets deliver their oracles. The setting is a public gathering at the city gate where the prophets prophesy before the two kings who are seated on thrones and clothed in royal robes.21 The setting and the royal accoutrements heighten and emphasise the power and authority of the kings as they preside like judges over a court and receive public council on their war plans.22
1999, 94. For a full discussion, see B. Oded, War, Peace and, Empire: Justifications for War in Assyrian Royal Inscriptions, Wiesbaden 1992.
21 The phrase בגרן פתח שער שמרון in 1 Kgs 22:10 should be understood as the city gate, an important location for ‘the legal and ritual procedures respectively of a community’. N. 22 A similar public setting is described in AEM 1/1 206, where a prophetic oracle is also delivered. See S.B. Parker, ‘Official Attitudes Toward Prophecy at Mari and in Israel’, VT 43 (1993), 56.



Prophets, Kings and Honour in the Narrative of 1 Kgs 22 71
The prophets then gather before the kings and deliver Yahwistic oracles in support of the war effort just as their social role demands.23 The message of the prophets in 1 Kgs 22:10 varies in form from the earlier report of 22:6, but the oracles are consistent in content. One prophet named Zedekiah ben Chanaanah furnishes himself with a set of iron horns ( קרני ברזל ) presumably to demonstrate his message of support before the assembly. He possibly uses these horns in a prophetic sign-act to visualise his proclamation of victory.24 Publicly performed sign-acts add legitimacy to both the oracle and the prophet; they confer honour upon the performer, and increase the persuasiveness of the prophetic message.25 In 1 Kgs 22:11, Zedekiah also uses the prophetic speech formula כה אמר יהוה , which is found throughout the Hebrew Bible, and his oracle borrows Deuteronomic and prophetic imagery (Deut. 33:17; Mic. 4:13). All of these features of Zedekiah’s portrayal in this part of the narrative reinforce his credibility, power, status and authority.26 Before Micaiah ben Imlah enters the scene, the narrative casts him as an antagonist of the king with a reputation for never bringing good news (1 Kgs 22:8). Ahab is familiar with Micaiah and hates his persistent tendency to speak misfortune ( רע ). Therefore, he stands in contrast to the supportive prophets of 1 Kgs 22:10 before he even gives his message. While this is clearly

part of a literary strategy to portray him as a heroic and critical outsider to the story’s proceedings, it also seems that Micaiah is no stranger to the royal court.27 The narrative plot reaches a twist when Micaiah defies his reputation and repeats the oracle of the positive prophets nearly verbatim. Perhaps with some irony, Micaiah’s positive word arouses the king’s concern, and Ahab responds by commanding him to speak רק־אמת בשם יהוה in 1 Kgs 22:16. What is the truth in this context? Following a socio-historical reading, Ahab’s demand is for Micaiah to speak loyally or reliably,28 and Ahab’s concern with אמת is the threat posed by messages which would undermine his rule.29 In this context, it appears that Ahab might not desire to set a precedent where a prophet can stand in a public assembly and misrepresent his or her view of the divine will. Ultimately Ahab was right to suspect that there was more to Micaiah’s message, as the prophet gives a negative oracle in response to the original question (1 Kgs 22:17; cf. 22:6).30

Prophets, Kings and Honour in the Narrative of 1 Kgs 22 73
4 Prophets Discrediting their Rivals (1 Kgs 22:19–23, 24–25)
Micaiah delivers a second oracle in 1 Kgs 22:19–23 in the form of a vision of the divine council of Yahweh.31 This heavenly setting is the space where the deliberations, plans and judgments of Yahweh are decreed and subsequently revealed to those privileged enough to either see, hear or participate in the proceedings. Access to this heavenly space was an assumed part of the office of a prophet,32 and such access confers a degree of status and authorisation to those fortunate enough to be there.33 Another way of legitimising prophetic speech is by claiming to participate in this communicative chain. In his vision report, Micaiah reasserts his negative oracle with the claim that Yahweh enticed (√
פחה ) Ahab by sending a רוח שקר in the mouths of the prophets (1 Kgs 22:21–22).34 These prophets delivered their oracles ‘in good

conscience, for Yahweh – but Yahweh had deceived them’35 since they were not made aware of the plans of the divine council. In this way, Micaiah does not reject the supportive prophets tout court like severe passages from Jeremiah or Zephaniah. Instead he criticises their ability to know the will of Yahweh, and this is a clear attempt to undermine their status as rivals. If their source of inspiration is misleading, then the credibility and usefulness of their oracles is compromised.36 Conversely, discrediting one’s rivals can reinforce one’s own authority, and Micaiah stands to benefit from the loss of honour and status suffered by the untrustworthy prophets. Their loss is his gain.37 The vision also catches Ahab in a double bind. If he trusts Micaiah and abstains from his military campaign, he is implicitly acknowledging that he, Zedekiah and the court prophets were all misled. But if he persists and marches upon Ramoth-gilead, then the
רוח שקר did in fact succeed in enticing him to go to war as Yahweh intended (1 Kgs 22:19).38 Zedekiah takes the opportunity to respond to Micaiah in 1 Kgs 22:24 in this critical moment where social rank and status are at stake.39 The interaction between the two prophets is clearly adversarial and contentious. Striking Micaiah on the cheek, Zedekiah questions how he knows about the movement of the spirit of Yahweh between the two prophets. Micaiah retorts by telling Zedekiah that he will understand when he hides (√ חבה ) in his inner-room (cf. 1 Kgs 20:30; 2 Kgs 9:2), a threat which hints at the experience of some kind

Prophets, Kings and Honour in the Narrative of 1 Kgs 22 75
of duress.40 While these interactions are somewhat opaque, they clearly demonstrate an interest in the connection between the prophet and his message. The ad hominem critiques and attacks from each prophet are intended to undermine the status of the opponent by casting doubt on his abilities and his source of prophetic inspiration.
5 Conclusion
Key elements of the narrative of 1 Kgs 22:1–38 demonstrate a clear interest in issues related to social standing and honour in a setting of prophetic conflict. Consulting prophets prior to battle is an expected part of the process by which kings seek positive evaluations of their rule and justification for their military actions (22:2–9). A public setting where prophetic oracles are delivered allows the opportunity for prophets to demonstrate their ability and prowess (22:1012, 15–18). It also gives prophetic figures the opportunity to advance their social position by discrediting their rivals (22:19–23, 24–25). The royal consultation of prophets, the public delivery of prophetic oracles, and inter-prophetic conflict, all hinge upon the social standing of the relevant parties involved. Assessing the narrative of 1 Kgs 22:1–38 in a socio-historical perspective involves analysing contextually implicit social values and indications of social status, power and prestige. These elements of the story suggest that the prophets had to earn trust in ancient Israelite society, which faced some difficult decisions when seeking Yahweh’s will.
40 S.E. Balentine, ‘A Description of the Semantic Field of Hebrew Words for “Hide” ’, VT 30 (1980), 140–41.
