Text and History of Isaiah 20: Its Development within the Isaianic Tradition
Reinoud Oosting
1 Introduction
In the current composition of Isaiah, the account about the prophet Isaiah in chapter 20 is part of the oracles against the foreign nations (chapters 13–23). The passage distinguishes itself from the surrounding chapters not only by the narrative style in which the account is written but also by its chronological heading. While in Isaiah 13–23 we frequently meet the superscription מַ שָּ ֺא (‘the oracle concerning’) followed by a reference to a nation1 or a location,2 the heading of Isaiah 20 contains a historical reference to the Assyrian military campaign against Ashdod. Despite the differences between Isaiah 20 and the bulk of the material in Isaiah 13–23, its current position in the collection of oracles against the nations can easily be explained. Isaiah 20 is linked to the oracles against Ethiopia and Egypt in Isaiah 18–19 by the references to Egypt and Ethiopia in vv. 3–5. Furthermore, it is worth noting that the highway from Egypt to Assyria mentioned in Isa. 19:23 is the same route that the Assyrians in Isaiah 20 take to march against Ashdod and then use to lead the people of Egypt and Ethiopia away as exiles. Though the narrative in chapter 20 fits the current context, the question remains of how this passage became part of the Isaianic tradition. How did this account enter the literary history of the Book of Isaiah? In answering that question, current researchers usually concentrate on historical references and redactional layers within Isaiah 1–39. Their studies offer a wealth of information about the cultural and historical background of the narrative, but pay less attention to the linguistic and literary signals within the text itself. In order to gain insight into the development of Isaiah 20, the present paper focuses on textual data rather than on historical processes and redactional history. The linguistic and literary data of the text of the Hebrew Bible in its current form are used as an independent source of research. The starting point for the examination is the text of Isaiah 20 in its final form. The linguistic and literary
1 Cf. Isa. 13:1 ‘Babylon’; 15:1 ‘Moab’; 17:1 ‘Damascus’; 19:1 ‘Egypt’; 21:11 ‘Dumah’; 21:13 ‘Arabia’; 23:1 ‘Tyre’. 2 Cf. Isa. 21:1 ‘the wilderness of the sea’; 22:1 ‘the valley of vision’.



Text and History of Isaiah 20 77
features of this passage will be analyzed first and then its relationship to other biblical texts and extra-biblical sources. In giving priority to textual evidence, this paper seeks to gain insight into the development of Isaiah 20 within the Isaianic tradition.
2 Historicity of Isaiah 20
The short narrative about the prophet Isaiah in chapter 20 contains clear historical references. Isaiah 20 is the only passage in the Hebrew Bible in which the Assyrian King Sargon II (722–705 BCE) is explicitly mentioned. Furthermore, this chapter refers to the military campaign of King Sargon against Ashdod. Assyrian inscriptions indicate that Azuri, the king of Ashdod, refused to pay any more tribute and sent messages to the kings of the surrounding countries to rebel against the King of Assyria. On account of that, the Assyrians replaced Azuri by his younger brother Ahimiti. When the Assyrians had turned their back, however, the people of Ashdod deposed Ahimiti and made Yamani ruler over them. In response to that rebellious act, the Assyrian king Sargon marched against the city of Ashdod and conquered it.3 It is widely accepted that the beginning of Isaiah 20 refers to Sargon’s campaign against Ashdod. At the same time, it is not entirely clear whether all details of the biblical account are accurate. Isaiah 20 recounts Sargon sending his commander-in-chief, the Tartan, to lead the military campaign against Ashdod. In the Assyrian inscriptions, however, Sargon claims that he himself besieged and conquered Ashdod. Furthermore, it appears that, although Egypt and Ethiopia are explicitly mentioned in this passage, they were not involved in the rebellion of Ashdod against Assyria. On the contrary, when the leader of the rebellion, Yamani, fled to Egypt, the Egyptians handed him over to the Assyrians. These observations suggest that the text and the historicity of Isaiah 20 are not fully in line with each other. One gets the impression that the account in Isaiah 20 is not historically accurate. It is worth exploring, therefore, how current exegetes deal with the discrepancy between the text and historicity of Isaiah 20.
3 The Assyrian accounts concerning the conquest of Ashdod have been confirmed by the finding of three fragments of an Assyrian stele at Tell Ashdod, which contain several lines of texts that have parallels in other texts of King Sargon II, cf. Z.J. Kapera, ‘The Ashdod Stele of Sargon II’, Folia Orientalia 17 (1976), 87–99.



78 Oosting
3 Current Approaches to Isaiah 20
In current exegetical literature, one encounters different answers to the question concerning the relationship between the text and historicity of this passage. The first answer to be mentioned is the traditional view of John Oswalt, who maintains that Isaiah 20 is a historical narrative about the prophet Isaiah. Like the other narratives in chapters 6–8, 22, and 36–39, it has been written by the prophet Isaiah himself or by someone close to him. Oswalt explains the discrepancy between the text and historicity of Isaiah 20 by emphasizing the ‘diversity and complexity of the prophetic writings’.4 With regard to Isaiah 20, Oswalt asserts that Ashdod’s fate was not the major issue in Isaiah 20. ‘The real issue was the fate of Egypt, fomenter of the rebellion.’5 The second answer is the one given by Csaba Balogh in his reworked doctoral dissertation on Isaiah 18–20. Balogh holds to the unity of this passage. He asserts that ‘this narrative has only ever existed in the final form that it now has.’6 Unlike Oswalt, Balogh does not regard this passage as a historically reliable account. He explains the discrepancy between the text and history of Isaiah 20 by assuming that the narrative in Isaiah 20 is not contemporary with the events it narrates but originated at a later time. In his view, Isaiah 20 was most likely written in the final years of the kingdom of Judah.7 The third answer that requires attention is the view of Ronald Clements, who focuses on the discrepancies within the text. According to Clements, an older oracle of Isaiah (against the Philistines) has been edited. Clements leaves open the question as to whether the narrative in the third person was originally formulated by the prophet in the first person.8 His main conclusion is that discrepancies are best explained by assuming that an older text has been reworked by a later editor, who lived shortly before the siege of Jerusalem in 701 BCE. The last answer to be discussed here is the interpretation of Joseph Blenkinsopp. He suggests that Isaiah 20 is not part of the Isaianic core of the Book of Isaiah but has a ‘Deuteronomistic origin’. In his view, Isaiah 20 has ‘either been excerpted from Dtr [the Deuteronomistic History] by an editor . . .
4 Cf. J.N. Oswalt, The Book of Isaiah: Chapter 1–39 (NICOT), Grand Rapids 1986, 383. 5 Cf. Oswalt, Book of Isaiah, 386.
6 Cf. C. Balogh, The Stele of YHWH in Egypt: The Prophecies of Isaiah 18–20 concerning Egypt and Kush (OTS 60), Leiden 2011, 319. 7 Cf. Balogh, Stele of YHWH in Egypt, 330: ‘A third and most likely option is to place the composition of Isa. 20 in the final years of the kingdom of Judah.’ 8 Cf. R.E. Clements, Isaiah 1–39 (NCBC), Grand Rapids 1980, 174.



Text and History of Isaiah 20 79
or that, together with chs. 7 and 36–39, it is part of a Deuteronomistic redaction of chs. 1–39’.9 Blenkinsopp thus explains the discrepancy between text and historicity by assuming that chapter 20 has been written from a Deuteronomistic perspective.
4 A Data-Oriented Approach
These observations show that current exegetes deal with the discrepancy between the text and historicity of Isaiah 20 in different ways. Oswalt simply denies the ambiguities between the biblical account and the extra-biblical account. In his view, it is neither necessary to question the historicity of the biblical account, nor its literary unity. Others, like Clements, hold to the historical reliability of Isaiah 20, but they question the literary unity of the passage. According to Clements, the discrepancies are best explained by assuming that the original oracle has been edited. Balogh and Blenkinsopp, on the other hand, hold to the literary unity of the Isaiah 20, but they cast doubt on the historical reliability of the account. They believe that Isaiah 20 is a colored version of the fall of Ashdod written from a later perspective. Apart from the traditional view, exegetes thus tend to explain the discrepancy between the text and historicity of Isaiah 20 in two different ways: Either they question the literary unity of the passage (Clements) or they question the historical reliability of the account (Balogh, Blenkinsopp). In my view, both the unity and the reliability of Isaiah 20 should be called into question. Because we merely have the text of Isaiah 20 in its present form, we cannot exclude the possibility that this text has been reworked in the course of time. In addition, Isaiah 20 has been transmitted in the course of time as part of the Book of Isaiah. We cannot exclude the possibility that later editors adapted the existing account with a view to its position in the Isaianic tradition. For those reasons, both the unity and the reliability of Isaiah 20 will be examined here. As a starting-point for the examination, this paper looks first at the linguistic and syntactic features of the text of Isaiah 20 as it now stands. Then we draw attention to the literary parallels between Isaiah 20 and other parts of Isaiah 1–39. Next the relationship between Isaiah 20 and other biblical books, especially 2 Kings, is scrutinized. Finally, we examine the relationship between
9 Cf. J. Blenkinsopp, Isaiah 1–39 (AB 19A), New York 2000, 322; cf. also Idem, ‘The Prophetic Biography of Isaiah’, in: E. Blum (ed.) Mincha: Festgabe für Rolf Rendtorff zum 75. Geburtstag, Neukirchen-Vluyn 2000, 20: ‘the Deuteronomic character of 20:1–6, which is not in the History, is beyond reasonable doubt’.



80
Oosting
the account of the fall of Ashdod in Isaiah 20 and the references to the Assyrian military campaign against Ashdod in extra-biblical sources. In choosing this order, the present paper gives priority to the analysis of textual data over the examination of the cultural and historical context of this passage. In doing so, it aims to shed new light on the discrepancy between the text and historicity of Isaiah 20.
5 Linguistic Observations
In examining the linguistic and syntactic features of Isaiah 20 in its current form, we concentrate on the first two verses of this passage, which read:
In the year that the Tartan who was sent by Sargon king of Assyria, came to Ashdod and fought against Ashdod and captured it; [2] at that time Yhwh spoke through (literally, בְּ יַ ד ) Isaiah son of Amoz, saying . . .10
In these two verses we encounter two doublets. First, there are two time references: the phrase בִּ שְ נַ ת ‘in the year that’, at the beginning of v. 1 and the phrase ׇבּעֵ ת ׇה ִהיא , ‘at that time’, at the beginning of v. 2. Furthermore, the name of Ashdod is used twice, the second reference almost immediately after the first, which is rather unusual. When the capture of a city is described, the name of the city is usually mentioned once, and then it is referred to by means of pronominal suffixes (cf. Josh. 10:36–37; 19:47; 2 Kgs 12:18; 18:9–10). These two doublets – marked below – suggest that the current text of Isa. 20:1–2 is a revised edition of an older text.
In the year that the Tartan who was sent by Sargon king of Assyria, came to Ashdod and fought against Ashdod and captured it; [2] at that time Yhwh spoke through Isaiah son of Amoz, saying . . .
That conclusion is supported by the syntactic constructions found in Isa. 6:1 and 14:28. In both cases a subordinated clause, which begins with the temporal phrase
בִּ שְ נַ ת , ‘in the year that’, is immediately followed by the main clause, which serves as introduction to the remainder of the passage:
Isa. 6:1 In the year that King Uzziah died, I saw Yhwh sitting on a throne . . .
10 Isa. 20:1–2.



Text and History of Isaiah 20 81
Isa. 14:28 In the year that King Ahaz died, this oracle came:
The suggestion that the first two verses of Isaiah 20 have been reworked is not new. In his study on the earliest stages of the Isaiah tradition, Matthijs de Jong brings forward comparable arguments.11 In addition, he points out that the combination of the verbs לחם Niphal (‘to fight’) and לכד Qal (‘to capture’) at the end of v. 1 is ‘a common way to describe a successful siege of a city’.12 For that reason, he considers the last part of v. 1 to be a secondary addition. On that basis, De Jong comes to the conclusion that an older text of vv. 1–2 probably was:
In the year that the Tartan who was sent by Sargon king of Assyria, came to Ashdod and fought against Ashdod and captured it; [2] at that time Yhwh spoke through Isaiah son of Amoz, saying . . .
Though I largely agree with the above reconstruction, I would like to offer a slightly different proposal. In my view, the previous version of vv. 1–2 read:
In the year that the Tartan who was sent by Sargon king of Assyria, came to Ashdod and fought against Ashdod and captured it; [2] at that time Yhwh spoke through Isaiah son of Amoz, saying . . .
An argument favoring the latter reconstruction is the fact that at the end of v. 1, which is regarded by De Jong as being a secondary addition, the combination of the verb לחם Niphal (‘to fight’) with the preposition בְ (‘against’) is employed: ‘and he fought against Ashdod’. When looking at the way this verb is used throughout Isaiah 1–39, it becomes clear that לחם Niphal occurs with different prepositions. In some texts it governs the preposition בְ , while in other texts it is construed with the preposition 13:עַ ל
לחם Niphal + בְ : to fight + against (cf. Isa. 19:2; 20:1; 30:32) לחם Niphal + עַ ל : to fight + against (cf. Isa. 7:1; 37:8)
11 Cf. M.J. de Jong, Isaiah among the Ancient Near Eastern Prophets: A Comparative Study of the Earliest Stages of the Isaiah Tradition and the Neo-Assyrian Prophecies (VT.S 117), Leiden 2007, 151–152.
12 Cf. De Jong, Isaiah among the Ancient Near Eastern Prophets, 152, n. 488.
13 Besides the examples of Isa. 7:1 and 37:8, the combination of the verb לחם Niphal (‘to fight’) with the preposition עַ ל (‘against’) is found in Deut. 20:10, 19; Josh. 10:5, 34, 36, 38; 2 Kgs 12:18; 19:8; Jer. 21:2; 32:24, 29; 34:1, 7, 22; 37:8.



82
Oosting
This variety in the use of the prepositions בְ and עַ ל with the verb לחם Niphal is observed not only in the Masoretic Text of Isaiah (MT), but also in the Isaiah manuscripts found among the Dead Sea scrolls. Though most of the manuscripts are fragmentary, they all reflect the same variety in how the two verbal patterns are used:14
Isaiah 1–39 Verb MT 1QIsaa 1QIsab 4QIsab
על + עַ ל + Niphal לחם Isa. 7:1 Isa. 19:2 לחם Niphal + ב + בְ + ב Isa. 20:1 לחם Niphal + ב + בְ + ב Isa. 30:32 לחם Niphal + בְ + ב על + על + עַ ל + Niphal לחם Isa. 37:8
It is noteworthy that both Isa. 7:1 and 37:8, in which the verb לחם Niphal occurs with the preposition עַ ל , have a parallel in 2 Kings: Isa. 7:1 is parallel to 2 Kgs 16:5; Isa. 37:8 is parallel to 2 Kgs 19:8. The common view is that both texts have been borrowed from the Book of Kings.15 The texts of Isa. 19:2 and 30:32, on the other hand, in which the verb לחם Niphal governs the preposition בְ , are usually seen as being connected to the earliest stages of the Book of Isaiah. De Jong, for example, claims that they are both early Isaianic texts. With regard to Isa. 19:2, he notes that this verse belongs to the oracle Isa. 19:1b–4, which probably dates back to the time of the prophet Isaiah himself.16 With regard to Isa. 30:32, De Jong comments that this verse is part of the unit Isa. 30:27–33, which contains ‘various elements that allude to other early texts within First Isaiah’.17 The combination of the verb לחם Niphal (‘to fight’) with the preposition בְ (‘against’) at the end of v. 1 is thus an indication that the expression ‘and
14 These observations are based on E. Ulrich (ed.) The Biblical Qumran Scrolls: Transcriptions and Textual Variants (VT.S 134), Leiden 2010. 15 Cf., e.g., M.J. de Jong, ‘From Legitimate King to Protected City: The Development of Isaiah 7:1–17’, in: A.L.H.M. van Wieringen, A. van der Woude (eds) ‘Enlarge the Site of your Tent’: The City as Unifying Theme in Isaiah (OTS 58), Leiden 2011, 24, who comments on Isa. 7:1: ‘verse 1 is commonly regarded as being dependent on 2 Kgs 16:5, and rightly so.’
16 Cf. De Jong, Isaiah among the Ancient Near Eastern Prophets, 150: ‘The context [of Isa. 19:1b–4] is probably the same as for the rest of the early material within 18–22*, the situation of 705–701 BCE.’
17 Cf. De Jong, Isaiah among the Ancient Near Eastern Prophets, 118.



Text and History of Isaiah 20 83
he fought against Ashdod and captured it’ was likely part of an older account underlying the current text of Isa. 20:1–2. The conclusion that an earlier version of Isaiah 20 has been reworked is substantiated by other older elements we encounter in vv. 1–2. Particularly noteworthy is the combination of the imperative form לך (‘go’) followed by the conjunction ו and the second person masculine singular perfect form פתחת (‘and loose’) in Isa. 20:2. A comparable syntactic construction is found in Isa. 6:9:
Isa. 20:2 Go, and loose the sackcloth from your loins Isa. 6:9 Go, and say to this people
Since most exegetes agree that the call narrative in Isaiah 6 substantially comes from the earliest literary layers of Isaiah 1–39, the syntactic parallel between Isa. 6:9 and 20:2 clearly indicates that Isaiah 20 contains older elements.18 On the basis of the linguistic parallels between Isaiah 20 and other early texts, it is likely that this passage was part of the earliest stages of the Isaiah tradition. At the same time, it can be observed that this earlier version of Isaiah 20 has been reworked. This raises the question as to whether we can find clues for the development of the narrative in Isaiah 20 within the Isaianic tradition.
6 Literary Observations
When examining the literary parallels between Isaiah 20 and other passages in Isaiah 1–39, we find striking similarities between words and phrases used in Isa. 20:1 and 36:2. Both Isaianic texts describe how the Assyrian king sends one of his main commanders to march against a rebellious city:
Isa. 20:1 In the year that the Tartan who was sent by Sargon king of Assyria, came to Ashdod . . . Isa. 36:2 The king of Assyria sent the Rabshakeh from Lachish to Jerusalem to King Hezekiah with a great army.
Besides the lexical and thematic similarities, the connection between Isa. 20:1 and 36:2 requires attention because of the parallel to Isa. 36:2 in 2 Kgs 18:17. Unlike Isa. 36:2, 2 Kgs 18:17 narrates that King Sennacherib commanded not
18 For a discussion on the authenticity of Isa. 6:1–11, see H.G.M. Williamson, The Book Called Isaiah: Deutero-Isaiah’s Role in Composition and Redaction, Oxford 1994, 30–36.



84 Oosting
only the Rabshakeh but also the Tartan and the Rabsaris to march from Lachish to Jerusalem:
2 Kgs 18:17 The king of Assyria sent the Tartan, the Rabsaris, and the Rabshakeh from Lachish to King Hezekiah at Jerusalem with a great army.
The reference to the Tartan in 2 Kgs 18:17 is interesting because the same military title is used in Isa. 20:1. Apart from 2 Kgs 18:17 and Isa. 20:1, this Assyrian military title is found nowhere else in the Old Testament. These observations raise the question of how the three texts, 2 Kgs 18:17, Isa. 20:1 and 36:2, are related to each other. Following the common view that Isaiah 36–39 has been borrowed from the Book of Kings, it is conceivable that the editor who copied 2 Kings 18–20 into the Isaianic tradition also reworked the older account in Isaiah 20.19 One can imagine that the one who was responsible for leaving out the reference to the Tartan in Isa. 36:2 was also responsible for inserting that phrase in Isa. 20:1. In doing so, the editor created a contrast between the military campaign of the Assyrian king Sargon against Ashdod and the campaign of the Assyrian king Sennacherib against Jerusalem. The people of Ashdod put their trust in the Egyptians and the Ethiopians, and their city was captured by the Assyrians. King Hezekiah and his people, however, put their trust in Yhwh, and their city was delivered. The Assyrians were not able to conquer Jerusalem (cf. Isa. 37:10, 33–35; 38:6). Assuming that a later editor reworked an earlier version of Isaiah 20, the question arises: who was responsible for editing the older text?
19 In recent years, several exegetes have defended the opposite perspective. For example, K.A.D. Smelik, ‘Distortion of Old Testament Prophecy: The Purpose of Isaiah xxxvi and xxxvii’, in: Crises and Perspectives: Studies in Ancient Near Eastern Polytheism, Biblical Theology, Palestinian Archeology and Intertestamental Literature (OTS 24), Leiden 1986, 70–93, claims that Isaiah 36–37 was originally written as part of the Book of Isaiah. Though Smelik makes clear that Isaiah 36–37 fits the context of the Book of Isaiah well, the chronological heading in Isa. 36:1 (parallel 2 Kgs 18:13) is in my view still a strong argument for the majority opinion that 2 Kings 18–19 has been copied into the Isaianic tradition.



Text and History of Isaiah 20 85
7 Indications of Redactional Activity
On the basis of similarities between the narratives in Isaiah 7, 20 and 36–39, Blenkinsopp assumes that the account in chapter 20 has a Deuteronomistic origin. In his view, the Deuteronomistic background of this passage is supported by the presence of several characteristics of Deuteronomistic-type historiographical narrative in the text. With regard to vv. 1–2, Blenkinsopp mentions three expressions he identifies as Deuteronomistic elements:20
The temporal phrase בׇּ עֵ ת ׇה ִהיא (‘at that time’) at the beginning of v. 2;21 The combination of the verb דבר Piel (‘to speak’) with the phrase ביד (‘through’) in v. 2;22 The reference to the prophet’s full name, ‘Isaiah son of Amoz’, in v. 2.
Though there is good reason to adopt Blenkinsopp’s suggestion that these elements have a Deuteronomistic background, it is questionable whether they prove that Isaiah 20 as a whole is a Deuteronomistic composition. It is also possible that they have been added by a Deuteronomistic editor.23 The latter option is supported by three observations. First, the time reference, ׇבּעֵ ת ׇה ִהיא (‘at that time’) at the beginning of v. 2 is identified by Blenkinsopp as a Deuteronomistic element. The reconstruction of Isa. 20:1–2 presented above, however, showed that the temporal phrase ‘at that time’ is probably a later addition. Second, the combination of the verb דבר Piel (‘to speak’) with ביד (‘through’) in v. 2 has an unusual meaning. This verbal pattern usually expresses that Yhwh
20 Cf. Blenkinsopp, Isaiah 1–39, 321. In his view, ‘the most telling indication of Deuteronomistic origin’ is the reference to Isaiah as ‘the servant of Yhwh’ in v. 3. 21 Besides Isa. 20:2, this temporal phrase occurs in Isa. 18:7 and 39:1 (parallel 2 Kgs 20:12). 22 According to Balogh, Stele of YHWH in Egypt, 307, the combination of the verb דבר Piel (‘to speak’) with ביד (‘through’) has a parallel in Hag. 2:1–2: ‘Even though the syntax of Isa. 20:2 is unusual, it is not entirely without parallels. Hag. 2:1–2 provides a strikingly similar case’. In my view, Hag. 2:1–2 does not offer help for understanding the syntactic construction in Isa. 20:2, because the word of Yhwh is not addressed here to Haggai but instead to Zerubbabel. The pattern in Hag. 2:1–2 is probably a variant to the syntactic construction used in Hag. 1:1–2 and 1:3–4. 23 Cf. W.A.M. Beuken, Jesaja 13–27 (Herders Theologischer Kommentar zum Alten Testament), Freiburg 2007, 208–9; De Jong, Isaiah among the Ancient Near Eastern Prophets, 151.



86
Oosting
speaks by means of one of his servants to someone else.24 Here, Yhwh does not speak through the prophet Isaiah but instead Isaiah himself is addressed. Isaiah is ordered to loose the sackcloth from his loins and to take his sandals off his feet and to walk naked and barefoot. The unusual meaning of the verbal pattern can be explained by assuming that an older account has been reworked. By adding the phrase ביד (‘through’) the editor changed Yhwh’s command to Isaiah to loose his sackcloth and take off his sandals into a message of Yhwh for the people of Jerusalem. In v. 3, it is made clear that Yhwh commissioned his servant Isaiah to walk naked and barefoot for three years as sign and portent against Egypt and Ethiopia. The personal command of Yhwh to the prophet to walk naked and barefoot as a sign of mourning (cf. Mic. 1:8) was thus changed into a symbolic act to warn the people of Jerusalem not to rely on Egypt and Ethiopia. Finally, the reference to the prophet’s full name is worth examining. Apart from Isa. 20:2, the designation ‘Isaiah son of Amoz’ occurs six times in the Book of Isaiah. The prophet’s full name is used in the superscriptions in Isa. 1:1, 2:1, and 13:1. Furthermore, this designation occurs three times in the narrative section Isaiah 36–39, namely, Isa. 37:2, 21, and 38:1. The fact that the prophet’s full name occurs three times in Isaiah 36–39 does not necessarily indicate that Isaiah 20 as a whole has a Deuteronomistic origin. As has been made clear above, it is plausible that the editor who copied 2 Kings 18–20 into the Isaianic tradition also reworked the older text of Isaiah 20. That editor was acquainted with the prophet’s full name and might well have been responsible for inserting the designation ‘Isaiah son of Amoz’ into Isa. 20:2. The above considerations lead to the conclusion that Blenkinsopp’s hypothesis regarding the Deuteronomistic origin of the account in Isaiah 20 cannot be proved.25 The expressions in this passage that have been identified as Deuteronomistic elements can better be regarded as later additions that were inserted by a Deuteronomistic editor. At the same time, it can be observed that the Deuteronomistic elements are closely tied with the other elements in
24 Cf. Exod. 9:35; Lev. 10:11; Num. 17:5; 27:23; Josh. 20:2; 1 Sam. 28:17; 1 Kgs 8:53, 56; 12:15; 14:18; 15:29; 16:12, 34; 17:16; 2 Kgs 9:36; 10:10; 14:25; 17:23; 21:10; 24:2; Jer. 37:2; 50:1; 2 Chron. 10:15. 25 This conclusion is in line with Brekelmans’s observation that Old Testament scholars tend to ‘ascribe too many things [in the prophetic writings] to the deuteronomistic movement’, cf. C.H.W. Brekelmans, ‘Deuteronomistic Influence in Isaiah 1–12’, in J. Vermeylen (ed.) The Book of Isaiah. Le livre d’Isaïe: Les oracles et leurs relectures unité et complexité de l’ouvrage (BETL 81), Leuven 1989, 176. See also J.R. Porter, ‘The Supposed Deuteronomic Redaction of the Prophets: Some Considerations’, in: R. Albertz et al. (eds) Schöpfung und Befreiung: Für Claus Westermann zum 80. Geburtstag, Stuttgart 1989, 69–78.



Text and History of Isaiah 20 87
the current composition. For that reason, it is likely that the Deuteronomistic elements are not individual secondary additions, but instead are part of a broader revision of the existing text. This suggestion is supported by the proposed reconstruction of vv. 1–2, which shows that the Deuteronomistic phrase ׇבּעֵ ת ׇה ִהיא (‘at that time’) is one of the elements the editor of the older account used in expanding the existing text. Moreover, this reconstruction indicates that the editor who inserted the Deuteronomistic elements into Isaiah 20 was probably the same one who reworked the earlier account underlying the current text of Isaiah 20.
8 Relationship to Extra-Biblical Sources
The next point that needs our attention is the relationship between the biblical account in Isaiah 20 and the references to the military campaign against Ashdod in extra-biblical sources. As was noted above, Assyrian inscriptions indicate that King Sargon II personally conquered the city of Ashdod. In the Khorsabad Annals King Sargon claims:
[In a sudden rage] I marched quickly – (even) in my state-chariot and (only) with my cavalry which never, even in friendly territory, leaves my side – against Ashdod, his royal residence, and I besieged and conquered the cities Ashdod, Gath (Gi-im-tu) (and) Asdudimmu26
This report is not consistent with the account in Isaiah 20, which says that King Sargon sent his commander-in-chief, the Tartan, to Ashdod. Some biblical scholars explain the discrepancy between the biblical account and the Assyrian inscription by calling into question the historical reliability of the Assyrian report. For example, Oswalt points out that ancient kings ‘were prone to claim full responsibility for all victories in annalistic reports’.27 A similar view is defended by Balogh. He suggests that the account in Isaiah 20 is more accurate, since ‘the king’s contention may be, as usual, ideologically motivated’.28
26 Cf. Khorsabad Annals, lines 249–62; see also Display Inscription, lines 90–112. The translation of the Assyrian inscriptions has been taken from J.B. Pritchard (ed.), Ancient Near Eastern Texts Relating to the Old Testament, Princeton 31969, 286. ‘Asdudimmu’ is the Assyrian name of the port Ashdod-Yam, that is, Ashdod-on-Sea. 27 Cf. Oswalt, Book of Isaiah, 383.
28 Cf. Balogh, Stele of YHWH in Egypt, 328.



88 Oosting
Another attempt to harmonize the two accounts is offered by Hayim Tadmor, who suggests that the Assyrian campaign against Ashdod did not take place in 711 BCE as is usually assumed, but instead in 712 BCE. In the Eponym Chronicles of the Assyrian Empire, the entry for the year 712 is ‘in the land’. From that Tadmor deduces that King Sargon stayed in the land and that the punitive expedition against Ashdod was probably led by his commander-inchief, the Tartan. Tadmor assumes that the reason that Sargon II remained behind was that his presence ‘was required by the building of his new residence, Dur-Šarrukin’.29 It is, however, more likely that the entry ‘in the land’ in the Eponym Chronicles does not describe the situation of the king, but the situation of the royal army.30 Furthermore, if the punitive expedition against Ashdod had taken place, one expects that this military campaign would be referred to under the year 712 in the Eponym Chronicles.31 For those reasons, it is better to hold to the common view that the campaign against Ashdod took place in 711 BCE and was led by King Sargon himself. In my view, there is no need to try to harmonize the two accounts on the Assyrian campaign against Ashdod. The discrepancy between Isaiah 20 and the Assyrian inscriptions may well be explained by the reworking of the biblical account. As demonstrated above, the first two verses of Isaiah 20 were probably edited by a later writer who inserted the reference to the Tartan in order to relate the Assyrian military campaign against Ashdod to the Assyrian campaign against Jerusalem referred to in Isaiah 36–37. As a consequence, the account in Isaiah 20 no longer depicted King Sargon as the one who led the military campaign against Ashdod, but instead his commander-inchief, the Tartan. Assuming that an older version of Isaiah 20 depicted Sargon as the one who led the punitive expedition against Ashdod, it is reasonable to suppose that this earlier account survived from near the time of the event, that is, the time of the prophet Isaiah himself. An argument supporting this conclusion
29 Cf. H. Tadmor, ‘The Campaigns of Sargon II of Assur: A Chronological-Historical Study’, JCS 12 (1958), 79, n. 208. The same view is defended by K.L. Younger, ‘Recent Study on Sargon II, King of Assyria: Implications for Biblical Studies’, in: M.W. Chavalas, K.L. Younger (eds), Mesopotamia and the Bible: Comparative Explorations, Grand Rapids 2002, 315.
30 Cf. A.R. Millard, The Eponyms of the Assyrian Empire: 910–612 B.C. (SAAS 2), Helsinki 1994, 5. 31 Cf. A. Fuchs, Die Annalen des Jahres 711 v. Chr. nach Prismenfragmenten aus Ninive und Assur (SAAS 8), Helsinki 1998, 86: ‘Hätte sich der Feldzug gegen Ašdod im Jahre 712 ereignet, so wäre infolgedessen ein Eintrag “gegen Ašdod” oder zumindest “die Großen gegen Ašdod” zu erwarten. Wenn dort stattdessen die Bemerkung “im Land” zu lesen steht, so kann im Jahre 712 überhaupt kein Feldzug stattgefunden haben.’



Text and History of Isaiah 20 89
is the fact that the account of the campaign against Ashdod has no parallel elsewhere in the Old Testament and that this is the only explicit reference to King Sargon II at all.32
9 Development within the Isaianic Tradition
The analysis of the linguistic and syntactic features of the text of Isaiah 20 in its final form has made clear that this passage contains several elements that allude to other early texts within Isaiah 1–39. Those elements indicate that an older version underlies the current text of Isaiah 20. The fact that we only have the text of Isaiah 20 in its current form makes certainty concerning the earlier account impossible. Though an attempt can be made to reconstruct the earlier version of Isaiah 20, the reconstruction itself is not the main purpose of this examination. In examining the development of Isaiah 20 within the Isaianic tradition, it is more important to know that the text has been edited than to know exactly what the older version looked like.33 The conclusion that an earlier version of Isaiah 20 has been reworked shows that texts in the Isaianic tradition were not static, but instead were part of a living tradition. The historical references in Isaiah 20 and the close parallels between the older version of Isaiah 20 and the Assyrian reports provide a basis for supposing that the earlier account was written shortly after the fall of Ashdod. One can imagine that the conquest of Ashdod left a deep impression on the people of Judah. From Assyrian sources we know that the king of Ashdod, Yamani, called the rulers of Palestine, Judah, Edom, and Moab to join him in rebelling against the king of Assyria. Nineveh Prism A reads:
[to] the rulers of Palestine (Pi-liš-te), Judah (Ia-ú-di), Ed[om], Moab (and) those who live (on islands) and bring tribute [and] tâmartu -gifts to my lord Ashur – [he spread] countless evil lies to alienate (them) from me34
32 Cf. H.G.M. Williamson, ‘In Search of the Pre-exilic Isaiah’, in: J. Day (ed.) In Search of Pre-exilic Israel: Proceedings of the Oxford Old Testament Seminar (JSOT.S 406), London 2004, 185.
33 In my view, P.M. Cook, A Sign and a Wonder: The Redactional Formation of Isaiah 18–20 (VT.S, 147), Leiden 2011, 126, n. 2, concludes too hastily that leaving aside ‘the wealth of Deuteronomistic expressions in these verses [Isa. 20:1–4], very little would remain without them.’ Even if we do not know exactly what remains of Isaiah 20 without later additions, we still can assume that there is an older text, which underlies the current one. 34 Nineveh Prism A, fragment D, lines 28–32. Cf. Pritchard, ANET, 287; see also Z.J. Kapera, ‘The Oldest Account of Sargon II’s Campaign against Ashdod’, Folia Orientalia 24 (1987), 33.



90 Oosting
We don’t know whether the Judeans accepted the invitation to join the rebellion against the Assyrians. But in either case, they had reason to be terrified about their own future. If the people of Ashdod were not able to resist the king of Assyria, how would they escape? At a later time, the older account was reworked by an editor, who related the narrative in Isaiah 20 to the narrative in Isaiah 36–37 about the siege of Jerusalem. In doing so, the editor created a contrast between the Assyrian military campaigns against Ashdod and Jerusalem. Whereas the city of Ashdod was captured because the people of Ashdod put their trust in Egypt and Ethiopia, Jerusalem was delivered because King Hezekiah and his people put their trust in Yhwh. The fall of Ashdod was thus no longer described as an isolated event in the later composition of Isaiah, but was related to the deliverance of Jerusalem described in Isaiah 36–39. In the current composition of Isaiah, chapter 20 is not directly connected to the narrative section in Isaiah 36–39, but instead is part of the oracles against the nations (Isaiah 13–23).35 Within this collection, the narrative in Isaiah 20 is linked to the preceding chapters by the references to Ethiopia and Egypt in vv. 3–5. For that reason, it is often considered to be the concluding passage of the literary unity Isaiah 18–20 about Ethiopia and Egypt.36 Furthermore, Isaiah 20 is connected to the previous passage, Isa. 19:18–24, by the reference to the great maritime highway in Isa. 19:23. This highway, which ran along the Palestinian coast from Egypt and then crossed eastwards to Syria and Mesopotamia, is the same route the Assyrians take in Isaiah 20 to march against Ashdod and then use to lead away the Egyptians and the Ethiopians as exiles. In Isa. 19:18–24, however, the highway is not used for military activities or deportation, but instead for pilgrimage. Verse 23 reads:
Isa. 19:23 On that day there will be a highway from Egypt to Assyria, and the Assyrians will come into Egypt, and the Egyptians into Assyria, and the Egyptians will worship with the Assyrians.
The previous passage on the harmony between Egypt and Assyria thus sheds new light on the narrative in Isaiah 20. Verse 23 promises that the highway
35 Cf. A.K. Jenkins, ‘The Development of the Isaiah Tradition in Isaiah 13–23’, in: J. Vemeylen (ed.), The Book of Isaiah. Le livre d’Isaïe: Les oracles et leurs relectures unité et complexité de l’ouvrage (BETL, 81), Leuven 1989, 247: ‘the later understanding of a prophecy . . . could be altered not only by internal modifications . . . but by its positioning in the collection of associated material.’ 36 Cf., e.g., Blenkinsopp, Isaiah 1–39, 321; Cook, A Sign and a Wonder, 147.



Text and History of Isaiah 20 91
from Egypt to Mesopotamia will no longer be used for military activities or deportation, but instead for pilgrimage. The maritime highway will serve to aid both the inhabitants of Egypt and Assyria to worship together the God of Israel.37
10 Conclusions
Isaiah 20 has been handed over to us as part of the Book of Isaiah. Though we merely have the text of this passage in its current form, we are able to examine the development of Isaiah 20 within the Isaianic tradition on the basis of the linguistic and literary features of Isaiah 20 itself and on the basis of the parallels between Isaiah 20 and other biblical and non-biblical texts. The examination shows that Isaiah 20 has not been a static text, but instead is the product of a living tradition. The linguistic and syntactic features of Isaiah 20 show that an older version of Isaiah 20 underlies the current text. On the basis of the syntactic parallels between Isaiah 20 and other passages in Isaiah 1–39 that are considered to be early, it is likely that this older version belonged to the earliest stages of the Isaiah tradition. In later times, the older account was reworked by an editor who related the Assyrian military campaign against Ashdod under Sargon II to the siege of Jerusalem under King Sennacherib. In doing so, the editor created a contrast between the fall of Ashdod in Isaiah 20 and the deliverance of Jerusalem in Isaiah 36–39. In the current composition of Isaiah, the narrative in Isaiah 20 is part of the oracles against the foreign nations (chapters 13–23). More specifically, it concludes the collection about Ethiopia and Egypt in Isaiah 18–20. The conclusion that Isaiah 20 is the product of a living tradition explains why the account in Isaiah 20 is not fully historically accurate. The main aim of the writers and the copyists of the Isaianic corpus was not the historical reliability of this passage, but its relation to the other parts of the book. It was their job to adapt and rearrange the account on the military campaign against Ashdod, so that it kept its relevance within the Isaianic tradition.
37 Cf. B.S. Childs, Isaiah, A Commentary (OTL), Louisville 2001, 144.

From Salvation to Doom: Isaiah’s Message in the Hezekiah Story
Thomas Wagner
The Hezekiah story has been investigated in many respects during the last decades by focusing on its original context and its redactional shape in the Book of Kings and the Book of Isaiah. Even if the origins of the story are still controversial it seems that OT research found a common sense due to the redactional shape of the story especially within the Book of Isaiah. Since chapters 36–37 belong to a late 7th-century or early 6th-century edition of the book, chapters 38 and 39 were added in the process of merging First and Second Isaiah in early post-exilic times.1 Finally the psalm of Hezekiah in ch. 38 was integrated in later times displaying the image of a pious king.2 In their redaction-critical analyses most of the investigators focus on the questions of the interrelation of Isa. 36–39 to other texts of First Isaiah and on connections between First and Second Isaiah demonstrating that Isa. 36–39 serve as link between these two parts of the book.3 In this article I will go into that matter by highlighting compositioncritical aspects of the Hezekiah story, their interconnections in the so-called
1 Cf. M.A. Sweeney, Isaiah 1–39: With an introduction to prophetic literature (FOTL XVI), Grand Rapids 1996, 457. For the diverse redaction critical investigations of Isa 38 cf. W.A.M. Beuken, Jesaja 28–39 (HThKAT), Freiburg, Basel und Wien 2010, 423–5. 2 Cf. P.R. Ackroyd, ‘An Interpretation of the Babylonian Exile’, JSOT 27 (1974), 328–52, Beuken, Jesaja 28–39, 420, 442.
3 Cf. P.R. Ackroyd, ‘Isaiah 36–39: Structure and Function,’ in: idem, Studies in the Religious Tradition of the Old Testament, London 1987, 116–19; P.R. Ackroyd, ‘An Interpretation of the Babylonian Exile: A Study of II Kings 20 and Isaiah 38–39,’ in: idem, Studies in the Religious Tradition of the Old Testament, London 1987, 160. Further cf. H.G.M. Williamson, The Book Called Isaiah: Deutero-Isaiah’s Role in Composition and Redaction, Oxford 1994, 189–211, who observes that prospective interrelations to Second Isaiah are too weak to assume the chapters were originally written as a link between First and Second Isaiah. But in the final book they serve as link. In favor of a later addition of Isa. 36–39 argue R.E. Clements, ‘The Unity of the Book of Isaiah,’ Int 36 (1982), 117–29 (esp. 122), O. Kaiser, Das Buch des Propheten Jesaja: Kapitel 13–39 (ATD 18), Göttingen 31983, 291, and U. Berges, Das Buch Jesaja: Komposition und Endgestalt (HBS, 16), Freiburg a.o. 1998, 306. For trenchant descriptions of the research history in different phases of modern scholarship cf. C.R. Seitz, Zion’s Final Destiny: The Development of the Book of Isaiah, Minneapolis 1991, 37–46, B.S. Childs, Isaiah (OTL), Louisville 2001, 26066, and P. Höffken, Jesaja: Der Stand der theologischen Diskussion, Darmstadt 2004, 134–39.



From Salvation to Doom 93
‘Großjesajabuch’, and the development of the prophet’s image.4 Starting with a brief description of narratological aspects of the redactional unit Isa. 36–39 to outline the authors’ intentions, the article will then show in which texts the structure identified in this unit is included. Finally it will deal with the question with which consequences this structure was adopted.
1 Isaiah in the Hezekiah Story
The unit Isa 36–39 is subdivided into three passages, which begin with various temporal formulas. Chapters 36–37 are introduced with ויהי בארבע רהשׂע נהשׁ למלך חזקיהו , ‘in the fourteenth year of the king Hezekiah’, chapter 38 with בימים ההם , ‘in those days’, and chapter 39 with בעת ההוא , ‘at that time’. “These formulas tie the subunits together as three separate episodes that relate to the same event: Sennacherib’s invasion of Judah in 701.”5 In this unit the prophet Isaiah occurs four times with alternating functions. His first entrance takes place during the siege of Jerusalem in Isa 37 in the house of the prophet. Eljakim, the royal housekeeper, and Shebna, the scribe, were sent by Hezekiah to ask the prophet to intercede to save the rest of the inhabitants of Jerusalem. Here the prophet is asked to function as an intercessor. Isaiah answers as diviner using the formula אל־תירא , by which a word of salvation is introduced. He mainly serves as divine messenger. The second time the prophet appears, he delivers a divine response to Hezekiah’s prayer. In this situation he functions again as a herald. Different from his first appearance the prophet takes the action and passes on the
4 Cf. the semantical links mentioned by Williamson, Book Called Isaiah, 210, and Berges, Buch Jesaja, 276: “Hätten diese Kapitel im Jesajabuch nun die Funktion einer Überleitung zum Ende der babylonischen Zeit, dann könnten sie erst nachdeuterojesajanisch ins Buch gelangt sein; gegen die Möglichkeit, die HJE seien zusammen mit dem Anschluß von deuterojesajanischem Material ins Buch gelangt, spricht die Tatsache, daß die Thematik des Gotteshauses ‘ תיב ’ als Ortes des gemeinsamen Gotteslobes, die der J-Text in Differenz zum K-Text so stark betont (38,19–20), in 40–55 nicht, wohl aber in 56,5.7 und 66,20 auftaucht; gehört diese Klammer um 56–66 zur letzten Redaktionsschicht, dann müssen 36–39 im Zuge eben dieser Schlußredaktion in die Mitte des Buches plaziert worden sein. Sollten sich die Eunuchen von 56,3 auf die von 39,7 beziehen, ware das ein weiteres Indiz dafür, daß die HJE im Zuge der tritojesajanischen Anfügung in die Jesajarolle integriert worden sind.” Supporting the thesis of Berges this article points to a linking between Isa. 38 and Isa. 57 by which the assignment of Isa. 36–39 to the last redactional layer of the book becomes ambiguous. 5 Sweeney, Isaiah 1–39, 455.



94 Wagner
promise that the Assyrian king will be judged due to his haughtiness (Isa. 37:29) and that he will not conquer Jerusalem (vv. 33–35). As a sign of confirmation God promises to Hezekiah to be able to farm his vineyards in the third year (vv. 30–31). In terms of a narratological investigation of Isa. 37 the basic opposition within the text is the change from the lament of Hezekiah expressed by tearing his clothes apart, which is a mourning ritual, to the killing of the king by his sons. In other words, the threat against Hezekiah and his kingdom turns to the doom of Sennacherib. As basic opponents appear the Assyrian king and the God of Israel,6 which is already expressed in the message delivered by the royal cupbearer Rabshakeh in Isa. 36:7–8, 15, 18.7 The message of Isaiah delivered in this context is obvious: The LORD will judge the Assyrian king for his arrogance and therefore he will save Jerusalem (Isa. 37:4).8 The announcement of doom for the Assyrian king is a promise for Jerusalem not to be delivered into the hand of Sennacherib (Isa. 37:10). Thus far the prophetic message contains a word of judgment against the Assyrian king, which implies a word of salvation for Jerusalem – not a general word for the everlasting existence of the city but rather one specifically for this situation.9 In the second passage of the Hezekiah story in Isa. 38 the king fell seriously ill. Isaiah appears again in vv. 1–8 and vv. 21–22. The prophet was sent to deliver a divine word (vv. 1–8) and to cure the king (vv. 21–22), which means he functions as herald and as healer. The narratological structure of this part of the story is determined by the antagonism between God and the Davidic king. The divine word in v. 1 contains just the prediction without any explanation for the announced occurrence. Hezekiah answers Isaiah’s message by praying to God and bringing to mind his faithfulness towards God during his entire lifetime. Hearing Hezekiah’s plea, God calls Isaiah to return to the king and to give him notice of an extension of his lifetime for another fifteen years. This prediction is confirmed by a divine sign (Isa. 38:7). The first announcement of the prophet serves as introduction to a story, by which the faithfulness of Hezekiah
6 Cf. K.A.D. Smelik, ‘Distortion of Old Testament Prophecy: The Purpose of Isaiah xxxvi and xxxvii,’ in: A.S. van der Woude (ed.), Crises and Perspectives: Studies in Ancient Near Eastern Polytheism, Biblical Theology, Palestinian Archaeology and Intertestamental Literature (OTS, 24), Leiden 1989, 80; D.M. Carr, ‘What Can We Say about the Tradition History of Isaiah?: A Response to Christopher Seitz’s Zion’s Final Destiny,’ in: Society of Biblical Literature, 1992 Seminar Papers (SBL.SP, 31), Atlanta 1992, 593, and Sweeney, Isaiah 1–39, 471–72. 7 As Sweeney, Isaiah 1–39, 482, points out Rabshakeh is the counterpart of the prophet Isaiah. Therefore both function as messenger for the respective opponent. 8 Cf. H. Wildberger, Jesaja 28–39 (BKAT X/3), Neukirchen-Vluyn 1982, 1431. 9 Cf. R.E. Clements, Isaiah 1–39 (NCB), Grand Rapids, London 1980, 100–2.



From Salvation to Doom 95
shall be highlighted. By receiving the message of his imminent death the king points to God’s sovereignty and his dependence on God’s decisions. The climax of the story is the second divine word with God’s promise to save the king’s life10 and the preservation of the capital. The way the promise is expressed points to a restriction to the contemporary situation. By calling the LORD the God of Hezekiah’s father David (v. 5), the author reminds the reader of the promise of the eternal Davidic kingship.11 But God does not renew his pledge. Instead he points to an extension of Hezekiah’s lifetime and the stand of Jerusalem during the contemporary siege. Other than in Isa. 36–37 the message of the prophet is not a word of doom against Assur but a word of salvation for Hezekiah. In the third passage of the story in Isa. 39, Isaiah appears in front of Hezekiah to inquire after his foreign visitors and their purposes. The prophet announces the deportation of the treasure of the house of David (Isa. 39:6) and of the sons of Hezekiah to serve at the king’s palace in Babel (v. 7). He functions as herald again and delivers a word of judgment concerning the exile of the house of the king in later generations. The divine word receives a twofold interpretation: the prophet points to the word as an announcement of judgment against the house of the king (vv. 6–7) while Hezekiah reads it as a word of personal salvation (v. 8). The basic opposition appears as the change of status for the Davidic kingdom. At the beginning of the story the Babylonian king sends gifts to Hezekiah in relation to his recovery, at the end the Davidic king is foretold that his sons will not be accepted as sovereigns like their father, but will serve as butlers for the Babylonian king. Even if the author lets us know that Hezekiah understands the prophetic prediction as a word of salvation, it is a word of doom for the house of David.12 Summarizing the development of Isaiah’s message, it detracts from the prospects for the Davidic kingship. Admittedly Hezekiah could avoid the fall of his city and his soon-coming death by praying to God, but he finally has to accept the downgrading of his sons to servants of the Babylonian king. The salvation is limited to Jerusalem and Hezekiah while the doom relates to the Assyrian
10 Cf. Williamson, Book Called Isaiah, 202: “The climactic ‘and he recovered’ forms an effective contrast to the ‘you shall not recover’ in verse 1.” 11 Cf. Berges, Buch Jesaja, 313: “Die Bezeichnung JHWHs als Gott Davids ‘ דוד יהל ’ steht mit der dtr Vorstellung von der göttlichen Gnade um seines Knechtes David willen im Verbindung”. 12 Cf. Seitz, Zion’s Final Destiny, 155: “Chapter 39, whatever else is to be said about it, points to judgment, not restoration. It does so not metaphorically, nor in a veiled way, but by means of direct prophecy (39:5–7). Davidic kingship will come to an end, and Hezekiah’s sons will be sārîsîm in the palace of the king of Babylon (39:7).”

king and the house of David. This development is dependent on the formation of the Book of Isaiah. Isaiah’s words are interconnected with texts of different stages, in which the image of the prophet displayed in Isa 36–39 changes.
2 The Doom Against the Assyrian King
In the mid 1970s Hermann Barth pointed to a redactional layer added to the First Isaianic collection in the late 7th century BCE called ‘Assur-Redaktion’. In this addition, the redactor emphasizes God’s rejection of the Assyrian king due to his haughtiness as presented in Isa. 10:5–19*.13 Barth discovers the end of this redactional layer in Isa. 31:5, 8b–9 as addition to the basic layer of the ‘Assur-Zyklus’ in Isa. 28–31. The basic layer includes five words introduced with הוי in Isa. 28:1–4, 29:1–8, 29:15–16, 30:1–5 and 31:1–3. These words contain threats against the Jerusalemites and the Davidic kingdom to refrain from coalitions with Egypt (especially words four and five). As expressed in word two, God will terrify Jerusalem by calling the nations to besiege the city. This event just serves to terrify the Judeans, as it is expressed in the redactional addition following the last הוי -word in Isa. 31:4–5, 8–9:14
4 For thus the Lord has spoken to me: As a lion roars, and a young lion over his prey, when a multitude of shepherds is summoned against him, He will not be afraid of their voice nor be disturbed by their noise. So the Lord of hosts will come down to fight for Mount Zion and for its hill. 5 Like birds flying about, so will the Lord of hosts defend Jerusalem. Defending, He will also deliver it; passing over, He will preserve it. 8 Then Assyria shall fall by a sword not of man, and a sword not of mankind shall devour him. But he shall flee from the sword, and his young men shall become forced labor. 9 He shall cross over to his stronghold for fear, and

his princes shall be afraid of the banner, says the LORD, whose fire is in Zion and whose furnace is in Jerusalem.
The announcement of divine judgment informs the reader about the end of the Assyrian siege of Jerusalem and describes a situation very similar, though not identical, to the pledge of Isaiah in Isa. 37. The LORD will come to set an end to the Assyrian siege by killing the troops and by letting the king escape from his sword. “Die Weissagung göttlichen Schutzes ‘ גנן ’ und Errettung ‘ נצל ’ (hif.) zugunsten Jerusalems (31,5) löst JHWH fast wortwörtlich in 37,35; 38,6 ein.”15 However, Isa. 37 also contains several differences to the announcement in Isa. 31:4–5, 8–9. It is not the LORD himself but his messenger who kills 185.000 soldiers. The Assyrian king was finally murdered by his own sons. This aspect is missing in the announcement of the 7th century redaction (cf. Isa. 14:24–27). Taking into account these differences and the closed structure of the narration in Isa. 37 it is justifiable to believe that Isa. 36–37 were written independently and added at a later stage to confirm the announcement of Isa. 10:5–19* and Isa. 31:4–5, 8–9.16 The story affirms that the expectation of the failure of the Assyrian occupation became true. is that collections of prophetic words in their written form miss words of confirmation. In this case that was solved by adding the narration of the failure of the Assyrian campaign.17 Asking for the narratological function of Isa. 36–37 for the entire Book of Isaiah one may assume that the story serves to complete the controversy between God and the Assyrian king.18
3 The Loss of the Davidic Kingship in Isa 39
As several investigations show, Isa. 39 belongs to another literary layer and therefore needs to serve in the Book of Isaiah at a later redactional stage.19 Peter Ackroyd, Ronald Clements, and in their line many other commentators point to chapter 39 as a literary link between First and Second Isaiah. According to them, the announcement of doom to Hezekiah reflects the fall of Jerusalem and the Davidic dynasty, which was not mentioned in First Isaiah but which is a precondition for understanding the situations described in Isa. 40–55.20 Clements outlines the problems of this assumption: he shows that as in the Book of Kings the announcement of doom for Hezekiah refers to the events of 598 BCE. However, in the context of the Book of Isaiah the story needs to justify the judgment against Judah in 587 BCE. Clements argues that Hezekiah’s healing serves to describe the persistence of the house of David in 598 BCE
17 Cf. Berges, Buch Jesaja, 303, who emphasizes the meaning of Isa. 36–37 for readers of the final form of the book: “Für den Leser des Buches bedeutet das: Wenn sich die antiassyrischen Orakel der חזון des Propheten bereits zu seinen Lebzeiten so eindeutig erfüllten, dann werden sich auch seine Verheißungen im zweiten Teil der Rolle mit eben solcher Sicherheit erfüllen!”
18 Cf. J.W. Groves, Actualization and Interpretation in the Old Testament (SBL.DS 86), Atlanta 1987, 197, who points to diverse interconnections of Isa. 36–37 to texts of the ‘Assur-Zyklus’ (Isa. 30:1–8, 30:15, 31:1–3) and the ‘Assur-Redaktion’ (10:8–11, 14:13–14). C. Hardmeier, Prophetie im Streit vor dem Untergang Judas: Erzählkommunikative Studien zur Entstehungssituation der Jesaja- und Jeremiaerzählungen in II Reg 18–20 und Jer 37–40 (BZAW 187), Berlin, New York 1990, 125–26, emphasizes three aspects by which Isa. 36–37 is related to First Isaiah: 1. JHWH’s acting for Jerusalem, 2. the call for trust (Isa. 7:9b) which is fulfilled by Hezekiah, and 3. the end of the Assyrian arrogance. 19 Cf. These two chapters do not accord with the prophet Isaiah’s Zionist theology, which advocates the inviolability of Jerusalem and the favored status of YHWH’s chosen ruler.” 20 Cf. Ackroyd, ‘Isaiah 36–39’, 113: “Its theme is of submission to Babylon, the inevitability of exile, the downfall of the Davidic monarchy”.



From Salvation to Doom 99
and the announcement of doom for Hezekiah the fall of the Davidic kingship 11 years later.21 From a redaction-critical point of view his thesis might explain the redactional process. However, from a composition-critical perspective one needs to ask how a reader, who is not intimately familiar with the exact course of the historical events, should read the story in this way and detect the function of Isa. 39 for the Book of Isaiah. So it seems to me that it is necessary to ask for another reference point than the twofold siege of Jerusalem to understand the function of Isa. 39. The outlined narratological aspects may help to find it. In his doctoral thesis Michael Pietsch investigated the reception of the promise to David in the entire Hebrew Bible. He shows that in Chapters. 40–66 the Book of Isaiah points to the promise only in transformation but not in its original sense.22 This sense is relevant for the controversy between the prophet and the Judean kings Ahaz and Hezekiah in Isa. 7 and Isa. 38–39 and in the announcements of the new king in Isa. 9 and Isa. 11. Moreover, it is surprising that the theme does not emerge in Isa. 40–66 anymore. The visit of the Babylonian delegation and Hezekiah’s frankness to show all his property results in the prophet’s word about the fall of the Davidic kingship. Isaiah announces in Isa. 39:6–7 that the entire property of the house of David will be brought to Babylon and that a number of Hezekiah’s sons will have to serve as eunuchs at the Babylonian royal court. The basic opposition of the text already points to that development: at the beginning Hezekiah and the Babylonian king appear as equal figures. At the end, the members of the Davidic dynasty have to serve at the Babylonian royal court. The text emphasizes the loss of meaning and acceptance of the house of David.23

Taking this observation in account, the function of this narration for the Book of Isaiah as a book merged from First and Second Isaiah is to initialize the announcement of Cyrus as the LORD’s Anointed in Isa. 44:24–45:7. This is the only text in Isa. 40–66, in which a royal figure appears as a person appointed by God. He is designated to take the role of the announced king of Isa. 9 and 11 since the Davidic covenant is related to God’s people (Isa. 55:3).24 Since Isa. 11 points to the house of David as the origin of the new king, Cyrus is a foreign ruler without any relation to the former Judean dynasty. Referring to a synchronic reading of the Book of Isaiah it is necessary to compensate for this contradiction. Cyrus could not become the Anointed One as long as the promise to the house of David is still relevant. With the announcement of the fall of the Davidic dynasty and the subordination of the descendants under the Babylonian king, the call of Cyrus becomes possible in the notion of the reader.25 What is formulated as an anticipation of the deportation of king Jehoiachin and his sons in the book of Kings in 2 Kgs 20,26 is used as an explanation for why Cyrus could be called by the LORD even if this contradicts the expectation of Isa. 11.27

Ackroyd, ‘Isaiah 36–39’, 119; Sweeney, Isaiah 1–39, 511: “The guarantee to the people is therefore based on the old Davidic covenant, but the guarantee of security is applied to them, not to the monarch.” And as he fills this out in another article: “Among other concerns, it [the interrelationship between Isa. 56–66 and the Book of Isaiah TW] points to a new understanding of the Davidic covenant in these chapters that accounts for the realities of Persian rule and the demise of the house of David. This is especially noteworthy in that the following discussion shows that Isaiah 11,1–16, the oracle concerning the establishment of a righteous Davidic monarch over a reconstituted and reunited Israel, plays a particularly prominent role in the message of Trito-Isaiah” (M.A. Sweeney, ‘The Reconceptualization of the Davidic covenant in Isaiah,’ in: J. van Ruiten, M. Therefore the hope of a reinstallation of a Judean king lasted until the times of Haggai and Zechariah. This corresponds a. with the assumption of the merging of First and Second Isaiah in post-exilic times, and b. with the observation that the narration in 2 Kgs 20 serves to look ahead to the deportation of Jehoiachin’s sons, which is not mentioned in Second Isaiah, and that the function of the story thus needs to vary in the Book of Isaiah.



From Salvation to Doom 101
The call of Cyrus is an announcement of a future event, which is surprising for the reader. Like in Isa. 36–39 the prophet acts as a messenger heralding God’s impact on history. This means his prophecy is orientated towards the course of history by referring to unique historical events. Thus the function of the prophet does not change even if the genres of texts included in Second Isaiah are different from the prophetic narration in Isa. 36–39.
4 Hezekiah’s Prayer in Isa 38:9–20
In the last stage of the literary history of the Hezekiah story this prayer was added. The text is missing in the parallel version in 2 Kgs 20. There must be a reason for including the prayer due to the literary concept of the Book of Isaiah in this stage. As pointed out by several investigations the psalm was composed in later Persian times. Therefore Hezekiah is presented as a faithful king parallel to his image in Chronicles. Referring to these investigations, Hezekiah became a role model for a true believer as appearing in Isa. 56–66. However, the investigators miss showing how a reader of the Book of Isaiah should identify the king as a role model by reading the later texts.28 The prayer contains semantic correlations to numerous Isaianic texts. But most of the semantic links do not constitute a thematic relationship between these texts. There is only one exception: as regards the subject matter there is an interrelation between the prayer of Hezekiah and the word about the wicked in Isa. 57:1–13.29 The two texts are dealing with the destiny of an individual, a motif which appears rarely in the Book of Isaiah but which is important for the formation of the eschatological temple community. Isa. 38:9–20 emphasizes the death agony of king Hezekiah by pointing to the way he felt in the agony and highlighting that he cried for help to the LORD for the entire night (Isa. 38:13, 16). In contrast to this Isa. 57 describes the way of the infidels who could call the LORD but who ask idols for help (Isa. 57:13). Even if it seems that God
28 See, e.g., Sweeney, Isaiah 1–39, 504: “Hezekiah [. . .] presents a model of faithfulness to the Jewish community, assuring that trust in YHWH, expressed through temple worship, would see the community through its period of crisis.” The question remains how this kind of reading shall work since the king does not plead to God in the temple but in his palace, which does not exist anymore. 29 For the unit of Isa. 57:1–13 cf. Childs, Isaiah, 465. Former interpreters separated vv. 1–2 from vv. 3–13 but they had the problem to explain the function of vv. 1–2 in the context of Isa. 56:9–12 and Isa. 57:3–13. Childs reads the entire passage as a critique against leaders of the post-exilic community.

Wagner
will give up the faithful, they will rest in peace (Isa. 57:1–2). But the wicked will pass away without any honor. Those believing in God will inherit the land (Isa. 57:13). The differences between the two texts are apparent: Isa. 38 describes the agony of a king who survived, while Isa. 57 emphasizes the destiny of the wicked after the death of numerous faithful. Isa. 57 answers the question, who will belong to the holy community and inherit the land. And the answer is simple: those who are acting like Hezekiah and never give up faith in the LORD will inherit the land (Isa. 57:13) even if it seems that the faithful will die without any regard (Isa. 57:1–2).30 The basic opposition of this text is a change of respect towards the faithful. Hezekiah becomes one of these faithful, who already died but rests in peace.31 He serves as a role model for the members of the post-exilic community32 by keeping the faith even in his agony and by showing how to act in deepest personal doubts. What is missing in Isa. 57 is the royal perspective. The idea of the fall of the Davidic kingdom and the loss of kingship in Judah is described in Isa. 39 but is already marked in the prayer in a conspicuous wordplay.33 In Isa. 38:12 Hezekiah laments that his
דור was plucked up. In general, the term דור means ‘descent’ or ‘generation’, but not in this context. Due to the comparison the term describes the homestead of the king since the antecedent is כאהל רעי , ‘like the tent of a shepherd’. The term receives the meaning of a homestead,34 which is equivalent to the ‘house of the king’ or the dynasty.35 In the prayer Hezekiah describes the plucking up of his דור as happening during his agony.36 In the

From Salvation to Doom 103
retrospective of the post-exilic author the homestead of the king is already plucked up and the dynasty has fallen. With the prayer he highlights the relation of Hezekiah to the LORD and the impact of a faithful life for an individual as role model for believers in the post-exilic community. The situation of Isa. 57 as in the rest of the chapters 56–66 does not point to a unique historical event but to an eschatological formation of the holy community. With this modification the role of the prophet changes. Since he is the one heralding major historical events as effected by the LORD in First and Second Isaiah, he becomes a teacher announcing the destiny of individuals due to their faith and deeds.
5 Conclusions
The investigation of Isa. 36–39 shows that the Hezekiah story serves on various redactional levels with different functions, which means nothing else than that the message of the prophet was reinterpreted in several stages. At the beginning Isa. 36–37 serves to conclude the divine judgment against the Assyrian king. By merging First and Second Isaiah the aspect of the fall of the house of David was highlighted to interrelate the messianic announcements in Isa. 9 and 11 with the call of Cyrus as the LORD’s Anointed. Finally the Hezekiah story functions not only as the bridge between First and Second Isaiah but also as a connection between the times of the kings and the times of the postexilic temple community. The redactor’s adding Hezekiah’s prayer already looks ahead to the post-exilic era. Human kingship, even the Davidic kingship, is not a paragon for the post-exilic community, but a faithful person who keeps trusting in God even in the worst phases of his life. Moreover, it is an ideal role model for all believers, who are joining the community. The role of the prophet as a counterpart of the Davidic king finally got lost in the process of literary history for two reasons: 1. The king as receiver of divine words was changed to an ideal believer and a role model for later community members. His personal relationship to God is highlighted. He and (in the reception of his figure) the post-exilic community member possess a personal relationship to the LORD and they do not need the prophet to ask for intercession. 2. The message of the prophet changes. In the Hezekiah story and also in Isa. 44–45 he heralds future historical events, which are not conceivable for his counterparts. In Isa. 57 he outlines the divine acting towards the believers and the unbelievers in terms of an eschatological judgment. The leading question is the realization of divine righteousness less in terms of personal fate than in terms of true faith and right cultic practice. Thus the prophet’s image changes from a diviner heralding divine words to a teacher conveying true faith, its practice and the expectation of a future divine judgment against the enemies of the community.

Huldah: A Cunning Career Woman?
Blaženka Scheuer
Prophetess Huldah is one of five women in the Hebrew Bible referred to as nebîʾāh, and one of seven named prophetesses in the rabbinic count.1 Her single appearance in the Hebrew Bible is documented at a time of political insecurity that called for prophetic mediation. It is Huldah, not Jeremiah, who the Deuteronomists chose to authorize the words of the book of the Law, making Huldah the person who, in Claudia Camp’s words, ‘not only interprets but also authorizes the first document that will become the core of scripture for Judaism and Christianity.’2 The short account of Huldah’s background and prophecy is recorded in 2 Kgs 22:14–20 and again almost verbatim in 2 Chron. 34:22–28.The text is placed within the narrative about Josiah (2 Kgs 22–23:30), which in turn stands at the very end of the Book of Kings and thereby at the end of the Deuteronomistic History. The importance of Josiah has been widely discussed in scholarship, and the fact that Josiah is greatly idealized by the Deuteronomists (2 Kgs 23:25) is broadly acknowledged.3 The placement of Huldah’s oracle in the narrative about Josiah’s reform is significant: the oracle seems to be the factor that triggers Josiah’s extensive reformation of the cult (2 Kgs 23:1–24), thereby
1 The five prophetesses are Miriam, Deborah, Huldah, Noadiah and the unnamed prophetess of Isa. 8:3. Rabbinic sources account for seven prophetesses of the Bible among which Huldah is also mentioned: ‘Forty eight prophets and seven prophetesses prophesied for Israel’ (b. Megillah 14a). The seven prophetesses are: Sarah, Miriam, Deborah, Hannah, Huldah, Abigail, and Esther. Brenner refers to an alternative list which counts nine female prophets in the Hebrew Bible, adding Rachel and Leah, see A. Sweeney stresses the vital role of prophecy, and thereby the importance of Huldah’s prophecy, in the Deuteronomistic presentation of history: ‘prophecy is a major force in the historical presentation of the DtrH, in that prophets generally give voice to the DtrH interpretation of Israel’s and Judah’s history and to its overall theological viewpoint’, 180. See also M. Pietsch, ‘Prophetess of Doom. Hermeneutical Reflections on the Huldah Oracle’, paper read at the AAR/SBL Annual Meeting (Deuteronomistic History: Redaction of the Book of Kings Session), SBL 2007, available at http://www.sbl-site.org/assets/pdfs/Michael_Pietsch.pdf, 2. Huldah: A Cunning Career Woman? 105
makingHuldahthemostimportantamongtheprophetsof theDeuteronomistic History.4 The main questions elicited by this text and discussed in biblical scholarship concern the history of the text on the one hand and the choice of Huldah over Jeremiah on the other. When and why was the text written, and why was Huldah chosen to deliver a message of destruction to Josiah? In the following, I shall give a short review of the answers proposed before focusing on the fate of Huldah after her appearance in the two biblical books asking: how was Huldah understood by subsequent readers?
1 The History of the Text
The oracle is generally divided into two parts: the oracle of judgment upon Judah and the people (2 Kgs 22:15–17), and the oracle of assurance or of salvation to Josiah (2 Kgs 22:18–20).5 Both parts are comprised of an answer to Josiah who sent his men to ask Huldah for prophetic clarification and guidance, or even intersession.6 Literary tension within the text, such as the juxtaposition of the oracle of judgment (vv. 15–17) with the oracle of salvation (vv. 18–20), has led scholars to conclude that the oracle has been extensively reworked by the redactors of the Josianic narrative.7
4 Thus, Tal Ilan concludes that ‘Huldah’s words to the king are a stock phrase of the Deuteronomist, used occasionally in the Book of Kings but shared only by Jeremiah. In the absence of Jeremiah himself from the Book of Kings, Huldah remains the single most powerful Deuteronomistic voice in it.’ T. Ilan, ‘Huldah, the Deuteronomic Prophetess of the Book of Kings’, Lectio Difficilior 1 (2010), 8.
5 These two oracles are chiastically structured; see, among others, Pietsch who argues for the literary uniformity of Huldah’s oracle on account of this structure which he sees as constitutive for the oracle; Pietsch, ‘Prophetess of Doom’, 3–4. 6 Sara Japhet, discussing the question of the nature of the oracle in 2 Chron. 34, argues that Josiah did not ask for guidance as much as for intercession, a nuance which can be seen in the rare use of the preposition beʿad, ‘for, on behalf of’, see S. Japhet., I & II Chronicles, London 1993, 1032. 7 For a recent brief review of scholarly discussion of the literary tensions within Huldah’s oracle, see M.A. Sweeney, King Josiah, 45–51. Some scholars have pointed out that Huldah’s oracle could be removed without disturbing the narrative about Josiah, a fact that indicates later editing. Thus Pietsch dates the oracle to exilic times and argues that it was included subsequently in an earlier narrative in the Book of Kings, see Pietsch, ‘Prophetess of Doom’, 6. 106 Scheuer
One particular aspect in the text that complicates the discussion of the literary history of the oracle is the ambiguity of Huldah’s message to Josiah: ‘Therefore, I will gather you to your ancestors, and you shall be gathered to your grave in peace; your eyes shall not see the disaster that I will bring on this place’ (2 Kgs 22:20). On account of the similarity of the Hebrew phrase ‘to be gathered to one’s fathers in peace’ with Jer. 34:4 and 1 Kgs 2:6, Huldah was understood to be referring to Josiah’s death.8 The problem is obvious: Josiah did not die in peace, but died at the young age of 39 in a battle he himself pursued (2 Kgs 23:28–30), a fact that leaves Huldah’s prophecy, as it stands now, to be the only false prophecy of the Hebrew Bible.9 Whether one chooses to go this far or not, that Huldah was avoiding giving a straightforward answer to the king remains, a fact that raises a number of questions: Why would editors of this text let a prophet of Yahweh speak only half the truth to the king? And why would they leave Huldah’s potentially false prophecy undisputed? Some awareness of the problem among biblical authors later on is indicated by Chronicler’s version of Josiah’s story in which Josiah himself seems to be blamed for the un-fulfillment of this part of the prophecy (2 Chron. 35:22).10 Yet, it would be interesting to know how this ambiguity in Huldah’s prophecy

Huldah: A Cunning Career Woman? 107
was understood by subsequent readers. What kind of a prophet was Huldah regarded to be and how was her message understood by an early readership? Before I turn to these issues, I will briefly review the scholarly answer to the second question: Why Huldah?
2 Why Huldah?
The fact that a woman prophet was chosen to be consulted on account of the book found in the temple is considered by most scholars a fact puzzling enough to be taken up for discussion.11 A number of interesting reasons for the choice of Huldah have been proposed. First, Huldah was a cult prophetess. Married to a temple official, she was probably involved in temple service as a prophet. Textual support for this view could be seen in the typically priestly phrase she used: ‘be gathered ( אסף ) to your fathers’.12 In addition, connected to the temple, Huldah would be more likely to be loyal to the king:13 She may have been connected to the royal court in a way Jeremiah was not, a detail that would make her an expected candidate for royal consultant.14 Second, Huldah was the most influential Deuteronomistic prophet of her day. She was aligned with the Deuteronomistic movement and was, therefore, a part of Josiah’s cult reform.15 Third, Huldah represents the interest of a certain group. Duane L. Christensen suggests that this group consists of the ‘men of Anatoth’, who
11 Scholars have pointed out the fact that Huldah’s message echoes a number of (male) voices, most prominently Jeremiah’s and Moses’ but also the voice of Solomon’s vision in 1 Kgs 9. See A.G. Auld, ‘Prophets Shared – but Recycled’ in: T. Römer (ed.), The Future of Deuteronomistic History, Leuven 2000: 25.
12 Thus, in a short note J. Priest, ‘Huldah’s Oracle’, VT 30 (1980), 366–68. 13 Gray, J., I & II Kings. A Commentary. London 1970, 726. See also Gwilym H. Jones, 1 and 2 Kings, Volume II: 1 Kings 1–2 Kings 25:30 (NCB). Grand Rapids, London 1984, 612, who finds it to be more likely that Shallum was working at the temple rather than the royal court on account of the reference to a similar official in the temple of Baal in 2 Kgs 10:22. Thus also Gafney, Daughters of Miriam, 103, who sees Huldah as a cult-prophetess due to the orthodoxy of her oracle. 14 This view is further corroborated by the parallels in Assyrian documents: see Tikva Frymer-Kensky in: Reading the Women of the Bible: A New Interpretation of Their Stories, New York 2002, 325. 15 Thus Ilan, ‘Huldah, the Deuteronomic Prophetess’, 10. Ilan argues that she was the prophet which the author of the Book of Kings chose in order to deliberately bypass Jeremiah. Jones sees a connection between Huldah and Deuteronomists in the reference to her neighborhood in Jerusalem, the Second Quarter, taken to be the expansion of Jerusalem towards the north where refugees from the Northern kingdom would have settled as well.
