were critical of the social stratification in the society brought about by the monarchy.16 Restoring social justice – for women too – would be of importance to this group, just as it was for the authors of Deuteronomy. Fourth, as a woman, Huldah had both a cultural and a rhetorical importance demonstrated by the fact that Asherah-worship was associated with women, explaining why a woman’s voice of condemnation was required to abolish this practice. Only a woman prophet would be able to get the women to reform. In her message, nevertheless, Huldah functioned as ‘a “female alter ego” of Jeremiah.’17 All these suggestions come down to one basic idea: Huldah was chosen by the redactor of the Book of Kings/DtrH because she was a woman. First, women prophets were numerous in other ANE traditions and texts, particularly in Assyrian sources, and the fact that she was, through her husband, connected to the temple and/or royal court made her a legitimate prophet in spite of her gender.18 Thus Mari texts are particularly interesting due to the

In some of these stories oracles would be required in order to proceed with major reforms undertaken by kings.20 In general, in ancient Israel’s cultural surroundings, women prophets were quite common and, in Huldah, DtrH found a candidate who fits the part perfectly. Furthermore, DtrH was familiar with traditions about Miriam, Rahab, Deborah, Hannah, and Abigail. Adding Huldah was a fitting end to the Deuteronomic history in which women have significant roles.21 Tikva Frymer-Kensky writes about Huldah:
The last prophet in the Deuteronomic history, she provides closure to the period of the occupation of the land (introduced by Rahab), to the monarchy (proclaimed by Hannah), and to the throne of David (proclaimed by Abigail).22
All these women function ‘as the agents of social transformation’, yet this time not in the realm of family affairs but in the realm of the Israelite history.23 Huldah marked the transition from monarchy to theocracy, from the time of

the prophets to the time of Torah.24 In this process, the power and positions of men prophets is not endangered: the recognition for a job well-done would still go to Jeremiah. To sum up, the choice of Huldah over Jeremiah seems to be deliberate and quite understandable, yet the oracle attributed to her and preserved in the Hebrew Bible seems to be ambiguous and unique. To find out more about Huldah, I now turn to the representation of Huldah in later, inner-biblical and rabbinic tradition.
3 How was Huldah Read and Understood by a Later Readership?
The Hebrew Bible itself is not particularly negative towards Huldah and it does not make any active efforts to diminish her role. The diminishment of Huldah’s importance in the Hebrew Bible is more passive in character. In the final statements in both the Deuteronomistic and Chronistic accounts of history, male prophets – Jeremiah in particular – are referred to as being sent to predict a disaster for Judah. In one of the last reflective appraisals of the destruction of Jerusalem, the redactors of the Book of Kings declare: ‘He sent them against Judah to destroy it, according to the word of the Lord that he spoke by his servants the prophets’ (2 Kgs 24:2b). The prophet per se that spoke of this punishment is Huldah, yet she is not mentioned by name. Even though the masculine plural for prophets mentioned in the Hebrew Bible might include both genders, both Huldah as a person and Huldah as the prophet are obscured in the grammar of the scriptures which she declared as authoritative. It is worth remarking on the fact that the redactors of the Book of Kings, who made Huldah exceptional as the first prophet (and the only one in DtrH) to give authority to the written word as the word of Yahweh, did not acknowledge her importance in a more explicit way. In Wilda Gafney’s words, ‘Huldah’s case demonstrates both an apparent lack of gender bias on the part of the king and priests who consulted her (including the High Priest) and the appearance of bias on the part of the tradition preservers and shapers of the Hebrew Bible who did not conserve her broader oracular legacy.’25 On the other side, while
24 Cf. Naʾaman: ‘The reference to the “Book of the Law” in 2 Kings 22–23 may thus be interpreted as symbolizing the transition of authority from the prophet and the temple to the divine word embodied in the “Book of the Law” ’; Naʾaman, ‘The “Discovered Book” ’, 54. 25 Gafney, Daughters of Miriam, 102. Of course, the writers of the story of Huldah and the editors of the Book of Kings could be the same people. Nevertheless, it is interesting to observe this discrepancy. The fact that Huldah is defined as a wife of Shallum does not



Huldah: A Cunning Career Woman? 111
we have seen that Huldah’s role as a prophetess was obscured by the grammar of 2 Kgs 24:2b, 2 Chronicles goes further: Huldah’s role is diminished and subdued, replaced by the prophet Jeremiah. In an appraisal of the destruction of Jerusalem, the Book of Chronicles concludes that the events occurred ‘to fulfill the word of the Lord by the mouth of Jeremiah’ (2 Chron. 36:21 and again in the addendum in verse 22).26 Again, Huldah is left out of the history. Tradition takes on another prophet to promote.
3.1 Rabbinic Skepticism
The honor given to Huldah’s person and deed in rabbinic tradition is vast and in certain aspects unique.27 In Atalyah Brenner’s words, tradition gave Huldah an office within temple walls, two gates to the Temple Mount, and a grave in the Jerusalem hills.28 However, rabbinic literature had quite a decisive understanding of appropriate female roles. In their culturally-formed view, women should not speak in public at all – an understanding that raised particular complications when evaluating prophesying women. In the eyes of the rabbis, Huldah was primarily a woman and secondarily a prophet; her prophetic role was subdued as a result of her gender. The particularly negative tradition connected to Huldah and recorded in the Talmud (BT Megillah 14b) focuses on this very subject.
need to be seen as a diminishing factor. Studies have shown that lay female prophets are frequently presented as wives in order to ‘indicate their social accountability’, see Gafney, Daughters of Miriam, 69 citing A. At the same time, systematic exposition of rabbinic understanding of Huldah is not possible due to the nature of source material which is, in Judith Baskin’s words, ‘characterized by a multivocal dialectical structure that preserves not only the definitive opinions of the majority, but also retains minority points of view.’ J. 3.1.1 Megillah 14b
Having clarified the fact that Huldah’s credentials were based on her being married to the ‘right guy’ and having friends in high places,29 the rabbis went on to discuss the essentials of her character.
R. Nahman said: Haughtiness does not befit women. There were two haughty women, and their names are hateful, one being called a hornet and the other a weasel. Of the hornet it is written, And she sent and called Barak, instead of going to him. Of the weasel it is written, Say to the man, instead of ‘say to the king’.30
Louis Ginzberg further clarifies the above in two separate comments:
Megillah 14b. Here also it is remarked: Eminence is not for women; two eminent women are mentioned in the Bible, Deborah and Huldah, and both proved to be of a proud disposition. Deborah was haughty towards Barak (. . .) and the prophetess Huldah spoke of Josiah as the “man” (. . .) without giving him the title of king. This “unpleasant” feature of their character is indicated by their “ugly” names, the former was called Deborah “bee”, and the latter Huldah “weasel”.31
And again:
Megillah 14b. Here it is also stated: Pride is unbecoming to women; the prophetesses Deborah and Huldah were proud women (. . .) and both bore ugly names (Deborah = “bee” and Huldah = “weasel”).32
The rabbinic evaluation of Huldah is not based on her actions or on any doubt of her being a true prophet33 but, rather, the judgment is based on her gender. Huldah’s major and only fault was that she acted as a woman in a typically
29 In his comments to the text in 2 Kgs 22, Rashi goes even further, combining all these elements and writes that Huldah was a prophetess as a reward to her compassionate husband Shallum, see Rashi (trans.) in A.J. Rosenberg, II Kings: A New English Translation, New York 1989, 341, and 411. 30 Talmud Bavli, Megillah 14b.
31 L. Ginzberg, The Legends of the Jews, vol. 6, Notes to Volumes III and IV: From Moses in the Wilderness to Esther, Philadelphia 1987, 377, n. 117. 32 Ginzberg, Legends of the Jews, vol. 6, 196, n. 75.
33 The very fact that the deuteronomistic redactors left the Huldah story in this shape, as well as the fact that she was well received as a respected prophetess in early tradition,



Huldah: A Cunning Career Woman? 113
male role. When women behave like this, their strength is seen as arrogance, their pride as haughtiness, and their eminence as presumptuousness.34 As a consequence Huldah receives an ugly, hateful name: a ‘weasel’.35 Yet, what does this really mean and why is ‘weasel’ considered an ugly name? This question is based on two assumptions. First, considering the fact that rabbinic writings such as this one were popular in character and oriented towards the daily life of those who were adhering to it, we must assume that such a depiction of the prophetess must have yielded specific connotations. What would these connotations be and what would a member of an audience or a reader of later times think of Huldah, the weasel? Second, these connotations would in turn influence the audience’s/readers’ understanding of Huldah and possibly bring in new meanings of the biblical story. If so, what would these understandings be? In order to answer these questions, I will proceed in three steps. First, I will examine the characterization of weasels within the rabbinic literature. Second, I will look into the Greco-Roman world and some specific texts that will help illuminate the image of weasels further, and, third, I will briefly return to rabbinic texts and end this inquiry with a new perspective on Huldah’s story in the Hebrew Bible.
3.1.2 Weasel: The Animal in the Rabbinic Sources There are two specific features of weasels discussed in the rabbinic sources which I wish to point out as relevant for the later readership’s understanding of the prophetess Huldah. These features are weasels’ cunning, and the peculiar view on weasels’ sexual reproduction.36 In the first volume of Ginzberg’s Legends of the Jews, the weasel is mentioned in a context of the account of creation: ‘[f]or every species on land, excepting
indicates that Huldah was understood to be a true prophet of Yahweh. See Edelman ‘Huldah the Prophet’, 242. 34 See also Baskin, Midrashic Women, 31–2. 35 The name Huldah is by all probability derived from the Hebrew root חלד , which is, in one of its two meanings, rendered as ‘dig, hollow out’. The Hebrew noun ḥolēd, referred to in Lev 11:29, is to be derived from the same root. The animal spoken of is probably a weasel, a mole or even a rat. See K.M. Beyse,’ ֶחלֶ ד cheledh’, TDOT 4: 397–9. For the animal in Lev. 11, see J. Milgrom, Leviticus 1–16: A New Translation with Introduction and Commentary, Anchor Bible 3, New York 1991, 671. The Greek translation of the word in the Septuagint is γαλῆ. 36 Rabbinic writings are here represented by Ginzberg’s collection.



114 Scheuer
only the weasel, there is a corresponding species in the water.’37 The reason for this exception seems to be explained later on in the text:
The weasel is a cunning animal that learned from the fox. When God decided to send the Angel of Death to kill the animals by throwing them into the sea, the weasel tricked the Angel of Death into thinking that a representative of his kind has already been thrown into the sea. Therefore neither fox nor weasel is “represented in the water, while all other animals are.”38
Thus, the weasel, just as the fox who taught him the trick, used their cunning in order to escape the death sentence upon them, thereby tricking God himself. The other aspect of the animal taken up in rabbinic sources is connected to the way weasels procreate. In the same volume, in a context of the aftermath of the great flood, the text describes an incident where Noah curses ravens because a raven in the ark spoke spitefully against him. Noah asked the raven to go out and check the water level on earth. The raven refused to do so because he was convinced that Noah wanted him dead so he could take advantage of Mrs. Raven. Insulted by this appalling notion, Noah cursed the raven with an unnatural procreation:
“May thy mouth, which has spoken evil against me, be accursed, and thy intercourse with thy wife be only through it.” All the animals in the ark said Amen. And this is the reason why a mass of spittle runs from the mouth of the male raven into the mouth of the female during the act of copulation, and only thus the female is impregnated. Altogether the raven is an unattractive animal.39
In a footnote to this text in volume five, Ginzberg explains this peculiarity further:
37 Ginzberg, Legends of the Jews, vol. 1, Bible Times and Characters: From the Creation to Jacob, Philadelphia 1988, 26. 38 Ginzberg Legends vol. 1, 40–41. Here the text speaks of a fox and a cat, yet in the footnote to this story Ginzberg states: ‘According to ancient sources (com. note 115), it is the weasel, which lives on the dry land, and if we want to be accurate, we ought to read “weasel” instead of “cat” in the text.’ L. Ginzberg, The Legends of the Jews, vol. 5, Notes to volumes I and II: from the Creation to the Exodus, Philadelphia 1987, 57, n. 188. 39 Ginzberg, Legends vol. 1, 38–39.



Huldah: A Cunning Career Woman? 115
According to Barnabas, 10.8, it is the weasel which is impregnated through the mouth. This, however, inaccurately reproduces the statement of Aristeas 165, according to which the Bible has prohibited the enjoyment of this animal because it is impregnated through the ears and gives birth through the mouth. This widespread view is also mentioned by Aristotle in De Generatione Animalium, III, 6.5 who, however, scoffs at it.40
Even though the rabbis did not always keep their animals in order, the idea of the sexual oddity and repulsiveness of weasels is clearly demonstrated through the view on their unnatural manner of procreation: weasels are impregnated through the mouth/ears, and give birth through the mouth. These two main peculiarities of weasels – their remarkable cunning and sexual abnormality – are not generic to rabbinic texts but can be found in Greek mythology as well, in which, in all probability, the rabbinic stories about weasels originated.41 To these we now turn.
3.2 Greek Mythology
The link between women and weasels is ancient. In his extensive study of the subject, classicist Maurizio Bettini shows the width of popular belief and tradition connecting women and weasels throughout history, arguing that it all starts with the myth of Heracles’ birth.42
3.2.1 The Myth of Heracles’ Birth and the Story of Alcmena and the Weasel In Homer’s Iliad, we read about the circumstances of the great hero Heracles’ birth. Heracles is a child of Zeus and his earthly mistress Alcmena. The birth of the hero is as dramatic as could be expected. Dense with passion, strong emotions, and a great deal of pain, the story discloses the threat of death to the
40 Ginzberg, Legends vol. 5, 55, n. 177. 41 Jewish exegetes in the Hellenistic world were well aware of Greek traditions and mythology as well as of the ways to interpret it, in particular in Alexandria where Homer’s epics were studied. See M.R. Niehoff, Jewish Exegesis and Homeric Scholarship in Alexandria, Cambridge 2011. For the extent of the influence of Greek thought upon the view on women in post-biblical Judaism see, T. Frymer-Kensky, In the Wake of Goddesses: Women, Culture and the Biblical Transformation of Pagan Myth, New York 1992, 203–12. 42 M. Bettini, Women & Weasels: Mythologies of Birth in Ancient Greece and Rome, Chicago, London 2013. I am not going to attempt to establish a generic connection between this particular myth and the rabbinic text on Huldah. Rather, I see the connection as a strong influence of motifs and ideas which were well developed in different traditions of the Greco-Roman world.



116 Scheuer
unborn child and to a mother in labor. The story of agonized Alcmene about to give birth is a story that emerges from different accounts, meaning it does not exist in one complete version.43 For the purposes of this paper, I present the Roman poet Ovid’s later version of the story from his Metamorphoses, translated by Bettini. The story is narrated by Alcmene herself. As she was to give birth she encountered divine opposition. Because Alcmene was impregnated by Zeus, the goddess Hera, angry and jealous, instructed the goddess of childbirth, Ilithyia, to prevent the child from being born.44 As Alcmene endures the agony of hindered labor, our weasel-to-be, Galanthis, enters the scene:
One of the helpers (una ministrarum) came to me, a woman from the common folk, Galanthis, with fair hair, prompt in carrying out orders and whose services were much appreciated. She realized that this was happening because of immortal Juno, going frequently in and out of the room she saw the goddess seated on the altar, with her crossed fingers upon her knees. “Whoever you are”, she said to the goddess, “rejoice with my mistress! Alcmena of Argos has given birth and can now fulfill the vow she made as an expectant mother”. Lucina, the goddess of the womb, then leaped to her feet and, shocked, she opened the hands which she had been holding clasped together; meanwhile, my bonds were loosened, and I was freed of my burden. They say Galanthis started to laugh since she had deceived one of the gods. But the cruel goddess grabbed her by the hair as she was still laughing, and pulled her down and then kept her from getting back up again: her arms turned into paws. She still has her former zeal, and her color has not changed, but her shape is different from before: And because she used her deceitful mouth to help a woman give birth to a child, she gives birth through the mouth, and she continues to inhabit our houses, just like before.45
As a punishment for tricking the goddess, Galanthis was to be transformed into a weasel and would thus procreate through her cunning mouth. This story presents weasels as animals so deceptive that even gods can be tricked by
43 Bettini, Women & Weasels, 25, 45. 44 In this paper, Greek names for gods (Zeus, Hera, Eileithyia/Ilithyia, Heracles) are used, while Ovid uses corresponding Roman names (Jupiter, Juno, Lucina, Hercules). 45 The text is cited from M. Huldah: A Cunning Career Woman? 117
them, and so repulsive that they give birth through their mouths. Analyzing the story, Bettini shows how the weasel is used to characterize a cultural model of a laughing, cunning girl:
This young woman is sharp and canny, as the weasel is described in ancient fables and folklore. Ovid thus brings to life before our eyes a little weasel, swift and sure of itself, going in and out of ‘our’ houses (although that ‘our’ makes clear the cultural distance that divides us from Ovid), a creature who, oddly enough, supposedly gives birth through the mouth.46
Generally speaking, the idea of giving birth through the mouth is considered by most cultures of the time to be ‘unclean’, or at least unpleasant and repulsive. However, and what is interesting for the study of Huldah in such stories, this fact does not carry only negative connotations. In Greco-Roman tradition this idea of giving birth through the mouth is used also as a metaphor for knowledge, speech, and/or evidence of an interpretative mind. A couple of examples illustrate this fact. First, the Greek philosopher Plutarch, in his treatise on the myth and mysteries of Isis and Osiris, says that the oddity of the sexual reproduction of the weasel symbolizes knowledge which enters in through the ears and comes out through the mouth: ‘For as to the weasel, many still think and say that as it is impregnated through the ear and brings forth by the mouth, it is a likeness of the birth of reason (logos).’47 Second, in his elaborations on the theory on Evemerus and the question of the true nature of gods, Plutarch comments on the tradition about the Greek god Hermes: ‘For instance, the Egyptians tell us that Hermes had a short-armed body’.48 The expression ‘short-armed’ reads in
46 Bettini, ‘Laughing Weasels’, 211. 47 I am using here the older English translation by Mead simply because it is easily accessible. I am well aware of Brian P. Copenhaver’s warning for Mead’s ‘theosophical motivations’, although I doubt any such motivations will have impact on the particular texts I am referring to. See G.R.S. Mead, ‘Concerning the Worship of Animals, and Totemism’, in: Thrice-Greatest Hermes: Studies in Hellenistic Theosophy and Gnosis, Vol. 2-Sermons, London 1906, 356 (LXXIV:3). Accessed 10 June 2014. Available online: http://www.gnosis .org/library/grs-mead/TGH-v1/th140.html. Plutarch discusses here the Egyptian animal worship, questions of taboo and totemism. Mead ‘Concerning the Theory of Evemerus’, Thrice-Greatest Hermes: Studies in Hellenistic Theosophy and Gnosis, Vol. 1-Prolegomena, London 1906, 295. Available online:



118 Scheuer
Greek γαλι-άγκωνα, which can be translated as ‘weasel-armed’. In his comment on this text, Mead writes: ‘Now, as we are told further on (lxxiv. 3) that the weasel (γαλῆ), or marten, was fabled to conceive through the ear and bring forth through the mouth, this animal was evidently a symbol of mindconception. “Weasel-armed” may thus symbolise some faculty of the interpretative mind (Hermes).’49 Thus, Greek texts and traditions perceive weasels in the following manner: First, the characteristics invested in weasels (always female), i.e. confidence, efficiency, velocity and cunning, are used by her in order to trick a goddess or a god and thus save a life – her own or someone else’s. Second, the sexual oddity of the weasel is expressed by their procreation through the mouth. This idea once conceived lives a life of its own. The conception through (usually) the ear and the birth through the mouth symbolize the birth of reason (logos), of knowledge, and the expression of the interpretative mind. It is a divine ability to proclaim a divine message.
3.3 Back to the Rabbinic Readings and Audience
We have already seen that the rabbinic reading characterizes weasels as exceptionally cunning, generally distasteful, and sexually odd. The question is whether the symbolism of knowledge and divine inspiration which is found in Greek traditions have further influenced rabbinic understanding of the story of Huldah in 2 Kgs 22 and 2 Chron. 34. In rabbinic writings from Hellenistic contexts, there are traces of similar thinking concerning the symbolism of the weasels’ odd procreation. In the Letter of Aristeas, the High Priest elaborates on the reason and logic of the Mosaic food laws,50 according to which weasels are declared unclean and are forbidden to be eaten:
For you must not fall into the degrading idea that it was out of regard to mice and weasels and other such things that Moses drew up his laws with
http://www.sacred-texts.com/gno/th1/th127.htm#fr_1000., 295. See also J. Gwyn Griffiths, Plutarch’s de Iside et Osiride, Cambridge 1970, 375.
49 Mead, ‘Concerning the Theory of Evemerus’, 295, fn 3. For the explanation of such popular belief about weasels’ sexual reproduction, see Bettini, Women & Weasels, 99–100. 50 For the close analysis of the narrative see T. Rajak, Translation and Survival: The Greek Bible of the Ancient Jewish Diaspora, Oxford 2009, 30–34.



Huldah: A Cunning Career Woman? 119
such exceeding care. All these ordinances were made for the sake of righteousness to aid the quest for virtue and the perfecting of character.51
The argument holds that the prohibition against eating weasels is philosophically and morally relevant. The weasels are declared unclean because they stand for a metaphor of hearing the word through the ear and speaking the word through the mouth. Interestingly, while Plutarch used the metaphor in a positive manner as a symbol of divine inspiration, Aristeas saw a symbolism of evil speech:
The species of weasel is unique: Apart from the aforementioned characteristic, it has another polluting feature, that of conceiving through its ears and producing its young through its mouth. So for this reason any similar feature in men is unclean; men who hear anything and give physical expression to it by word of mouth, thus embroiling other people in evil, commit no ordinary act of uncleanliness, and are themselves completely defiled with the taint of impiety. Your king’s action in removing such people – for so we are informed – is amply justified.52
However, there are examples in the rabbinic writing where the metaphor of procreation through the mouth is used in a positive sense. In rabbinic discussions on the question of male infertility, rabbis write that those among rabbinic men who cannot procreate for any reason (i.e. cannot have children) could instead procreate symbolically, through ‘insemination of Torah-knowledge and the production of disciples.’53 Knowledge of Torah is conceived through the ear and can be brought forth through the mouth in the form of rabbinic teaching which in turn will produce disciples: ‘Torah production, then, is a cultural equivalent of physical reproduction.’54
51 Aristeas 165–166, available online: http://www.ccel.org/c/charles/otpseudepig/aristeas. htm. Accessed 10 June 2014. See also R.J.H. Shutt, ‘Letter of Aristeas (Third Century B.C.First Century A.D.): A New Translation and Introduction’, in: J.H. Charlesworth (ed.), The Old Testament Pseudepigrapha, Vol. 2: Expansions of the “Old Testament” and Legends, Wisdom and Philosophical Literature, Prayers, Psalms, and Odes, Fragments of Lost JudeoHellenistic Works, London 1985, 7–34. 52 Shutt, ‘Letter of Aristeas’, 23. 53 Baskin, Midrashic Women, 139.
54 H. Eilberg-Schwartz, ‘The Nakedness of a Woman’s Voice, the Pleasure in a Man’s Mouth: An Oral History of Ancient Judaism’, in: H. Eilberg-Schwartz, W. Doniger (eds), Off with Her Head! The Denial of Women’s Identity in Myth, Religion, and Culture, Berkeley/Los



120 Scheuer
It is fair to assume that the Hellenistic cultural heritage and symbolic connotations connected to weasels would have been fairly well known to Jewish readers during the centuries around the turn of the Common Era. Connecting Huldah the prophetess to the weasels would trigger associations which were widespread in the Hellenistic and Jewish world. The early audience would understand the prophetess Huldah to be a clever woman who was cunning enough to trick Yahweh, and whose sexual oddity could be understood as divine inspiration aimed at the production of knowledge, and thereby possibly disciples. With these connotations in mind, I now turn back to the biblical text and ask: How would Huldah’s story in the Hebrew Bible be read and understood through these cultural lenses?
4 2 Kings 22 Once More
Huldah the prophetess seems to have been a highly successful career woman acting in a pivotal time in the history of Judah. In Megillah 14b, her professional accomplishments have been put aside on account of her gender.55 Being called a weasel is the punishment for stepping out of her gender-prescribed role in society. Interestingly, it seems as if this explicitly negative characterization of Huldah’s name in rabbinic writings was surpassed, and possibly suppressed by the cultural connotations connected to weasels in the Hellenistic world. Although unintended by the rabbis, the audience of rabbinic texts and teachings might have found another image of Huldah: Huldah as the weasel. I will illustrate this by exploring a new potential reading of the story of Huldah in 2 Kgs 22.
4.1 Cunning
In the same manner as the weasel of rabbinic writings having to face the Angel of Death, Huldah too had a threat of death to deal with. The death she faced as
Angeles 1995, 172. See further H. Eilberg-Schwartz, The Savage in Judaism: An Anthropology of Israelite Religion and Ancient Judaism, Bloomington 1990, 232–4.
55 Thus Daniel and Cathy Skidmore-Hess write: ‘Perhaps the most amazing aspect of these Talmudic criticisms of these prophetesses is that they elicit no counter-argument, critical discussion, or opposing viewpoint – an absence truly remarkable in a text that is admirably and justly famous for its typically lengthy debate and interpretive creativity’, D. Skidmore-Hess and C. Skidmore-Hess, ‘Dousing the Fiery Woman: The Diminishing of the Prophetess Deborah’, Shofar: An Interdisciplinary Journal of Jewish Studies 31, 9; available online: http://muse.jhu.edu/journals/shofar/v031/31.1.skidmore-hess.html. Accessed 10 June 2014.



Huldah: A Cunning Career Woman? 121
she took the book in her hands was double sided. On the one hand, she herself was a part of the people she confirmed would be destroyed. On the other hand, King Josiah, who hoped for her compassion and prophetic ability to change things for the better, was a man powerful enough to have her killed, or at least to destroy her career, if he did not like what she had to say. What was she to do? What would the reader/the audience do? It is hardly an overstatement to say that her answer was canny: it will happen – but not in your time, O King! In other words, it will not happen in her time either. She too used her cunning, just as the weasel used hers to avoid the violent death hovering upon her, either through the judgment of God or by the hand of the king. In a like manner, Huldah’s cunning can be seen in the much debated ambiguity of her last words to Josiah: ‘Therefore, I will gather you to your fathers and you will be gathered to your grave in peace; your eyes shall not see the disaster that I will bring on this place’ (2 Kgs 22:20). The biblical redactors, as well as the audience, knew that Josiah died violently at Megiddo (2 Kgs 23:28–30). What exactly did her words mean? Is the king going to die in peace or is he going to be buried in peace? Could he die a violent death and then be buried in peace? Was Huldah a false prophet? The scholarly discussion of our time has been dominated by these very questions, which seem to have found a fairly convincing solution in the redaction critical outlines of the textual history of 2 Kgs 22. The problem of unfulfilled prophecy might yet be resolved in a different manner. Being collected to one’s grave in peace might be an expression of a cunning answer to a tricky and potentially dangerous question. The judgment will not happen in your lifetime, O King, you will die before it starts, and you will be buried as a king, in your grave, not elsewhere, you will be properly mourned, your bones will receive the royal rest – before the judgment comes. There was no escape route for the people, but there was one for the king. Such a prophetic assurance might very well have been the sign the king needed in order to gather strength and meet the mighty Egyptian army at Meggido. In other words, Huldah gave an ambiguous but agreeable answer to the king; it was an answer that left Josiah with the assurance that his final destination as a king – his royal grave – was to be a peaceful one. But the path to it was in his hands really.56
56 Given the fact that connotations connected to the weasels were a part of the cultural milieu long before the Hellenistic period and must have been known to the editors and redactors of the Book of Kings – and the Huldah episode in particular – it is not unlikely that the redactors of the Huldah text understood her prophecy as a particularly clever one. For sure, the risk of interpreting Huldah as the only false prophet of the Hebrew Bible would not escape their keen eye. Even so, the weasel connection brings in a new



122 Scheuer
4.2 Sexual Oddity
The sexual oddity of the weasels might have found a point of recognition in the fact that Huldah was understood to be out of her place. Instead of caring for her husband and her children, she was moving in a man’s world, operating on reason and speech – methods that were understood to be typically masculine.57 Now, as we have seen, sexual oddity, conceiving through the mouth, or rather ear, and giving birth through the mouth has both positive and negative connotations. The word – good or bad – is heard through the ear, is processed, and then spoken through the mouth. The analogy to the prophetic office is obvious: ‘The Lord God has given me the tongue of those who are taught, that I may know how to sustain the weary through the word. Morning by morning he wakens my ear to listen as those who are taught. The Lord has opened my ear, and I was not rebellious, I did not turn backward’ (Isa. 50:4–5). Like a weaselarmed divine messenger, Huldah received the word from Yahweh and passed it on to King Josiah: ‘Thus says Yahweh.’ The rabbis acknowledge this through their criticism of her gender, not of her prophetic message. Interestingly, while attempting to diminish Huldah’s person through the eroticization of her mouth to the image of a weasel, the rabbis achieved the opposite and made Huldah into a woman who procreated through knowledge of Torah – a highly esteemed form of cultural reproduction, yet reserved for men exclusively.58 The commanding silence about Huldah in the Hebrew Bible might be a part of an attempt to keep her barren. The identification with weasels, though, made her abundantly fruitful: impregnated by the Word of Yahweh, Huldah, the woman prophet completely outside of her gender prescribed role, delivered through her mouth a message which on the one hand gave the king peace and, on the other, gave him strength to act as a great leader who had nothing to lose.
possibility of interpretation. I will explore these issues further, together with a study of connotations connected to Deborah’s name in my current work on Prophesying Weasels and Judging Wasps: Hebrew Bible Career Women in Early Rabbinic and Christian Sources. 57 H. Eilberg-Schwartz, ‘Introduction: The Spectacle of the Female Head’, in: EilbergSchwartz, Doniger (eds.), Off with Her Head!, 1. The sexual oddity of Huldah is also introduced through her lineage. In rabbinic texts she is a descendent of Rahab the Harlot. Tal Ilan argues that such lineage is a deliberate act through which the rabbis further discredit Huldah: ‘They cannot rid themselves of the notion that all women in the public eye, as righteous as they might be, are in some way always prostitutes (cf. b. Megillah 15a).’ Ilan, ‘Huldah, the Deuteronomic Prophetess’, 9. 58 See Eilberg-Schwartz, The Nakedness, 172, who shows that women in the rabbinic view were ‘excluded from the genealogy of knowledge’. Huldah seems thus to be an unintended exception. The audience could have hardly missed this irony.



Huldah: A Cunning Career Woman? 123
5 Conclusion
In Meggilah 14b the prophetess Huldah was rebuked and punished with an ugly name: a ‘weasel’. The connotations raised by the weasel-reference brought up images which were widely spread in the Hellenistic world. In both Greek and Jewish texts, two major connotations come to the fore: cunning and sexual oddity. Both these aspects influenced the later readership’s views on Huldah. Given the historical circumstances within the Josiah narrative, only a weasel-prophetess could be clever enough to protect, in one oracle, the integrity of both the deity, Yahweh, and the messianic king, Josiah. At the same time, the image of a weasel-prophetess seems to do away with the exegetical crux raised in biblical scholarship. Through her cunning, Huldah authorized the first Scripture and gave the king what he wanted, all while saving her career and her life. Through her oddity in gender and in agency, Huldah created posterity for herself. She might be content knowing that her few words preserved in splendid isolation in the Hebrew Bible have been transmitted throughout history, puzzling the minds of women and men for hundreds of years after their first delivery. In Huldah, therefore, later readership could see a woman whose ugly name turned out to be a disguise for an impressive legacy.
