Rewriting the Past in Light of the Present: The Stories of the Prophet Jeremiah
Matthijs J. de Jong
1 Introduction
Studies of the narrative sections in the Book of Jeremiah usually start with the source model of Mowinckel, then turn to the redaction-critical models of Thiel, Pohlmann, Stipp, Albertz, and others, and finally add some new insights to the discussion. This contribution assumes a quite different starting-point, taking as its basis the words that are on a small lead strip, found at the site of the Oracle of Dodona in Ancient Greece:
God . . . Good Luck. About possessions and about a place to live: whether (it would be) better for him and his children and his wife in Kroton? (Side A) In Kroton. (Side B)1
A man, whose name remains unknown, asked the deity, Zeus of Dodona, a question. Would he and his family be better off in Kroton? On the reverse of the strip we find the clear answer he received from the god he consulted.2 The main point of this article is that we know what divination was about, and that we know what prophecy was about, and that it is time to use this knowledge to guide our critical analysis of the biblical prophetic books. For too long the exegetical study of the biblical prophetic books has been dominated by an ‘inner logic’, instead of being governed by an outwardly directed perspective. Studying the development of these texts without a proper
1 Text and translation: E. Eidinow, Oracles, Curses, and Risk among the Ancient Greeks, Oxford 2007, 76, no. 5. 2 Of the many lamellae (lead strips) found at the site of the oracle at Dodona, only a portion has been published (some 200 out of some 1,100), dating from 6th to 3rd cent BCE. All lamellae contain a private petition, a question posed to the deity that requires a clear and straightforward answer. In those cases where the answer has been written on the reverse, the answer is indeed straightforward. See F.G. Naerebout and K. Beerden, ‘ “Gods cannot tell Lies”: Riddling and Ancient Greek Divination’, in: J. Kwapisz, D. Petrain, M. Szymanski (eds), The Muse at Play. Riddles and Wordplay in Greek and Latin Poetry, Berlin 2012, 130–32.



Rewriting the Past in Light of the Present 125
historically informed view of what prophecy entailed in the ancient world, is, in my view, like walking down a dead-end alley. The logic of divination on the other hand can function as a helpful outwardly directed perspective for improving the study of biblical prophetic literature.
2 Prophecy and Divination
2.1 The Function of Divination
The function of divination was to reduce uncertainty in life. Divination aimed to provide divine guidance when needed, that is, in uncertain situations, both private and public.
[D]ivination is the human action of production – by means of evocation or observation and recognition – and subsequent interpretation of signs attributed to the supernatural. These signs can be anything which the supernatural is perceived to place in the world with the intention to communicate, whether evoked or unprovoked, whether visible, auditory, tactile, olfactory or gustatory: in all cases the individual must recognize a sign as coming from the supernatural in order to consider it as a divinatory sign. Once this has occurred, the signs need to be interpreted whether this task is straightforward or difficult. This (culturally specific) interpretation produces a clear message.3 [T]he function of divination is to diminish uncertainty and although divination is future oriented, it is also concerned with past and present.4
Divination did not provide one with a blueprint of the future, but it diminished the fear of making the wrong choice which would lead to a negative future. It was, in other words, divine guidance to help one take the right course of action. No guarantees were given, of course. The anonymous man planning to go to Kroton was not guaranteed a happy and peaceful life. Perhaps he would anger a deity next day and lose a leg. Nobody knew. He only knew that at this very moment the god advised him to do as he had planned: to go to Kroton, and he most certainly felt reassured. Divination played a constructive, reassuring role in ancient society. It gave certainty to those in doubt in the face of a range of possible options. With
3 K. Beerden, Worlds Full of Signs: Ancient Greek Divination in Context (Religions in the GraecoRoman World, 176), Leiden 2013, 20. 4 Beerden, Worlds Full of Signs, 21.



126 de Jong
respect to political decision making, divination supported the stability of the state. In the words of Joel Sweek:
In the ancient Near East, as in many cultures, divination may be described as an assemblage of methods for ascertaining hidden knowledge necessary for the proper functioning of society. (. . .) the inquiry of diviners participates in the society’s central concern: the effort to secure the order and peace vital for its survival.5
2.2 Prophecy as a Form of Divination
This description applies to prophecy as well, which was, as we now realise, a form of divination.6 Prophets were supposed to take a mediating position between god and man, and the oracles they delivered were supposed to reflect decisions taken by the gods. In the ancient Near East we see prophets at work especially during times of national emergency, that is during wars, political crises and power conflicts. We see four basic types of message. (1) Prophets gave encouragement in times of emergency. Through the oracles, the gods gave the king and his people a declaration of their support. They granted their help, and promised victory and the destruction of the enemies. (2) Gods also used prophets to present their claims. We find divine claims for offerings, food for a banquet, and for property. After having given support, the gods expected generous gifts from the king. Furthermore, prophets could criticize the activities of the king and remind him of his duties.7 The gods were believed to control the king, and they could do so through the prophets. Prophetic oracles therefore sometimes contained reproaches directed against kings who failed to fulfil their duties. The purpose of such reproaches was to receive compensation for this neglect.8
5 J. Sweek, ‘Inquiring for the State in the Ancient Near East: Delineating Political Location’, in: L. Ciraolo and J. Seidel (eds), Magic and Divination in the Ancient World, Leiden 2002, 44. 6 M. Nissinen, ‘What is Prophecy? An Ancient Near Eastern Perspective’, in: J. Kaltner and L. Stulman (eds), Inspired Speech: Prophecy in the Ancient Near East, Essays in Honor of H.B. Huffmon (JSOTS, 378) London 2004, 21–22. See M.J. de Jong, Isaiah among the Ancient Near Eastern Prophets. A Comparative Study of the Earliest Stage of the Isaiah Tradition and the NeoAssyrian Prophecies (VTS, 117) Leiden 2007, 287–318, for a general description with literature. 7 See in particular M. Nissinen, ‘Das kritische Potential in der altorientalischen Prophetie’, in M. Köckert and M. Nissinen (eds), Propheten in Mari, Assyrien und Israel (FRLANT, 201) Göttingen 2003), 1–32. 8 For examples, see Nissinen, ‘Das kritische Potential’, 1–32; De Jong, Isaiah among the Ancient Near Eastern Prophets, 307–313.



Rewriting the Past in Light of the Present 127
(3) Prophets, in their role as guardians of the well-being of the state, a role they shared with the other diviners, could harshly denounce persons whom they perceived as posing a threat to the well-being of the state. This concerned not only external, but also internal enemies: people from within society whose actions or plans posed a threat to the well-being of the state. (4) A fourth function of the prophets was to announce the occurrence of disasters planned by the gods, with the purpose of averting them.9 In such cases, a god, it was believed, was angry because of some neglect or misconduct, and planned revenge. The announcement of this threat aimed to avert its outcome. If the right action was undertaken (a ritual, a prayer), the angry god(s) would be appeased and the evil averted. By announcing disasters, prophets did not stand in opposition to their society, but served the interest of king and state. If the predicted outcome was successfully averted, this did not make the prophecy false. On the contrary, that was the whole point of the divinatory intervention. To summarize, prophecy was part of a broader structure of divination and functioned within the existing order. Prophets spoke and acted for the benefit of social and cosmic stability. They were not necessarily pro-king (as the interest of the cosmic and social-political order could transcend the interests of an individual king), but they were always pro-state.10 No ancient Near Eastern prophet rejected the institution of kingship or announced the unconditional divine repudiation of his own state or society.11 Given the function of prophecy as part of the larger structure of divination this would have been unthinkable. We know the function of divination, as we know the logic behind it. And we should make use of this in our study of biblical prophecy. But first we must take one further step.
9 Negative apodoses to omens are formulated in the same way, ‘some disaster is going to happen’, but likewise with the purpose of averting the disaster, by performing an apotropaic ritual. Diviners were to ascertain whether an unfavourable consequence was foreshadowed, so that if this were the case, it could be averted. See S. Maul, ‘How the Babylonians Protected Themselves against Calamities Announced by Omens’, in T. Abusch and K. van der Toorn (eds), Mesopotamian Magic. Textual, Historical, and Interpretative Perspective, Groningen 1999, 123–129. For this aspect of prophecy in the context of divination, see L.-S. Tiemeyer, ‘Prophecy as a Way of Cancelling Prophecy – The Strategic Uses of Foreknowledge’, ZAW 117 (2005), 329–350. 10 Diviners were not passive ‘yes-men’ automatically supportive of the rulers. For the latitude and limitations of diviners, including the prophets, within the Mesopotamian societies, see Sweek, ‘Inquiring for the State’, 41–56. 11 Nissinen, ‘Das kritische Potential’, 30.



128 de Jong
3 Prophecy in Literary Guise
When looking at the evidence from Mesopotamia and Ancient Greece, we find a clear difference between the practice of prophecy and other forms of divination on the one hand, and the way in which all of this is depicted in literary texts on the other.
3.1 Mesopotamia
For the Mesopotamian sources, this has not yet been studied systematically. However, it is clear that a process of ‘literary rewriting of prophecy’ has taken place.12 Examining the existing evidence, one can say that the examples relatively close to the actual oracles are concise, concrete messages, dealing with one issue, and of immediate relevance to the addressee, whereas the oracles in literary guise are extended, embellished, and broadened in time frame and scope. Although the ipsissima verba of the prophets are forever lost, I suggest that it is in the letters in which oracles are reported that we may get as close as we can hope to get to the original messages. And what we see, both in letters from Mari and from Neo-Assyrian times, is that the oracles reported in letters are concise, concrete, deal with one issue and are of immediate relevance to the addressee. A few examples may suffice here.13
(1) Oracle reported by Inib-šina (ARM 26 197)
The peacemaking of the man of Ešnunna is false: beneath the straw water runs! I will gather him into the net that I knot. I will destroy his city and I will ruin his wealth, which comes from time immemorial.
(2) Oracles reported by Mar-Issar (SAA 10 352)
12 M. Nissinen, ‘How Prophecy became Literature’, SJOT 19 (2005), 153–72; B. PongratzLeisten, Herrschaftswissen in Mesopotamien. Formen der Kommunikation zwischen Gott und König im 2. und 1. Jahrtausend v. Chr. (SAAS, 10) Helsinki 1999; De Jong, Isaiah among the Ancient Near Eastern Prophets, 395–442. See also J. Stökl, Prophecy in the Ancient Near East. A Philological and Sociological Comparison (CHANE, 56) Leiden 2012, 131–141.
13 The three examples quoted are taken from M. Nissinen e.a., Prophets and Prophecy in the Ancient Near East (SBLWAW, 12) Atlanta 2003, 7 (ARM 26 197), 164–165 (SAA 10 352), 171 (SAA 16 59).



Rewriting the Past in Light of the Present 129
You will take over the kingship. ... I have revealed the thieving polecat of my lord and placed it in your hands.
(3) Oracle reported by Nabû-reḫtu-uṣur (SAA 16 59)
This is the word of Nusku: The kingship is for Sasî! I will destroy the name and the seed of Sennacherib!
In the literary reworking of oracles, of which we find clear examples in the neo-Assyrian sources, the oracles were not only extended and embellished. We also see that the time frame is broadened. The situational perspective of the actual oracles makes way for an episodic perspective. The scope is broadened, and generalized. Various scholars, such as Martti Nissinen and Beate PongratzLeisten, have written on this, and earlier, I too discussed several examples of literary prophecy in detail.14 The developments we discern can be explained from the ex eventu factor. Afterwards, the course of events is clear. In hindsight it is evident what the gods in fact foresaw when they gave their advice and declared their support. In retrospect, everything is presented as if it happened by divine necessity: it had to go that way. Furthermore, the principle of ancient historiography that the winner takes all was applied in the reworking of prophecy too. To give one example: in the depiction after the event, the war between Assurbanipal and his brother Šamaš-šum-ukin is not described as a strife with an uncertain ending between two powerful men, but as a war between a god-fearing supreme king destined to win, and a sinful, godless contester, doomed to lose. It is all a black-andwhite situation, of life for Assurbanipal and death for Šamaš-šum-ukin. The whole course of events, including the divinatory practice that had guided it, was afterwards rewritten from the knowledge of the outcome, and from the perspective of the winner.
3.2 Greece
When we turn to ancient Greek sources, we find a similar picture. Here we find an even greater difference between the actual oracles on the one hand and the
14 See literature mentioned in note 12.



130 de Jong
way they are depicted in literary sources, such as in the Histories of Herodotus, on the other. This has been shown in several recent studies.15 The oracular standard people have in mind is heavily coloured by the famous examples of oracles from Delphi as presented in the Histories of Herodotus. However, these ‘oracles’ are clothed in a literary guise. If we forget for one moment what Herodotus writes and look at the epigraphical evidence and the Athenian law-court speeches, it becomes clear that the basic form of enquiries put to the oracle of Apollo was of this type: ‘Would it be more profitable and better for us to . . . .’.16 I will briefly mention one example, which is fully discussed by Hugh Bowden. When the Persian campaign against Greece started in 481 BCE, the Athenians realized that the Persians were determined to destroy their city, out of revenge for their part in the Ionian revolt and the battle of Marathon. So surrender was not an option, and the Athenians planned to leave their city and to rely on their fleet. Abandoning the city in the certain knowledge that it was going to be destroyed was not only a hard decision, but it also meant abandoning the temples of the gods, and this could not be done without divine permission. A consultation of the Delphic oracle therefore was necessary.17 The usual form of query put to the Delphic oracle was ‘Would it be better and more profitable for us to do so-and-so?’ This was very much like the kind of question put to the oracle of Dodona. The questions normally received a straightforward answer.18 The Athenians thus asked the god: ‘Would it be better and more profitable for us to abandon our city, and take to the ships?’19 In response, they received the divine approval for doing what seemed best to them: to embark on the ships and leave their city. Apart from a general probability that the question put to the oracle was of this type, there are several more specific indications supporting this view. One later author, referring to earlier, fourth-century BCE accounts, states that ‘the oracles said that it was more profitable for the Athenians to leave their
15 Naerebout and Beerden, “Gods cannot tell Lies”, 121–147 and H. Bowden, Classical Athens and the Delphic Oracle. Divination and Democracy, Cambridge 2005.
16 Bowden, Classical Athens and the Delphic Oracle, 23–24 and 33: ‘a man asked a woman a question, and the woman answered. All the other aspects of Delphi, including the literary tradition that grew up about the oracle, serve, deliberately or not, to obscure this central simplicity’.
17 Bowden, Classical Athens and the Delphic Oracle, 102–107. 18 Bowden, Classical Athens and the Delphic Oracle, 22. 19 Bowden, Classical Athens and the Delphic Oracle, 103.



Rewriting the Past in Light of the Present 131
native land and embark on their ships.’20 Furthermore, an analogy with the city of Delphi also points in this direction: somewhat later, with the advance of the Persian army, the citizens of Delphi found themselves in a similar situation. Preparing to abandon their city, they asked Apollo’s permission to depart (and to leave the temple treasures largely unprotected), to which Apollo gave his consent. To the Athenians, two options were left: either to flee or to fight at sea. The Athenians chose the second, undoubtedly supported by good portents and divine approval. Their decision to fight the Persians at sea was to become a crucial moment in the Persian war. They defeated the Persian naval forces at the sea battle at Salamis. This victory was one of the major events that eventually changed the course of the Persian campaign for the worse. In the Histories of Herodotus (Histories VII–VIII), written some decades after the events, the answer of Apollo of Delphi is not simply a ‘yes’ any more, but now consists of two pieces in hexameters of twelve lines each. In these texts, the whole sequence of events – involving the abandonment and subsequent destruction of Athens, the Greek reliance on the ships, and the victory at sea at Salamis – is referred to. As often in Herodotus the prophecy functions as a plot-driver. Cited at the beginning of the episode, the predictions are fulfilled one by one as the story unfolds.21 Herodotus furthermore attributes the right understanding of the so-called prophecy to Themistocles, the hero of this episode. Herodotus presents the whole episode from the particular perspective of hindsight, namely to illustrate that the Persian defeat was according to the will of the gods, and was achieved by the bravery of the Athenians lead by Themistocles. Herodotus used these two pieces of a literary oracle as the kernel around which he masterfully wove his story. For a long time, commentators have suspected that this literary oracle is an ex eventu piece of work, and they are likely to be right. However, it seems unlikely that Herodotus himself invented it. Hugh Bowden now offers a fruitful suggestion: it may well have been the oracle of Delphi itself that, after the events, published this written prophecy, not as a fraud, of course, but in order to show what Apollo in fact foresaw correctly when he gave his consent to the Athenians to abandon their city and to rely on their fleet. Afterwards, it was clear that all had happened by divine necessity, and afterwards, the true implications of Apollo’s ‘yes’ could be explained and extended. And this was done,
20 Aelian, On the characteristics of Animals 12.35; see Bowden, Classical Athens and the Delphic Oracle, 104.
21 The term plot-driver comes from R. Stoneman, The Ancient Oracles: Making the Gods Speak, New Haven 2011.



132 de Jong
says Bowden, by the Delphic oracle itself, in order to demonstrate the authority of the oracle – a form of ancient advertisement.
3.3 Conclusion
Both in the Mesopotamian and in the Greek sources we find a similar phenomenon: after the events there followed a rewriting of prophecy. After it has become clear what the gods implied with their message, the message could be spelled out and extended. Thus we see a clear distinction between ‘prophecy in practice’ and ‘prophecy in literature’. With this distinction in mind, we will look at the Jeremiah stories.
4 Stories of Jeremiah
4.1 Two Different Perspectives
I will focus on the narrative material concerning Jeremiah that relates to the reign of Zedekiah (i.e. Jer. 20:1–6, 21:1–10, 27–29, 34:1–6, and 37–38). In these stories, we find two different messages with regard to the fate of Judah and Jerusalem, both attributed to Jeremiah. The dominant line states that the destruction of Judah and Jerusalem is inevitable. Yahweh is determined to let this happen, due to his anger with the wickedness of the people. Nothing can be done to avert this threatening fate. This is demonstrated by Jer. 37:10.22
Even if you defeated the whole army of Chaldeans who are fighting against you, and there remained of them only wounded men in their tents, they would rise up and burn this city with fire.
Yet, there are also clear traces of a different prophetic message, namely that Judah’s survival was possible; it depended on accepting Babylonia’s supremacy. According to this message, resistance to the Babylonians was against Yahweh’s will: it would bring death and destruction. The disasters, however, could be prevented. Accepting Babylonia’s supremacy was acting in accordance with Yahweh’s will, and would lead to survival. This message is found in several chapters:
Chapter 27: ‘Serve the king of Babylon and live’ (27:11, 12, 17) Chapter 21 and 38: ‘Surrender to the Babylonians and live’ (21:9, 38:2) Chapter 29: ‘In Babylon’s welfare you will find your welfare’ (29:7)
22 Biblical texts are cited here according to the NRSV.



Rewriting the Past in Light of the Present 133
Chapter 34: If you submit to the Babylonians, you, i.e. Zedekiah, shall die in peace and receive royal burial honors (implied by 34:4–5)23 Chapter 38: ‘If you, i.e. Zedekiah, surrender to the Babylonians, your life will be spared and this city will not be burned down; you and your family will live.’ (38:17)
We encounter two different perspectives on the destruction of Jerusalem, both attributed to Jeremiah.24 According to the one, Jerusalem’s destruction is something that can and should be prevented by taking the right course of action. According to the other, due to Yahweh’s decision Jerusalem has to be destroyed as a divine necessity. The first message depicts Jeremiah as operating ‘pro-society’, seeking Judah’s survival; the second portrays him as a classical prophet, a mouthpiece of Yahweh’s irrevocable ‘No!’ to his people.25 Various commentators have noted these two different perspectives. Some of them, such as Christopher Seitz, and William McKane, have even defended the redaction-critical position that in my view is the right one,26 namely that the earliest stratum presents Jeremiah as aiming at the survival of Jerusalem and Judah (Seitz calls this the Scribal Chronicle), whereas a reworking of this, now the dominant line in the stories, has given a decisive twist to it (Seitz calls this the Exilic redaction). Although I consider these studies as of great importance, I want to press a different argument here. The first part of this contribution provides a perfect
23 For these verses, see C.R. Seitz, Theology in Conflict: Reactions to the Exile in the Book of Jeremiah (BZAW, 176), Berlin 1989, 250–51. 24 Seitz offers an analysis of these two perspectives, distinguishing between a ‘Scribal Chronicle’ and an ‘Exilic Redaction’, Seitz, Theology in Conflict, 203–291, esp. 239, 246, 250, 262, 290. 25 These two perspectives cannot be harmonized by the suggestion that the former was Jeremiah’s initial message that at some stage was overruled by the latter, since 38:17–18 presents Jeremiah at the brink of the Babylonian takeover as still delivering the message that Jerusalem’s destruction can be prevented (see also Seitz, Theology in Conflict, 239). Moreover, in other passages Jeremiah is portrayed as a spokesman of Yahweh’s irrevocable ‘No!’ from the very outset of his prophetic career. 26 My position basically resembles Seitz, Theology in Conflict. Seitz however does not deal with the divinatory context, and his position on Jeremiah’s preaching before 598 BCE is in my view unconvincing. Other authors have noticed the two different perspectives within the Jeremiah material too: W. McKane, A Critical and Exegetical Commentary on Jeremiah (2 vols; ICC) Edinburgh 1986–1996, 2:875–877, 965–966, 971, and R.P. Carroll, Jeremiah: A Commentary (OTL), London 1986, 641–43.



134 de Jong
explanation for the two different perspectives in the stories. One of them mirrors actual prophetic activity, the other is another example of the ex eventu rewriting of prophecy. The message that the people should accept Babylonia’s supremacy in order to survive portrays Jeremiah as a prophetic figure striving for the survival of his society. He neither foretold nor intended the destruction of his country. Instead, he acted as was expected of a diviner: he revealed the divine will with regard to a matter of major importance in order to provide the information necessary for the political decision-makers to act wisely to secure the common welfare. The second perspective results from a reworking. At a later stage, the messages attributed to Jeremiah were modified, and Jeremiah was turned into a mouthpiece of the inevitable destruction of Judah and Jerusalem. In this way, he became a figure foretelling total destruction that could not be averted. This later reworking of the earlier oracles corresponds to the reworking of prophecy we see in Assyria and Greece.
4.2 Prophetic Messages in a Historical Situation
Let us first have a closer look at the earliest traditions that present Jeremiah as a figure supporting his society. This tradition is preserved as some sort of substratum in the narratives.27 The traces that remain relate to the reign of Zedekiah, partly to the time before the revolt against Babylonia,28 and partly to the years of the revolt and the siege of Jerusalem (in 588–586 BCE).29
(1) The early tradition as preserved in chs. 27–29 relates to the time before the revolt. The earliest parts of these chapters – as argued by various critical studies30 – present a coherent picture.31
27 Jer. 20:1–6*, 21:9, 27–29*, 34:4–5*, 37–38*. 28 Jer. 28:1 mentions the fourth year of Zedekiah’s reign (circa 594 BCE).
29 For the chronology, O. Lipschits, The Fall and Rise of Jerusalem: Judah under Babylonian Rule, Winona Lake 2005, 72–75. 30 A nucleus of these chapters may have consisted of Jer. 27:2–4.11, 28:2–4*.11, 13–15* and 29:1.3–7*, 25–28*. For this reconstruction, see McKane, Jeremiah, 2:695–708, 716725, 735–748; A. Graupner, Auftrag und Geschick des Propheten Jeremia. Literarische Eigenart, Herkunft und Intention vordeuteronomistischer Prosa im Jeremiabuch (BiblischTheologische Studien, 15), Neukirchen-Vluyn 1991, 61–97; F.L. Hossfeld und I. Meyer, Prophet gegen Prophet. Eine Analyse der alttestamentlichen Texte zum Thema: Wahre und falsche Prophetie (BibB, 9), Freibourg 1973, 90–111. Seitz, Theology in Conflict, 208–214. 31 See for this episode, see M.J. de Jong, ‘The Fallacy of “True and False” in Prophecy Illustrated by Jer 28:8–9’, JHS 12/10 (2012), 1–29.



Rewriting the Past in Light of the Present 135
First, the message not to revolt against Babylonia is symbolically acted out by Jeremiah, by a yoke he put on himself (27:2–4, 11). Jeremiah, as ordered by Yahweh, walks around with a yoke of straps and bars on his neck, and publicly delivers the following accompanying message: ‘Bring your necks under the yoke of the king of Babylon, and serve him and his people, and live.’ This is his message for Zedekiah and the people of Judah. He has a similar message for the royal envoys from Edom, Moab, Ammon, Tyre and Sidon, that were gathered in Jerusalem at the time: ‘Any nation that will bring its neck under the yoke of the king of Babylon and serve him, I will leave on its own land, says the LORD, to till it and live there.’ The idea is, that Yahweh has granted the Babylonians supremacy, and that submission to them leads to survival and well-being. In response to Jeremiah’s oracle, the prophet Hananiah delivers a contradictory oracle. He announces that Yahweh will break the yoke of the Babylonian king. Hananiah takes Jeremiah’s yoke, and breaks it, saying: ‘Thus says the LORD: This is how I will break the yoke of King Nebuchadnezzar of Babylon from the neck of all the nations within two years’ (28:11). Jeremiah, in turn, counters Hananiah’s oracle, by proclaiming: ‘Thus says the LORD: You have broken wooden bars only to forge iron bars in place of them!’ And with respect to Hananiah, he announces: ‘Within this year, you will be dead’ (28:13, 16). Subsequently, in ch. 29, we find a letter from Jeremiah to the Judean community in Babylonia. In this letter, he informs them of the following oracle:
4 Thus says the LORD (. . .) 5 Build houses and live in them; plant gardens and eat what they produce. 6 Take wives and have sons and daughters; take wives for your sons, and give your daughters in marriage (. . .), multiply there, and do not decrease. 7 And seek the welfare of the city where I have sent you into exile, and pray to the LORD on its behalf, for in its welfare you will find your welfare.
This letter receives a furious reaction from Shemaiah, one of the Judeans in Babylonia (29:25–28*); he wants Jeremiah to be silenced. And once again, Jeremiah declares that it is the word of Yahweh that Shemaiah will die. These traditions form a coherent picture: Jeremiah announces, in word and deed, that Babylonia will prosper and stay in power. Judah’s chances of survival therefore depend on accepting Babylonia’s supremacy. For the Judean community in Babylonia this means that they must find their well-being in Babylonia’s well-being.



136 de Jong
The message is countered, in both cases. Hananiah breaks the yoke and delivers a contradictory message and Shemaiah writes a furious response to Jeremiah’s letter. In both cases, an oracle spoken by Jeremiah, announcing a terrible personal fate for his opponent, concludes the scene.32
(2) Now, let us turn to the early tradition that relates to the time of the revolt and the siege of Jerusalem, found in chs. 37–38.33 The earliest parts of these chapters (37:11–21 and 38:14–28, and to some degree in 38:1–13) as argued by various critical studies,34 confirm the picture we already had already seen. These stories about the final stage of Judah’s monarchy depict Jeremiah as announcing that surrender to the Babylonians will lead to survival. Even during the revolt Jeremiah urged the king to surrender to the Babylonians in order to stay alive, and to save Jerusalem from destruction, in Jer. 38:17:
Thus says the LORD, the God of hosts, the God of Israel, If you will only surrender to the officials of the king of Babylon, then your life shall be spared, and this city shall not be burned with fire, and you and your house shall live.
But the message was rejected. In these stories, Jeremiah is treated as a traitor, an enemy of the state. But let us put this into the right, historical, perspective. Many of the Judeans in fact regarded submission to Babylonia as the better choice.35 Many of them had fled to the area of Benjamin, where the Babylonians probably already had installed a seat of government in Mizpah with Gedaliah as their appointee.36 These Judeans had in fact acted upon Jeremiah’s message: surrender to the Babylonians in order to survive. When Jerusalem was under siege, it was isolated from the rest of the country. Those remaining in the city, led by the king and the political leaders, persisted in the revolt. It was these particular circumstances that put Jeremiah in a dangerous position. From the perspective of the revolters, he was an enemy
32 Just as in Jer. 20:1–6*. 33 And furthermore in Jer. 21:8–10* and 34:4–5*. 34 McKane, Jeremiah, 2:932–945, 962–971; Lipschits, Fall and Rise, 76–77, 93–94, 316–325; Seitz, Theology in Conflict, 238–241, 272–73. 35 During the first revolt (599–597 BCE), after Jehoiachin had ascended the throne, the surrender to the Babylonians had secured the survival of the state and the royal house. During the second revolt (589–586 BCE), Jeremiah’s messages supported those who tried to achieve a similar result. For a reconstruction, see Lipschits, Fall and Rise, 70–88. 36 Lipschits, Fall and Rise, 88–97.



Rewriting the Past in Light of the Present 137
of the state, because his message undermined the belief in the success of the revolt. This period was the cradle of the stories about the imprisonment and the attempts to kill Jeremiah.37 Here the image of Jeremiah as a suffering figure probably has a historical basis. At a later stage, this was taken as paradigmatic for Jeremiah’s ministry as a whole. Ex eventu, he became portrayed as a figure against society, whose adversaries not only were the leaders of the revolt, but Judah’s society at large. In historical terms, however, part of Judah’s population, including members from the highest circles such as Gedaliah, had heeded Jeremiah’s message.38 This is the picture as presented by the early Jeremiah tradition.39 I do not want to claim that this picture is purely historical. I do want to claim, however, that the early tradition probably mirrors actual prophetic activity during the final years of Judah’s political existence. At that time, the main issue was whether to submit to Babylonia’s supremacy or to resist. Both options had their advocates among the Judean leadership, and both had religious implications. Resisting Babylonia needed the divine assurance that Yahweh would protect his people against the enemy threat, whereas submitting needed divine permission. Jeremiah’s messages were immediately relevant to the actual situation, as they provided the divine consent for submission. Jeremiah revealed that Yahweh had granted the Babylonians supremacy, so that to act according to Yahweh’s will was to accept the Babylonian rule. From this perspective, the real threat to Judah’s well-being did not come from the Babylonians, but from those Judeans propagating resistance contrary to Yahweh’s will. Before the revolt, Jeremiah urged Zedekiah and the Judeans not to rebel; for the Judean community in Babylonia, his message was: ‘You will find well-being in Babylonia’s well-being’. During the revolt, his message to the people was: Leave Jerusalem, surrender to the Babylonians, and you will stay alive; for the Jerusalem leadership it was: Hand over the city to the Babylonians in order to prevent death and destruction. These messages had an immediate relevance, as did all oracles in the ancient world. Jeremiah acted as a prophetic figure in support of society, consistent with the practice of ancient divination.40
37 Seitz, Theology in Conflict, 256–257; Lipschits, Fall and Rise, 76–77, 93–94, 316–325. 38 E.g. Lipschits, Fall and Rise, 77, 84–88, 94. 39 The same message again occurs in the Gedaliah story (Jer. 40:7–41:18), in which Jeremiah does not play a role. Gedaliah repeats Jeremiah’s message, as part of an oath (Jer. 40:9), ‘Do not be afraid to serve the Babylonians. Stay in the land and serve the king of Babylon, and it shall go well with you.’ For the Gedaliah episode, R. Albertz, Israel in Exile. The History and Literature of the Sixth Century B.C.E. (trans. David Green) Atlanta 2003, 4–7. 40 De Jong, Isaiah among the Ancient Near Eastern Prophets, 308–313.



138 de Jong
4.3 The ex eventu Reworking
The destruction of Jerusalem proved that Judah’s leaders had made the wrong decision. Afterwards, it was strongly felt, at least in some circles, that Jeremiah’s message had been true and should have been acted upon, and Jeremiah was vindicated as the spokesman of the true word of Yahweh. At this stage, the prophecies attributed to Jeremiah were of course understood from the new reality. After the downfall had become a fact, the oracles and stories of Jeremiah acquired a new meaning. Originally his message was intended to prevent the downfall, as we may safely conclude from our insight into the logic of divination. Afterwards however, those who guarded the Jeremiah tradition believed they saw the true meaning of Jeremiah’s words, namely that the fall was something that had happened not by mistake or by misfortune, but by divine necessity because of the disobedience of Jeremiah’s contemporaries, who had refused to listen to him. The simple line, ‘submit to the king of Babylonia, and live’, acquired a second layer: but because you refused to do so, Yahweh has decided to destroy you. This is the logic as found in ch. 38:17–23, where 22–23 in fact anticipate the wrong choice by Zedekiah. Similarly, and more clearly, this is the logic in ch. 42, where the ‘leftovers’ of Judah are compared with the inhabitants of Jerusalem, with regard to the choice they have: submit to Babylonia and live. But since they do not obey, and have decided to go to Egypt, they will be destroyed by Yahweh. All this is part of the same ‘prophecy’, clearly consisting of two layers. The explanation for the destruction was: Yahweh had decided to punish his people because of their sinfulness. This motif, ‘due to divine anger’, was, in fact, the typical ancient Near Eastern explanation for historical calamities.41 So the earlier prophecies were rewritten, from the fact of the destruction, and from the insight that it was a divine punishment for the sins of the people. The reason why the prophecies were rewritten is easy to see. In their original form, they had become irrelevant. Their true meaning, from the ex eventupoint of view, had to be made explicit. The exilic reworking of the Jeremiah tradition pointed out that Judah’s society had failed in its entirety, including the prophets and priests. Judah as a whole was destroyed, because Judah as a whole had sinned. It was furthermore
41 In the ancient Near East ‘calamities are conceived as divinely contrived punishments’; K. van der Toorn, Sin and Sanction in Israel and Mesopotamia: A Comparative Study (SSN, 21), Assen 1985, 56. For an example, see M.J. de Jong, ‘Biblical Prophecy – A Scribal Enterprise: The Old Testament Prophecy of Unconditional Judgement Considered as a Literary Phenomenon’, VT 61 (2011), 55–56.



Rewriting the Past in Light of the Present 139
emphasized that Yahweh time and again had announced this to happen, and Jeremiah became portrayed as the mouthpiece of this irrevocable punishment. Not only was the message rewritten, but also the messenger. From a prophetic figure aiming at Judah’s survival, Jeremiah became the proclaimer of the inevitable destruction of his society in its totality. At the same time, Jeremiah’s opponents were reframed as liars and deceivers. The uncertainty of the present, where one prophetic message was contradicted by the other, where one could only hope to have made the right choice, all this made way for a clear-cut, black-and-white certainty: Jeremiah was the only true mouthpiece of Yahweh, his opponents, now ‘the prophets’ as a collectivity, were simply deceiving liars. It was all crystal clear, and Jeremiah’s contemporaries were seen as ignorant because of their lack of understanding, and evil, because of their refusal to listen to Jeremiah’s words.42
5 Conclusion
Prophecy as divination had a direct relevance to the current situation, and often took the form of divine guidance vis-à-vis a proposed course of action. The oracles provided the guidance the recipients needed in order to confidently take the course of action that seemed the best way to victory or survival. The oracles were an indispensable part of the political decision making. The sources also confront us with a literary kind of prophecy. Literary prophecies are marked by an episodic perspective instead of the situational perspective of actual prophecy, and by a black-and-white perspective of good and bad. Once the outcome is known, the uncertainty that surrounds all human decisions has gone. In retrospect, Assurbanipal was described as the beloved of the gods, whose position could not be threatened; Šamaš-šum-ukin was merely a wicked criminal, whose terrible fate, sealed beforehand, was turned into an example for any future enemy. After the victory at Salamis it was clear that the Athenians’ decision to abandon the city and rely on the fleet had been the right one. By then it was
42 During the exilic period a textual complex began to take shape that may be called the written Jeremiah-tradition. Scribes incorporated existing materials, such as the ‘early traditions’ presented earlier. M.J. de Jong, ‘Why Jeremiah is not among the Prophets: an analysis of the terms נביא and נביאם in the Book of Jeremiah’, JSOT 35 (2011), 500–508; K. van der Toorn, Scribal Culture and the Making of the Hebrew Bible, Cambridge MA 2007, 188–194.



140 de Jong
clear what Apollo had foreseen when he granted the Athenians permission to leave their city. For Herodotus, Themistocles was the hero: thanks to him, the Athenian fleet had been enlarged earlier on, and he had been the successful leader of the battle at sea. For this reason, Herodotus depicted Themistocles as the only one who before the events had understood the right meaning of the ‘prophecy’ from Delphi. Those taking an opposite view are depicted as a bunch of poor interpreters and cowards. After the event, everything is described as having happened with divine necessity. The literary prophecies bear relevance for later readers, instead of the original addressees of the message. The reworking of prophecy was not seen as manipulation or fraud, but simply as the making explicit of what was already implicit in the original oracles. The outcome of the events, it was held, had already been seen by the gods, and it happened as they decided. In one important respect the rewriting of Jeremiah’s oracles differed from the rewriting of the Assyrian and Greek oracles. In contrast with the latter two, the divine guidance provided by Jeremiah was not heeded by Judah’s main political decision-makers. The message was proved right in the end, but what his message was designed to prevent did happen: Jerusalem was destroyed and the state of Judah abolished. This required a twist in the literary version of Jeremiah’s message. The divine foreknowledge allegedly implied in Jeremiah’s oracles was the insight that the divine guidance would not be heeded, and that, for this reason, Jerusalem would not survive, and the state of Judah would come to an end. The literary version thus included the notion of rejection of the message, and its fatal consequences. But to call this a twist is, of course, our modern, critical perspective. For those who kept alive the Jeremiah tradition it was not a twist at all. For them it was crystal clear, what from the outset had been the true meaning of Jeremiah’s oracles, and they diligently spelled out this true meaning in the expanding Jeremiah-corpus. They shaped the image of Jeremiah as a figure prophesying utter doom for a disobedient and evil society, driven by the sincere belief that this was the true meaning and the enduring relevance of the words of God that Jeremiah had spoken.

King Asa and Hanani the Seer: 2 Chronicles 16 as an Example of the Chronicler’s View of Prophets and Prophecy
Pancratius C. Beentjes
1 Introduction
It is beyond any doubt that the author of the Book of Chronicles, henceforth referred to as ‘the Chronicler’, has left his particular mark(s) on the narrative about King Asa as it was found by him in 1 Kings. There are several clues to substantiate this claim.1
• First, whereas 1 Kgs 15:9–24 runs to a total of sixteen verses, the Chronicler’s
account fills no less than forty seven verses (2 Chr 14:1–16:14).2
• Second, the Chronicler’s narrative has a remarkable structure. Whereas
2 Chronicles 14–15 at great length describe the positive aspects of Asa’s kingship over thirty four years, in Chapter 16 – which by the way fills a remarkably short period of seven years –, the negative sides of this king are brought to the fore.
• Third, in 2 Chronicles 16 the Chronicler has adopted the sequence of the
verses as handed down in 1 Kgs 15:17–24. However, already at first glance one can observe that he has remodeled it in order to create a new text of his own, as can be demonstrated in the following chart:
1 This traditional point of view, however, is questioned by a group of scholars putting up a case for the view that Chronicles does not depend on Samuel-Kings, but that both are rewritings of an older common source. And even the question is posed whether or not there might have been an expansion or, what’s more, a re-editing of the Book of Samuel and the Book of Kings in the times of the Chronicler or even afterwards. See, e.g., G. Auld’s contribution to this volume, and U. Becker, H. Bezzel (eds), Rereading the relecture? The Question of (Post)chronistic Influence in the Latest Redactions of the Books of Samuel (FAT 2. Reihe 66), Tübingen 2014.
2 For specific literature relating to the Chronicler’s account on King Asa, see, e.g., R. Dillard, ‘The Reign of Asa (2 Chronicles 14–16): An Example of the Chronicler’s Theological Method’, JETS 23 (1980), 207–218; G.N. Knoppers, ‘ “Yhwh Is Not with Israel”: Alliances as a Topos in Chronicles’, CBQ 58 (1996), 601–626; W. Rudolph, ‘Der Aufbau der Asa-Geschichte (2 Chr XIVXVI)’, VT 2 (1952), 367–371; G. Snyman, ‘ “Tis a Vice To Know Him”. Reader’s Response-Ability and Responsibility in 2 Chroniclers 14–16’, Semeia 77 (1997), 91–113.

2 Chronicles 1 Kings
16:1a —16:1b–6 15:17–22 16:7–12 ——— 15:23 16:13a 15:24a 16:13b–14 —
• Fourth, both in the positive and in the negative part of the Asa narrative the
Chronicler has inserted addresses by prophetic figures who elsewhere in the Hebrew Bible are completely unknown. A prophecy by a certain Azariah son of Oded is found in 2 Chr 15:1–7, whereas in 2 Chr 16:7–10 a seer called Hanani is addressing King Asa. In both of these episodes we come across the Chronicler’s most significant theological signature(s).
2 2 Chronicles 16
For several reasons, the narrative of 2 Chronicles 16 appears to be a good example to demonstrate in what way the Chronicler brings his political and theological intentions to light. More specifically, Hanani’s speech appears to be a fine example of the Chronicler’s method of working and his theology. Attention will be paid to the structure of his address, its context, and some of its connection threads. This episode is structured into three parts:
(1) 16:1–6 The alliance of Asa and Ben-Haddad against Baasha (2) 16:7–10 Disapproval by the seer Hanani (3) 16:11–14 The final years of Asa; peculiarities relating to his funeral
Whereas both the first and the third part can be linked with the Chronicler’s Vorlage (1 Kgs 15:17–22 and 1 Kgs 15:23–24), the middle section is not. Therefore, attention will first be paid to the way the Chronicler has adopted and/or reworked his source material.

• First, the context of both passages is quite different. In 1 Kgs 15:16 it is said
that ‘there was war between Asa and King Baasha of Israel all their days’. The action of Baasha against Ramah as reported in 1 Kgs 15:17 is to be observed therefore as an illustration of it. In 2 Chronicles 16, however, Baasha’s building activities in Ramah mark a completely new episode. Until that very moment, Asa had been free from war: ‘there was no war until the thirty-fifth year of the reign of Asa’ (2 Chr 15:19). According to the Chronicler, the attack of Baasha on Ramah in fact opens the ‘negative’ period of Asa’s reign.
• A second marked feature relates to the Chronicler’s shortening of Asa’s
action towards Ben-Hadad (2 Chr 16:2) as opposed to the one in 1 Kgs 15:1819. Not only all kinds of redundant words, collocations, and phrases have been skipped,4 Ben-Hadad has also been deprived of his patronyms (‘son of Tabrimmon, the son of Hezion’) and the pejorative šoḥad (‘bribe’) from 1 Kgs 15:19 has been left out. And whereas 1 Kgs 15:22 has a quite rigorous message (‘King Asa issued an order to all Judah – no one was exempt – ’), the Chronicler has a more neutral version: ‘King Asa brought all the men of Judah’ (2 Chr 16:6).
• Third, there is a different approach as to geographical specifications. ‘All
Kinnereth in addition to Naphtali’ (1 Kgs 15:20) has been altered into ‘all the store cities of Naphtali’ (2 Chr 16:4). The phrase ‘Baasha withdrew to Tirzah’
4 E.g. ‘all the silver and gold that was left; He entrusted it to his officials’.



146 Beentjes
(1 Kgs 15:21) becomes a more general statement in the Chronicler’s report: ‘Baasha abandoned his work’ (2 Chr 16:4).
And from the point onwards that both passages can be read again in a synoptic view, it strikes the eye that the Hebrew of the Chronicler’s account of 2 Chr 16:11–14 has only twelve words in common with his parent text in 1 Kgs 15:23–24.5
3 Hanani’s Address (2 Chr 16:7–10)
The alliance between Asa of Judah and Ben-Hadad of Aram induced the Chronicler to put the seer Hanani on the scene. This passage is structured by a marked literary feature. Both the opening words (16:7) and the concluding collocation (16:10) create a perfect inclusion: ‘At that time’. It is a well-known fact that for the Chronicler prophetic addresses are the most suitable occasions to present his own theological message. And the words he makes Hanani say are no exception to that. In the Book of Chronicles a total of eighteen prophetic addresses is found, of which no less than fourteen have no parallel in 1–2 Samuel or 1–2 Kings and can therefore be characterized as a creation by the Chronicler himself, a phenomenon which is often referred to with the technical term Sondergut.6 At least ten times these prophetic addresses are put into the mouth of persons who nowhere else in the Hebrew Bible are known as prophet, seer, or man of God.7 In a number of instances they are expressly presented with the help of a special introductory formula of divine inspiration: ‘the spirit clothed . . .’ (1 Chr 12:19; 2 Chr 24:20); ‘the spirit of God came upon . . .’ (2 Chr 15:1; 20:14).
5 ‘the events of Asa’; ‘written in the book of the kings of Judah’; ‘feet’ ‘Asa rested with his ancestors’; ‘the city of David’. 6 Among this category are the narratives relating to the following characters: the prophet Shemaiah (2 Chr 12:5–8), the inspired messenger Azariah (2 Chr 15:1–7), the seer Hanani (2 Chr 16:7–10), the visionary Jehu (2 Chr 19:1–3), the inspired Levite Jahaziel (2 Chr 20:14–17), the prophet Eliezer (2 Chr 20:37), the inspired messenger Zechariah (2 Chr 24:20–21), a man of God (2 Chr 25:7–9), a nameless prophet (2 Chr 25:15–16), the prophet Oded (2 Chr 28:911). For details, see R.K. Duke, The Persuasive Appeal of the Chronicler: A Rhetorical Analysis (JSOTSS 88), Sheffield 1990, 175–176 (‘List 8: Prophetic Speech Material’). 7 J. Kegler, ‘Prophetengestalten im Deuteronomistischen Geschichtswerk und in den Chronikbüchern. Ein Beitrag zur Kompositions- und Redaktionsgeschichte der Chronikbücher’, ZAW 105 (1993), 481–497; here: 487 (Tafel 4).



King Asa and Hanani the Seer 147
The majority of the prophets and inspired messengers we meet in the Book of Chronicles have been ‘invented’ by the Chronicler and should therefore be characterized as ‘literary characters’ rather than historical persons. Consequently, the speeches delivered by these literary personages are the most appropriate place to look for the Chronicler’s own theological convictions and emphases. One will hardly wonder that it is in precisely these prophetic addresses that fundamental theological notions of 1–2 Chronicles are to be found.8 There might have been two grounds why the Chronicler has put an unknown seer on the scene. The first one relates to the immediate context of 1 Kings 15, which was the Chronicler’s Vorlage. For in 1 Kgs 16:1 and 16:7 one comes across the phrase ‘The word of YHWH came to Jehu son of Hanani against Baasha’. The second one most probably is the seer’s name, a shortened form of Hananiah that serves as a kind of theological substratum: ‘YHWH has taken pity’.9 The verb šʿn (‘to rely on’) which occurs no less than three times within Hanani’s address serves as a guiding principle and obviously dominates these verse lines. The seer opens with an accusation to Asa of Judah: ‘Because you relied on the king of Aram, and did not rely on YHWH your God’, that is followed by a statement referring to the consequence of this behaviour: ‘Therefore, the army of the king of Aram has escaped from your hand’ (16:7). A rhetorical question – ‘Were not the Cushites and Libyans a mighty army with great numbers of chariots and horsemen? – serves as an historical retrospective (2 Chr 14:8–12), and is explicitly followed by the central theme of Hanani’s address: ‘Yet when you relied on YHWH, he delivered them into your hand’. In the Hebrew text, parallelism and antithesis have been modeled in a magnificent way. The three statements in which the verb šʿn occurs form a tight unity. The first and third occurrence have exactly the same verbal form (
בהשענך , ‘in your relying on’); the first time it is followed by ‘the king of Aram’, the third time by ‘YHWH your God’). The first occurrence is additionally highlighted, since it is followed by an antithetical parallelism: ‘because you did not rely on YHWH your God’. It is obvious that the Chronicler with the help of the verb šʿn expresses a specific theological program, since the same verb is further used only in the
8 For an analysis of the prophetic address in 2 Chr 15:1–7, see P.C. Beentjes, ‘Prophets in the Book of Chronicles’, in J.C. de Moor (ed.), The Elusive Prophet. The Prophet as a Historical Person, Literary Character & Anonymous Artist (OTS 45), Leiden 2001, 45–53.
9 M. Noth, Die israelitischen Personennamen (BWANT 3/10), Stuttgart 1928 (repr. Hildesheim 1966), 187.

Beentjes
immediate context (2 Chr 13:18; 14:10), as well as in the same wording: ‘to rely on YHWH’. There also is an antithetic parallelism with the noun ידך , ‘your hand’:
– ‘The army of the king of Aram has escaped your hand’ (16:7b); – ‘YHWH has given the Cushites and the Libyans into your hand’ (16:8b).10
Subsequent to the rhetorical question (v. 8a) and the ‘conveyance formula’ (v. 8b),11 the next element of the historical review (v. 9a) is quite intriguing. For the Chronicler makes the seer Hanani utter a statement – ‘For the eyes of YHWH run to and fro throughout the whole earth’ – that is similar to Zech 4:10b – ‘the eyes of YHWH which run to and fro throughout the whole earth’.12
2 Chr 16:9
כי יהוה עיניו משטטות בכל־ארץ עיני יהוה המה משטטות בכל־ארץ Zech 4:10b
The quotation from Zechariah is followed by what can be characterized as the Chronicler’s own vocabulary.13 The phrase ‘to strengthen those whose heart is true to Him’, in the first place is clearly linked to 2 Chr 15:17b – ‘the heart of Asa was true all his days’ which belongs to the positive section of Asa’s reign.14 It is followed by an accusation and an announcement of judgment. Here a similar pattern is found as was the case in the first half of 16:9. The accusation (‘you have done foolishly in this’) immediately recalls the one by Samuel to King Saul (1 Sam 13:13), using not only the same rare verb סמל niph., but even the same conjugation. The announcement of judgment (‘for from now on you will have wars’), on the other hand, relates to a topic that is characteristic of the Book of Chronicles.15

King Asa and Hanani the Seer 149
However, it is not only the twofold alternation of a quotation from/reference to Scripture on the one hand and the Chronicler’s own theological idiom and framework on the other hand that mark Hanani’s speech. In addition, this twofold alternation functions as a perfect mirror image of a major topic that dominates the positive section of Asa’s reign:
2 Chr 14:5b ‘The kingdom had rest under him’ (cf. 13:23b);16 2 Chr 15:19b ‘There was no war until the thirty-fifth year of Asa’.
4 The Reaction of King Asa
King Asa’s response to Hanani’s address (16:10) is completely different from the king’s earlier positive reaction to Azariah’s prophecy (15:2–7). Whereas in the latter case ‘he took courage, and put away the abominable idols from all the land . . . and repaired the altar of YHWH . . .’ (15:8), he now orders to put Hanani into prison (literally ‘the house of the stocks’, בית מהפכת ). Elsewhere in the Hebrew Bible, this rare noun (‘stocks’) only occurs relating to the prophet Jeremiah (Jer 20:2–3; 29:26). One cannot get away from the impression that the Chronicler with the help of this very rare noun wanted to bring about a subtle reference to that prophet and his fate. The phrase ‘for he was in a rage with him because of this’ at the end of the Hanani episode (16:10b) is rather tautological as compared to the opening words of this verse: ‘Then Asa was angry with the seer’. The final words of the verse are said to be even cryptic: ‘And Asa inflicted cruelties on some of the people at that time’.17 Most probably the purport of this latter phrase will be that Hanai was not the only victim of King Asa.
16 In the book of Chronicles the verb נוח , ‘to rest’, is only used in the Chronicler’s Sondergut. An extensive analysis of the ‘rest’-motif is found in R. Braun, ‘Solomon, the Chosen Temple Builder: The Significance of 1 Chronicles 22, 28, and 29 for the Theology of Chronicles’, JBL 95 (1976) 581–590, esp. 582–586. 17 The text critical note in BHS referring to this is directly taken from W. Rudolph, Chronikbücher (HAT 21), Tübingen 1955, 248: ‘und weil man deshalb im Volk über ihn ungehalten war’. However, this emendation is neither supported by any Hebrew manuscript, nor by any of the versions.



150
Beentjes
5 The Summary of the Reign
The extensive account of King Asa’s reign (2 Chr 16:11–14) holds some salient details. The opening phrase contains a formula – ‘The first and last things’, viz. from beginning to end – that has been used several times before (1 Chr 29:29; 2 Chr 9:29; 12:15). In each of these three passages a prophet or seer was mentioned being the source of the records of the preceding narrative(s).18 Though the Chronicler has introduced two divine messengers to King Asa – Azariah in 2 Chr 15:2–7; Hanani in 2 Chr 16:7–10 – neither of them is mentioned in 2 Chr 16:11 as the source. Instead, for the first time in the Book of Chronicles there is a reference to ‘the Books of the Kings of Judah and Israel’, a formula that from now on will return rather frequently (2 Chr 25:26; 27:7; 28:26; 32:32; 35:27; 36:8). Considering that the Chronicler systematically intends to omit as many facts as possible relating to the Northern Kingdom, with respect to 2 Chr 16:11a the only inference can be that the author here refers to a source he had at his disposal which he wanted to respect. For if it would concern a formula of his own, he no doubt would have omitted ‘and Israel’.19 And what’s more, on several occasions he even calls this source ‘the Book of the Kings of Israel’ (1 Chr 9:1.2; 2 Chr 20:34; 33:18). Whether this might have been a document more or less identical to 1–2 Kings is not self-evident, since the Chronicler on many occasions in his work has many more details than what we know from the canonical books of 1–2 Kings. Towards the end of his reign ‘Asa was diseased in his feet’ (2 Chr 16:12). There has been much speculation as to the nature of this disease. Was he a bad walker? Did he become gangrenous? Or does it refer to a venereal disease? In the Hebrew Bible, from time to time the noun ‘feet’ is used euphemistically for genitals (Ex 4:25; Isa 6:2; 7:20; 2 Kgs 18:27 [qere]). The Chronicler’s text has no further details other than ‘his disease became severe’. Maybe this statement is to be interpreted with a hidden meaning. For the Chronicler has used here the collocation
עד־למעלה , ‘to a considerable extent’, an expression that in the Hebrew Bible is exclusive to 2 Chronicles (2 Chr 16:12; 17:12; 26:8). The negative context in which it has been used in 2 Chr 16:12 offers at least the possibility that the Chronicler – although this is both grammatically and ety
18 Samuel, Nathan, Gad in 1 Chr 29:29; Nathan, Ahijah, Iddo in 2 Chr 9:29; Shemaiah, Iddo in 2 Chr 12:15. 19 For a detailed overview, see H.G.M. Williamson, Israel in the Book of Chronicles, Cambridge 1977, esp. 102–110.



King Asa and Hanani the Seer 151
mologically incorrect – wanted to suggest a link with the verb מעל , ‘to be sinful’, ‘to transgress’, which is such a dominant theme in his work.20 The phrase ‘yet even in his disease he did not seek YHWH, but sought help from physicians’ (2 Chr 16:12b) might give rise to severe misunderstandings. The Chronicler links the disease of Asa and the fact that the king even in that situation did not come to realize the need to seek YHWH. The collocation ‘to seek YHWH’ occurs more than thirty times in the Book of Chronicles as a way to evaluate someone’s religious behavior.21 In itself it is no negative criticism that Asa has sought help from physicians. Sir 38:1–15, for instance, is a clear statement that there is no opposition between God and a physician. The same point of view is also documented in Rabbinic literature, among others in the first tractate of the Babylonian Talmud (Berakhot 60a). To the Chronicler, however, the central issue was that Asa sought only the physicians and kept himself apart from his God, who himself is characterized as a physician (Ex 15:26). Asa therefore is to blame not to have recognized the role and place of his God. The final verse (2 Chr 16:14) contains the most extensive description of a royal funeral in ancient Israel. On the basis of Jer 34:5 and 2 Chr 21:19 one can say with certainty that the report of King Asa’s funeral is a true reproduction of the usual royal funerary ritual.
6 Final Remark
Considering that in the Chronicler’s days not Hebrew, but Aramaic was everyday speech, the name of Asa gets a curious side effect. For in Aramaic the noun אסא has two different meanings: ‘physician’, and ‘myrtle’.22 We cannot exclude the possibility that the Chronicler in a subtle way has included these two echoes in his narrative.23
20 W. Johnstone, ‘Guilt and Atonement: The Theme of 1 and 2 Chronicles’, in: J.D. Martin & P.R. Davies (eds.), A Word in Season (JSOTSup 42), Sheffield 1986, 113–138; J. Haggai and Zechariah in the Stories of Ezra and 1 Esdras
Bob Becking
1 Introduction
Generally, the Books of Ezra and Nehemiah are not connected with the world of prophecy as has already been observed by Augustine, De Civitate Dei 18:36 ‘Esdras...who is historical rather than prophetical’. This longstanding tradition might be based on some sort of prejudice on two fronts: Ezra and Nehemiah are seen as expressions of a legalistic world view that is difficult to combine with the traditional view on prophecy as an individual expression of a free spirit. The last years have produced a shift in the basic view on both features. Ezra and Nehemiah are no longer seen as dull books presenting a legalistic view on reality.1 On the other hand, prophecy is more and more construed as part of the divinatory continuum.2 I therefore dare to talk about two stories and two prophets from the post-exilic era. The stories are:
(1) The report on the problematic rebuilding of the temple in Jerusalem after the Babylonian Exile as narrated in the biblical book Ezra 3–6, and (2) The relatively free rendition of that same report in the Greek book 1 Esdras.
The two prophets are Haggai and Zechariah who are referred to in both texts. My focal question would be to ask after the role these two prophets play in the
1 See, e.g., B. Becking, Ezra, Nehemiah, and the Construction of Early Jewish Identity (FAT 80), Tübingen 2011; L.S. Fried, Ezra and the Law in History and Tradition, Columbia 2014, 148–169. 2 There exists a wealth of literature on this topic, I confine myself here to F.H. Cryer, Divination in Ancient Israel and Its Near Eastern Environment: A Socio-historical Investigation (JSOT Sup 142), Sheffield 1994; J. Stökl, Prophecy in the Ancient Near East: A Philological and Sociological Comparsion (Culture and History of the Ancient Near East 56), Leiden 2012; S.M. Maul, Die Wahrsagekunst im Alten Orient: Zeichen des Himmels und der Erde, München 2013; M. Nissinen, ‘Prophecy as Construct: Ancient and Modern’, in: R.P. Gordon, H.M. Barstad (ed.), “Thus speaks Ishtar of Arbela”: Prophecy in Israel, Assyria, and Egypt in the Neo-Assyrian Period, Winona Lake 2013, 11–35; A. Lenzi, J. Stökl (eds), Divination, Politics, and Ancient Near Eastern Empires (SBL ANEM 7), Atlanta 2014.



Haggai And Zechariah In The Stories Of Ezra And 1 Esdras 153
development of the plot in both stories, or phrased differently: which function did they have in the coming about of the main narrative programme?3
2 Ezra 3–6
As I have argued elsewhere, Ezra 3–6 narrate the problematic trajectory of the rebuilding of the post-exilic temple in Jerusalem.4 I will not dwell here too much on questions of composition and emergence of the Books of Ezra and Nehemiah, although this is an interesting, but complex scholarly discussion.5 I just lay out my position:6 Nehemiah came first, the person as well as the book – at least its basic layer – are to be dated to the middle of the fifth century. In the Nehemiah story – memoir if you like the term – Ezra is only a minor character. Neh. 8 narrates that Ezra presents the ‘book of the law of Moses’ which is then read aloud by the Levites. Ezra explains the text and invites the people to study the book of the law. Out of this minor character, a pseudepigraphic book came into being, known to us as the Book of Ezra. The book was composed around 400. This time is relevant for several reasons.
(1) The Persians increasingly took an interest in the area of Yehud which after the regained independence of Egypt was on the border of the empire;
3 I will not discuss the narratological or semiotic categories involved here, but only refer to E.J. van Wolde, A Semiotic Analysis of Genesis 2–3: A Semiotic Theory and Method of Analysis Applied to the Story of the Garden of Eden (SSN 25), Assen 1989; M. Bal, Narratology. Introduction to the Theory of Narrative, Toronto 1985; S. Rimmon-Kenan, Narrative Fiction: Contemporary Poetics (second edition), Abingdon 2002.
4 Becking, Ezra, Nehemiah, 1–23; see also D.V. Edelman, The Origins of the ‘Second’ Temple: Persian Imperial Policy and the Rebuilding of Jerusalem, London Oakville 2005; D.V. Edelman, ‘Ezra 1–6 as Idealized Past, in: E. Ben Zvi, D. Edelman, F. Polak (eds), A Palimpsest: Rhetoric, Ideology, Stylistics, and Language Relating to Persian Israel, Piscataway 2009, 47–59.
5 From the abundance of scholarly literature, I only present a selection: C.C. Torrey, Ezra Studies, Chicago 1910; H.G.M. Williamson, Ezra and Nehemiah (OTGu), Sheffield 1987; J. Pakkala, Ezra the Scribe: The Development of Ezra 7–10 and Nehemiah 8 (BZAW 347), Berlin, New York 2004; J.L. Wright, Rebuilding Identity: The Nehemiah Memoir and its Earliest Readers (BZAW 348), Berlin, New York 2004; Edelman, Origins, 151–208; J. Blenkinsopp, Judaism, the First Phase: The Place of Ezra and Nehemiah in the Origins of Judaism, Grand Rapids 2009; R. Rothenbusch, “. . . abgesondert zur Tora Gottes hin”: Ethnische und religiöse Identitäten im Esra/Nehemiabuch (HBS 70), Freiburg, Basel, Wien 2012, 1–246. 6 Following to some degree the insights of Th. Willi, Esra: Der Lehrer Israels (Biblische Gestalten 26), Leipzig 2012.



154 Becking
(2) The Persians ended the ban on the cult that was imposed on the temple of Jerusalem after the defiling murder of Joshua by his brother Johanan in 408. With ‘Ezra’ a new episode in the history of the Second Temple starts.7
The narratives on the return from exile (1–2) and the rebuilding of the temple (3–6) function as a motivating introduction to 7–10. In those final four chapters of the Book of Ezra, the profile of an orderly community is depicted. The lengthy introduction is narratively needed to present the measures as approved by King and God. I therefore do not read Ezra 3–6 as a primary historical source, but as an ideological text that ‘uses’ historical elements to convince the audience of a specific view on temple, cult, and community. For the analysis of a narrative it is necessary to define its main narrative program. This remark is based on the conviction that a narrative text is characterized by a plot or a development of the story from a situation at the beginning of the story to the situation at the end of the story. This development in the story takes place as a result of the actions of the characters/individuals/actants. Ezra 3:1–6 relates the celebration of burnt sacrifices and the feast of Booths at the altar for YHWH in Jerusalem by a group of men who had returned from the exile. In a discursive remark in verse 6 it is noted: “The foundations of the temple of YHWH had not yet been laid, though.” At the end of Ezra 6, three features are narrated. Ezra 6:16 reads: “By the third of the month of Adar, in the sixth year of the reign of king Darius, this house was completed.” Ezra 6:17–18 narrates the dedication of the temple, while Ezra 6:19–22 relates the celebration of the Passover. These observations make clear that two shifts have taken place:
(1) From feast of Booths to Passover (2) From altar to Temple
These shifts are narrated in some fifteen smaller units, all presenting a step toward the end of the plot, or its complication. At the end of chapter 4, Artaxerxes is said to have delivered a decree on the basis of which the work on the house of God in Jerusalem is halt. In the next unit it is narrated:
7 See R. Albertz, ‘The Controversy about Judean versus Israelite Identity and the Persian Government: A new Interpretation of the Bagoses Story (Jewish Antiquities XI.297–301)’, in: O. Lipschits, G.N. Knoppers, M. Oeming (eds), Judah and the Judaeans in the Achaemenid Period: Negotiating Identities in an International Context, Winona Lake 2011, 483–504; S. Ruzicka, Trouble in the West: Egypt and the Persian Empire, 525–332 BCE, Oxford 2012, 35–48.



Haggai And Zechariah In The Stories Of Ezra And 1 Esdras 155
Now the prophets, Haggai the prophet, and Zechariah the son of Iddo, prophesied to the Yehudites who were in Judah and Jerusalem; in the name of the God of Israel they prophesied to them. Then rose up Zerubbabel the son of Shealtiel, and Jeshua the son of Jozadak, and began to build the house of God which is at Jerusalem; and with them were the prophets of God, helping them.8
The contents of the prophecy by Haggai and Zechariah are not revealed. Important is to note that the narrator expressis verbis mentions the source of the prophecy: ‘They prophesied bešum ʾelā yiśrāʾēl (in the name of the God of Israel)’. As a consequence, Zerubbabel takes the lead and restarts the rebuilding of the temple. It is interesting to note that in this building process they are ‘helped’ by the prophets. Two problems need to be discussed.
(1) Are the nebîʾayyāʾ, ‘the prophets’, identical with Haggai and Zechariah, or are they to be construed as a group of religious specialists? Both options can be defended. (2) What is the character of the prophetic help? The Aramaic verb seʾad here as a Participle of the Paʿel – is in Biblical Aramaic only attested at Ezra. 5:2. The verb, however, occurs in various Aramaic dialects.9 Its meaning is ‘to help; to support’. In the Aramaic version of Darius’ Bisitun inscription § 5 and parallels, the text proclaims that Darius became king while ‘Ahuramazda helped me’.10 The main Persian god has applied his heavenly powers in favour of the new king. In the same vein, the leaders in Ezra 5 were supported by the prophetic activity which in my opinion did not include the carrying of bricks, but should have been acts of seeking the divine for good omens.11
8 Ezra 5:1–2; see, e.g., J.M. Myers, Ezra, Nehemiah (AB 14), New York 1965, 43–44; F.C. Fensham, The Books of Ezra and Nehemiah (NICOT), Grand Rapids 1982, 78–79; H.G.M. Williamson, Ezra, Nehemiah (WBC 16), Waco 1985, 75–76; J. Blenkinsopp, EzraNehemiah (OTL), London 1988, 115–17. 9 See DNWSI, 795–96. 10 The phrase is repeated throughout the inscription; text: TADAE C2; see J.C. Greenfield and B. Porten, The Bisitun Inscription of Darius the Great Aramaic Version: Text, Translation and Commentary (Corpus Inscriptionum Iranicarum I, IV), London 1982; A. Kuhrt, The Persian Empire: A Corpus of Sources from the Achaemenid Period, London, New York 2007, 141–157; G. Granerod, ‘By the Favour of Ahuramazda I Am King: On the Promulgation of a Persian Propaganda Text among Babylonians and Judaeans’, JSJ 44 (2013), 455–480. 11 D.J.A. Clines, Ezra, Nehemiah, Esther (NCBC), Basingstoke 1984, 84: “presumably by impressing on the people that neglect of the temple was a token of neglect of God”; Williamson, Ezra, Nehemiah, 70: “Moral, rather than manual support is suggested by 6:14”.



156 Becking
All these activities induced by the prophecy met resistance from the side of the powers that be, Tattenai, the governor beyond the River, and some of his coworkers.12 The new king Darius, however, is presented as having a favourable stand toward the Yehudites and starts a search in the archives of Ecbatana – an action that retards the pace of the story. This element of Dehnung adds to the suspense of the story.13 All’s well that ends well: a document is found in the archives and the rebuilding of the temple is soon finished.14 Later in the story the narrator refers back to the prophetic initiative:
And the elders of the Yehudites were successful in building as a result of the prophecy of Haggai the prophet and Zechariah the son of Iddo. And they finished building according to the command of the God of Israel and the decree of Cyrus, Darius, and Artaxerxes king of Persia.15
The Aramaic preposition b in binbûʾat should be rendered with ‘as a result of’ and stresses the view of the narrator that the builders could not have been successful without the prophetic initiative.16 In short, the role of the prophecy in Ezra 3–6 seems to be rather clear: the prophecy functions as a cross-over point in the development of the plot.17 The resistance against the plans to rebuild the temple seemed to bring the plot of the story into a dead-end-street. As a result of the prophecy – presented as the embodiment of the divine power – the rebuilding is resumed with, as its final effect, that the festival of unleavened bread could be celebrated in the Temple. The prophetic role brought the narrative to its intended aim.
12 Ezra 5:3–5. 13 Rimmon-Kenan, Narrative Fiction, 43–58. 14 Ezra 6:1–5. 15 Ezra 6:14; see, e.g., Myers, Ezra, Nehemiah, 52–53; Fensham, Books of Ezra and Nehemiah, 92–93; Clines, Ezra, Nehemiah, Esther, 95; Williamson, Ezra, Nehemiah, 83–84; see, e.g., Blenkinsopp, Ezra-Nehemiah, 128–31. 16 See D.J.E. Nikolaishen, ‘The Restoration of Israel by God’s Word in Three Episodes from Ezra-Nehemiah’, in: Boda, Redditt (eds), Unity and Disunity in Ezra-Nehemiah, 184 (176–99). 17 With Nikolaishen, ‘Restoration of Israel by God’s Word’, esp. 190; pace T. Cohn Eskenazi, In an Age of Prose: A Literary Approach to Ezra-Nehemiah (SBL MS 36), Atlanta 1988, 46–60, who seems to have overlooked this important narrative clue in her analysis of the ‘First Movement’, Ezra 5:1–6:22.



Haggai And Zechariah In The Stories Of Ezra And 1 Esdras 157
3 1 Esdras
The Greek language book 1 Esdras is a free rendition of sections from the Hebrew – and Aramaic – texts from 2 Chronicles 35–36; the Book of Ezra, and Nehemiah 7–8.18 As such the Book is a piece of Biblical historiography and can be labelled as ‘rewritten scripture’.19 It should be noted that the ‘translator’ partially rearranged the material and – most importantly – added a narrative in the middle of the story. 1 Esdras 3–5 tells the beautiful story of the three young courtiers and their quest for ‘What is the strongest?’20 The historical context for the composing of 1 Esdras is – in my opinion – the Maccabean era. Hermeneutically, the story of 1 Esdras fits the ‘lock’ of the cultic cleansing by the Maccabeans, after Antiochus IV defiled the temple.
18 Good introductions into 1 Esdras are found in: K.-F. Pohlmann, Studien zum dritten Esra: Ein Beitrag zur Frage nach dem ursprünglichen Schluß des chronistischen Geschichtswerks (FRLANT, 104), Göttingen 1970; H.G.M. Williamson, ‘The Problem with 1 Esdras’, in: J. Barton and D.J. Reimer (eds), After the Exile, Essays in Honour of Rex Mason, Macon 1996, 201–216; L.L. Grabbe, Ezra-Nehemiah (OTR), London, New York 1998, 69–92; K. de Troyer, ‘Zerubbabel and Ezra: A Revived and Revised Solomon and Josiah? A Survey of Current 1 Esdras Research’, Currents in Biblical Research 1 (2002), 30–60; L.S. Fried (ed.), Did First Esdras Come First? (AIIL 7), Atlanta 2011; M. Bird, 1 Esdras: Introduction and Commentary on the Greek Text in Codex Vaticanus (SCS), Leiden 2012.
