158 Becking
The remark on the prophetic initiative is given at about the same stage of the narrative. At the end of 1 Esdras 5 the building of the temple has been stopped. Chapter 6 opens with:
ἐν δὲ τῷ δευτέρῳ ἔτει τῆς τοῦ Δαρείου βασιλείας ἐπροφήτευσεν Αγγαιος καὶ Ζαχαριας ὁ τοῦ Εδδι οἱ προφῆται ἐπὶ τοὺς Ιουδαίους τοὺς ἐν τῇ Ιουδαίᾳ καὶ Ιερουσαλημ ἐπὶ τῷ ὀνόματι κυρίου θεοῦ Ισραηλ ἐπ ̓ αὐτούς21
In the second year of the reign of Darius, Haggai and Zechariah, the son of Eddi, the prophets prophesied to all the Jews who were in Judea and Jerusalem in the name of the Lord, God of Israel, about them.
The texts of 1 Esdras 6:1 is an almost verbatim reproduction of LXX Ezra 5:1, the only difference being the spelling of the name of Zechariah’s father: Εδδι instead of Αδδω.22 The complex syntax – two adverbial adjuncts beginning with ἐπὶ, both dependant on the main verb is probably due to the contraction of the two clauses in the Aramaic text into one Greek sentence.23 In the next verse the prophetic call is answered by Zorobabel, Salathiel, and Jeshua the son of Jozadak. As in 1 Esdras 5:54; as well as in LXX Ezra 3:2,8 the dual leadership mentioned in MT Ezra – Zerubbabel the son of Shealtiel, and Jeshua the son of Jozadak – is rendered as a triumvirate in the Greek Esdras-traditions. As for 1 Esdras one is tempted to construe the threefold leadership as a narrative counterpart to the number of three bodyguards in the story of 1 Esdras. The leaders stand up and (re)start the building process of the house of God. As is the case in MT Ezra, in 1 Esdras 6:2 they are supported by the prophets of the Lord:
συνόντων τῶν προφητῶν τοῦ κυρίου βοηθούντων αὐτοῖς
the prophets of the LORD being with them, supporting them.
Although the wording is slightly different from LXX Ezra 5:2:
21 1 Esdras 6:1; see J.M. Myers, I & II Esdras (AB 42), New York 1974, 77; Z. Talshir, I Esdras: A Text Critical Commentary (The Story of the Three Youths [I Esdras 3–4] in Collaboration with David Talshir; SBL SCSS 50), Atlanta 2001, 324–25; Bird, 1 Esdras, 211–12. 22 On the variants of this name in various manuscripts and translations, see Bird, 1 Esdras, 212. 23 Bird, 1 Esdras, 75, adopted the view of Myers, I & II Esdras, 72, in construing ἐπ ̓ αὐτούς as an adverbial adjunct to be connected with ‘God’: ‘who is over them’.



Haggai And Zechariah In The Stories Of Ezra And 1 Esdras 159
μετ ̓ αὐτῶν οἱ προφῆται τοῦ θεοῦ βοηθοῦντες αὐτοῖς
with them were the prophets of God, who supported them,
the meaning of both versions is equivalent. Here, the same two problems as with MT Ezra 5 need to be discussed.
(1) Are the προφῆται, ‘the prophets’, identical with Haggai and Zechariah, or are they to be construed as a group of religious specialists? Both options can be defended. (2) What is the character of the prophetic help? The Greek verb βοηθέω is attested slightly over a 100 times in the Septuagint. Generally, it is the rendition of the Hebrew verb ʿzr, ‘to help’. The verb βοηθέω is widespread in classical Greek with as meaning ‘to help; to come to aid’.24 It is unclear whether the verb in 1 Esdras would refer to practical aid, or to a more specific prophetic activity supporting the building process in all its critical phases.
As a side remark, I would like to refer to the version Josephus has:
κατὰ τὸν καιρὸν ἐκεῖνον δύο προφῆται παρ ̓ αὐτοῖς Ἀγγαῖος καὶ Ζαχαρίας θαρρεῖν αὐτοὺς παρώρμων καὶ μηδὲν ἐκ τῶν Περσῶν ὑφορᾶσθαι δύσκολον ὡς τοῦ θεοῦ ταῦτα προλέγοντος πιστεύοντες δὲ τοῖς προφήταις ἐντεταμένως εἴχοντο τῆς οἰκοδομίας μηδεμίαν ἡμέραν ἀνιέμενοι
there were two prophets at that time among them, Haggai and Zechariah, who encouraged them, and bid them be of good cheer, and to suspect no discouragement from the Persians. So, God foretelling these things, in dependence on the prophets they applied themselves earnestly to building, and did not intermit one day.25
Josephus gives the impression that all Haggai and Zechariah did were acts of encouragement. They did support the builders in their activities with advice. 1 Esdras 6:1–7:15 follows the same narrative order as MT Ezra 4:24–6:22. This implies that – as in MT Ezra – the prophetic interruption is followed by a series
24 See LSJ, 320. 25 Josephus, Ant. 11.96; see C.T. Begg, “The ‘Classical Prophets’ in Josephus’ Antiquities”, Louvain Studies 13 (1988), 341–357; P. Höffken, Josephus Flavius und das prophetische Erbe Israels (Lüneberger Theologische Beiträge 4), Münster 2006, 29.



160 Becking
of reports concerning resistance to the building project and later on the finding of the document in Ecbatana (1 Esdras 6:22) which lead to the commission by the Persian king to finish the building of the temple. At the end of the narrative stands a note reflecting the prophetic initiative. Their conduct is summarized as follows:
καὶ εὔοδα ἐγίνετο τὰ ἱερὰ ἔργα προφητευόντων Αγγαιου καὶ Ζαχαριου τῶν προφητῶν καὶ συνετέλεσαν ταῦτα διὰ προστάγματος τοῦ κυρίου θεοῦ Ισραηλ καὶ μετὰ τῆς γνώμης Κύρου καὶ Δαρείου καὶ Ἀρταξέρξου βασιλέως Περσῶν συνετελέσθη ὁ οἶκος ὁ ἅγιος ἕως τρίτης καὶ εἰκάδος μηνὸς Αδαρ τοῦ ἕκτου ἔτους βασιλέως Δαρείου καὶ ἐποίησαν οἱ υἱοὶ Ισραηλ καὶ οἱ ἱερεῖς καὶ οἱ Λευῖται καὶ οἱ λοιποὶ οἱ ἐκ τῆς αἰχμαλωσίας οἱ προστεθέντες ἀκολούθως τοῖς ἐν τῇ Μωυσέως βίβλῳ
And so the holy works prospered, when Aggeus and Zacharias the prophets prophesied. And they finished these things by the commandment of the Lord God of Israel, and with the consent of Cyrus, Darius, and Artaxerxes, kings of Persia. The house was finished by the 23rd of the month of Adar in the sixth year of King Darius. And the sons of Israel and the priests and the Levites and the rest of those who returned from captivity were added to them, did that which was according to what was written in the books of Moses.26
The text of 1 Esdras 7:3–6 makes the impression of being an extended version of MT Ezra 6:14. Some details are added such as the remark that the building activities were according to Mosaic law, but some differences are detectable. One difference needs to be discussed. The Aramaic adverbial adjunct binbûʾat, ‘according to the prophecy’, in MT Ezra 6:14 is correctly rendered by LXX Ezra with ἐν προφητείᾳ. 1 Esdras 7 applies a participle προφητευόντων that I construe as a circumstantial clause. A circumstantial participle in Greek indicates the circumstance(s) under which the action of the main verb takes place.27 Phrased otherwise, according to 1 Esdras 7 Haggai and Zechariah were still prophesying during the whole process of the completion of the rebuilding of the temple. Here we meet a different concept.
26 1 Esdras 7:3–5; see Myers, I & II Esdras, 79–80; Talshir, I Esdras: A Text Critical Commentary, 368–77; Bird, 1 Esdras, 222–27.
27 A. Rijksbaron, The Syntax and Semantics of the Verb in Classical Greek: An Introduction, Chicago 2002, § 38; J.E. Beck, S.A. Malamud, I. Osadcha, ‘A Semantics for the Particle in and outside Conditionals in Classical Greek’, Journal of Greek Linguistics 12 (2012), 51–83.



Haggai And Zechariah In The Stories Of Ezra And 1 Esdras 161
Other than in MT Ezra, in 1 Esdras they not only initiated the escape from the cul-de-sac, but also accompanied the building process with prophetical direction whose contents are unknown to us. Would it be possible that through the Hellenistic context of 1 Esdras, a different view on prophecy was introduced? In the Hellenistic world, a person with the ability to deliver unsought for utterings of free speech is called a mantiké. A ‘prophet’ in the Hellenistic world was more of a religious specialist with an official position in the hierarchy of an oracular centre.28 Such a prophet could be consulted occasionally. The text in 1 Esdras 7 gives the impression that Haggai and Zechariah were seen as consultants during the whole of the process. In the overall plot of 1 Esdras, the interruption of Haggai and Zechariah has about the same narrative force: the prophecy functions as a cross-over point in the development of the plot. As a result of the prophecy, the rebuilding is resumed with, as its final effect, that the festival of unleavened bread could be celebrated in the Temple. The insertion of the story of the three young courtiers and their quest, however, shifts the balance a little bit. In the narrative order of 1 Esdras there is already an attempt to rebuild the temple in the earlier parts of the story.29 This attempt is situated in 1 Esdras after the declaration of the Edict of Cyrus and after the report of the return of the temple vessels but before the return of the bulk of the exiles. The narrative counterpart of this ‘pre-return’ attempt of 1 Esdras is found in Ezra after the list of the returnees and after another attempt to rebuild the temple was interrupted.30 The story of the three youths is placed between them in the 1 Esdras tradition. This story narrates that after a wisdom quest – centred on the question what is the strongest? – the Persian emperor endows the winner, Zerubbabel, on his bequest with the right to return to Jerusalem taking the temple vessels with him, and to:
ἀναβῆναι καὶ οἰκοδομῆσαι Ιερουσαλημ καὶ τὸ ἱερόν οὗ ὠνομάσθη τὸ ὄνομα αὐτοῦ ἐπ ̓ αὐτῷ καὶ ἐκωθωνίζοντο μετὰ μουσικῶν καὶ χαρᾶς ἡμέρας ἑπτά31
28 See, e.g., C. Forbes, Prophecy and Inspired Speech in Early Christianity and its Hellenistic Environment (WUNT 2.75), Tübingen 1995, 189–90; M.A. Flower, The Seer in Ancient Greece, Berkeley 2008; F. Young, God’s Presence: A Contemporary Recapitulation of Early Christianity, Cambridge 2013, 260–312. 29 1 Esdras 2:15–26; see Myers, I & II Esdras, 40–43; Bird, 1 Esdras, 135–41. 30 Ezra 4:7–24. 31 1 Esdras 4:63; see Bird, 1 Esdras, 187–89.



162 Becking
. . . go up, and to build Jerusalem, and the temple which is called by his name: and they feasted with instruments of music and gladness seven days.
I see this allowance as a preparation to the prophetic initiative. This implies that in 1 Esdras the cooperatio potestatorum is even more stressed than in MT Ezra. King and God determine hand in hand the future of Israel. Finally, it should be noted that within the composition of 1 Esdras prophets are mentioned at two decisive crossroads of the story.32 1 Esdras 2:1 reads as follows:
βασιλεύοντος Κύρου Περσῶν ἔτους πρώτου εἰς συντέλειαν ῥήματος κυρίου ἐν στόματι Ιερεμιου ἤγειρεν κύριος τὸ πνεῦμα Κύρου βασιλέως Περσῶν καὶ ἐκήρυξεν ἐν ὅλῃ τῇ βασιλείᾳ αὐτοῦ καὶ ἅμα διὰ γραπτῶν λέγων
Here too, the incentive for change is based on a divinely inspired prophetic persona. The ‘stirring up of the spirit of Cyrus’ is seen as an act of God and construed as a fulfilment of a word spoken by Jeremiah. As in MT and LXX Ezra 1:1, Jeremiah is not presented as a prophet. The collocation of words like ῥῆμα, ‘word’, στόμα, ‘mouth’, and συντέλεια, ‘fulfilment’, indicates clearly a prophetic understanding of Jeremiah. All this adds to the view of the author of 1 Esdras on history as divinely inspired and guided.
4 Ezra-Nehemiah Read as One Book
As noted above, I am of the opinion that the Books of Ezra and Nehemiah emerged as separate books.33 At a given moment in time the Nehemiah story and the Ezra report were joined and edited as one ‘book’. This joining of the stories was probably based on the mention of Ezra in the Nehemiah report. The moment at which the two were brought together is difficult to determine. The earliest pieces of evidence for the join are to be found in the manuscripts of the LXX that are to be dated after the turn of the era: Codex Alexandrinus and Codex Vaticanus. The Hebrew Codex L that treats Ezra-Nehemiah as one
32 See also S. Honigman, ‘Cyclical Time and Catalogues: The Construction of Meaning in 1 Esdras’, in: L.S. Fried (ed.), Did First Esdras Come First? (AIIL 7), Atlanta 2011, 191–208. 33 I will not summarize the existing discussion here; very instructive are the essays in M.J. Boda, P.L. Redditt (eds), Unity and Disunity in Ezra-Nehemiah: Redaction, Rhetoric, and Reader (HBM 17), Sheffield 2007.



Haggai And Zechariah In The Stories Of Ezra And 1 Esdras 163
book is even later. In Qumran, fragments of the Book of Ezra were found.34 A fragment of the Book of Nehemiah, however, has not been found or published thus far.35 It is of great importance to note that in this final composition – EzraNehemiah – a balance of mirroring prophecies can be detected. In contrast to the positive role of Haggai and Zechariah in Ezra stand the reports in Nehemiah 6. After Nehemiah had sent malʾākîm, ‘messengers’, to Sanballat, Tobiah, and Geshem (Neh. 6:3), Sanballat sent him a messenger with an ʾiggeret petûḥā, ‘open (= unsealed) letter’, that contains – amongst other topics – the reproach aiming at the incrimination of Nehemiah:
And you have also appointed prophets (nebîʾîm) to proclaim in Jerusalem concerning you: ‘A king is in Judah!’36
Later in that same chapter other activities occur that are labelled as ‘prophetic’. A certain Shemaiah the son of Delaiah, son of Mehetabel tries to ambush Nehemiah in the enclosure of the temple. Nehemiah, however, sees through his smarty plans and stays away from the ambush:
Because I perceived that surely God had not sent him, but he had spoken this prophecy against me because Tobiah and Sanballat had hired him.37
This Shemaiah the son of Delaiah, son of Mehetabel turns out not to be the only prophet in the service of Sanballat and his associates. In Neh. 6:14, Nehemiah asks God for redemption also for the ‘prophetess Noadiah’. Within
34 E. Ulrich e.a. in DJD XVI (2000); see also E. Ulrich, ‘Ezra and Qoheleth Manuscripts from Qumran (4QEzra and 4QQohAB)’, in: E. Ulrich e.a. (eds), Priests, Prophets, and Scribes: Essays on the Formation and Heritage of Second Temple Judaism in Honour of Joseph Blenkinsopp (JSOT Sup 149), Sheffield 1992, 139–157. 35 Despite earlier rumours that a fragment of Nehemiah was found in Cave 4 at Qumran, the forthcoming publication by T. Elgvin, Gleanings from the Caves: Dead Sea Scrolls and Artifacts from the Schøyen Collection (LSTS 71), London, New York 2015, does not contain a fragment of Nehemiah. 36 Neh. 6:7; see, e.g., Myers, Ezra, Nehemiah, 138; Fensham, Books of Ezra and Nehemiah, 20102; Clines, Ezra, Nehemiah, Esther, 174; Williamson, Ezra, Nehemiah, 256–257; Blenkinsopp, Ezra-Nehemiah, 268–69.
37 Neh. 6:12; see, e.g., Myers, Ezra, Nehemiah, 138–39; Fensham, Books of Ezra and Nehemiah, 204–06; Clines, Ezra, Nehemiah, Esther, 175–76; Williamson, Ezra, Nehemiah, 259; Blenkinsopp, Ezra-Nehemiah, 270–71.



164 Becking
the final composition Ezra & Nehemiah, this couple Shemaiah and Noadiah function as a counterpoint to Haggai and Zechariah. Instead of speaking in the name of God, they had placed themselves in the service of the inimical, or at the least unfriendly governor of Samaria. In conclusion, the combined composition – Ezra-Nehemiah – notes the importance of prophetic activity, but stresses the fact that not all prophets stand sympathetic towards the aims of YHWH. In fact the theme of true versus false prophecy is introduced in a subtle way.38 Finally, it should be noted that within the composition of Ezra-Nehemiah prophets who take a positive stand towards the aims of YHWH are mentioned at two decisive crossroads of the story. In the opening scene of MT Ezra it is stated:
Now in the first year of Cyrus king of Persia, in order to fulfill the word of YHWH by the mouth of Jeremiah, YHWH stirred up the spirit of Cyrus king of Persia.39
The incentive for change is based on a divinely inspired prophetic persona. The ‘stirring up of the spirit of Cyrus’ is seen as an act of God and construed as a fulfilment of a word spoken by Jeremiah. As in 1 Esdras 2:1 and LXX Ezra 1:1, Jeremiah is not presented as a prophet. The collocation of words like dābar, ‘word’, pêh, ‘mouth’, and the verb kālāh, ‘to complete’, indicates clearly a prophetic understanding of Jeremiah.40 All this adds to the view of the author of the combined composition Ezra-Nehemiah on history as divinely inspired and guided. In this historical view, the powers that be – the various Kings of Persia and the leadership of the benêy gōlāh in Yehud – play an important and cooperative role. The other decisive crossroad in the story is – as has been made clear above – the prophetic intervention in Ezra 5.

Hibbard, ‘True and False Prophecy: Jeremiah’s Revision of Deuteronomy’, JSOT 35 (2011), 339–58. 39 Ezra 1:1; see, e.g., Myers, Ezra, Nehemiah, 5–6; Fensham, Books of Ezra and Nehemiah, 42–43; Clines, Ezra, Nehemiah, Esther, 34–35; Williamson, Ezra, Nehemiah, 8–11; Blenkinsopp, Ezra-Nehemiah, 74–75; C. Karrer-Grube, ‘Scrutinizing the Conceptual Unity of Ezra and Nehemiah’, in: Boda, Redditt (eds), Unity and Disunity in Ezra-Nehemiah, 150–57 (136–59). 40 See J.G. McConville, ‘Ezra-Nehemiah and the Fulfilment of Prophecy’, VT 36 (1986), 20524; Nikolaishen, ‘Restoration of Israel by God’s Word’, 179–83.



Haggai And Zechariah In The Stories Of Ezra And 1 Esdras 165
5 Haggai and Zechariah and the Biblical Books Named after Them
A final question to be discussed is: who were Haggai and Zechariah? Traditionally, they have been identified with the ‘authors’ of the Books of Haggai and (Proto-)Zechariah living in the early post-exilic period. This identification is not without argument. The theme of Haggai is clearly temple building. Here it is reported that a drought has come over the land: “Because of My house which still lies desolate, while each of you runs to his own house” (Hag 1:9).41 At this Zerubbabel feels the impetus to rebuild the temple. In Zech. 6 it is prophesied that ‘the branch’ will rebuild the temple in full glory (Zech. 6:12–15).42 Thus far, I have treated Haggai and Zechariah as literary figures. For the authors and ancient readers of Ezra as well as 1 Esdras they were real people who had prophesied recently. The content of their oracles is neither narrated in Ezra nor in 1 Esdras. The direction of their words can only be deduced from the context. As for the relation between prophetic books and prophetic figures, I see two possibilities:
(1) The author of Ezra picked up from tradition the names of two prophets who were known for their pro-rebuilding position; (2) The books of Haggai and Zechariah were only composed after the completion of the pseudepigraphic Ezra-story.
A decisive answer to this question is not to be given. Provisionally, I would opt for the first possibility. It tallies with the way the author of Ezra uses tradition for his position and it leaves open the question of dating the two prophets as well as their compositions. The question, whether the Persian king Darius mentioned in the two prophetic books refers to Darius I or Darius II cannot be answered from my analysis of Haggai and Zechariah in the stories of Ezra as well as 1 Esdras – although I would have a preference for Darius II.43
41 See most recently E. Assis, ‘To Build or Not to Build: a Dispute between Haggai and His People (Hag 1)’, ZAW 119 (2007), 514–527.
42 On this unit and its message see W.H. Rose, Zemah and Zerubbabel: Messianic Expectations in the Early Postexilic Period (JSOT Sup 304), Sheffield 2000; A. Finitsis, Visions and Eschatology: A Socio-Historical Analysis of Zechariah 1–6 (LSTS 79), New York, London 2011. 43 Hag. 1:11 2:1.11; Zech. 1:1.7; 7:1. For Darius II as the implied king see already Scaliger, De emendatione temporum, Leiden 1583, V 224; Johannes Cocceius, Commentarius in Prophetas Duodecim Minores (Opera Omnia, III), Amstelodamus 1673, 215; and Edelman, Origins, 80–150.

Habakkuk in the Lions’ Den: Dan 14:33–39 (Bel 33–39)
Hannes Bezzel*
1 The Story of Daniel and Habakkuk in Context
There is only one Biblical narrative in which the prophet Habakkuk plays a part, and it is not to be found in the Book of the Twelve, but in the book of Daniel. In former days, this story influenced the Christian iconography of both Habakkuk and Daniel to a great extent,1 and above all it was taken to serve ‘as evidence for the possibility of the ascension of Christ’.2 Today, however, it can no longer be counted among the more popular prophetic narratives – at least from a Protestant point of view: Since Luther’s decision to use the Massoretic text as the basis for his translation of the Old Testament, following Karlstadt’s suggestion in his De canonicis scripturis libellus (1520),3 Dan 14 and the other ‘additions’ to the book of Daniel were demoted to the status of apocrypha. They were useful to read but not to be regarded as authoritative books of Sacred Scripture.4 In order that these beautiful “wild flowers” not be lost, however, Luther advised that they be planted in a separate “herbal garden.”5
* I would like to thank Prof. Dr. Paul Keim, Goshen, IN, most sincerely for proofreading this essay and correcting my English. 1 See B. Kreß, Art. ‘Daniel (Book and Person): VII. Visual Arts’, EBR 6 (2013), 128–131, with the example in column 130. 2 So Cyril of Jerusalem, Catech. 14.25; cf. K. Bracht, Art. ‘Daniel (Book and Person): IV. Christianity. A. Greek and Latin Patristics and Orthodox Churches’, EBR 6 (2013), 109–115, 111. 3 See A. Bodenstein (Karlstadt), De canonicis scripturis libellus, in K.A. Credner, ‘Zur Geschichte des Kanons’, Halle 1847, 291–412, 389. 4 Bücher: so nicht der heiligen Schrifft gleich gehalten: und doch nützlich und gut zu lesen sind’ (Title of the 1534 Bible, WA.DB 12, 2). 5 ‘Denn wir haben solche kornblumen [. . .] ausgeraufft, und doch, das sie nicht verdoe-r ben, hie jnn sonderliche wurtzgertlin oder bete gesetzt’ (‘Vorrhede auff die stucke Esther und Daniel’ of the 1534 Bible, WA.DB 12, 492). It is interesting to note that the canonical status of the “additions” was questioned already by some Church fathers, such as Hippolyt (cf. K. Bracht, Hippolyts Schrift In Danielem: Kommunikative Strategien eines frühchristlichen Kommentars (Studien und Texte zu Antike und Christentum 85), Tübingen 2014, 85–86, and Išoʿ bar Nun (cf. C. Molenberg, ‘Habakkuk’s Dinner: An Apocrpyhal Story and its Aftermath’, in: H.L.J. Vanstiphout, K. Jongeling, F. Leemhuis, G.J. Reinink (eds), Scripta Signa Vocis: Studies about Scripts, Scriptures, Scribes and Languages in the Near East, presented to J.H. Hospers, Groningen 1986, 155–162.



170 Bezzel
Some of the very special flowers in this garden are the stories of “Bel and the Dragon” which offer Habakkuk the opportunity for a short but important appearance. Daniel has been thrown into the famous lions’ den once again (in light of the wider context of the book) – this time not because of his faithful prayers, but because he violates the religious sentiments of the Babylonian citizens: his way of demonstrating the mortal character of a highly venerated dragon or serpent had been too successful. It had aroused the wrath of the crowd which forced the king to hand over the wise man to be delivered ad bestias. The dragon had burst open after devouring a ‘cake’ (μάζαν, 14:27) baked by Daniel. In revenge, the people want to see if the cook himself might be easier to digest, and they serve him to the hungry lions. This experiment fails, however, as it already had in Dan 6. The sage is neither tasted nor touched by the seven hungry beasts. He has been sitting in their uncomfortable presence for six long days, however, when Habakkuk enters the stage:
33 And it happened on the sixth day, and Habakkuk, who was carrying bread crumbled into soup in a pot and a jar of mixed wine, was on his way into the field to he harvesters. 34 And an angel of the Lord spoke to Habakkuk and said: Thus says (the Lord)6 God: Take the meal which you are carrying to Daniel (servant / son of’ Baltasar)7 to the den of lions in Babylon. 35 And Habakkuk said: Lord God, I have never seen Babylon and
6 ‘The Lord’ (κύριος) is missing in P 967 in v. 34, 37, but not in v. 35, 38 (v. 39–40 are not preserved), cf. A. Geissen, Der Septuaginta-Text des Buches Daniel: Kap. 5–12, zusammen mit Susanna, Bel et Draco sowie Esther Kap. 1,1a–2,15 nach dem Kölner Teil des Papyrus 967 (PTA 5), Bonn 1968, 276. For a comparison of Ziegler’s 1954 edition with the entire P 967, see T. McLay, ‘A Collation of Variants from 967 to Ziegler’s Critical Edition of Susanna, Daniel, Bel et Draco’, Textus 18 (1995), 121–34. 7 The strange classification τω του Βαλθασαρ is not attested by codex 88 and the Syrohexapla, but is found in P 967 (cf. A. Geissen, Der Septuaginta-Text des Buches Daniel: Kap. 5–12, 276). According to my own inspection of the fragment in question (p 34v), this reading of the genitive του for the last word in line four of 34v is indeed probable, if not necessarily cogent (cf. the presentation on the internet: http://www.uni-koeln.de/phil-fak/ifa/NRWakademie/ papyrologie/PTheol/PT34v.jpg). Commonly, in the Book of Daniel ‘Baltasar’ is Daniel’s Babylonian cognomen, not his father’s name, cf. Dan 1:7; 2:26; 10:1. Accordingly, Geissen does not want the genitive to be understood as a filiation (as, for example, the translation of the Septuaginta Deutsch, page 1465, but as indicating Daniel’s status as a ‘slave of BelshazzarBaltasar’ (Geissen, Septuaginta-Text, 277). Given this meaning, its purpose could be seen as an attempt to harmonise the chronology of Daniel and Habakkuk by taking notice of Dan 14:2, according to which Daniel was a ‘confidant of the Babylonian king’ (συμβιωτής τοῦ βασιλέως) and connecting the latter with a name from the Daniel tradition that was as close as possible to the assumed dating of Habakkuk, viz. Belshazzar / Baltasar from Dan 5.



Habakkuk in the Lions’ Den 171
I do not know where the den of lions is. 36 And the angel of the Lord lifted Habakkuk by the hair of his head and deposited him right above the den in Babylon. 37 And Habakkuk said to Daniel: Get up, eat the meal which the (Lord) God has sent you. 38 And Daniel said: So the Lord God has remembered me who does not forsake those who love him. 39 And Daniel ate. But the angel of the Lord put Habakkuk back to where he had taken him on the same day. But the Lord God remembered Daniel. 40 But after that the king came out, mourning for Daniel, and looking into the den he sees him sitting.
Delighted, the king praises the Lord as the only God and lifts his friend up from the pit. The real evildoers are thrown into the den instead, and the lions do not hesitate to demonstrate that their reticence toward Daniel had nothing to do with a sudden aversion to human flesh, since ‘they [i.e. the villains] were devoured’ (καὶ κατεβρώθησαν). This is the strange story of the short encounter between Daniel and Habakkuk according to its Septuagint (ο’) version, attested by papyrus P 967, the Codex Chisianus (88) and the Syrohexapla. As is well known, the textus receptus taken over by the Church has not been this version but the one which traditionally has been ascribed to Theodotion (θ’). How these two Greek versions of the Book of Daniel relate to each other has been a matter of debate and has been re-examined many times. I will not enter this discussion with regard to the book as a whole nor even the entire chapter of Dan 14. However, when it comes to the Habakkuk story I think that, compared with ο’, the θ’-version does not represent an independent version of the same tradition8 nor a younger (and better) translation of the same Semitic Vorlage.9 In general, it is not the
8 The most recent article of J.R. Trotter, for example, wants to deduce the differences between both versions in Dan 14:1–22 from two already different Semitic Vorlagen (cf. J.R. Trotter, ‘Another Stage in the Redactional History of the Bel Story (Dan 14:1–22): The Evidence of Polemic against Foreign Priests and the Focus on Daniel in the Old Greek’, JSJ 44 (2013), 481–496); cf. C.A. Moore, Daniel, Esther and Jeremiah: The Additions (AncB 44), New York 1977, 119. At least for the Habakkuk-story, this explanation seems rather unlikely to me: Here, Theodotion and LXX are too close to each other for being the result of two different translations of different Vorlagen. 9 Cf. O. Plöger, ‘Zusätze zu Daniel’, in: H. Bardtke/O. Plöger, Historische und legendarische Erzählungen: Zusätze zu Esther. Zusätze zu Daniel (JSHRZ I/1), Gütersloh 1973, 53–87, 65–66.



172 Bezzel
older text,10 but on the contrary shows clear signs of being a revision.11 It provides a smoother narrative continuity by a) shortening seemingly superfluous pieces of information and b) adding explanations and emphases where they seemed to be missing. Here are three examples. According to LXX, v. 33 gives a detailed description of the meal which the prophet-cook has prepared for his workers: ‘bread crumbled into soup in a pot and a jar of mixed wine’ (ἄρτους ἐντεθρυμμένους ἐν σκάφῃ ἐν ἑψήματι καὶ στάμνον οἴνου κεκερασμένου). θ’ emphasises the fact that the meal had been cooked by Habakkuk himself, but changes the details of the recipe and, what is more, omits the wine: ‘He himself had cooked a soup and crumbled bread into a pot’ (καὶ αὐτὸς ἥψησεν ἕψεμα καὶ ἐνέθρυψεν ἄρτους εἰς σκάφην).12 In so doing an intratextual connection with the parallel in v. 11 is missed. There, it is the statue of the false and dead god Bel who is served food and mixed wine (ο’: βρώματα [. . .] καί οἶνος κερασθεὶς; θ’: βρώματα καὶ τὸν οἶνον κεράσας); here, it is Daniel who is being served food and wine by the real and living God. At the same time, θ’ seems to feel the necessity to introduce Habakkuk properly. In the ο’-version he simply appears on stage in v. 33. Theodotion sets a new starting point, classifying the new character clearly as ‘the prophet’ and locating his whereabouts: ‘And there was Habakkuk, the prophet, in Judea’ (καὶ ἦν Αμβακουμ ὁ προφήτης ἐν τῇ Ἰουδαίᾳ). As a second and third example I would like to put the focus both on Habakkuk’s appearance and on his exit. According to ο’, in v. 32 Daniel has been sitting in the den ‘for six days’ (ἡμέρας ἕξ), and on that very day Habakkuk is called to action (v. 33): ‘And it happened on the sixth day’ (καὶ ἐγένετο τῇ ἡμέρᾳ τῇ ἕκτῃ). Theodotion on the other hand provides no date at all in this instance – summing it up rather with the information in v. 40 (‘after that’, μετὰ ταῦτα) and making the king enter ‘on the seventh day’ (τῇ ἡμέρᾳ τῇ ἑβδόμῃ).
10 Cf. K. Koch, Deuterokanonische Zusätze zum Danielbuch: Entsteung und Textgeschichte. Band II: Exegetische Erläuterungen (AOAT 38/2), Neukirchen-Vluyn 1987, 200–202. 11 Cf. J. Schüpphaus, ‘Das Verhältnis von LXX- und Theodotion-Text in den apokryphen Zusätzen zum Danielbuch’, ZAW 83 (1971), 49–72, 51; I. Kottsieper, ‘Zusätze zu Daniel’, in: O.H. Steck / R.G. Kratz / I. Kottsieper, Das Buch Baruch. Der Brief des Jeremia. Zusätze zu Ester und Daniel (ATD.A 5), Göttingen 1998, 211–85, 248.
12 Cf. A.M. Schwemer, Studien zu den frühjüdischen Prophetenlegenden Vitae Prophetarum II: Die Viten der kleinen Propheten und der Propheten aus den Geschichtsbüchern (TSAJ 50) Tübingen 1996, 110, n. 85. With the omission of the wine she sees encratitic moral principles (‘enkratitische[. . .] Moral’) at work (ibd.). Koch, who generally regards the θ’-version to be the older one, interprets the case differently: According to him, LXX adds the wine in order to adapt the menu to Greek culinary conventions (see K. Koch, Deuterokanonische Zusätze II, 176).



Habakkuk in the Lions’ Den 173
Thus the repetition is avoided, and at the same time the final act of salvation, Daniel’s rescue from the pit, is dated and brought into the focus. This observation draws attention to the end of our short episode and our third example, which appears in v. 38–39. In the ο’-version, the central point that God remembers is given twice, first in Daniel’s thankful praise (v. 38), and then again by the narrator (v. 39). This second reference is missing in θ’ and to my mind the best explanation for this fact is that the doublet was omitted in order to produce a smoother text.13 The second edition of the relevant Göttingen Septuaginta volume (XVI / 2) by Munnich illustrates that this is a common tendency and one to which editors are still prone today. While Ziegler reproduces the “repetition” in his ο’-text,14 it is marked as a “doublet” by Munnich, without any basis in the textual tradition and clearly against P 967.15 Munnich argues that the sentence would ‘make no sense in v. 39’.16 In doing so, Munnich creates a lectio facilior just as “Theodotion” had done, and tends to eliminate a phenomenon text-critically which – along with the doubling of the ‘six days’ in v. 32–33 – should be interpreted in a redaction-critical way instead. Coming back to the story (in its ο’–version) as a whole, the first question one might ask is: Why must poor Daniel starve for six long days before God intervenes and he is supplied with food? An initial, rather simple, answer is suggested by a redaction-critical interpretation. Daniel had to wait for six days because the duration of his detention was already part of the story before Habakkuk and his role were added to it. The twofold reference to God’s remembering in v. 38 and v. 39 as well as the six days mentioned in v. 32 and v. 34, which we discussed above from a text-critical point of view, both raise the redaction-critical suspicion of indicating literary seams. In both instances these repetitions look like a kind of catchword connection. In fact, if by way of trial one takes out the entire Habakkuk-episode, one can derive a fully coherent Daniel story: ‘32And the crowd threw Daniel into the den that he should be devoured and get no grave. And Daniel was in the den for six days. [. . .] But the Lord God remembered Daniel. 40And after that the
13 Collins, however, defines the relationship the other way round: ‘G expands’ – J.J. Collins, Daniel: A Commentary on the Book of Daniel (Hermeneia), Minneapolis, MN, 1993, 408.
14 Cf. J. Ziegler, Susanna. Daniel. Bel et Draco’ (Septuaginta. Vetus Testamentum Graecum Auctoritate Academiae Scientiarum Gottingensis editum 16/2), Göttingen 1954, 222.
15 Cf. O. Munnich, Susanna. Daniel. Bel et Draco edidit Joseph Ziegler. Editio secunda (Septuaginta. Vetus Testamentum Graecum Auctoritate Academiae Scientiarum Gottingensis editum 16/2), Göttingen 21999, 60–61, 406. 16 ‘In v. 39 ergibt der Satz keinen Sinn’; Munnich, Susanna. Daniel. Bel et Draco, 61.



174 Bezzel
king came out, mourning for Daniel, and when he looks into the den he sees him sitting.’ In this story, Daniel’s problem is not so much the lack of food but rather as in the Massoretic version of Dan 6 – the danger of becoming food for the lions himself. This problem is solved by the protection of God who remembers Daniel and has him brought up by the king who praises the only true God and condemns the real evildoers.17 Our episode, however, puts the focus on a different cause of distress: Daniel has been sitting in the pit for six days – he must be hungry. Therefore, the six days are highlighted, and Daniel’s praise that the Lord has finally remembered him refers now to his being fed – instead of not being food. One might also say that the date of Daniel’s deliverance is shifted from the morning of the seventh to the afternoon of the sixth day, or, expressed in terms of space rather than of time, from the brim of the den to its bottom. So the Habakkuk story takes up the two central points from its later context, the period of six days on the one hand and the fact that ultimately God remembers his friends on the other. These are transformed into a new story in which the prophet Habakkuk plays a major role. Taken together, these observations provide reason to doubt the literary unity of the entire Bel-chapter and instead regard the Habakkuk-episode as a secondary insertion into the surrounding narrative. But if one accepts this theory, then the question it raises about the literary character of this piece in relation to its context can be more closely defined and interpreted. Kottsieper, for example, argues that vv. 33–39 might represent 1) a once independent Habakkuk-Daniel tradition, a kind of novella whose 2) purpose was to demonstrate that kosher living was possible even outside Eretz Israel.18 However, I do not think that either thesis gets to the heart of the matter. Ad 1): The connection of the story to its immediate literary context is anchored much too deeply in its inmost corpus to be understood as the reworking of an independent text. This is shown best by Kottsieper’s reconstructed tradition itself. In order to derive a once-independent narrative, he has to strip it of all connections to its context and credit them to the work of a redactor. This includes themes such as the sixth day in v. 33 and every reference to the lions’ den (v. 34, 35, 36).19 The latter operation seems to be justified by the observation that the short Habakkuk-biography of the vitae prophetarum,
17 Cf. A. Wysny, Die Erzählungen von Bel und dem Drachen: Untersuchung zu Dan 14 (SBB 33), Stuttgart 1996, 115–16. 18 Cf. Kottsieper, ‘Zusätze zu Daniel’, 272–73. 19 Cf. Kottsieper, ‘Zusätze zu Daniel’, 262–63.



Habakkuk in the Lions’ Den 175
though aware of the prophet’s catering service, makes no reference to his counterpart sitting in any kind of pit. Schwemer, followed by Kottsieper, interprets this fact as an indication that both Dan 14,33–39 and Liv.Proph. 12,4–8 might independently refer to the same tradition.20 However, I think that a direct literary dependence of Liv.Proph. 12 on Dan 14 is more plausible.21 The ignoring of both lions and den by Liv.Proph. can be understood from its interpretation of Habakkuk’s travel. It is seen as a kind of symbolic action whereby the prophet envisages the imminent return of the golah (cf. Liv.Proph. 12:8). The exegetical focus is put on Babylon as the destination, and on speed. Habakkuk’s immediate return from Babylon (ταχέως, v. 6) symbolises the imminent return of the people (τάχιον, v. 8).22 Thus, ‘superfluous’ elements such as Daniel’s distress and the reasons for it are left aside. In the end, Kottsieper’s literary-critical analysis of Dan 14,33–39 results in a story in which a problem is solved that the reader had not known anything about. A certain Habakkuk delivers a certain dish of food to a certain Daniel who kindly says ‘thank you.’ But the reader or listener would not have known that the wise man was in the need of it in the first place. Ad 2) Furthermore I am not convinced that this story could have served the purpose Kottsieper ascribes to it. It does not actually offer a real solution to the problem of impure food in the diaspora – in contrast to Dan 1. This latter narrative, which Kottsieper gives as a parallel to Bel 33–39, resolves the food problem in the diaspora by recommending vegetarianism, offering an obviously healthy (cf. Dan 1:15–16) as well as practical way to deal with it. Compared to that, a heavenly prophetic airlift à la Dan 14:33–39 seems to be a rather largescale endeavour. To sum up: I cannot find a formerly independent Habakkuk-tradition in Dan 14:33–39 but instead regard the relevant piece as a Fortschreibung or narrative expansion of the Bel-story, written to address its own context.
2 Two Questions: Why Food? Why Habakkuk?
If one follows this redaction-critical interpretation and takes Dan 14:33–39 as an insertion that was written for an already existing narrative about the
20 Cf. Schwemer, Studien II, 109–111; Kottsieper, ‘Zusätze zu Daniel’, 250. 21 Cf. Koch, Deuterokanonische Zusätze II, 186, not speaking in terms of literary dependency but calling the Liv.Proph.-version ‘überlieferungsgeschichtlich’ younger (ibd.). 22 Cf. Schwemer, Studien II, 105.



176
Bezzel
activities of a Daniel who represents a kind of religious enlightenment, two questions come to the fore:
1. Apart from the matter of kosher living: why must Daniel be served food after six days while he is still sitting in the pit, when he will be rescued the next morning anyway? 2. If the fact is accepted that Daniel needs something to eat, then why must this food be delivered by Habakkuk the prophet?
I think that the answer to the first question will be revealed when we answer the second. Therefore I will deal with the second first: why Habakkuk? With regard to this question, the first suspicion might be that to the mind of the responsible redactor Habakkuk simply was the prophet of the time in question.23 Of course, the dating of the literary Daniel as well as of the literary Habakkuk is a well-known problem. The former is counted in Dan 1 among the people of the first Golah (597). The Massoretic version of the Lions’ Den story in Dan 6 is set at the time of a certain ‘Darius the Mede’ (
.cf ,דריוש מדיא
9:1, 11:1), it being a matter of debate whether he should be identified with the Persian Darius I (549–486)24 or whether he is ‘a literary fiction in the Book of Daniel’.25 To make matters more complicated, the ο’–version in this instance speaks of ‘Artaxerxes the Mede’ (Ἀρταξέρξης ὁ τῶν Μήδων), which most likely refers to Artaxerxes I (465–424, cf. Dan 9:1). In Dan 11:1 both θ’ and ο’ mention the first year of Cyrus (Κύρου). The stories in Dan 14, however, are not dated at all but speak only of ‘the king’ – at least according to ο’, while θ’ identifies him too with Cyrus. Another hint is given by the text-critically dubious classification of Daniel as ‘belonging to / son of Baltasar’ (τω του Βαλθασαρ) in v. 34,26 which probably reflects an attempt to connect our story with Dan 5 and synchronise it with a possible Habakkuk-chronography. One way or the other, Daniel is located between the early sixth and the middle of the fifth century BCE. As for the prophetic book named after Daniel’s partner in Dan 14, the Book of Habakkuk bears no explicit dating at all. Its position between Nahum and Zephaniah and the singular reference to the ‘Babylonians’ (
in Hab 1:6 )כשדים

suggest that the book purports to be set somewhere in the late Assyrian and early Babylonian era – that’s all. After all, it is perhaps possible that a rather old prophet Habakkuk might have met the sage Daniel in the very early years of his career. Even Martin Luther realised however that the chronologies of the two prophets made such a meeting unlikely.27 If the author’s primary concern would have been contemporaneity, there surely would have been better candidates for the job. For example, one might have thought of Jeremiah28 or, even more likely, of Ezekiel,29 who in addition would have been familiar with the local conditions at the destination and would even have had some experience of travelling by means of divine translation. It is quite obvious, that Habakkuk’s flight to Mesopotamia is modelled after Ezek 8:3. Another possible reason for the choice of Habakkuk could have been his name.30 The prophet bears a ‘rather strange name’ (‘[einen] eher komischen Namen’),31 which Luther derived from the root

Biblical prophet to act as a cook – would not the one called ‘cucumber’ seem to be the best qualified? However, I doubt whether even the author(s) of our episode, writing an addition to the already existing Dragon-story and therefore situated perhaps at some time in the second century BCE,35 knew enough Akkadian to understand this allusion, let alone their readers. Therefore, I think that neither the forced synchronisation of Habakkuk and Daniel nor the ‘strange name’ of the former provided sufficient reason to bring them both together. This was done primarily because they were understood to be kindred spirits. What then is the link between Habakkuk and Daniel? I would like to recall an observation made above: as pointed out, the little Habakkuk-episode has a different view of what constitutes Daniel’s main problem than has its context. The beginning of the Habakkuk story refers to, highlights – and I daresay is triggered by – the aforementioned six day time span. The hero’s dilemma is not that his life is in danger but that his deliverance has been delayed. It is time that has become Daniel’s problem. This problem of the delay of deliverance provides the hermeneutical key for our story. It is the tie that connects the prophet with the sage, since obviously both have to struggle with it. Of course, time is not so much a problem for the Daniel of the Massoretic version of the lion story in Dan 6. That Daniel spends one night only in the den. His danger is immediate, and from this he is saved by an angel who keeps the lions’ mouths shut (Dan 6:23). The same holds true for the other Aramaic novellas. Of course time is an explicit issue for Daniel, the seer, in the older strata of the visions in Dan 7; 8; 10–1236 – though there he is not troubled by it. On the contrary, the wise man is able to reveal to the king the mystery of time (
רז , cf. Dan 2) as it is planned and governed by God.

But time does trouble Daniel, the reader of the scriptures, in Dan 9 who meditates on the 70 year span of Jeremiah. His problem is exactly that of Daniel the starving prisoner of the Habakkuk legend: deliverance is being delayed. This too is the problem of Habakkuk whose lament in Hab 1:2 begins with the outcry ‘How long?’ ( 37.)עד־אנה Habakkuk not only shares this problem with the Daniel of Dan 9 and everybody who is able to identify her- or himself with him. For those who articulate this dilemma in the common language of lament his book also provides a way to deal with it: Habakkuk gets a divine answer to his request, in Hab 2:2–4. The prophet is told to write down what he hears on tablets like Moses,38 because ‘still the vision is for the appointed time, and it blows39 to the end and does not lie; though it tarry, wait for it. It will surely come, it will not delay’ (Hab 2:3). Whatever the difficult verse 2:4a might mean exactly,40 the general message of v. 3–4 is clear. Even if there is a delay, the vision will come true for sure; it is not a deception. What Habakkuk has to do is to wait ( חכה־לו ), for in the end (Hab 2:4b) the righteous will live by his faithfulness. The Daniel of Dan 14, who has been sitting in the lions’ den for six days and who is waiting for deliverance to come, shares his problem with the Daniel of Dan 9 who meditates on scripture, and with the prophet Habakkuk who asks desperately ‘how long?’ So too the community of Qumran, who, like Daniel in Dan 9, study the scriptures. The famous Habakkuk Pesher 1QpHab clearly shows the problem as well as its solution. In reference to Hab 2:3 the

Bezzel
commentators write that ‘the final age will be extended and go beyond all that the prophets say, because the mysteries of God ( רזי אל ) are wonderful’.41 If this is the answer to our second question, we now have to come back to the first one: Why food? As we have seen, Daniel’s problem is the delay of his deliverance. The food that Habakkuk serves him does not rescue him from the lions’ den – but obviously it enables him to endure. It serves the same purpose as the divine answer to Habakkuk the prophet in Hab 2:3–4.42 Thus the one may be identified with the other. The food Habakkuk has to offer to Daniel is the revelation written down on the tablets: wait for it, because in the end the righteous will live by his steadfastness ( באמונתו ). If the present times are hard to endure, ‘keep calm and carry on’ – for in the end, there will be deliverance for sure. Habakkuk’s food is his prophetic book (cf. Ez 3:1–3). Daniel consumes it and comes to recognise: ‘So the Lord God has remembered me who does not forsake those who love him.’ In other words, ‘the righteous will live by his steadfastness’.
3 Daniel and Habakkuk: Transformation and Interpretation
In conclusion, if this theory about the intention of the Habakkuk narrative in Bel and the Dragon 33–39 is correct, what can be said about its effect? I would like to emphasise two points.
1. Allegorisation. As for the character of Daniel, compared with its surrounding context, the Habakkuk-narrative works in an allegorising way. Without this little episode, the lions are merely lions, dangerous animals whose hunger is a mortal peril for the poor convict.43 Daniel appears to be a ‘champion de la rationalité, triomphant de l’obscurantisme supersti
41 1QpHab VII,7–8, translation according to F.G. Martínez, E.J.C. Tigchelaar (eds), The Dead Sea Scrolls Study Edition: Volume One: 1Q1–4Q273, Leiden 1997, 17. Nota bene: being able to reveal and explain the ‘mystery’ ( רז ) was the key competence of Daniel in Dan 2 (cf. Dan 2:18, 19, 27, 28, 29, 30, 37). 42 Cf. Haag, ‘Bel und Drache’, 30; id., Daniel (NEB 30), Würzburg 1993, 95, speaking of a ‘midrash’ on Hab 2:4. 43 Van der Toorn nicely elucidates the background of the lions’ den motif. He finds it in Babylonian literature as a metaphor for the team spirit predominant in intellectual circles whose members always are in fear of losing the king’s favour. In Dan 6 the pit and the lions become ‘real’ ones – ‘a classical case of misrecognition of a metaphor’; K. van der Toorn, ‘In the Lions’ Den: The Babylonian Background of a Biblical Motif’, CBQ 60 (1998), 638. Bearing this in mind, Dan 14:33–39 could be understood as a kind of re-metaphorisation.



Habakkuk in the Lions’ Den 181
tieux des païens’,44 and the six days are little less than a week. With Habakkuk bringing food, Daniel turns into a paradigm of the righteous in general, the lions become a symbol for any kind of afflictions those righteous have to endure, and the six day time span turns into the present time during which the righteous are desperately longing for the final act of salvation to come. Finally, the food Habakkuk has to offer is more than just soup, bread and wine. It is his prophetic book with its message: ‘Keep calm and carry on’. Different acts of eating and drinking are crucial for the coherence of the entire chapter:45 the dead idol Bel is unable to eat and drink – in contrast to his priests. The venerated serpent is a living being as demonstrated by its eating. But at the same time it proves itself to be mortal, and hence not divine, by eating. The lions, on the other hand, refuse to eat Daniel, the righteous, but fast until they are fed with wicked people again. The Habakkuk narrative fits this pattern quite well, and at the same time transcends it. What Daniel, along with the reader, is being served by Habakkuk on account of God in Dan 14:37 turns out to be a kind of πνευματικὸν βρῶμα, ‘spiritual food’. Given this interpretation, the entire chapter takes a different turn. Without the Habakkuk-story, Dan 14 speaks of the triumph of the true religion over Babylon and its idols. Mittmann-Richert, following Steussy, stresses this point. She interprets the entire chapter as a narrative demonstration that after the Maccabean crisis the prophecies of Jeremiah and Daniel had been fulfilled. Daniel should be regarded as a true prophet equal to Jeremiah and Ezekiel even though the great apocalyptic end had not come.46 Thus, the ‘additions’ of the Greek versions of the Book of Daniel in general would ‘shift the total impact of the book away from
44 P.M. de Viviés, ‘Les séjours de Daniel dans la fosse aux lions regard narratif synoptique’, in: C. Focant/A. Wénin (eds), Analyse Narrative et Bible: Deuxième Colloque International du RRENAB, Louvain-la-Neuve, Avril 2004 (BEThL 191), Leuven 2005, 141.

182 Bezzel
apocalyptic fervor toward exploration of the possibilities of life in “Babylon”.’47 With Habakkuk entering the story I rather doubt this optimistic twist. In the end, the righteous will be delivered. But the question of whether the reader should see him- or herself already living in the ‘afterwards’ (μετὰ ταῦτα, Dan 14:40) or still at the bottom of the den at the sixth day, is – at the very least – reopened. 2. Visualisation. As for the character of Habakkuk, the well-known message of Hab 2:4 is not only further promulgated through this story, but even more so it can now be visualised. With the image of the prophet dangling between heaven and earth on his flight to Babylon just to serve a meal to Daniel, the core point of the story, as well as of the book of Habakkuk, gets a great deal more vivid. This is demonstrated not least by its widespread reception in art history. ‘Keep calm and carry on’ for ‘the righteous shall live by his steadfastness’ and ‘God does not forsake those who love him’.

More than just a Pretty Coat: The Story of Joseph the Dreamer from Jewish, Christian and Islamic Perspectives
Elizabeth Hayes
1 Introduction
The interpretive history of Joseph the dreamer in Judaism, Christianity and Islam is both rich and complex. The aim of this study is to present an overview of trends in interpretation of the Joseph story in these three traditions. Although the boyhood garment given him by his father Jacob is often a focus of attention in Biblical interpretation, this paper will argue that the Joseph story is about far more than just a pretty coat. From the Biblical emphasis upon the politics of power and Joseph’s unique role in rescuing his family from famine to the Qur’an’s claim that Surah 12 is ‘the most beautiful tale’, the history of interpretation of the Joseph story is fascinating indeed.1
1.1 Two Primary Sources and a Multitude of Modifications
The contours of the Joseph story are similar in the Biblical account and the version found in the Qur’an, although there are some key differences in detail.2 While the version in the Qur’an exhibits connections to the Joseph story in Genesis 37–46 and to midrashic sources as well, it is by no means clear that it is solely a derivative work. For this reason, both the Biblical text and the text of the Qur’an are viewed as primary sources for this study.
1.2 Trajectories of Interpretation: Sources and Communities
Until recently, Western scholarship has tended to view the version of the Joseph story found in the Qur’an as derivative of the Biblical text. However, in some ways this perspective is insufficient to account for major differences and recent scholarship has moved towards viewing the version in the Qur’an and
1 This paper is dedicated to my friend Maryam, whose story telling ability caused the tale of Zulaykah and Yusuf to come alive: my view of Joseph has been delightfully altered forever. 2 M.S. Bernstein, Stories of Joseph: Narrative Migrations between Judaism and Islam, Detroit 2006, 35; S.B. Noegel and B.M. Wheeler, Historical Dictionary of Prophets in Islam and Judaism, Lanham, MD, London 2002, 175.



184 Hayes
subsequent Islamic accounts as a second independent tradition.3 Regarding Joseph in the Qur’an, John Renard notes:
Non-Muslim readers may be tempted to interpret the story primarily as a retelling of the biblical account in Genesis 37–46. Comparative study can indeed shed important light on both traditions, but ceding authority or definitive form to earlier tradition may diminish the value of the latter. The two accounts arise out of very different circumstances and communicate different messages: each must be read on its own terms.4
While interpreters reading the Joseph account from within a faith tradition may be slow to acknowledge two more or less independent Joseph traditions and struggle with the idea of ‘ceding authority’, such decoupling of the Biblical and Islamic accounts of the Joseph story might represent an important step forward for constructive dialogue. The move opens the door for Jewish, Christian and Islamic faith communities each to read and reflect upon its own canonical scriptural text without demanding consensus regarding either the literary shape of the story or the theological construction resulting from interpretation within a given context. Additionally, understanding the development of the two versions as separate traditions allows academic freedom to explore the ramifications of separate literary texts without demanding priority for either.5
1.3 Joseph is a Dreamer (But is he a Prophet?)
While both the Biblical and Qur’an accounts agree that Joseph is a dreamer, the question of prophet-hood still remains. What do the dual traditions have to say about Joseph as prophet? In the Qur’an, events within the narrative have been seen as a series of tests of Joseph’s fitness for prophet-hood while the biblical account of Joseph is set in a time frame prior to that of the writing prophets. Because of this, there is no explicit reference to Joseph as a prophet in the biblical account. However, the biblical account presents the role of Joseph as a dreamer and as an interpreter of dreams, thus as one who heard directly from God. In neither the Qur’an nor the biblical text does Joseph receive a particular message with instructions for a given population; rather he receives knowledge
3 Bernstein, Stories of Joseph, 35; V.K. Robbins and G.D. Newby, ‘A Prolegomenon to the Relation of the Qur’an and the Bible’, in: J.C. Reeves (ed.), Bible and Qur’an: Essays in Scriptural Intertextuality (Symposium), Atlanta 2003, 23–26.
4 J. Renard, Seven Doors to Islam: Spirituality and the Religious Life of Muslims, Berkeley 1996, 49. 5 This is not meant to diminish any source critical studies involved with these texts, but rather to create an environment for productive reading and discussion of this significant story.



More than just a Pretty Coat 185
and authority that allow him to advance in his situation.6 Within the Qur’an this knowledge and authority are marks of prophet-hood, while in the Biblical text they serve to elevate Joseph’s status in service of preserving his family and thus, the family line of the covenant people of YHWH.7
2 Primarily Speaking
As previously mentioned the Joseph Story in the MT and in the Qur’an follow similar but not identical narrative lines and this creates different emphases within the two traditions. A brief overview of the two accounts and an analysis of the key differences follow.
2.1 A Pretty Coat and More: The Joseph Narrative in the Masoretic Text More than just a pretty coat: this describes the Joseph narrative in the MT, where Joseph’s sartorial splendor (and deprivation thereof) serves as a narrative marker for the ups and downs in his personal and family life. Gordon Wenham provides the following short outline of the Genesis version, demonstrating that it is chiastic in form:8
1. Gen. 37:2–36: Joseph sold into slavery (A) 2. Gen. 38:1–30: Tamar and Judah (B) 3. Gen. 39:1–20: In Potiphar’s house (C) a. Gen. 39:1–11: Joseph and Potiphar b. Gen. 39:12–18: Joseph and Mrs. Potiphar 4. Gen. 39:21–40:23: Joseph in prison (D) 5. Gen. 41:1–57: Joseph in the palace (D1) 6. Gen. 42:1–38: First family visit to Egypt (C1) 7. Gen. 43:1–45:28: Second family visit to Egypt (B1)
6 The Biblical Joseph is not portrayed with any of Petersen’s six characteristics of ANE prophets: Joseph does not have an intense experience of the deity; he does not speak or write in a distinctive way; he does not act in a particular social setting; his distinctive personal qualities are limited to being exceptionally handsome along with the ability to interpret dreams; he does not act as an intermediary between the people and the deity; and finally, he does not have a distinctive message. D.L. Petersen, ‘Defining Prophecy and Prophetic Literature’, in: M. Nissinen (ed.), Prophecy in Its Ancient near East Context: Mesopotamian, Biblical and Arabian Perspectives (Symposium), Atlanta 2000, 33–39. 7 Noegel and Wheeler, Historical Dictionary of Prophets, 175. 8 G.J. Wenham, Exploring the Old Testament: A Guide to the Pentateuch 1, Downers Grove 2002, 51.



186
Hayes
8. Gen. 46.1–47.31: Third family visit to Egypt (A1) 9. Gen. 48:1–50:26: The last days of Jacob and Joseph
Section 1, Gen. 37:2–36, describes Jacob/Israel’s gift of a cloak to Joseph and the consequences of the gift. Genesis 37:3 states, ‘Now Israel loved Joseph more than any other of his children, because he was the son of his old age; and he had made him a long robe with sleeves.’ The unusual Hebrew term used here, כתנת פסים – ketonet passîm, indicates that this is not a typical garment.9 Here Jacob’s favoritism is stated outright and it is not surprising that his other 11 sons would become yet more jealous upon Joseph’s receipt of the pretty coat – the outward sign of this favoritism. Consequently, the brothers plot to get rid of Joseph. Only Reuben’s intervention prevents a case of fratricide, with Joseph cast into a pit instead. But how will they explain the missing brother to their father? Within Section 1, Genesis 37:18–33 describes the brothers’ plot. Genesis 37:23 states: ‘So when Joseph came to his brothers, they stripped him of his robe, the long robe with sleeves that he wore.’10 Joseph is summarily cast into the pit and subsequently sold to a passing caravan of Ishmaelites. But how do they explain the missing brother to their father? In a ruse that is reminiscent of Jacob’s clever manipulation of Isaac’s blessing, his own children fool Jacob with a slaughtered goat and a garment. Genesis 37.31–33 reads:
Then they took Joseph’s robe, slaughtered a goat, and dipped the robe in the blood. They had the long robe with sleeves taken to their father, and they said, “This we have found; see now whether it is your son’s robe or not.” He recognized it, and said, “It is my son’s robe! A wild animal has devoured him; Joseph is without doubt torn to pieces.”
At this point, Section 2, Genesis 38 (the tale of Tamar and Judah) interrupts the Joseph story. In this part of the narrative, it is Judah who is tricked with clothing as Tamar exchanges her regular garments for a prostitute’s veil and back again. The narrative returns to Joseph in Potiphar’s house in Section 3, Gen. 39:1–18. When Potiphar’s wife says ‘lay with me’ she ends up with only Joseph’s garment in her hand. And Joseph ends up in prison with the Baker and the Cupbearer. There is good to come from this. Within Section 4, Genesis 39:21–40:23, Joseph proves as adept at interpreting dreams as he is at having them. In Section 5,
9 The collocation is further only attested in the description of Tamar (2 Sam. 13:18). 10 All biblical text is taken from the NRSV version.



More than just a Pretty Coat 187
Genesis 41:1–57 Pharaoh has a disturbing dream. He sends for Joseph, who true to form, changes his clothes again. Verse 41:14 states: then Pharaoh sent for Joseph, and he was hurriedly brought out of the dungeon. When he had shaved himself and changed his clothes, he came in before Pharaoh. After a discussion regarding seven fat cows and seven skinny ones, Pharaoh gives Joseph a promotion and a raise. Within Section 5, Genesis 41:41–43 states:
And Pharaoh said to Joseph, “See, I have set you over all the land of Egypt.” Removing his signet ring from his hand, Pharaoh put it on Joseph’s hand; he arrayed him in garments (begadîm) of fine linen, and put a gold chain around his neck. He had him ride in the chariot of his second-incommand; and they cried out in front of him, “Bow the knee!” Thus he set him over all the land of Egypt.
Joseph is now in a position that allows him to aid his family, preserving the line of the Abrahamic covenant. Thus, clothing serves as a narrative marker in the MT. From Jacob’s childhood gift of the ketonet passîm, to a garment left in the hands of Mrs. Potiphar, to the fine linens of Egypt, Joseph’s garments (or lack thereof) raise doubts about the phrase ‘clothes make the man’. As a matter of fact, it appears that the loss of clothing to Mrs. Potiphar is what actually makes the man for Joseph. His unwillingness to compromise results in some quiet time in prison: time to think, time to interpret dreams, and time to emerge as a man of influence. Yet this is only half of the story. The remainder of the narrative is taken up with episodes in which Joseph’s family journey to Egypt, were they find sustenance at the hand of Joseph, whose dream is thereby fulfilled.
2.2 The Joseph Narrative in Sura 12 of the Qur’an
As Bernstein’s summary outline of Joseph in the Qur’an demonstrates, Surah 12, like the biblical text, also exhibits a chiastic structure.11
Joseph in the Qur’an: Surah 12 (a) Joseph’s dream (vv. 4–6); (b) The brother’s plot against Joseph (8–18); (c) The attempt by the wife of al-ʿAziz to seduce Joseph (23–29); (d) A similar attempt by Egyptian ladies (30–31); (e) Joseph’s imprisonment (35); (f) The king’s dream (43–44). (f’) The interpretation of the kings dream (45–49);
11 Bernstein, Stories of Joseph, 35.



188 Hayes
(e’) Joseph’s release from prison (50); (d’) The confessions of the Egyptian ladies, followed by; (c’) The confession of the wife of al-ʿAziz (51); (b’) The lesson learned by the brothers (58ff.); and finally, (a’) The fulfilment of Joseph’s dream (100)
Such narrative continuity is unusual in the writings of the Qur’an and while the text does exhibit structural similarity to that of the biblical narrative, there are at least five areas where the Biblical and Qur’anic accounts diverge significantly:
(1) The Qur’an makes a claim for revelation regarding the Joseph story (Surah 12:1–3; 102).12 ‘These are the verses of the scripture that makes things clear – We have sent it down as Arabic Qur’an so that you [people] may understand. We tell you [Prophet] the best of stories in revealing this Qur’an to you. Before this, you were one of those who knew nothing about them.’ (2) The story is told with didactic intent (Surah 12:7).13 There are lessons in the story of Joseph and his brothers for all who seek them. (3) There is an account of a miracle (Surah 12:94).14 (4) God is mentioned throughout and Joseph prays openly (Surah 12:101).15 (5) The Qur’an version contains extra dramatis personae in the form of the ‘women of the city’, who have an expanded role in the narration (Surah 12:30–34).16 This section, which is not included in the Biblical account, features heavily in later Islamic interpretations of the Joseph story.
A curious feature of Surah 12 is that Joseph’s pretty coat is nowhere to be found (nor is the double dream report: only the dream regarding the stars is mentioned). Rather, there is a matter of fact report of the brothers presenting Joseph’s shirt, ‘deceptively stained with blood’ to their father.17 Other mentions of clothing include the narration of Potiphar’s wife tearing Joseph’s shirt from behind. This provokes the astute observation of a household member,
12 A.M.A. Haleem, The Qur’an, Oxford, New York 2004, 145, 152. 13 Haleem, The Qur’an, 145. 14 Haleem, The Qur’an, 151. 15 Haleem, The Qur’an, 152. 16 Haleem, The Qur’an, 147. 17 Haleem, The Qur’an, 146.



More than just a Pretty Coat 189
who claims that the location of the tear is indicative of the guilty party in the seduction scene: if torn in the front, it would be Joseph’s doing; if torn in the back, Joseph was running away, hence the wife’s doing.18 The final mention of clothing is found in another episode not present in the biblical text: while caravanning to Egypt, Joseph’s father, though blind, claims to smell Joseph. Then a ‘bearer of good news’ places ‘the’ shirt over the father’s eyes and he is healed from his blindness.19
3 History of Interpretation/Reception History
The earliest interpretation of the Joseph story is, of course, found within the Jewish community. Since the early Christians shared the same text, they also share some of these perspectives, while simultaneously expanding on many of them by incorporating a uniquely Christian viewpoint. In later interpretation, the Joseph Story becomes a cultural icon, as explained by Bernard Lang in his volume ‘Joseph in Egypt: A Cultural Icon from Grotius to Goethe’.20 Lang organizes his analysis around the topics of piety, Joseph’s handsome appearance and his emergence as a statesman, all of which were part of general cultural knowledge in the 18th century. Joseph’s faithfulness to his Lord and his handsome appearance are two aspects of the story that are discussed across traditions. These topics are especially prevalent in interpretation of the Qur’anic version. However, the Qur’an itself downplays Joseph’s role as statesman while later Jewish and Christian interpretations give this topic a good deal of attention. Because there is no dearth of interpretive material, this paper will concentrate upon a few examples from each tradition with comments upon the emergence of trends within and across traditions.
3.1 Jewish Tradition
Ancient Jewish interpreters have much to say about the Joseph story. However, according to Kugel, the Joseph story is more straightforward than some other biblical material; thus ancient Jewish interpreters tended to retell the Joseph story with an emphasis upon Joseph’s virtues and to look closely at small
18 Haleem, The Qur’an, 146. 19 Haleem, The Qur’an, 151. 20 Lang’s categories include ‘Icon of Piety: Joseph for Children’; ‘Icon of Chastity: the Handsome Hebrew’ and ‘Icon of Leadership: Joseph the Statesman’; see B. Lang, Joseph in Egypt: A Cultural Icon from Grotius to Goethe, New Haven 2009.



190 Hayes
details.21 One such example is taken from Midrash Abkir in Yalqut Shimoni Gen. 39:14, written in response to the sudden switch from singular forms to plural in Genesis 39:14.22 In this passage, Potiphar’s wife attempts to engage those around her in making the accusation that Joseph had tried to rape them as well. Genesis 39:14 reads ‘. . . she called out to her servants and said to them, “Look, he had to bring us a Hebrew to dally with us!”. Midrash Abkir explains that:
On that particular day, they all went to their idolatrous rites, but she made herself out to be sick. When her [female] friends came back, they went to visit her. They said to her: what is wrong that your face is thus? She told them the entire episode. They said to her: you have no remedy but to tell your husband thus and so, so that he will shut him [Joseph] up in prison. She said to them: I beg of you – each of you say that he also sought the same from you. And so they did.23
Suddenly, not only Potiphar’s wife, but a cadre of friends as well, is included in making the false accusation. This elevation of minor characters to major dramatis personae is also evident in the Qur’an. There, the Egyptian ladies both accuse Joseph (Surah 12, 30–31) and confess their falsehood (Surah 12.51). As Maren Niehoff observes, various early Jewish interpreters such as Philo, Josephus and Genesis Rabbah each view the character of Joseph in slightly different ways.24 In her volume, The Figure of Joseph in Post-biblical Jewish Literature, Niehoff notes that Philo approaches Joseph from the perspective of his own Greek background and is influenced by two types of biography: allegorical and political. Thus, his paraphrase of the Joseph story both allegorizes certain aspects of Joseph’s life and places an emphasis upon the political aspects of Joseph’s career.25 Josephus views Joseph through the lens of his own experiences particularly that of his relationship with other key figures who participated in the Jewish revolt. Josephus emphasizes Joseph’s innocence with regard to his behavior with his brothers.26 Finally, Niehoff notes that in Genesis
21 J.L. Kugel, Traditions of the Bible: A Guide to the Bible as It Was at the Start of the Common Era, Cambridge MA, London 1998, 438. 22 Kugel, Traditions of the Bible, 449. 23 Kugel, Traditions of the Bible, 449. 24 M. Niehoff, The Figure of Joseph in Post-Biblical Jewish Literature (Arbeiten zur Geschichte des Antiken Judentums und des Urchristentums 16), Leiden, 1992. 25 Niehoff, The Figure of Joseph, 143. 26 Niehoff, The Figure of Joseph, 144.



More than just a Pretty Coat 191
Rabbah, Joseph is subject to Divine retribution. She claims that his early misdeeds, including his behavior with his brothers, provide the reasons for his later punishment. Of these three approaches, Philo’s political take on Joseph contains important but often overlooked observations: Philo places emphasis upon his rise to power: the rise to power that not only fulfills the early dreams, but in turn makes a way for the survival of the people of the covenant, albeit in servitude. In this, the Joseph narrative serves as a link between the previous Abrahamic narrative and the Exodus narrative to follow.
3.2 Christian Tradition
As previously mentioned, early Christian interpreters shared some early Jewish interpretations while adding a uniquely Christian twist to others. Interpreters such as Ambrose and Chrysostom used the Joseph story as a preaching text. Chrysostom, in his ‘Homilies on Genesis 61.10’ declares that the events surrounding Joseph and his brothers were a ‘type of things to come, the outlines of truth being sketched out ahead of time in shadow.’ He states:
As Joseph went off to his brothers to visit them, to those who had no respect for brotherhood or for the reason of his coming and who first intended to do away with him and then sold him to foreigners, so too our Lord in fidelity to his characteristic love came to visit the human race. Taking flesh of the same source as ours and deigning to become our brother, he thus arrived among us.27
Chrysostom’s typological approach served to knit the Old Testament with the New, creating Christian theological constructions that included information from both Old and New Testament texts. Later Christian interpreters such as the eighteenth century Swiss reformed pastor Johann Caspar Lavater looked upon the Joseph story as a source of moral teaching. Lavater himself spent much time with this story, preaching and teaching adults and children alike. Lavater’s retelling of ‘Joseph’ for children follows the biblical text, while also being interwoven with numerous short commentaries on obedience, faithfulness and other virtues. Regarding the incident with Potiphar’s wife, he teaches:
Falsely accused by his master’s wife, the innocent boy was thrown into prison. Yet even there he did not despair – No, dear child, God never forgets
27 M. Sheridan and Th.C. Oden, Genesis 12–50 (Ancient Christian Commentary on Scripture Old Testament), Downers Grove 2002, 236.



192 Hayes
and forsakes anyone, least of all, those who put their trust in him. Whoever suffers for the sake of virtue cannot be unhappy at the bottom of his heart. Contrary to what we may think. – Joseph said to himself, “It is because I wanted to obey God that I am in fetters, God Knows that I am in prison and why I am here.”28
Lavater’s emphasis upon Joseph as a moral character provided life lessons for his hearers, adult and child alike. The tendencies to reinterpret Joseph in light of Jesus and to view the story as a moral tale are two consistent trends in Christian interpretation, while somewhat less is made of Joseph’s political rise to power.
3.3 Islamic Tradition
The telling and retelling of the Joseph story in the Qur’an and in Islamic tradition is intriguing, indeed, for it is here that the story is presented as scripture and enjoyed as ‘the best of stories’ as well. My own first encounter with Joseph from an Islamic perspective was through conversations with Maryam, my flat mate in Oxford one summer. She was writing her master’s thesis on a group of 16th century Persian miniatures from Iran. Maryam’s retelling of the Joseph story echoed Zulaykah and Yusuf, a poem by the 15th century Persian poet, Jami. Jami recast the Joseph story as a love poem with two strong characters, Yusuf and Zulaykah (Potiphar’s now-named wife). Maryam’s story followed Jami’s ordering of events: after making the false accusation, Zulaykah’s friends confront her, saying: ‘What do you want with him? He’s a foreigner?’ She replies, ‘Well, I’ll show you!’ She calls her female friends in for a meal, providing them with oranges and knives . . . and stunned by his beauty, they swoon and cut themselves. After Zulaykah is punished, she ends up married to Yusuf and they live happily ever after. This correlates to the findings of Merguerian and Najmabadi, who, noting the wide reuse of the Qur’anic version of the Joseph story in written works of many genres, observe that the Joseph story is attractive in part because of its ability to travel between genres. They argue skillfully that in its journey from Qur’an to commentary to history and ethics and from there to love poetry and popular tales, reuse and interpretation of the Joseph story has followed a trend. This trend began with the Qur’anic story of a prophet which, by accretion, acquired elements of ‘the guile of women’ stories, moved on to become a love story and finally became a moral tale.29 For the
28 Lang, Joseph in Egypt, 68.
29 Goldman notes that women (and men) in the ancient Near East used their wiles to further their cause, both in the Bible and the Qur’an; see Sh. Goldman, The Wiles of Women/



More than just a Pretty Coat 193
Islamic communities, this moral tale has become a cultural icon that affects ‘the cultural construction of gender in Islamic societies.’30
4 Joseph as Cultural Icon
Around the same time Maryam and I were flat mates for the summer, British TV was broadcasting a talent show called ‘Any Dream will Do’, which was essentially a ‘Joseph-off’ to determine which of 12 potential Josephs would make the final cut for a re-mounting of Andrew Lloyd Webber’s ‘Joseph and the Amazing Technicolor Dream Coat’. The popularity of this musical, which remains quite close to the biblical account, demonstrates that the Joseph story continues to be a popular cultural icon outside of synagogue and church as well. Lloyd Webber’s musical skills and dramatic flair make it an attractive story and he keeps to the narrative line found in the biblical version. The delightfully ‘70’s lyrics in the number ‘Potiphar’ address the situation between Joseph and Potiphar’s wife. Mrs. Potiphar’s sultry ‘Come and lie with me love’ draws a response from the narrator, who sings: ‘Joseph wanted to resist her, Till one day she proved too eager, Joseph cried in vain . . .’ followed by Joseph’s response, ‘Please stop! I don’t believe in free love!’31 This comical exchange is totally befitting of the turmoil surrounding 70’s sexual revolution, yet faithfully addresses Joseph’s dilemma at the hands of Mrs. Potiphar.
5 In Conclusion
This comparison between the Biblical and Qur’anic versions of the Joseph story and their interpretation demonstrates that the two traditions both converge and diverge in interpretive stance, but that the story remained suitable for groups of people who needed to remember that they had received promises from God. Some early Christian interpreters took a typological approach, tying OT and NT together. Islamic versions move from a test of prophet-hood tale, suitable for actualization by a limited number, to the explorations of the
the Wiles of Men: Joseph and Potiphar’s Wife in Ancient near Eastern, Jewish, and Islamic Folklore, Albany 1995, 47–54. 30 G.K. Merguerian and A. Najmabadi, ‘Zulaykha and Yusuf: Whose “Best Story” ’, Int. J. Middle East Stud. 29 (1997), 485–508. 31 See http://www.stlyrics.com/lyrics/josephandtheamazingtechnicolordreamcoat/poti phar.htm; Accessed 3 June 2014.



194 Hayes
story such as those in Persian art and poetry that render the story in an engaging way that is attractive to broad audiences. Even modern musical theatre, using the biblical narrative structure, creates an effective retelling by clothing the story in modern guise, a story that has broad cultural appeal as well. Each of the multiple Josephs serves to illustrate ideas important in given cultural contexts. They seem to contain a common thread: that of the more universal ideals of faithfulness and obedience.
