From Noah to Nuḥ: The Making of a Prophet
Marcel Poorthuis
1 Introduction
Noah features as a prophet in Qurʾān and post-Qur’anic literature. Whereas Western Christianity is accustomed to reserve that title for prophets after the promulgation of the Mosaic law, Islam does not hesitate to reckon Nuḥ (henceforth: Noah) to the prophetic ranks. Noah is called both rasūl (messenger) and nabī, a double honor vouchsafed to Ishmael, Moses, Jesus and Muhammad only.1 In Noah’s case, some remarkable divergences from the Biblical account can be pointed out. In the Bible, there is the flood, but there is no explicit mention of warning the people of the inherent danger. On the contrary, the catastrophe of the Flood seems to unfold itself without any indication that the repentance of the people might have prevented it. God sees the evil, announces the impending disaster to Noah and Noah starts to build the ark (Gen. 6:5–22). In contrast, the Qurʾān points time and again to Noah’s plea to the people to listen to his message. Noah even accuses the people of stupidity for their refusal to realize the consequences of their disobedience. There is no doubt that Muhammad’s own bitter experience with a ‘stubborn’ audience finds its echoes in Noah’s plight. The people accuse Noah of being nothing more than a mere mortal and not an angel, as they would have expected (Q. 23:24). Noah emphasizes that he is not an angel and does not pretend to be one (Q. 6:50). Exactly the same happens with Muhammad in confrontation with his audience (Q. 6:8–9; 11:12; 25:7).2 Noah warns his people time and again, but they put their fingers in their ears (Surat Nuḥ Q. 71:1–21). The difference with the Biblical Noah is striking indeed. With the possible exception of Abraham, there is hardly a Biblical figure with whom Muhammad identified so strongly as with Noah. We might assume that Noah’s persistent plea to the people to repent has been developed out of this resemblance with Muhammad’s plea to his own ‘stubborn’ audience. This, however, is not the case, as I will demonstrate. Pre-Islamic sources are familiar as well with a warning Noah. From the point of view of method, a comparison of the Biblical account with Qur’anic and post-Qur’anic literature in order to trace influences has only limited value. In the more than thousand years between the Bible and the Qurʾān, a wealth of commentaries, retold
1 See: R. Tottoli, Biblical Prophets in the Qurʾān and Muslim Literature, Richmond 2002, 73. 2 Tottoli, Biblical Prophets, 52, n. 12.



From Noah to Nuḥ: The Making of a Prophet 215
Biblical histories, embellishments and paraphrasing translations have seen the light. This milieu, consisting of Judaism and Eastern Christianity as well as of a plethora of Jewish-Christian currents and ‘sects’, forms the lens through which the Qurʾān and the post-Qur’anic stories, the Qiṣaṣ al-Anbiyāʾ, perceive the patriarchs and prophets. This is not to say that these stories were always communicated in written form by adherents of those religions: oral stories and testimonies of converts to Islam may have been responsible for it as well. Neither should we think of a slavish adoption of these stories by Islam. It can be proven that these stories were subjected to a highly creative process of Islamization. Scholarly theories about a Jewish original, copied by Islam, but sometimes only half understood, betray a biased perspective, which does not reckon with this process of Islamization. In spite of that, the quest for Jewish and Christian sources of the Islam remains indispensable in order to understand this process of Islamization. As to Noah, the Qurʾān gives the impression that the audience of Muhammad had already been familiar with some basic elements of the stories about this figure. Hence the Qurʾān is not a revelation of stories that were totally unknown, but admonishes an audience by referring to moral examples that are more or less familiar to them. There is no doubt that all post-Qur’anic literature is heavily indebted to the Qurʾān itself. It can be proven, however, that pre-Qur’anic literature did have its share in shaping post-Qur’anic literature. By going backward from the Tales of the Prophets to pre-Islamic literature, this hermeneutical process of adopting and transforming existing stories may be clarified. By doing so, we will be able to offer a more refined explanation of the making of the Islamic Noah. For our purpose we will focus upon the episode of the preparation of the ark. The method of tracing the sources to their origin has been inspired by Geza Vermes’ generic research on midrash. Starting with a retold Biblical story, elaborately retold in late midrashic literature, he traces the way back to the Bible itself.3 Still, some of his presuppositions should be challenged, e.g. it is not certain that the later sources are the most elaborate. This becomes all the more essential when one wishes to cross the boundaries between Judaism, Christianity and Islam. Older studies are often limited to listing the sources, implicitly assuming a one-sided borrowing from Judaism by Islam.4 My research
3 G. Vermes, Scripture and Tradition in Judaism: Haggadic Studies, Leiden 1961; idem, Postbiblical Jewish Studies, Leiden 1975.
4 See the wealth of sources in: M. Grünbaum, Neue Beiträge zur semitischen Sagenkunde, Leiden 1893; more limited D. Sidersky, Les origines des légendes musulmanes dans le Coran et dans Les Vies des Prophètes. Paris 1933.



216 Poorthuis
will try to proceed further, while avoiding these traditional pitfalls.5 I will start then with the youngest literature and work our way back via the Qurʾān to Christian and Jewish sources subsequent to the Bible. It is not my aim to trace the transmission of motifs precisely from one author to another: this would require an exhaustive research of the whole early Islamic literature and the isnād chains (i.e. the list of authoritative transmitters). By choosing three different representative authors I will offer a sample of this literature, exemplified by Noah. Wherever I point out Jewish and Christian predecessors, I do not suggest a direct borrowing, but argue for ultimate influence upon our Islamic authors, often via earlier Islamic sources.6
2 The Qiṣaṣ al-Anbiyāʾ on Noah
The Tales of the Prophets by al-Kisaʾī (approximately 13th century CE) contain quite a few embellishments and digressions of a miraculous nature. Although this collection obviously draws upon the Qurʾān and upon post-Qur’anic collections of stories, it contains motifs not found in previous collections.7
It is Gabriel who gave Noah the glad tidings of his prophethood and apostleship to his people. (. . .) Drawing near, the angel clothed Noah with the garb of God’s warriors, bound his head with the Turban of victory, girt him with the sword of Splendor and said: “Go to Darmesel son of Avel, son of Lamel and his people and call them to worship God”. Then he departed and ascended back into heaven. It turned out be one of their festivals. From the time of Cain (Qābīl) it was their habit to take out the idols, carry them on thrones and bring sacrifices. (. . .) After that they drank wine, beat tambourines, danced and unashamedly fornicated like beasts with the women.
When Noah cries that they have to obey God only, the idols topple from their pedestals and the king likewise falls from his throne. Angrily he orders Noah to
5 See for some additional methodological prolegomena my article: ‘Hagar’s Wanderings: Between Judaism and Islam’, Der Islam 90/2 (2013), 213–237. 6 See for a survey of the sources of al-Ṭabarī and other authors: T. Nagel, Die Qiṣaṣ al-Anbiyāʾ. Ein Beitrag zur arabischen Literaturgeschichte, Bonn 1967. 7 See: I. Eisenberg (ed.), Vita prophetarum auctore Muhammed ben ʿAbdallah al-Kisaʾī, pars secunda, Brill Leiden 1923, 87; translation in: W. Thackston (tr.), Tales of the Prophets, Muhammad ibn ʿAbdallah al-Kisaʾī, Chicago 1987, 93.



From Noah to Nuḥ: The Making of a Prophet 217
appear before him: “I am a messenger of the Lord of the Universe” (rasūl Rabbi al-Ālamīna), Noah states. Further on he explains: “All I need is for you to say that there is no god but God (Allah) and that I, Noah, am the messenger (rasūl) of God”. The differences with the Biblical account are striking indeed. The Bible does not know of a specific idolatrous cult practised by the people of the Flood, who are, moreover, not explicitly identified with pedigrees of Cain. Obviously, the theme of the feast serves to highlight their idolatrous, orgiastic behavior. The idols toppling from their pedestal is a motif that has been used centuries earlier to describe what happened when the child Jesus entered the land of Egypt (Gospel of Pseudo-Matthew 23). However, the motif is probably more directly derived from Muhammad’s cleansing of the Kaʿba from its 360 idols, as told in the ḥadith.8 Noah introduces himself with words that exactly parallel the Shahada, the Islamic confession of faith, except that Muhammad’s name is replaced by Noah’s. Noah is called the rasūl Allah. His vocation by the angel Gabriel parallels Muhammad’s own vocation, as told in the Sīra. The Qurʾān portrays Muhammad in colors and traits similar to Muhammad’s, as we will see, but here even the reverse takes place: Noah is described with traits belonging exclusively to Muhammad. The story continues by relating how Noah calls upon the people to worship God and to cease their disobedience. This he does for three centuries! However, Noah is beaten by women and children. Then he prays two rakʿas, begging that his patience may increase. In the fourth century, the people put their fingers in their ears and stone him. Noah is revived by the wings of birds sprinkling water on his face. The people consider Noah a madman. At the end Noah prays to God to destroy this people. Only then God commands him to build the ark, together with Gabriel and Noah’s children and faithful followers. The people mock: “You began to take up prophecy; now you have taken up carpentry. We complain of drought and you are building for a flood. All this is caused by your madness”. The people try to set fire to the ark, but cannot harm it. Worms, however, damage the ark until upon God’s behest Noah use four pegs upon which the names of Muhammad’s companions are written. Even then the people could have been saved as the ark itself started to speak: “There is no God but God and Noah is God’s prophet (nabī)”. Still, the people did not believe and Noah makes a pilgrimage (Hajj) and sees the Kaʿba. To protect the ark during his absence, angels lift it up into the sky. This whole event took place where now the mosque of Kufa stands. Noah’s family enters the Ark, together with Adam’s body.
8 See: Bukhari, Saḥiḥ, Volume 5, book 59, no. 583.



218 Poorthuis
The story is interspersed with miraculous events. Obviously Noah is strongly islamicized: as a Muslim he conducts the rakʿas and the hajj and passes by the Kaʿba. The mosque of Kufa (Iraq) still marks Noah’s doings, we are told. The story is even more Muhammadocentric than the Qurʾān: Noah’s stories are read and retold in the light of Muhammad’s experiences.9 Strikingly differing from the Biblical account is the emphasis upon the opportunity offered to the people to repent: no less than four centuries long. Still, at the end there is once more a call to repent. Noah is called both messenger and prophet, probably because the call to repent is considered the hallmark of prophecy. Searching our way back we come across Al-Thaʿlabī (11th century CE).10
3 Al-Thaʿlabī
Ibn Abbās11 told that there were two clans of the children of Adam, one in the plain and one upon the mountain.12 The women of the plain were beautiful, as were the men on the mountain. Iblis disguised himself as a lad and introduced some sort of shepherd’s flute, which formed the basis of a yearly festival full of immorality. God sent Noah, who was by then 50 years old, and he stayed with them for one thousand less fifty years, calling them to God and warning them.
9 I owe the term ‘Muhammadocentric’ to M. Accad, ‘Muhammad’s Advent as the Final Criterion for the Authenticity of the Judeo-Christian Tradition’, in: B. Roggema, M. Poorthuis, P. Valkenberg (eds), The Three Rings. Textual Studies in the Historical Trialogue of Judaism, Christianity and Islam, Leuven 2005, 217–236.
10 See: Al-Thaʾlabī, ʿArāʾis al-Majālis fī Qisas al-Anbiyāʾ, Beyruth 2004, 48–54; Al-Thaʾlabī, ʿArāʾis al-Majālis fī Qisas al-Anbiyāʾ or “Lives of the Prophets”, translated and annotated by W. Brinner, Leiden 2002, 92–104; M. Klar, Interpreting al-Thaʾlabī’s Tales of the Prophets, Temptation, Responsibility and Loss, London 2009; Nagel, Die Qiṣaṣ al-Anbiyāʾ, 80; W.A. Saleh, The Formation of the Classical Tafsīr Tradition. The Qurʾān Commentary of al-Thaʾlabī, Leiden 2004.
11 Ibn Abbās is a highly important spokesman of Isrāʾīlliyyāt from the 7th century CE, and Muhammad’s uncle. Whether this attribution can be trusted remains uncertain, as many stories were told in his name because of this reputation. See for Ibn Abbās: I. Wolfensohn, Kaʾb al-Aḥbar und seine Stellung im Ḥadīt und in der islamitischen Legendenliteratur, Jerusalem 1933, 42–47. 12 As we will point out further on, in Syriac Christianity this story is based upon a demythologizing interpretation of the “sons of God descending to the daughters of men” (Gen. 6). Hence not angels but descendants of Seth still living on the slopes of the Paradise mountain and later on descending to the children of Cain in the plain, are the protagonists. The first to introduce this explanation is the Christian author Julius Africanus, end second century CE.



From Noah to Nuḥ: The Making of a Prophet 219
Finally God revealed that no one would believe (Q. 11:36: “None of your people shall believe, but he who already believed”). Noah quotes the Qurʾān (71:24), in which he accuses them of idolatry for their statement: “Don’t give up your gods. Don’t give up Wadd, Suwāʿ, Yaghūh, Yaʿūq and Nasr. They have led many astray”.13 Noah planted trees in order to get the wood for the ark. After forty years he cut them down. Incidentally, this timespan cannot be counted as an opportunity to repent, for God had already stated that no one would repent any more. The people mock Noah when he starts to build the ark. They laughed at him and this was as he had said. “So he was building the ark and whenever a council of his people passed by him they scoffed at him. And Noah said: ‘If you scoff at us, we will surely scoff at you’ ” (Q. 11:38). When the Ark is finished, Adam’s body is brought into the Ark, serving as a barrier between the men and the women, according to Ibn Abbās. The structure of this account is different from al-Kisaʾī’s, as here the integration of the Qur’anic verses into the narrative is dominant. Both narratives know of a pagan festival, but al-Thaʿlabī offers an aetiological explanation both of the people – the mingling of the children of Cain and the children of Seth and of the festival itself. His emphasis upon the occasion to repent is similar to al Kisaʾī’s, but different in detail: Noah preaches for 950 years to the people (cp. Q. 29:14). There is less emphasis upon the miraculous in this account. Noah is not explicitly called a prophet.
4 Al-Ṭabarī
In a different genre, that of a world chronicle, al-Ṭabarī (839–923 CE) relates the story of Noah as well, sometimes in different versions. Unlike in his monumental commentary on the Qurʾān, he does not decide in his Taʾrikh (Chronicle) which version he deems correct. He aims at historical identifications. The people in Noah’s time committed wickedness and drank wine and were preoccupied with musical instruments rather than with God (I:184). In conformity with the Qurʾān (71:24), Noah accuses them of idolatry for their statement: “Don’t give up your gods. Don’t give up Wadd, Suwāʿ, Yaghūh, Yaʿūq and Nasr. They have led many astray”.
13 The same quotation can be found in al-Tabarī, Taʾrīkh (ed. De Goeje) I, 184; The History of al-Ṭabarī, From the Creation to the Flood (tr. F. Rosenthal), New York 1987, p. 354. The interpretation is difficult for it seems that the first line quotes the people themselves and the last line a critical voice, God, Muhammad or Noah.



220 Poorthuis
Al-Tabarī (Taʾrikh I:185) offers several figures of how old Noah was when he, the first prophet, started to preach: 50 years, 350 years, 480 years. In any case he preached several decades, if not centuries. Then Noah plants a tree upon God’s command and after 40 years cuts it down. Passers-by ask him why he builds an ark on dry land. Noah waited generation after generation. The people started to choke him until he became unconscious. When he regained consciousness he prayed: “O God! Forgive my people for they do not know!” Finally he complained to God about their behavior and said: “My God! I have called my people night and day, but my calling has only made them shun me more” (Q. 71:5). ( . . ) “My Lord! Let no unbeliever stay on earth!” (Q. 71:26). People mock him and ask why he was first a prophet and now a carpenter. For the details of the ark, al-Ṭabarī refers to the people of the Torah. Al-Ṭabarī is less clear about the festival, but like al-Thaʿlabī he uses an aetiological explanation of musical instruments to explain the debauchery. Noah is molested but ready to forgive. It was only after more than hundred years at the least, that Noah begins to despair. Noah takes Adam’s body into the Ark where it serves as a barrier between the men and the women (I:193). There is a debate whether Adam’s body goes to a Cave or to Jerusalem (I:163). Al-Ṭabarī does not seem to be familiar with the story of the children of Seth on the mountain and the daughters of Cain on the plain. It means that al-Thaʿlabī must have used other sources than al-Ṭabarī only, with whose work he was probably acquainted.14 I will argue that here a crossing-over between Islam and Christianity must have taken place. Although I do not suggest that al-Thaʿlabī had direct access to the Christian document of the Cave of Treasures, there can be no doubt that ultimately his story goes back to it. The sons of Seth and the daughters of Cain play a leading role in it, whereas the Qurʾān does not refer to this episode at all.
5 The Christian Evidence
With the Cave of Treasures, a retelling of the Biblical story from the 6th century CE, containing older material from the 4th century CE,15 we enter the Christian
14 Klar, Interpreting al-Thaʾlabī’s Tales of the Prophets, 10. Her research is limited to Islamic sources. 15 Su-Min Ri, Commentaire de la Caverne des Trésors, Louvain 2000, defends the 4th century CE for the Syriac document as such.



From Noah to Nuḥ: The Making of a Prophet 221
fold.16 This document attaches great importance to genealogical connections. Prophecy, kingship and priesthood are transmitted from Adam on, and his body has been brought from the Cave of Treasures to the ark. The evil on earth had been spread by the daughters of Cain, who, induced by demonic flutes made by Jubal and cymbals and tambourines made by Tubal-Cain, committed all kinds of debauchery. The old women were even more passionately enraged than the young ones and father and daughter and brother and sister mingled. The children of Seth became curious because of the noise and descended to mingle with them. The nude daughters of Cain threw themselves upon them like wild beasts. Noah remained celibate during 500 years, after which he married Haikal, Enoch’s granddaughter. God commanded Noah to build the ark, because after 130 years there would be the flood. Noah had to beat on the wood of the ark three times a day, to call the artisans to their work and to indicate the time to eat. The people would hear this sound and Noah had to tell them that God would send the flood. The interior of the ark mirrors the church: the separation between men and women, the orientation towards the East, the peace between the different inhabitants, the sacrifice of bread and wine, the body of Adam serving as bema, “all the mysteries of the Church were in the ark” (XVIII:3). The Cave of Treasures emphasizes again the warning to the people by mentioning 130 years during which there is the sound of hammering on wood. Hammering on wood is a habit still practised in some Eastern convents to call the people to the prayer service.17 There is, however, no mention of a reaction from the people, such as mocking or molesting Noah. The origin of the pagan atrocities is related extensively, without, however, suggesting that a festival had taken place. The children of Cain and of Seth are assumed to have mingled, which has caused the debauchery. Noah is not called a prophet, but his threefold ministry – priest, king and prophet – is implied via Adam, and will be transmitted until Christ at the end. Adam’s body will be brought to Jerusalem, to Golgotha. Adam’s body in Jerusalem is part of a profound Christian
16 Su-Min Ri (ed. and tr.), La Caverne des trésors. Les deux recensions Syriaques (CSCO 207), Leuven 1987, 33. 17 Related texts such as The Conflict of Adam and Eve with Satan (see hereafter), speak about trumpets calling the people to repent (III.8). An Arabic catena, ascribed to pseudoHippolyte, but in reality related to the Cave of Treasures, be it probably some centuries later, knows of a bell. See for this last document: P. de Lagarde, Materialien zur Kritk und Geschichte der Pentateuch, Leipzig 1867, 1–182, esp. 72; and my article: ‘Tradition and Religious Authority’, Hebrew Union College Annual 66 (1995), 187–200.



222 Poorthuis
theology in which Adam reaches salvation by contact with the redeeming blood of Christ. A document identical or strongly comparable to this one must have formed an important source of some of the Islamic accounts.18 Christian-Arabic translations of this document existed from the 7th century onward.19 Traces of the use of the Cave of Treasures have been documented in Islamic papyri from about 800 CE.20 All three authors quoted above seem to contain elements of the story as documented in the Cave of Treasures. Al-Ṭabarī and al-Kisaʾī know of the demonic origin of the flute. However, as stated above it is al-Thaʿlabī who shows the most striking parallels with the Cave of Treasures, such as the daughters of Cain, and the sons of Seth, the demonic musical instruments, and the transport of Adam’s body into the Ark (although he may have derived this motif from al-Ṭabarī). Fortunately, we can be somewhat more precise about the ways Islamic authors could have derived motifs from the Cave of Treasures. With the author al-Yaʿqūbī we tread on solid ground.
6 Al-Yaʿqūbī
Al-Yaʿqūbī (9th century CE), one of the earliest Islamic historians, has reworked the Cave of Treasures in his Kitāb al-Taʾrīkh into an Islamic story by condensing and removing all Christian elements, such as: the gold, myrrh and frankincense of Adam, his threefold ministry, the Ark as symbol of the Church, the sacrifice of bread and wine, and the ark making the sign of the cross.21 He maintained the motif of Noah as a celibate during 500 years, though.22 As Al-Yaʿqūbī knew Syriac, his source must have been the Syriac version of the Cave of Treasures
18 Later re-workings of the Cave of Treasures, such as S.C. Malan (ed.), The Book of Adam and Eve, or The Conflict of Adam and Eve with Satan, London 1882 (= A. Dillmann, Das christliche Adambuch des Morgenlandes, Göttingen 1853), should not be confused with the Cave of Treasures itself, as J.P. Lewis, A Study of the Interpretation of Noah and the Flood in Jewish and Christian literature, Leiden 1968, 34, n. 3 does. This latter document, which survived in Ethiopic, does not seem to have influenced our Arabic writers. 19 Su-Min Ri, Commentaire de la Caverne des Trésors, 63–66. 20 See N. Abbot, Studies in Arabic Literary Papyri I, Historical Texts, Chicago 1955, 38 ff. 21 See: G. Smit, Bijbel en legende bij den Arabischen schrijver Yaʾqubi, 9e eeuw na Christus, Leiden 1907; E.L. Daniel, ‘Al-Yaʾqūbī and Shiʾism reconsidered’, in: J.E. Montgomery (ed.), ʿAbbasid studies; Occasional Papers of the School of ʿAbbasid Studies, Leuven 2004, 209–239. 22 This motif is known in midrash and Christian sources (see J.P. Lewis, Study of the Interpretation., 35, n. 2). Its featuring in Islamic writers is best explained as derived from the Cave of Treasures, possibly via Yaʾqūbī.



From Noah to Nuḥ: The Making of a Prophet 223
and not an Arabic translation. His work may have been the source of all later Islamic authors using motifs that parallel the Cave of Treasures.23 In fact, his work shows a consistent attempt to harmonize the Qur’anic account with the story of the Cave of Treasures, although he uses less explicit quotations from the Qurʾān than our authors quoted above. The most conspicuous difference is that the Qurʾān portrays Noah as preaching to “his people”, whereas the Cave of Treasures speaks about the mixed race of sons of Seth and daughters of Cain. Hence, it is plausible that Islamic authors such as al-Ṭabarī and especially al-Thaʿlabī have used al-Yaʿqūbī as their source for a story identical or highly similar to the Cave of Treasures. These authors face the challenge to integrate this source together with the sometimes disparate verses of the Qurʾān about Noah into one coherent story. The absence of specific Christian elements in these Islamic authors is best explained by pointing to al-Yaʿqūbī’s version, in which the Christian elements have already been removed. Having stated this thus far, we have answered the question of the origin of the embellishments of the Noah story in post-Qur’anic literature. However, the Islamic motif of Noah preaching like a prophet and offering the people ample time to repent can be found in the Qurʾān itself. This motif is itself not caused by a parallel to Muhammad’s preaching – although it certainly reinforces that motif – but it ultimately stems from earlier Christian or Jewish sources as well. We have not yet answered why especially Christian sources put such an emphasis upon Noah preaching like a prophet. To answer that question, we have to address ourselves to another Syriac source: Ephrem the Syrian.
7 Ephrem Syrus
Ephrem Syrus (4th century CE) is often considered as a writer akin to the Cave of Treasures.24 Still, in his Commentary on Genesis he puts the emphasis quite differently, although some details are similar. He relates how the sons of Seth mingled with the daughters of Cain. Then he quotes the Bible:
The Lord saw that the wickedness of man had become great on earth (Gen. 6:5).
23 Still we do not know whether the earlier traces of the Cave of Treasures in Islamic papyri have constituted a whole document. Several trajectories are possible, even when we leave out the possibility of oral communication. 24 Su-Min Ri, Commentaire de la Caverne des Trésors, claims that Ephrem has used the Cave of Treasures.



224 Poorthuis
Then Ephrem quotes a Biblical verse that requires an elaborate explanation:
The Lord said: I will destroy man whom I have created from the face of the earth; both man and beast and the creeping thing and the fowls of the air; for I repent that I have made man (6:7).
This verse might suggest that God had been mistaken. Hence Ephrem continues: not because God did not see this beforehand, but He wanted to reveal the wickedness for the generations to come. God, who does not know remorse, was brought to remorse by their wickedness. Moreover, God hoped that the people would feel remorse upon hearing that the Being that does not know remorse would have remorse, in Ephrem’s highly paradoxical statement. In other words, God’s initiative was not a prediction, but was meant to bring the people to repentance. Noah built the ark in a hundred years and during that whole period the people did not repent. On the contrary, they mocked Noah: “How can animals and birds be brought together from all directions of the earth?” The animals were brought together in a miraculous way, but even then the people did not repent. Then God spoke: “About seven days I will destroy all flesh that I have made” (Gen. 7:4). Even now God offered them postponement. Initially they had been granted a postponement of 120 years (cp. Gen. 6:3), but because of the bad experience of 100 years this period was shortened by 20 years. Even that was beneficial, for it implied that the sinners were 20 years less guilty. With Ephrem we enter a wholesale theological debate about God’s justice. Ephrem seems to defend himself against an imaginary opponent, who accuses God of being not all-knowing, of knowing remorse about decisions, and of being eager to destroy mankind. Ephrem emphasizes how much time the people have received: 100 years (although the Bible according to Ephrem’s reading had even promised 120 years).25 Noah’s situation hardly receives any attention: he is not molested and his despair is hardly mentioned. Ephrem offers an exegesis of Scripture in the same way the Islamic authors al-Ṭabarī and al-Thaʿlabī offer an exegesis of the Qurʾān. The calculation of the periods is derived from Scripture by an ingenious exegesis. Like al-Thaʿlabī, Ephrem explicitly links the descent of the sons of God to the daughters of man with the Flood, in a highly similar explanation, but of course here connected to exegesis of Scripture, i.c. Genesis 6:1. Still, Ephrem is far more theologically pre-occupied than the Islamic authors.
25 Cp. Aphraat, Demonstratio II.9 and VII:8.



From Noah to Nuḥ: The Making of a Prophet 225
Who may have been Ephrem’s imaginary opponent? There is no doubt that he was thoroughly acquainted both with Marcion and with Valentinian Gnostics, who exercised their influence from the second century CE onward.26 Both considered the flood an act unworthy of the highest god. According to Marcion, the god of the Old Testament is the creator-Demiurge and a just lawgiver, but should be distinguished from the loving Father of Christ. This unknown god of love has nothing to do with creation and commandments. Irenaeus reports that, according to Marcion, the rebels against the Demiurge such as Cain, the generation of Sodom and the Egyptians, will be saved by Christ, whereas Abel, Enoch and Noah will not share in the salvation (Adversus haereses I:27,3).
The Gnosis knew of an even a more aggressive condemnation of the Flood and of Noah. Curiously, Noah’s wife, who is portrayed as disobedient to God in the Qurʾān (66:10), features also in Gnostic texts. Precisely because of her obstruction in building the Ark, the Gnosis considers her far superior to Noah himself. By setting fire to the Ark, she resists a lower god (archon), who wants to destroy her and mankind. This lower god repents and is revengeful: thus his Gnostic epithets: unknowing, foolish, blind and jealous. Noah’s wife is aware of the true gnosis (knowledge) of the highest hidden god. Apparently, the motif of the disobedience of Noah’s wife, known from the Qurʾān 66:10, existed already in the second century CE, to be transformed into a gnostic reversal.27 Of course, Christian rebuttals of this criticism of God’s behavior with the Flood can be documented in the same 2nd century as well. The emphasis upon Noah preaching repentance became stronger and stronger. A charming exegesis identifies the Greek Deucalion, who experienced a flood as well, with Noah: Δεῦτε καλεῖν (Deute kalein), “come to call”.28 Noah becomes the paragon of the prophet calling to repentance. Positive Christian interpretations of Noah remain even after the Gnostic threat has become something of the past.
26 Ephrem wrote several anti-Marcionic tracts. See: C.W. Mitchell (ed.), S. Ephraim’s Prose Refutations of Mani, Marcion and Bardaisan, Oxford 1912.
27 See more elaborated my article: ‘Rudolph Otto Revisited: Numinosity vis-à-vis Rabbinic, Patristic and Gnostic Interpretations of Scripture’, in: M. Poorthuis & J. Schwartz (eds), Purity and holiness. The heritage of Leviticus, Leiden 2000, 123–25. Noah’s wife plays a considerable role in English mystery plays. 28 Theophilus, Ad Autolycum III:19.



226 Poorthuis
8 Jewish Evidence
Pre-rabbinic Jewish interpretation of Noah, as can be found in the Jewish historian Flavius Josephus and in Philo, is outspokenly positive. Here no polemic with Marcion or Gnosis plays a role yet; Philo and Josephus want to present Noah in a favorable light vis-à-vis their non-Jewish audience.29 Similar to the later rabbinic midrash, they already insert the concept of repentance to heighten the ethical plausibility of God’s actions. Philo argues that the seven days of delay after entering the Ark refer to God’s benevolence (Questions and answers on Genesis II:13). Josephus tells that Noah was displeased by their behavior and “persuaded them to change their dispositions and their acts for the better” (Antiquities I:3:74). Later on, Rabbinic literature would strongly emphasize the opportunity to repent, conforming with the overall approach of rabbinic literature to insert repentance wherever possible.30 The 120 years mentioned in Gen. 6:3 were in fact the timespan offered for repentance, the Rabbis and Church Fathers maintain.31 Already 1 Peter 3:20 emphasizes the ‘long suffering’ of God regarding the generation of the Flood.32 Islamic authors use a wide array of sources, among which there are definitely Jewish ones. The preaching Noah, mocked by his audience, familiar already from the Qurʾān, is attested in Rabbinical sources as well:
Noah planted cedars. Passeers-by asked him: “Why do you plant trees?” He answered: “God wants to bring a flood over the world and said to me to build an ark so that I and my house can find refuge”. They laughed at him and mocked him because of his words. (. . .) When they did not repent, God brought the flood over them (Midrash Tanḥuma Noah 5, cp. Gen. Rabba 30:7).
The preaching Noah and the mocking people are highly similar to the picture in the Qurʾān (Q. 11:38), which is quoted by post-Qur’anic authors as well. The planting of the cedars is not mentioned in the Qurʾān, but both al-Ṭabarī and
29 L. Feldman, Josephus, Judaism and Christianity, Detroit 1978.
30 Mekhilta Shirata 5; Targums on Gen. 6:3. The whole book of Leviticus has been re-read in Rabbinic literature by inserting repentance. 31 Cp. J.P. Lewis, Noah and the Flood, 130, n. 8. 32 Cp. E. Grypeou & H. Spurling, The Book of Genesis in Late Antiquity, Leiden 2013, 147–198 (the Flood story in Jewish and Christian exegesis), 176. This book offers inter alia a splendid overview of Jewish and Christian views of Noah. The Marcionite and Gnostic influences are, however, somewhat neglected.



From Noah to Nuḥ: The Making of a Prophet 227
al-Thaʿlabī relate how Noah plants a huge tree, which supplied the wood for the Ark.33 Although, according to Rabbinical literature, Noah preaches ample time before the Flood itself rises, some statements portray Noah in a rather unfavorable light. Noah was righteous, but only in comparison with his wicked generation (Bab. Talmud Sanhedrin 108 a). The Biblical text: “I repent that I made them and Noah found grace in his eyes” (Gen. 6:7–8) is read as: “I repent that I made them ànd Noah” (Gen. Rabba 28:8). Probably because of the Christian appreciation of Noah as uncircumcised and as father of mankind, especially of the nonJews, Jewish texts may have reacted by downplaying Noah.34 Christian texts could not afford themselves a critical attitude to Noah because of Marcionite and Gnostic criticism of Noah. Islamic texts are also outspokenly favorable to Noah.
9 The Two Motifs from the Syriac Cave of Treasures
Although the absence of Christian elements in Islamic authors might be explained by pointing to those Jewish sources that are positive about Noah, another trajectory to account for the Jewish elements in the Islamic interpretations of Noah is plausible as well. It is clear that the Cave of Treasures itself has been influenced by Jewish midrash. In a creative transformation, it uses Jewish stories to proclaim the Christian truth.35 Islamic authors may have taken over many motifs from this Christian document, transforming, adapting and curtailing it until it fitted their Islamic narrative. However, the preaching Noah and the mocking people may have been derived from the Qurʾān directly. The planting of a tree/trees may have been derived from Jewish midrash. This cannot be maintained, however, for two other important narrative motifs: the sons of Seth descending towards the daughters of Cain, and the transfer of Adam’s body into the Ark. The motif of the sons of Seth and the daughters of Cain points to a strong influence of the Cave of Treasures upon Islamic authors, especially upon al-Thaʿlabī. The “Sons of God” are interpreted in Jewish sources such as Pirke de
33 This motif is less prominent in the Cave of Treasures. 34 Another Rabbinic device to counter Christian claims is to present Noah as born circumcised (Tanḥuma Noah 5).
35 E.g. Adam as priest, prophet and king has its Jewish precedent in Moses as priest, prophet and king (see Philo, Life of Moses II.1), but in this text, these themes will be connected to Christ via the frankincense, the myrrh and the gold.



228 Poorthuis
rabbi Eliezer as fallen angels, in conformity with older Jewish explanations as found in 1 Enoch and related sources. Only some centuries after midrash Pirke de rabbi Eliezer (9th century), Jewish writers explicitly quote the Christian interpretation of the sons of Seth descending towards the daughters of Cain.36 Hence the Christian Syriac Cave of Treasures must have been the ultimate source of Islamic writers who use this motif, probably via earlier Islamic transmitters. In addition, the central event related both by Christian and by Islamic sources of the removal of Adam’s body from the Cave of Treasures into the Ark (and, according to Christian sources, later on to the place of Golgotha), has no Jewish counterpart either. Adam’s body is not carried into the Ark according to Jewish tradition! Hence there is no doubt that in these cases the Christian Syriac text of the Cave of Treasures has been the main source of Islamic writers, such as al-Thaʿlabī and al-Ṭabarī. The debate in al-Ṭabarī whether the body will finally rest in Jerusalem points to a close proximity to Christian traditions. Adam’s body in Jerusalem is, as we have noted, an essential element in Christian theology. Of course this has been suppressed by Islamic tradition, but the motif of Adam’s body in the Ark and even his transport to Jerusalem has survived. The fact that both these writers draw upon the Cave of Treasures, but in different ways, and that al-Thaʿlabī cannot be dependent upon al-Ṭabarī in the case of the sons of Seth and the daughters of Cain,37 proves that the Cave of Treasures has served as a source of inspiration for Islamic writers several times.
10 Conclusion
The foregoing allows for the following reconstruction:
1. The Hebrew Bible on Noah does not mention explicitly the possibility of repentance. Early Jewish and Christian literature inserted the concept of
36 See: Grünbaum, Neue Beiträge, 75. Some early traces of the identification of the “sons of God” with “noble men” may be detected in Gen. Rabba 26:5, but the relationship with the sons of Seth coming from the mountain is not developed. The Jewish Chronicles of Jerahmeel, a ‘retold Bible’, not before the 12th century, documents the story of the daughters of Cain and the Sons of Seth explicitly (XXIV:11). Because of its contrast with the rest of this midrash, which draws upon the “sons of God” as angels, this may well be interpolated from a Christian document. Cf. Grypeou & H. Spurling, Book of Genesis in Late Antiquity, 147–198 (the Flood story in Jewish and Christian exegesis), esp. 185. 37 For a more general comparison between al-Ṭabarī and al-Thaʾlabī and the differences between their respective stories, see: Wolfensohn, Kaʾb al-Aḥbar, 68.



From Noah to Nuḥ: The Making of a Prophet 229
repentance to stress the prophetic nature of Noah’s behavior. In contrast, the people’s reaction, mocking Noah, was introduced. 2. Because of Marcionite and Gnostic criticism of God’s behavior, Christian tradition strongly developed this appeal to repentance. 3. Rabbinic literature likewise stressed the concept of repentance, but allowed itself more criticism of Noah, due to the Christian extolling of Noah as a type of Christ and as an uncircumcised father of the gentiles. 4. Christianity developed the (possibly embryonic Jewish) idea of the sons of Seth on the mountain mingling with the daughters of Cain in the plain as an exegesis of Genesis 6. 5. Syriac Christianity became heir both of the emphasis upon repentance against Marcionite and gnostic disparagement of God’s behavior, and of the explanation of the Sons of god as sons of Seth upon the mountain slopes of paradise, descending to mingle with the daughters of Cain, the latter reveling in demonic music, debauchery and walking nude. 6. Islamic authors from the 9th century onward harmonized these accounts with the Qur’anic account of the people mocking Noah. The exegesis of Genesis 6:1 had lost their significance and likewise the polemic with Marcion and Gnosis in that context.38 Instead, the embellishments of the Noah story became a parallel story to Muhammad’s experiences with his preaching. 7. Whereas al-Ṭabarī displayed already some elements from pre-Qur’anic Syriac traditions, it is al-Thalabī who integrated most of the Syriac narrative tradition into a single whole, interspersed with Qur’anic quotes. Al-Kisaʾī showed less traces of the Cave of Treasures, but developed a Muhammadocentric reading of the story of Noah. The integration of earlier narrative elements and a ‘Muhammadocentric’ reading of earlier Christian traditions, by removing all too ‘Christian’ elements, can be considered as a creative re-reading of older traditions in the light of the Qurʾān, in order to achieve their Islamization.
38 Still, Islamic anti-heretical tracts do refer to Marcion. See: M. Frenschkowski, ‘Marcion in arabischen Quellen’, in: G. May & K. Greschat (eds), Marcion und seine kirchengeschichtliche Wirkung, Berlin 2002, 39–63.

An Early Modern Young Prophet: The Heavenly Messages of Evert Willemsz Bogaert and their Recognition, 1622–23
Willem Frijhoff
Prophecy, as is well known, has never been limited to adults or to gender roles, to religious trends, geographic areas, or specific time periods. From the viewpoint of the cultural historian, prophecy is the (mostly individual) expression of a strong, often dramatic concern about the future that aims at alerting a community and persuading them to change their life-style or their views and values, by appealing to a higher, ultimate authority. As a rule, a prophet has an acute sense of what goes on in his or her community. He or she benefits from a pre-existing readiness to believe the messenger and to adopt the views on past, present and future brought up by the prophet. The more so as prophets tend to frame their message in a familiar symbolic language that by its timeless content and its references to former instances of successful prophecy resists the wear of time and makes the message credible. But structures of credibility are always embedded in the practical forms which communication adopts in a given time and space. It is precisely the topical form taken by the prophet’s agency in the communicative process that interests the historian. In this contribution I want to present and briefly analyse a case of early modern prophecy that shows with a particular clarity how such prophets benefit from the preexisting structures and symbolic language of prophetic agency and capitalize on the current affairs of their religious and secular communities.1
1 An Unprivileged Youngster
During the summer of 1622 and the following winter, the small Holland town of Woerden on the Rhine was buzzing with rumours. A fifteen year old boy called Evert Willemsz [Evert, son of Willem], native of the town, had been assailed by a sudden illness, physical problems, and a spiritual experience. He claimed to
1 For a more general approach to prophecy in early modern Holland I may refer to my study ‘Prophétie et société dans les Provinces-Unies aux XVIIe et XVIIIe siècles, in: M.S. DupontBouchat, W. Frijhoff, R. Muchembled, Prophètes et sorciers dans les Pays-Bas aux XVIIe et XVIIIe siècles, Paris 1978, 263–362.



An Early Modern Young Prophet 231
communicate, through an angel, with his heavenly Father. The boy lived at the local orphanage, together with his older brother Pieter and two younger halfbrothers. Cornelis (d. 1669), their eldest brother, probably trained as a tailor, lived nearby in town. Evert’s family name was Bogaert, but as a literate man he later used only its Latin form Bogardus – so at Leiden University, where he matriculated in 1627, and in the small, newly founded town of New Amsterdam on the island of Manhattan, present-day New York, where in 1632 he finally was appointed a minister of God’s Holy Word. He worked there, in the immense territory that was his parish, until his sudden, unexpected death in a shipwreck in September 1647, on the way back to his fatherland for the settlement of a long-standing quarrel with the governor of the colony. By then, he was only 40 years old.2 The family members he left in the new world – his own four young sons, his Scandinavian wife Annetgen Jans, and her five surviving children from a previous marriage – may have been aware of his prophetic past in Holland, but apparently they have not cherished it in their historic memory. His troublesome American life course, with its own prophetic accents, had become for them a memorable event in its own right. We have no certain information concerning Evert’s parents. He presumably lost his natural father Willem Bogaert when he was very young. He was certainly educated by his stepfather Muysevoet, who must have died, just as Evert’s natural mother did, some years before the onset of the boy’s spiritual
2 For an extensive and detailed analysis of Bogardus’s spiritual experience and his subsequent life course in Europe (the Dutch Republic), West Africa (the Guinea Coast), and America (New Netherland), see W. Frijhoff, Wegen van Evert Willemsz. Een Hollands weeskind op zoek naar zichzelf, 1607–1647, Nijmegen 1995; translated by M. Heerspink Scholz in a shorter version as Fulfilling God’s Mission: The Two Worlds of Dominie Everardus Bogardus 1607–1647 (The Atlantic World, XIV), Leiden/Boston, 2007. The first, Woerden-related part of this book has been re-issued in a new version as Evert Willemsz. Een zeventiende-eeuws weeskind op zoek naar zichzelf, Nijmegen 2013. I refer to that study for references to the local source material and the secular and spiritual background of the events. The theoretical issues of this biography have been discussed by W. Frijhoff, ‘The Improbable Biography: Uncommon Sources, a Moving Identity, a Plural Story?’, in: V.R. Berghahn, S. Simone Lässig (eds), Biography between Structure and Agency: Central European Lives in International Historiography (Studies in German History, 9), Oxford/New York 2008, 215–233. The story had already been pointed out by C.J.W. Gravendaal, ‘Een wonder te Woerden?’, in: Heemtijdinghen 7:2 (June 1971), 13–15, and critically by A.Th. van Deursen, Plain Lives in a Golden Age: Popular culture, religion and society in seventeenth-century Holland, Cambridge 1991, 272. On the American mythology surrounding Rev. Bogardus’s life: W. Frijhoff, ‘Emblematic myths: Anneke’s fortune, Bogardus’s farewell, and Kieft’s son’, in: L. Cruz, W. Frijhoff (eds), Myth in History, History in Myth: Proceeedings of the Third International Conference of the Society for Netherlandic History (New York: June 5–6, 2006) (Brill’s Studies in Intellectual History, 182), Leiden/Boston 2009, 117–146.



232 Frijhoff
experience, perhaps in the plague year of 1617–18. The four younger sons were then put in the town orphanage of Woerden. As the administration of the Woerden orphan chamber makes no mention of any property, the family must have been without means. Other members of his stepfather’s family are known to have been small artisans, mostly shoemakers. Evert’s stepfather, too, may have been involved in one of the wooden trades, as a carpenter perhaps, a cabinet-maker, or a cooper. Without parents or a local family, without money or property, and without any proper education, the boy’s chances of climbing the social ladder were virtually non-existent. But Evert had something else; he had intelligence, and he had faith. And he was able to secure the recognition of his heavenly messages by capitalizing on a local network of co-religionists and sympathizers.
2 Bodily Experience and Heavenly Messages
We know what happened to Evert Willemsz in 1622–23 from two contemporary pamphlets, reprinted several times.3 Their initial editor was Lucas Zas (c. 15911636), the headmaster of the small local grammar school, and an eye-witness of the events. In a certain sense, however, the pamphlets’ author was Evert himself, for the text consists largely of the messages he wrote down during his spiritual experience, when he was temporarily smitten with deafness and dumbness (see the diagram). At this time, he communicated with the people around him via written notes, referred to in the pamphlets as ‘copies’ (kopije, i.e. literal transcripts), and printed in a larger font. Some slips of paper contained the questions and answers pertaining to a dialogue held between Evert and those who came to discuss with him. Other notes contained the spiritual messages, which he had written down on impulse, following an angel’s appearance or an ecstatic experience. Evert’s own version of the summer experience of 1622, written down some weeks afterwards in two long hymns, was also included.
3 [Lucas Zas], Waerachtighe ende seeckere gheschiedenisse, dewelcke is gheschiedt binnen de Stadt Woerden., hoe dat Godt almachtich zijn Wonder-werck heeft betoont aen een seecker Weeskindt genaemt Evert Willemsz. hetwelck hy heeft gesocht met doofheyt ende stomheyt, ende hoe dat hem wondere dingen zijn wedervaren, dewelcke hem, so hy schrijft, vanden Engel Gods zijn geopenbaert [. . .], Utrecht 1623 [The Hague, Royal Library, Pamphlet 3501; another printing in the Library of the University of Amsterdam]; second, enlarged edition Amsterdam 1623 [The Hague, Royal Library, Pamphlet 3500, another printing: Pamphlet 3500a]; 5th edition Arnhem 1623 [Münster in Westfalen, Universitäts- und Landesbibliothek].

Diagram Times, rhythms, and modalities of Evert’s spiritual experience.



234 Frijhoff
In order to understand the first calling of the later minister, we have to examine what happened to the boy in the spring of 1622. Having completed his elementary education, and while continuing to live in the orphanage, Evert was apprenticed to Master Gijsbert Aelbertsz, a tailor who had a workshop a few streets away, on the river Rhine. The orphanage and the workshop were distinct sites, but they were quite similar in atmosphere. Evert had virtually fulfilled his two-year apprenticeship with this master, whom he loved greatly for his piety and for his readiness to discuss God’s Word during their work together. It may have been this very companionship that revealed to him his true vocation: to be not a tailor, but a minister of the Word. According to the story in the pamphlets, Evert had for some time been seriously ill. He had hardly recovered when other physical symptoms made their appearance. During the nine days between 21 and 30 June 1622 he neither ate nor drank. He even isolated himself from the community. Evert’s refusal to attend the common meals, which served as a daily aggregation ritual for the community, was for him the most efficient way to gain the attention of both the orphans and the trustees. The pamphlets show clearly how concerned the matron of the orphanage was about his well-being, and illustrate her embarrassment concerning the disarray in the group caused by Evert’s dealings with heaven. This first phase of physical isolation was followed by a second phase, which lasted 70 days, from 30 June to 8 September. Evert suddenly became both deaf and dumb. He could neither hear nor speak, and occasionally even lost his sight, ‘as also for a long time the proper use of his reason’. This phase of physical paralysis, the loss of almost all his senses, suggests a slowly intensifying spiritual struggle, leading naturally to a crucial moment of transition. It also prepared Evert for a third, ecstatic phase, which again lasted nine days, from 8 to 17 September 1622. Evert began fasting again; still he could neither hear nor speak. This final phase began with the appearance of an angel of the Lord, and ended with a second visitation. This double appearance points to the true meaning of this episode, that is: communication with heaven. The angel delivered to him a message from the Father (Evert’s heavenly Father, who had taken the place of his earthly father): he had to convert people and warn them to repent and renounce their sins. According to the angel, the heavenly source of the message and the veracity of Evert’s encounter with heaven would be proven by his deliverance from the physical disabilities God had visited upon him, and by his full recovery. Incidentally, the angel also conveyed a second message, meant for Evert himself. On September 17th, just before his first redemption from illness, he wrote the following message:



An Early Modern Young Prophet 235
I hope that God will release me this night so that I may again hear and again speak: I do not know this by myself, but through the Spirit of God, which will enlighten me. [. .] If He has the power to inflict things upon me, He also has the power to deliver me again: for do we not read in God’s word that He made the deaf hear, the blind see, the crippled walk, the dumb speak [Mt. 11:5; 15:30]? [. .] Does He not then have the power to give back two of my five senses? Oh yes, I have had that trust and I still have it. As soon as I have recovered my speech and my hearing, it pleases God and the Spirit of God that I go to school until the time has come for me to do the work through which I shall be blessed. I intend then to become a minister and nothing else. Then you will see what the Spirit of God will work through me. I no longer must sew, once I have finished my clothes; for it pleases God almighty and the Spirit of God that such shall no longer be my work. I must fear the Lord, as the angel of the Lord has commanded me, and this I must do.
After the angel’s first appearance, Evert went into a kind of trance, a long period of ecstatic writing. For days on end he wrote down his heavenly communications on little slips of paper, mostly simple messages of a repetitious nature:
Spread the word, spread the word, for God is sorely displeased that word of his wondrous works is not spread. Oh spread the word, oh my dear friends, I beg of you, spread the word, for God is displeased that his divine things are not communicated throughout the whole world. Spread the word, then, oh spread the word.
The message was as simple as the outlook on the world of a young tailor’s apprentice could be. He repeated it throughout the pamphlets. There are good men and bad; God wants the good to repent; therefore, his word must be spread, and the signs read. In the background we can detect a very simplified form of belief in double predestination, as defended by orthodox Calvinism and confirmed three years earlier by the Synod of Dordrecht. It resembled the everyday theological discussions at grassroots level in Woerden, which crop up in documents concerning the confessional struggles in that deeply divided town and whose message was: the bad are damned and the good elect, but God will punish even the good if they do not publicly behave as his flock of perfect believers.



236 Frijhoff
3 Legitimizations
Evert’s messages were easily legitimized for the faithful, because the signs of God’s wrath were quite near: repeated plagues, scarcity (implying famine), and war. This traditional triad was presaged by the famous comet of 1618 (the comet of Halley), interpreted as God’s ‘rod’. But the most important sign of all in Woerden was, of course, ‘God’s wondrous work’ in his faithful child Evert Willemsz, as the second pamphlet about his agency announces it proudly in its title. Having first smitten him with sickness and deprived him of the use of his bodily functions, He would now sovereignly deliver him at a moment of His own choosing, announced by an angel. To believe in the truth of Evert’s spiritual experience was, therefore, to believe in God’s work in his elect. Evert had no doubt about his own election. The affliction which God had brought upon him proved it: God afflicts the one He loves. Evert felt akin to Christ, who too had suffered for his Father but had finally reconciled his will to God’s will (Lk. 22:42). He stated his firm confidence to belong to the 144,000 elect who would sing the hymn of the Lamb (Rev. 14:1–5; 15:3). But unlike some other contemporary prophets, he did not take the place of Christ. Although he may have been tempted by the role of a divine mediator, in the end he remained in his human place, as a simple messenger and minister of God. Evert’s texts reveal a form of youthful radicalism that related well with the strong positions taken by the Calvinist adherents of the double predestination who dominated the Reformed community in Woerden. However, he was not naive. He followed the apocalyptic mainstream of orthodox Protestantism but kept his eyes fixed upon his own destiny. He utilized current conventions to achieve autonomy. He willingly used church and civil authority to attain his divine calling, but he refused to become the unresisting victim of higher powers. In fact, Evert’s first spiritual experience and the events related to it had not exceeded the limits of the orphan community, and he needed a wider audience. But of course his activities drew attention in the town, all the more so since the young man had now found a convinced supporter in Master Lucas Zas, who helped him to voice his experiences and to realize his calling. Master Zas had worked as a teacher of Latin and French at Utrecht, the old capital of the former bishopric, and in the nearby small town of Montfoort. He probably taught Evert French after school time, as was customary for gifted boys attending the Latin grammar school. Apart from publishing the two pamphlets related to Evert, he also wrote a play on parental responsibility for children’s education and the choice of a profession, Borgerlijcke Huyshoudingh (Rotterdam 1628). This pious play includes a panegyric on the sacred ministry,



An Early Modern Young Prophet 237
which may have inspired young Evert’s views on his own ecclesiastical calling. In 1631, Zas edited a rhymed translation of Juan Luis Vives’ rules of life, the Introduction to True Wisdom. In the introductory poems, he overtly criticized the hypocrisy of the new Calvinist elites. In his person, neo-Stoicism and the Bible came together. Master Zas was Woerden’s independent intellectual, the ideal partner for the independent believer Evert must have been: a convinced Calvinist, but always insisting on his own responsibility in the design of his life. Zas came running whenever Evert had him called; he collected the messages and had them printed. He understood what was happening. Evert had good reason to choose headmaster Zas; his call to repentance was intended for outsiders, but for his real calling, the sacred ministry, he had first to attend the Latin school before being able to matriculate at the Theological College or the University at Leiden. By joining the headmaster, Evert used the impact which the event made on the local community to give his life a new direction. However, to be credible, Evert’s calling had to be legitimated above all by a higher authority. This legitimization, as announced by an angel of the Lord, was to take place through a ritual of redemption. For Evert, the healing process was the expression of his supernatural calling made visible to man. Evert’s first redemption took place nine days after the beginning of his ecstatic experience – a spiritual metaphor of the nine months of gestation preceding his rebirth as a Christian convert. Evert himself spoke of his ‘shedding the old Adam, in order to begin a new life, in all virtue and godliness’. He adopts here a characteristically pietistic idiom, in which illness was closely linked to sin, just as recovery was to conversion from a sinful life – to the regeneration of the old Adam as a true Christian, instigated solely by the Holy Ghost. Evert, then, was spiritually reborn in a way similar to his physical birth fifteen years before. In the almighty God, who caused his rebirth, he recognized his new and only father. The place of this rebirth was the orphanage, where the orphans, the matron, and rector Zas stood around him. Singing together Psalm 100, they helped to bear his spiritual birth pains, as he had predicted in one of his notes. Carried away by the dynamics of the ritual, all of a sudden Evert found himself singing with them. He had recovered his powers of speech and hearing, and was now completely his previous self, reborn as a new Adam. Three days later, the magistracy of Woerden, convinced that God Himself supported Evert’s words, authorized him to leave the tailor’s shop and to attend the Latin school, without having to earn his own living any more.



238 Frijhoff
4 Community Involvements
In itself, Evert’s message was not, of course, sufficient to win the unconditional trust of all the people around him. Evert’s spiritual dealings provoked scepticism and resistance in town. As the boy himself pointed out in several messages, critics threatened to beat his deafness and dumbness out of him. In fact, Woerden was not in all respects an average Dutch town. It was a profoundly divided town, torn apart by three competing confessions: Lutheranism, liberal Arminianism, and orthodox Calvinism, the latter established only three years before at the Synod of Dort (1618–19). Confronted with two oppositional groups, who both claimed precedence and good faith and were probably much more numerous, but invested with the secular power in the town since the political intervention of the stadtholder, prince Maurice of Orange, in September 1618, the orthodox Calvinists were badly in need of a justification of their claims. Within this religious context, Evert’s spiritual experience was bound to be interpreted in a partisan way as that of an orthodox believer. We do not know for certain whether his parents were orthodox Calvinists, but the Woerden orphanage functioned as one of the main agencies of Calvinization. Evert’s elder brother Cornelis Bogaert frequented orthodox circles at Leiden. He married the sister of a radical young Calvinist with a Flemish background, Cornelis Paludanus who in his adolescence actively combated Arminianism at Woerden, taught himself theology, and obtained a parish near Woerden. Evert’s younger half-brother Pieter Muysevoet became an orthodox schoolmaster in the nearby village of Linschoten. Evert himself obtained in 1632 his ministry at New Amsterdam because he was a favourite of the orthodox party within the West India Company and within the Amsterdam consistory, which then had the authority to appoint ministers in churches overseas. Young Evert may have been inspired by his relative Paludanus’ fervour, but he was astute enough to reject the difficult path of the self-taught theologian. He claimed admission to the grammar school and the university, which would permit him to quit the small, narrow-minded community of Woerden Calvinism and spread his wings in the larger world, first on the Gold Coast in Africa (the Coast of Guinea, present-day Ghana), then in America. In fact, Evert and his two brothers must have felt close to a zealous Calvinist minister called Vincent Muysevoet or Meusevoet (1560–1624), probably a brother of their (step)father and therefore their uncle. He was the son of a Flemish shoemaker, who, as a convinced Calvinist, had fled in exile to Norwich in England, just like many other fellow Protestants. Back in Holland in 1586, Vincent served, first, as a minister at Zevenhoven near Woerden, and then at Schagen near Alkmaar, where he terrorized the Arminians.



An Early Modern Young Prophet 239
Between 1598 and his death in 1624 Vincent Meusevoet translated more than thirty Puritan and pietistic treatises from English into Dutch, including virtually all the writings by the famous Puritan divine William Perkins (1558–1602), three by the Genevan theologian Theodorus Beza (1519–1605), and several others by King James I. In 1621, immediately preceding Evert’s spiritual experience, Vincent published his own translation of the apocalyptic treatise Apocalypsis Apocalypseos (Latin version: Frankfurt 1609; earlier English translations: Amsterdam 1611 and 1615) by the influential Puritan theologian Thomas Brightman (1562–1607) who has been called one of the ‘high priests of chiliasm’. Directly or indirectly, this erudite work, much read and commented upon in Puritan and pietistic circles, may well have influenced the young boy’s prophetic orientation. Indeed, Meusevoet has played a leading part in introducing Puritan pietism in the Netherlands. Hence Evert’s familiarity with pietistic idiom and the Puritan doctrine of regeneration. He may as well have read Meusevoet’s translation (1599) of Perkins’ treatise on the spiritual meaning of sickness and death, and applied it to his own physical disabilities. In the second phase of his spiritual experience, Evert’s aim was no longer to promote himself as an agent of God, but to ensure the adherence of as many social groups as possible to his heavenly message. Indeed, as a candidate for adulthood he now had to submit himself to the public, reasoned acknowledgement of his fellow adults. In Woerden many still remained rather sceptical about what had happened to him. It was therefore vital that he finished his task by making his experiences credible to unbelievers. The second stage of his spiritual adventure was therefore more emphatically instrumental in character than the first, and the local community’s expectations were more heavily involved. Four months after his first regeneration, in the morning of Wednesday, 18 January 1623, Evert awoke with a severe headache. Refusing to eat or drink, he predicted that he would again lose the ability to hear and speak. This, indeed, occurred around noon. This time the loss of his senses lasted only three days. Evert’s experience was now more intense and more exhausting, and his message was passed on quickly enough. Surrounded by his fellow orphans Evert called one by one the representatives of the various social institutions within his horizon into a room that had been put at his disposal. Together they legitimized his actions before the whole town. First came the matron, as a representative of the household; then Evert’s older brother, as the next of kin and representative of the family; next, the rector of the Latin school, the main representative of literate culture in the town; subsequently, his former employer, the master tailor Gijsbert Aelbertsz; then the ruling mayor, the building contractor Jan Florisz van Wijngaarden;



240 Frijhoff
and, finally, in the name of the church council, minister Henricus Alutarius. The whole community in all its branches, lay and clerical, public and private, paraded symbolically past Evert’s chair. Evert’s second redemption was public in nature. It again occurred in the orphanage, though this time Evert announced it well in advance. He made certain that not only the rector but also both ministers were present – three qualified and critical witnesses. When, at a particular moment, he saw the other orphans writing and talking around the hearth fire (it was in the middle of a cold winter), his consciousness was reactivated. He wrote on a slip of paper that the rector and the ministers should be fetched, and then asked everybody present to sing a psalm – a most appropriate one indeed, apparently found by opening the Bible at random: ‘Out of babes and sucklings hast thou ordained strength . . .’ (Ps. 8:2). During the singing, Evert successively regained the use of his various senses until he left the state of babyhood mentioned in the psalm and sang along with the rector and the ministers as an adult. For the ministers, this was sufficient proof. Evert’s authenticity was warranted by the visible reality of God publicly intervening in the life of an individual, an event perceived, and hence sanctioned, by the community present. On the next day, a Sunday, Evert was allowed to read in church, before the whole congregation, the responses of the Heidelberg Catechism to Question 35, Sunday 14. This, again, was an appropriate text: ‘What do we call what is received from the Holy Ghost?’ Had Evert not repeatedly emphasized that the Holy Ghost was working through him? We may consider this reading as the final rite of passage by which Evert gained official entry into public life. From this point onwards, he was empowered to speak in public with adults. However, this confirmation of the change in the orientation of his life provoked again harsh criticism in Woerden town. Evert then played his last trump card. Two nights later, his two little brothers who slept next to him in the same bed, as well as the other orphans, were awakened by Evert talking aloud in his sleep. Someone went downstairs to fetch the matron. She anxiously woke Evert and asked him whether something was wrong. Evert calmed everyone down and persuaded them to go back to bed. He had hardly fallen asleep himself when he again started speaking out loud, as if he were dreaming. His brother Pieter Bogaert, five years older than he and also still an inmate of the orphanage (not to be confused with their younger half-brother Pieter Muysevoet), obviously understood what Evert was up to. He had a pen, a piece of paper and a candle ready at hand, and wrote down Evert’s long, rhymed message. Its beginning and end perfectly set the tone:



An Early Modern Young Prophet 241
Woe, oh woe, people who live in pride and excess, Oh, ye people mean and heartless, Your lives today are so godless. [. . .] And so that people know Through the work of God the Lord That through me they should repent.
This account of Evert’s dream brought the last Calvinist critics over into Evert’s camp. God Himself, as the author of dreams, here legitimated Evert’s spiritual experiences and the direction which he wanted to give to his life, and to his public mission. The next day, the burgomasters called the witnesses, including Evert’s brothers, before them, authenticated the story, and ordered the church council to have it published as quickly as possible in Amsterdam by an orthodox Calvinist bookseller. This bookseller was Marten Jansz Brandt (c. 1590–1649), the privileged printer of the party of the praxis pietatis but also of the newly founded West India Company, who had established his shop next to the city hall and the New Church under the telling sign ‘The Reformed Catechism’. And that, indeed, is what happened. The pamphlet even got a second printing at Amsterdam, and another version was issued some weeks later at Arnhem, the seat of the provincial government of Gelderland, where liberal and orthodox Calvinists also opposed each other fiercely. During some weeks, Evert was the talk of the day in the world of orthodox Calvinism and early pietism, comforted in their political struggle for recognition by the help of his heavenly messages, and sharing his hopes for the future of a godly community.
